# F

# Introduction

<!-- chunk -->

### or more than twenty-five years as a psychiatrist and neuroscience

<!-- chunk -->

### researcher, I have been asked the question “What causes mental

<!-- chunk -->

### illness?” countless times by patients and their family members. When I

<!-- chunk -->

### first began my career, I would give long answers that made me sound

<!-- chunk -->

### educated and competent. I’d talk about neurotransmitters, hormones,

<!-- chunk -->

### genetics, and stress. I would describe the treatments we would be using and

<!-- chunk -->

### offer hope that they would make things better. After a few years of this,

<!-- chunk -->

### however, I began to feel like a fraud. You see, people often weren’t getting

<!-- chunk -->

### that much better. Treatments would sometimes work for a few months, or

<!-- chunk -->

### even a year or two, but more often than not, the symptoms would come

<!-- chunk -->

### back. At some point, I began telling people the simple truth: “No one knows

<!-- chunk -->

### what causes mental illness.” Although we understand many risk factors, no

<!-- chunk -->

### one knows how they all fit together. I still tried to offer hope by assuring

<!-- chunk -->

### people that we had many different treatments at our disposal and that we

<!-- chunk -->

### would try one after another until we found one that worked. Sadly, for many

<!-- chunk -->

### of my patients, we never did.

<!-- chunk -->

### That all changed for me in 2016 when I helped a patient lose weight.

<!-- chunk -->

### Tom was a thirty-three-year-old man with schizoaffective disorder, a cross

<!-- chunk -->

### between schizophrenia and bipolar disorder. He had suffered from

<!-- chunk -->

### hallucinations, delusions, and mental anguish every day of his life over the

<!-- chunk -->

### last

<!-- chunk -->

### thirteen years. He was tormented by his illness. He had tried seventeen

<!-- chunk -->

### different medications, but none had worked. The medications sedated him,

<!-- chunk -->

### which reduced his anxiety and agitation, but they didn’t stop his

<!-- chunk -->

### hallucinations or delusions. What’s more, they’d caused him to gain over

<!-- chunk -->

### one hundred pounds. He had long been plagued by low self-esteem, and

<!-- chunk -->

### being so overweight only added to this. He had become a near hermit, and

<!-- chunk -->

### our weekly sessions were some of his only excursions into the outside

<!-- chunk -->

### world. This is partly why I agreed to help him lose weight: I was the doctor

<!-- chunk -->

### he saw most often, and he wasn’t in the market for a referral to a specialist

<!-- chunk -->

### he’d never met. More to the point, it was highly unusual for him to take

<!-- chunk -->

### action to improve his health in some way. Maybe losing weight could help

<!-- chunk -->

### him gain a sense of control over his life. After experimenting with several

<!-- chunk -->

### approaches without success, we decided to try the ketogenic diet—a diet

<!-- chunk -->

### low in carbohydrates, moderate in protein, and high in fat.

<!-- chunk -->

### Within weeks, not only had Tom lost weight, but I began to notice

<!-- chunk -->

### remarkable and dramatic changes in his psychiatric symptoms. He was less

<!-- chunk -->

### depressed and less sedated. He began making more eye contact, and when

<!-- chunk -->

### he did, I saw a presence and spark there that I had never seen before. Most

<!-- chunk -->

### astonishingly, after two months, he told me that his longstanding

<!-- chunk -->

### hallucinations were receding and that he was rethinking his many paranoid

<!-- chunk -->

### conspiracy theories. He began to realize that they weren’t true and probably

<!-- chunk -->

### never had been. Tom went on to lose 150 pounds, move out of his father’s

<!-- chunk -->

### home, and complete a certificate program. He was even able to perform

<!-- chunk -->

### improv in front of a live audience, something that would have been

<!-- chunk -->

### impossible for him prior to the diet.

<!-- chunk -->

### I was flabbergasted. I had never seen anything like this in my entire

<!-- chunk -->

### career. While it’s possible that losing weight might reduce anxiety or

<!-- chunk -->

### depression in some people, this man had a psychotic disorder that had

<!-- chunk -->

### resisted more than a decade of treatment. Nothing in my knowledge or

<!-- chunk -->

### experience suggested that the ketogenic diet would treat his symptoms.

<!-- chunk -->

### There seemed to be no reason it should.

<!-- chunk -->

### I began digging into the medical literature and discovered that the

<!-- chunk -->

### ketogenic diet is a longstanding, evidence-based treatment for epilepsy. It

<!-- chunk -->

### can

<!-- chunk -->

### stop seizures even when medications fail to. I quickly realized an important

<!-- chunk -->

### connection—we use epilepsy treatments in psychiatry all the time. They

<!-- chunk -->

### include medications like Depakote, Neurontin, Lamictal, Topamax, Valium,

<!-- chunk -->

### Klonopin, and Xanax. If this diet also stops seizures, maybe that’s why it

<!-- chunk -->

### was helping Tom. Based on this additional information, I began using the

<!-- chunk -->

### ketogenic diet as a treatment with other patients and when it continued to be

<!-- chunk -->

### successful, I soon found myself collaborating with researchers around the

<!-- chunk -->

### world to explore it further, speaking globally on this topic, and publishing

<!-- chunk -->

### papers in academic journals demonstrating its effectiveness.

<!-- chunk -->

### I set out on a journey to understand how and why this diet worked for

<!-- chunk -->

### my patients. Along with its use in epilepsy, the ketogenic diet is also used in

<!-- chunk -->

### treating obesity and diabetes, and is even being pursued as a treatment for

<!-- chunk -->

### Alzheimer’s disease. At first, this was confusing and a bit overwhelming.

<!-- chunk -->

### Why would one treatment work for all of these disorders, even if only in

<!-- chunk -->

### some people? Ultimately, it was this question that opened the door to

<!-- chunk -->

### something much bigger than the inquiry I’d begun with. It forced me to

<!-- chunk -->

### uncover the connections between these different disorders and integrate this

<!-- chunk -->

### understanding with everything that I already knew as a neuroscientist and

<!-- chunk -->

### psychiatrist. When I finally put all the pieces together, I realized that I had

<!-- chunk -->

### stumbled upon something beyond my wildest dreams. I had developed a

<!-- chunk -->

### unifying theory for the cause of all mental illnesses. I call it the theory of

<!-- chunk -->

### brain energy.

<!-- chunk -->

### This is not a book about the ketogenic diet—or any diet at all. It’s also not

<!-- chunk -->

### solely concerned with serious mental illness; the scientific insights in this

<!-- chunk -->

### book apply to mild depression and anxiety as well. In fact, it may change

<!-- chunk -->

### the way you think about all human emotions and experiences. I am not

<!-- chunk -->

### offering a simple cure-all for mental illness, or advocating for any single

<!-- chunk -->

### treatment. The unexpected effectiveness of that particular treatment was

<!-- chunk -->

### only the first clue that started me on the path to a new way of understanding

<!-- chunk -->

### mental illness. This book will share that understanding with you, taking you

<!-- chunk -->

### on a journey that I hope will transform the way you think about mental

<!-- chunk -->

### illness and mental health.

<!-- chunk -->

### Here’s a quick overview of what’s in store:

<!-- chunk -->

### •I’ll begin by reviewing where we are now in the mental health field:

<!-- chunk -->

### the problems and questions that plague us, and why they matter.

<!-- chunk -->

### •You’ll learn something that may seem shocking—mental disorders

<!-- chunk -->

### are not distinct entities. This includes diagnoses like depression,

<!-- chunk -->

### anxiety, PTSD, OCD, ADHD, alcoholism, opioid addiction, eating

<!-- chunk -->

### disorders, autism, bipolar disorder, and schizophrenia. There is

<!-- chunk -->

### tremendous overlap in symptoms for different disorders, and many

<!-- chunk -->

### people are diagnosed with more than one. And even among disorders

<!-- chunk -->

### with symptoms that are very different, the underlying biological,

<!-- chunk -->

### psychological, and social factors overlap significantly.

<!-- chunk -->

### •I’ll explore the surprising connections between mental disorders and

<!-- chunk -->

### some physical ones such as obesity, diabetes, heart attacks, strokes,

<!-- chunk -->

### pain disorders, Alzheimer’s disease, and epilepsy. In order to truly

<!-- chunk -->

### understand what causes mental illness, these connections need to be

<!-- chunk -->

### understood as well.

<!-- chunk -->

### •This will all come together to reveal that mental disorders are

<!-- chunk -->

### metabolic disorders of the brain.

<!-- chunk -->

### •In order to understand what this means, you’ll need to understand

<!-- chunk -->

### metabolism. It’s a lot more complicated than most people realize, but

<!-- chunk -->

### I’ll do my best to make it as simple as possible. Tiny things called

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondria are key. Metabolism and mitochondria can explain all

<!-- chunk -->

### the symptoms of mental illness.

<!-- chunk -->

### •I’ll discuss the differences between normal mental states and mental

<!-- chunk -->

### disorders. For example, we all experience anxiety, depression, and

<!-- chunk -->

### fear at different times in our lives. These experiences aren’t disorders

<!-- chunk -->

### —they are a normal part of being human. However, when these

<!-- chunk -->

### things happen at the wrong time or in an exaggerated way, they may

<!-- chunk -->

### cross the line from mental state to mental disorder. You’ll

<!-- chunk -->

### see that all mental states, even normal ones, relate to metabolism. For

<!-- chunk -->

### example, “stress” is a mental state that affects metabolism—it takes a

<!-- chunk -->

### metabolic toll. If it occurs for prolonged periods of time or is

<!-- chunk -->

### extreme, it can lead to mental illness. But so can anything else that

<!-- chunk -->

### affects your metabolism.

<!-- chunk -->

### •I will share with you five broad mechanisms of action that can

<!-- chunk -->

### explain the clinical and neuroscientific observations we see in all

<!-- chunk -->

### mental disorders.

<!-- chunk -->

### •I’ll show you that all the known contributing factors to mental

<!-- chunk -->

### illness, including things like genetics, inflammation,

<!-- chunk -->

### neurotransmitters, hormones, sleep, alcohol and drugs, love,

<!-- chunk -->

### heartbreak, meaning and purpose in life, trauma, and loneliness, can

<!-- chunk -->

### be tied directly to effects on metabolism and mitochondria. I’ll

<!-- chunk -->

### demonstrate how all these contributing factors affect metabolism,

<!-- chunk -->

### which then affects the function of cells, which can then result in

<!-- chunk -->

### symptoms of mental illness.

<!-- chunk -->

### •You’ll learn that all current mental health treatments, including the

<!-- chunk -->

### psychological and social ones, likely work by affecting metabolism.

<!-- chunk -->

### •This new understanding of mental illness leads to new treatments,

<!-- chunk -->

### ones that offer the hope of long-term healing as opposed to just

<!-- chunk -->

### symptom reduction. They will sometimes be more difficult than just

<!-- chunk -->

### taking a pill, but they are well worth the effort. While more research

<!-- chunk -->

### will lead to additional new treatments, the exciting news is that many

<!-- chunk -->

### therapeutic options are available today.

<!-- chunk -->

### To be clear, I am not the first to suggest that metabolism and mitochondria

<!-- chunk -->

### are related to mental illness. In fact, I am building on decades of research.

<!-- chunk -->

### Without these other researchers and their pioneering work, this book

<!-- chunk -->

### wouldn’t exist. I’ll share many of their groundbreaking studies in the pages

<!-- chunk -->

### to come. However, for the first time, this book puts the pieces of the puzzle

<!-- chunk -->

### together to reveal one coherent theory. This theory integrates existing

<!-- chunk -->

### biological, psychological,

<!-- chunk -->

### and social research, and offers one unifying framework for explaining and

<!-- chunk -->

### treating mental illness.

<!-- chunk -->

### Brain Energy not only provides long-elusive answers, it offers new

<!-- chunk -->

### solutions. I hope it will end the suffering and change the lives of millions of

<!-- chunk -->

### people throughout the world. If you or someone you love is affected by a

<!-- chunk -->

### mental illness, it might just change your life, too.

# Part I

# Connecting the Dots


# What We’re Doing Isn’t Working

# MENTAL HEALTH TODAY

<!-- chunk -->

### he World Health Organization estimated that in 2017, almost

<!-- chunk -->

### million people on our planet suffered from mental health disorders.

<!-- chunk -->

### This represents a bit over 10 percent of the world’s population, or one

<!-- chunk -->

### in every ten people. When substance use disorders are included in the

<!-- chunk -->

### count, the number climbs to 970 million people, or 13 percent of the global

<!-- chunk -->

### population. Anxiety disorders were the most common, affecting about 3.8

<!-- chunk -->

### percent of people around the world, followed by depression, affecting about

<!-- chunk -->

### 3.4 percent.


<!-- chunk -->

### Rates of these disorders are higher in the United States, with

<!-- chunk -->

### approximately 20 percent, or one in five people, diagnosed with a mental or

<!-- chunk -->

### substance use disorder.

<!-- chunk -->

### These numbers give us a snapshot of the prevalence of mental disorders

<!-- chunk -->

### over a specific one-year period. But lifetime prevalence rates are much

<!-- chunk -->

### higher. In the United States, data now suggest that about 50 percent of the

<!-- chunk -->

### population will meet the criteria for a mental disorder at some point in their

<!-- chunk -->

### lives.


<!-- chunk -->

### Yes—half of all people.

<!-- chunk -->

### Estimating the rates of mental illness is difficult. People often deny their

<!-- chunk -->

### mental health problems to others or even to themselves. Having a mental

<!-- chunk -->

### illness is stigmatized pretty much everywhere in the world. While societies

<!-- chunk -->

### have made important strides in recognizing things like depression and

<!-- chunk -->

### anxiety disorders as “real” illnesses, this progress is relatively recent, and it

<!-- chunk -->

### is far from universal. There are still people who see those suffering from

<!-- chunk -->

### these disorders as simply “whiny” or “lazy.” On the other hand, while

<!-- chunk -->

### people with psychotic disorders are usually believed to have “real”

<!-- chunk -->

### illnesses, they face a different kind of stigma. Many people are afraid of

<!-- chunk -->

### them or dismiss them as “crazy.” Then there are those with substance use

<!-- chunk -->

### disorders—not only do many see them as self-centered or morally weak, in

<!-- chunk -->

### some countries, such as some Middle Eastern ones, they are classified as

<!-- chunk -->

### criminals and can be incarcerated for using even alcohol. The effects of

<!-- chunk -->

### stigma can range from shame to outright discrimination, but stigma of any

<!-- chunk -->

### kind can motivate people to minimize or lie about symptoms. As such,

<!-- chunk -->

### prevalence statistics are most likely underestimates of the true scope of

<!-- chunk -->

### these disorders.

<!-- chunk -->

### And as dire as these statistics are, the problem seems to be getting

<!-- chunk -->

### worse.

# A Growing Epidemic

<!-- chunk -->

### We have the best data for this in the United States, where researchers have

<!-- chunk -->

### been tracking mental health statistics for decades now. Rates of mental

<!-- chunk -->

### illness are on the rise. According to the CDC (Centers for Disease Control),

<!-- chunk -->

### US adults over the age of eighteen had higher rates of mental illness in

<!-- chunk -->

### 2017 than in all but three of the years between 2008 and 2015. Of note, the

<!-- chunk -->

### youngest group (those aged eighteen through twenty-five) had the largest

<!-- chunk -->

### increase—rising 40 percent between 2008 and 2017.

<!-- chunk -->

### The rate of ADHD (attention deficit/hyperactivity disorder) is rising in

<!-- chunk -->

### children and adolescents, increasing 41 percent in children four through

<!-- chunk -->

### seventeen between 2003 and 2012. This particular diagnosis and

<!-- chunk -->

### its reported upward trend attract considerable controversy. Some suggest

<!-- chunk -->

### that we are simply getting better at recognizing this disorder and providing

<!-- chunk -->

### treatment to children who need it to thrive. Others suggest that we are

<!-- chunk -->

### medicating normal behavior—that society and schools have come to expect

<!-- chunk -->

### too much from children, and that our expectations are unrealistic for what

<!-- chunk -->

### they are capable of at certain ages. Still others argue that the attention span

<!-- chunk -->

### of the American population has decreased across the board, likely due to

<!-- chunk -->

### increased time spent in front of screens, and this is being mistaken for

<!-- chunk -->

### ADHD. Is the rate of this disorder truly increasing, or are these other factors

<!-- chunk -->

### responsible for what we see in the data? We’ll further consider questions

<!-- chunk -->

### like this shortly. But ADHD isn’t the only diagnosis on the rise.

<!-- chunk -->

### Depression in children, adolescents, and young adults is increasing as

<!-- chunk -->

### well. From 2006 to 2017, rates of depression in the US increased by

<!-- chunk -->

### percent in children ages twelve to seventeen. In people ages eighteen to

<!-- chunk -->

### twenty-five, there was an increase of 49 percent. For adults over the age of

<!-- chunk -->

### twenty-five, the rate of depression supposedly stayed stable.

<!-- chunk -->

### However, much of this information is gleaned from surveys, and both

<!-- chunk -->

### the questions we ask and the way we ask them matters. Although surveys

<!-- chunk -->

### suggest that the rates of depression in adults are not increasing, many

<!-- chunk -->

### reports suggest that burnout is on the rise. Burnout is not an official

<!-- chunk -->

### psychiatric diagnosis in DSM-5 (the Diagnostic and Statistical Manual of

<!-- chunk -->

### Mental Disorders, Fifth Edition), but the World Health Organization

<!-- chunk -->

### recently added it to its list of mental disorders—the ICD-11 (International

<!-- chunk -->

### Classification of Diseases, Eleventh Revision). The criteria are similar to

<!-- chunk -->

### those for depression, but focus primarily on the stress of work and the work

<!-- chunk -->

### environment. There has been a lot of debate about whether burnout is

<!-- chunk -->

### simply a work-related form of depression, and for good reason: In one study

<!-- chunk -->

### looking at physician burnout, they found that those with mild burnout were

<!-- chunk -->

### three times more likely to meet criteria for major depression. Those with

<!-- chunk -->

### severe burnout were forty-six times more likely,


<!-- chunk -->

### suggesting little, if any,

<!-- chunk -->

### difference between these diagnostic labels. Like depression, burnout is also

<!-- chunk -->

### associated with much higher rates of suicide. Because burnout is not yet an

<!-- chunk -->

### official diagnosis in the DSM-5, US agencies don’t track its prevalence.

<!-- chunk -->

### However, a 2018 Gallup poll

<!-- chunk -->

### found that 23 percent of employees reported feeling burned out at work

<!-- chunk -->

### often or always, while another 44 percent felt burned out sometimes.


<!-- chunk -->

### These

<!-- chunk -->

### rates are much higher than those of depression.

<!-- chunk -->

### Suicide rates are increasing across most age groups. In 2016, nearly

<!-- chunk -->

### 45,000 people died by suicide in the United States alone. In general, for

<!-- chunk -->

### every person who dies by suicide, approximately thirty others attempt to

<!-- chunk -->

### kill themselves—putting the rate of suicide attempts at well over one

<!-- chunk -->

### million people per year. From 1999 to 2016, suicide rates increased in most

<!-- chunk -->

### US states, with twenty-five states seeing an increase of 30 percent or more.

<!-- chunk -->

### Another statistic, deaths of despair, tracks combined deaths in the United

<!-- chunk -->

### States from alcohol, drugs, and suicide. This statistic more than doubled

<!-- chunk -->

### between 1999 and 2017.

<!-- chunk -->

### Anxiety disorders are the most common mental disorders, but the

<!-- chunk -->

### criteria for diagnosing them continue to evolve. This makes it difficult to

<!-- chunk -->

### assess changes over time. Some have argued that the rates have not changed

<!-- chunk -->

### in recent years.


<!-- chunk -->

### However, an annual household survey of approximately

<!-- chunk -->

### forty thousand US adults suggests that anxiety is increasing. Survey

<!-- chunk -->

### participants were asked, “How often did you feel nervous during the past

<!-- chunk -->

### days?” with five response options ranging from “all of the time” to “none of

<!-- chunk -->

### the time.” From 2008 to 2018, rates of anxiety increased by 30 percent. In

<!-- chunk -->

### the youngest group, ages eighteen to twenty-five, there was an 84 percent

<!-- chunk -->

### increase.


<!-- chunk -->

### Sometimes the more “common” diagnoses like depression and anxiety

<!-- chunk -->

### are thought of separately from mental disorders like schizophrenia—the

<!-- chunk -->

### term “serious mental illness” is often used by mental health professionals to

<!-- chunk -->

### talk about disorders that involve significant impairment and disability, such

<!-- chunk -->

### as those with psychotic symptoms. While this category includes some

<!-- chunk -->

### severe forms of depression and anxiety, it mostly refers to diagnoses like

<!-- chunk -->

### schizophrenia, bipolar disorder, autism, and the like. So what about these

<!-- chunk -->

### disorders? What’s happening with them? They’re increasing, too. Between

<!-- chunk -->

### 2008 and 2017, there was a 21 percent increase in serious mental disorders

<!-- chunk -->

### in those over the age of eighteen in the United States. For the younger

<!-- chunk -->

### group, those aged eighteen to twenty-five, the rate of serious mental illness

<!-- chunk -->

### doubled during that same period—less than a decade.


<!-- chunk -->

### The diagnosis of autism is increasing at an alarming rate.


<!-- chunk -->

### In 2000, autism

<!-- chunk -->

### affected about 1 in 150 children in the US; by 2014, it was about 1 in 59.

<!-- chunk -->

### The statistics for bipolar disorder are also concerning. From the mid-

<!-- chunk -->

### 1970s to 2000, the prevalence of bipolar disorder was somewhere in the

<!-- chunk -->

### range of 0.4 to 1.6 percent. By the early 2000s, it had increased to 4 to

<!-- chunk -->

### percent.


<!-- chunk -->

### In children and adolescents, this diagnosis was almost nonexistent

<!-- chunk -->

### prior to 1994, but it’s increasingly common now.

<!-- chunk -->

### These statistics are difficult to comprehend. Diagnoses like autism and

<!-- chunk -->

### bipolar disorder aren’t supposed to increase exponentially over such a short

<!-- chunk -->

### period of time. While anxiety and depression can be situational, these other

<!-- chunk -->

### disorders are generally regarded as firmly “biological,” and many

<!-- chunk -->

### researchers believe they are determined in large part by genetics. Clearly,

<!-- chunk -->

### the human species didn’t have an epidemic of genetic mutations.

<!-- chunk -->

### Researchers, clinicians, and society at large are struggling to understand

<!-- chunk -->

### what to make of the sharp increase in mental illness. While there is no

<!-- chunk -->

### consensus explanation, plenty of people have theories—and generally

<!-- chunk -->

### speaking, these theories can be divided into two categories.

<!-- chunk -->

### The first category relies on the belief that the statistics are wrong, or

<!-- chunk -->

### that they don’t mean what we think they do. Many people think it’s

<!-- chunk -->

### impossible that the rates of mental disorders could increase so rapidly; they

<!-- chunk -->

### believe that these statistics are the result of doctors and/or patients seeing

<!-- chunk -->

### “disorders” that are not there. Here are three of the most prominent theories

<!-- chunk -->

### in this category:

<!-- chunk -->

### 1.It’s the pharmaceutical companies! They are looking to sell pills to

<!-- chunk -->

### as many people as possible, and to sell pills, they have to convince

<!-- chunk -->

### both doctors and the public that they need them. They spend billions

<!-- chunk -->

### of dollars every year on marketing, sending samples to doctors to

<!-- chunk -->

### keep the name of their product top of mind. They run television ads

<!-- chunk -->

### that ask if the viewer has any of a variety of vague symptoms, like

<!-- chunk -->

### “decreased enjoyment.” If you do, you are instructed to “talk to your

<!-- chunk -->

### doctor and see if ‘drug X’ is right for you.” These types of

<!-- chunk -->

### advertisements feed into people’s tendencies to be hypochondriacs.

<!-- chunk -->

### These worried people then go to doctors and emerge with a new

<!-- chunk -->

### diagnosis, and of course, the pills to treat it.

<!-- chunk -->

### 2.It’s laziness! People today don’t want to work at things. They also

<!-- chunk -->

### don’t want to experience any discomfort or think that they should

<!-- chunk -->

### have to. They increasingly categorize run-of-the-mill human

<!-- chunk -->

### emotions or experiences as “symptoms.” They flock to therapists to

<!-- chunk -->

### relieve these “symptoms.” Sometimes, they even go to the doctor to

<!-- chunk -->

### complain about them. People want quick and easy fixes; doctors are

<!-- chunk -->

### overworked and busy, and the easiest thing for them to do is write a

<!-- chunk -->

### prescription.

<!-- chunk -->

### 3.It’s this new generation of kids! Given that the rates are increasing

<!-- chunk -->

### most dramatically in children and young adults, it is clear that the

<!-- chunk -->

### blame lies with them—or their parents. Parents have pampered and

<!-- chunk -->

### spoiled this younger generation—catering to their every whim. These

<!-- chunk -->

### children and young adults have never been disciplined and also don’t

<!-- chunk -->

### have much willpower or perseverance. They get easily frustrated and

<!-- chunk -->

### overwhelmed. When their parents are no longer there to fix things, or

<!-- chunk -->

### when anyone tells them “no,” they go into a crisis. These meltdowns

<!-- chunk -->

### get them diagnosed with some mental disorder. Or, unable to handle

<!-- chunk -->

### life in the real world, they look for a “mental illness” to blame.

<!-- chunk -->

### As appealing as these types of theories may be, they are likely not the

<!-- chunk -->

### answers. If you do not suffer from a mental health issue yourself, or have a

<!-- chunk -->

### child who does, or have daily contact with patients who do, it’s easy to

<!-- chunk -->

### assume that these people are simply whiners and complainers, and that

<!-- chunk -->

### doctors, patients, and parents are looking for quick fixes. It’s easy to

<!-- chunk -->

### dismiss a problem when that problem is far removed from you. However,

<!-- chunk -->

### when you are confronted with the real people behind these statistics and

<!-- chunk -->

### witness their suffering, these blanket assumptions become impossible to

<!-- chunk -->

### sustain. When someone you know to be a “good parent” has a

<!-- chunk -->

### seven-year-old who suffers from fits of rage, who is not sleeping, who is

<!-- chunk -->

### threatening to kill himself or other people, the problem begins to look like a

<!-- chunk -->

### real one. These behaviors are not normal. When a woman has such severe

<!-- chunk -->

### panic attacks that she stops leaving the house, this is not normal. When a

<!-- chunk -->

### person is so depressed that they sometimes cannot get out of bed in the

<!-- chunk -->

### morning, this is not normal.

<!-- chunk -->

### So, the second category of theories about the increasing rates of mental

<!-- chunk -->

### disorders accepts the statistics as real. These are people who believe that

<!-- chunk -->

### these illnesses are truly on the rise. They offer a variety of perspectives and

<!-- chunk -->

### possible explanations:

<!-- chunk -->

### 1.It’s a good thing! These statistics are positive—they reflect a

<!-- chunk -->

### broader understanding of mental disorders and more awareness of

<!-- chunk -->

### how to identify them. There are many programs in schools and work

<!-- chunk -->

### environments on recognizing the symptoms of mental and substance

<!-- chunk -->

### use disorders. There are public service campaigns focused on suicide

<!-- chunk -->

### prevention. Celebrities are speaking out about their own mental

<!-- chunk -->

### health struggles, and there is more coverage of mental health in the

<!-- chunk -->

### media that is explicitly aimed at raising awareness and reducing

<!-- chunk -->

### stigma. People are increasingly getting the help they need, being

<!-- chunk -->

### diagnosed, and treated.

<!-- chunk -->

### 2.It’s society! We have become increasingly dependent on technology

<!-- chunk -->

### and screens. As we sit and look at our phones, computers, or

<!-- chunk -->

### televisions, we have become both more sedentary and more isolated.

<!-- chunk -->

### We interact with each other less in “real life,” connecting through

<!-- chunk -->

### social media instead of spending time together or talking on the

<!-- chunk -->

### phone. People only post the parts of their lives that “look good,” so

<!-- chunk -->

### social media fosters unrealistic expectations and shame, not real

<!-- chunk -->

### connection. The pace of life is faster, too. Everyone is busy and over-

<!-- chunk -->

### scheduled—even children. Families aren’t having dinner together

<!-- chunk -->

### like they did in “the old days.” No wonder people feel burned out.

<!-- chunk -->

### No wonder so many people are developing mental disorders.

<!-- chunk -->

### 3.

<!-- chunk -->

### It’s toxins, chemicals, and fake food! It is not just the behavior of

<!-- chunk -->

### society that has changed, it is the physical world we live in. We are

<!-- chunk -->

### exposing ourselves to toxins every day. The foods we eat are filled

<!-- chunk -->

### with artificial ingredients. New chemicals are everywhere—on our

<!-- chunk -->

### lawns, in our water supply, in personal hygiene products that we use

<!-- chunk -->

### morning and night. We create and surround ourselves with

<!-- chunk -->

### compounds we’d never encounter in nature, the effects of which—

<!-- chunk -->

### especially in combination with so many other compounds—we do

<!-- chunk -->

### not fully understand. These are leading to increases in all sorts of

<!-- chunk -->

### illnesses, including cancer, obesity, and mental disorders, too, even if

<!-- chunk -->

### we don’t yet know precisely how.

<!-- chunk -->

### There are many more of these “second category” theories for the rise of

<!-- chunk -->

### mental health problems, but these are some of the most commonly

<!-- chunk -->

### discussed ones. None of these three are far-fetched. They might well play a

<!-- chunk -->

### role with at least some people, or at least some of the time. As I will explain

<!-- chunk -->

### later in this book, some of them almost certainly do.

<!-- chunk -->

### But as for number one on the list above—rationalizing the statistics as

<!-- chunk -->

### simply the result of improved recognition and diagnosis—the evidence

<!-- chunk -->

### shows that it isn’t just recognition that is increasing. The surveys comparing

<!-- chunk -->

### year-after-year data include samples of the entire population, regardless of

<!-- chunk -->

### whether people get diagnosed or not. These disorders are truly on the rise.

<!-- chunk -->

### Perhaps the most important point to note is that the rates of very

<!-- chunk -->

### different mental disorders—autism, bipolar disorder, depression, and

<!-- chunk -->

### ADHD, to name a few—are all increasing at the same time. Why would

<!-- chunk -->

### that be? We think of bipolar disorder, ADHD, and depression as very

<!-- chunk -->

### distinct from one another, with distinct contributing causes. If these

<!-- chunk -->

### disorders are genetic, what happened to our genes? Is there a toxin causing

<!-- chunk -->

### numerous mutations? If the culprit is the stress of our fast-paced modern

<!-- chunk -->

### society, why are all of the disorders increasing? Wouldn’t more stress

<!-- chunk -->

### simply lead to more depression and anxiety? Certainly, stress doesn’t cause

<!-- chunk -->

### autism and bipolar disorder. Or does it? These statistics raise more

<!-- chunk -->

### questions than they answer.

<!-- chunk -->

### To add insult to injury, the COVID-19 pandemic has taken an additional

<!-- chunk -->

### toll. In June 2020, an estimated 40 percent of all US adults reported

<!-- chunk -->

### struggling with mental health or substance use issues. Eleven percent of the

<!-- chunk -->

### adults surveyed said they had considered suicide in the past thirty days.


# The Price We Pay

<!-- chunk -->

### Mental disorders are costly to society. The financial toll worldwide was

<!-- chunk -->

### $2.5 trillion in 2010 and is expected to reach $6 trillion by 2030.


<!-- chunk -->

### These

<!-- chunk -->

### figures include the costs of direct mental healthcare services

<!-- chunk -->

### (hospitalizations, doctor and therapist visits) and prescription medications.

<!-- chunk -->

### But there are also other financial costs that are more difficult to measure,

<!-- chunk -->

### including lost productivity due to employees becoming less focused or

<!-- chunk -->

### taking sick leave. These losses affect employers and employees, societies

<!-- chunk -->

### and individual sufferers. Depression is now the diagnosis that tops the list

<!-- chunk -->

### of disabling illnesses—above all other illnesses, including cardiovascular

<!-- chunk -->

### disease, cancer, and infections. Mental and substance use disorders are the

<!-- chunk -->

### leading cause of “years lost to disability” and “overall disease burden” in

<!-- chunk -->

### the US.


<!-- chunk -->

### Much more important than the financial costs of mental disorders is the

<!-- chunk -->

### suffering they cause to individuals and their families. They cause untold

<!-- chunk -->

### misery and despair. Mental illnesses can ruin people’s lives. They may lead

<!-- chunk -->

### to social isolation, disrupt school and career plans, and limit what people

<!-- chunk -->

### can expect for themselves in heartbreaking ways. The suffering almost

<!-- chunk -->

### always extends beyond the person with the disorder. Family life can be

<!-- chunk -->

### thrown into chaos. Divorce is a common consequence. Those closest to the

<!-- chunk -->

### sufferer can themselves develop mental disorders like anxiety or PTSD

<!-- chunk -->

### (posttraumatic stress disorder); they can simply burn out and abandon their

<!-- chunk -->

### suffering friend or family member to preserve their own health. At least half

<!-- chunk -->

### of the people in homeless shelters suffer from mental or substance use


<!-- chunk -->

### The same is true of prisons.


<!-- chunk -->

### Mental disorders can contribute

<!-- chunk -->

### to violence—not just the school shooters who make it into the headlines,

<!-- chunk -->

### but also domestic

<!-- chunk -->

### violence. Mental disorders can result in such extreme hopelessness that

<!-- chunk -->

### people take their own lives.

<!-- chunk -->

### For the majority of people, however, mental disorders don’t show

<!-- chunk -->

### themselves in dramatic and easily visible ways. Instead, people suffer alone

<!-- chunk -->

### in silence. They are ashamed. They don’t know what to do about their

<!-- chunk -->

### symptoms. Oftentimes, they don’t even know they have an illness. They

<!-- chunk -->

### don’t think of their symptoms as “symptoms”; they think their suffering is

<!-- chunk -->

### just a natural part of existence. They might believe that they are weak or

<!-- chunk -->

### inferior to others. They may think that they just need to make the most of

<!-- chunk -->

### the life they were given. They experience their distress, their symptoms, as

<!-- chunk -->

### an integral part of themselves or their life experiences.

<!-- chunk -->

### For example, imagine a woman we’ll call Mary. Her father was an

<!-- chunk -->

### alcoholic and verbally and physically abusive. He found fault with

<!-- chunk -->

### seemingly everything that she did, and she grew to believe that she was

<!-- chunk -->

### stupid and had few redeeming qualities. She didn’t talk to people about her

<!-- chunk -->

### father’s rages; she assumed this would only cause more trouble and incite

<!-- chunk -->

### further punishment from him. By high school, she was depressed, isolated,

<!-- chunk -->

### and saw little hope for her future. This continued into her adulthood. Mary

<!-- chunk -->

### had trouble sleeping, had flashbacks of her father yelling at her, and startled

<!-- chunk -->

### easily at loud noises. It did not occur to her that any of this might constitute

<!-- chunk -->

### a “disorder,” much less be treatable. I see patients like Mary who have

<!-- chunk -->

### suffered like this for years before something brings them to treatment.

<!-- chunk -->

### Many, many people like Mary never seek treatment at all.

# What About Treatment?

<!-- chunk -->

### Treatment for mental disorders is vitally important. It can reduce suffering.

<!-- chunk -->

### It can prevent disability. It can restore people’s dreams and potential.

<!-- chunk -->

### Treatment can save lives. In fact, it does. Many people benefit

<!-- chunk -->

### tremendously from present-day mental health treatments. Patients overcome

<!-- chunk -->

### addiction, find relief from psychotic episodes, learn to manage anxiety,

<!-- chunk -->

### recover from

<!-- chunk -->

### eating disorders—these victories are real and significant. The treatments we

<!-- chunk -->

### have work. Unfortunately, they just don’t work all the time or for everyone.

<!-- chunk -->

### Let’s look at a success story first.

<!-- chunk -->

### John was a thirty-six-year-old engineer, married with two young

<!-- chunk -->

### children. Life had been pretty good for him . . . until he discovered his wife

<!-- chunk -->

### was having an affair. John wanted to save his marriage, but his wife wanted

<!-- chunk -->

### a different life, and she had decided to leave him. John was devastated and

<!-- chunk -->

### became severely depressed. He was unable to sleep more than two hours at

<!-- chunk -->

### a time. He couldn’t stop obsessing about how his life was now ruined. He

<!-- chunk -->

### couldn’t focus at work. He felt the only solution was to get his wife to

<!-- chunk -->

### return, but she wasn’t interested. He was tormented with guilt about all the

<!-- chunk -->

### ways he thought he’d failed as a husband, as a father, as a person. This went

<!-- chunk -->

### on for three months and showed no signs of improving—if anything, it was

<!-- chunk -->

### getting worse. Finally, John’s family encouraged him to see a psychiatrist.

<!-- chunk -->

### He came away with a prescription for an antidepressant and a sleep

<!-- chunk -->

### medication, and he started weekly psychotherapy.

<!-- chunk -->

### Within days, John was getting more sleep. This helped him to feel less

<!-- chunk -->

### disoriented and overwhelmed, but he was still distraught. Within a month,

<!-- chunk -->

### however, things started to turn around. His mood began to improve. He was

<!-- chunk -->

### able to stop the sleep medication and sleep normally on his own. He was

<!-- chunk -->

### managing to focus less on tortured ruminations and more on things that he

<!-- chunk -->

### could control. He focused on projects at work and around the house, and he

<!-- chunk -->

### made the decision to get into better shape. He started spending more quality

<!-- chunk -->

### time with his two children. He took the steps he’d been avoiding to finalize

<!-- chunk -->

### the divorce. After a few months, he was able to stop psychotherapy. After a

<!-- chunk -->

### year, he tapered off the antidepressant and continued to feel well. He started

<!-- chunk -->

### dating again.

<!-- chunk -->

### John’s story demonstrates the success of modern psychiatry. The

<!-- chunk -->

### combination of medications and psychotherapy alleviated his depression

<!-- chunk -->

### and anxiety and helped him cope with an extraordinarily stressful time in

<!-- chunk -->

### his life. John’s was not the only suffering lessened, either. Divorce is

<!-- chunk -->

### difficult for children—in fact, it increases their risk of experiencing mental

<!-- chunk -->

### health

<!-- chunk -->

### challenges of their own. Having a parent with severe depression also

<!-- chunk -->

### increases this risk. Treatment allowed John to be a better, more engaged

<!-- chunk -->

### father. So, helping John feel better was also beneficial to his children.

<!-- chunk -->

### John’s workplace benefited, too. While depressed, John still showed up at

<!-- chunk -->

### work every day, but he wasn’t able to focus, and he got less done.

<!-- chunk -->

### Successful treatment helped John be a more productive employee.

<!-- chunk -->

### There are countless stories like John’s, and it is easy to understand why

<!-- chunk -->

### researchers and clinicians in the mental health field like to tell them. It’s

<!-- chunk -->

### important to highlight that treatments can work. It’s important to encourage

<!-- chunk -->

### people to seek help, to let them know their suffering can end. And

<!-- chunk -->

### professionals in any field want to focus on their successes. They don’t tend

<!-- chunk -->

### to advertise what’s not working. Unfortunately, in the mental health field,

<!-- chunk -->

### there is a lot that isn’t working. Not everyone gets a positive outcome like

<!-- chunk -->

### John. In fact, most don’t.

<!-- chunk -->

### Depression is one of the most commonly diagnosed and treated mental

<!-- chunk -->

### disorders in the United States. In 2020, an estimated 21 million adults

<!-- chunk -->

### experienced at least one depressive episode, representing 8.4 percent of all

<!-- chunk -->

### US adults. About 66 percent of them received some form of treatment.


<!-- chunk -->

### So, what happens to all of these people who get treatment for

<!-- chunk -->

### depression? Do they get better—and most importantly, stay better—over

<!-- chunk -->

### the long run?

<!-- chunk -->

### One study tried to answer this by recruiting a group of people seeking

<!-- chunk -->

### treatment for major depression from five different academic medical centers

<!-- chunk -->

### and following them for twelve years.


<!-- chunk -->

### The study included 431 people, and

<!-- chunk -->

### researchers assessed their symptoms of depression on a weekly basis. What

<!-- chunk -->

### they found was that even with treatment, 90 percent had persistent

<!-- chunk -->

### symptoms. On average, over the twelve-year period, the people in the study

<!-- chunk -->

### had symptoms of depression 59 percent of the time. Their symptoms would

<!-- chunk -->

### fluctuate, sometimes going away but then coming back, even with

<!-- chunk -->

### treatment, even if they took medications every day. In other words,

<!-- chunk -->

### percent of the people were not cured of their depression. They either

<!-- chunk -->

### continued to have low-grade lingering symptoms or they would come in

<!-- chunk -->

### and out of bouts of major depression. Depression was found to be a chronic,

<!-- chunk -->

### but episodic, illness. These researchers found that if people had only one

<!-- chunk -->

### episode of

<!-- chunk -->

### depression, like John, the chances of a full and lasting recovery were

<!-- chunk -->

### greater. However, there weren’t many of those people.

<!-- chunk -->

### This study is not an outlier; it is a reflection of what anyone who has

<!-- chunk -->

### worked in the mental health field for a number of years already knows to be

<!-- chunk -->

### the case. Almost two-thirds of depressed patients don’t experience

<!-- chunk -->

### remission—meaning get all the way better, even temporarily—with the first

<!-- chunk -->

### treatment they are offered.


<!-- chunk -->

### As the statistics suggest, many people go on to

<!-- chunk -->

### suffer for years, despite trying treatment after treatment. It’s not just a

<!-- chunk -->

### failure of medications, either. Many people try numerous treatments—

<!-- chunk -->

### medications, psychotherapy, group therapy, meditation, positive thinking,

<!-- chunk -->

### stress management, and more. Some even try transcranial magnetic

<!-- chunk -->

### stimulation (TMS) or electroconvulsive therapy (ECT, also known as

<!-- chunk -->

### “shock therapy”). People for whom no treatment seems to be very

<!-- chunk -->

### successful are said to suffer from “treatment-resistant depression,” though

<!-- chunk -->

### there are many more people who do find some relief, but not in a complete

<!-- chunk -->

### or durable way. The fact that depression is the leading cause of disability in

<!-- chunk -->

### the world clearly speaks to the lack of effectiveness of our current

<!-- chunk -->

### treatments. What are we missing? Why can’t we get most people with

<!-- chunk -->

### depression all the way better and keep them better?

<!-- chunk -->

### You may be wondering about the outlook for mental disorders other

<!-- chunk -->

### than depression. Sadly, the statistics for many other disorders are even

<!-- chunk -->

### worse. I won’t go through the data for every condition, but disorders like

<!-- chunk -->

### OCD (obsessive-compulsive disorder), autism, bipolar disorder, and

<!-- chunk -->

### schizophrenia are all at least as bad as depression in terms of both treatment

<!-- chunk -->

### success and the chronic nature of their afflictions.


<!-- chunk -->

### Many of these patients

<!-- chunk -->

### are told that they have lifelong disorders and that they will need to lower

<!-- chunk -->

### their expectations about what they will be able to achieve in life.

<!-- chunk -->

### Understandably, many patients are frustrated by the ineffectiveness of

<!-- chunk -->

### mental health treatment. They hear stories like John’s and assume they

<!-- chunk -->

### should be cured like he was. They often come to believe that the

<!-- chunk -->

### professionals treating them are incompetent, or that they haven’t gotten the

<!-- chunk -->

### correct diagnosis, or that they just haven’t found the right pill yet.

<!-- chunk -->

### Unfortunately, these usually aren’t the reasons they aren’t getting better. For

<!-- chunk -->

### most people, it’s simply because our treatments don’t work all that well.

<!-- chunk -->

### Some professionals in the mental health field won’t like this assessment or

<!-- chunk -->

### approve of me sharing it this way. They may fear that pessimism about

<!-- chunk -->

### treatment will deter people from seeking help. This is a legitimate concern.

<!-- chunk -->

### It is important that those suffering from mental illness reach out for support

<!-- chunk -->

### from professionals—sometimes this can be enough to keep someone alive

<!-- chunk -->

### through a suicidal crisis. Nonetheless, the data I’ve presented is accurate; to

<!-- chunk -->

### claim that mental health treatment works for everyone (or even most

<!-- chunk -->

### people), and works completely, is misleading at best. A bigger concern is

<!-- chunk -->

### that these types of claims can serve to further shame and stigmatize those

<!-- chunk -->

### with mental disorders. If people are told that our treatments work, and then

<!-- chunk -->

### those people don’t get better, some will blame the treatment or

<!-- chunk -->

### professionals, but others will blame themselves. And it isn’t just the

<!-- chunk -->

### patients: If we make these kinds of claims to families, other clinicians, and

<!-- chunk -->

### society at large, what happens when patients don’t get better? Do we say

<!-- chunk -->

### that the patient must have a “treatment-resistant” version of the disorder,

<!-- chunk -->

### implying that they have a more severe form of mental illness (which may

<!-- chunk -->

### very well not be true) and possibly adding to the stigma they suffer? Or do

<!-- chunk -->

### we suggest that it is the patient’s fault? Is the person not trying hard enough

<!-- chunk -->

### in therapy? Does the person somehow “want” to be sick? Unfortunately,

<!-- chunk -->

### these kinds of implications are all too common from clinicians, family

<!-- chunk -->

### members, friends, and others. And so we are back where we began, with the

<!-- chunk -->

### choice to come clean and say that for most disorders, treatments don’t work

<!-- chunk -->

### long-term for the majority of people. This brings with it the risk of

<!-- chunk -->

### discouraging those who need it from seeking treatment in the first place.

<!-- chunk -->

### Given everything I’ve outlined in this chapter—that these disorders are

<!-- chunk -->

### common and becoming more common, that they are an enormous burden

<!-- chunk -->

### on society both in terms of economic impact and human suffering, and that

<!-- chunk -->

### our treatments have proved unequal to the task of relieving that burden—it

<!-- chunk -->

### seems clear that mental illness is a global health emergency. We have

<!-- chunk -->

### poured money into research in the hope of shedding light on the problem

<!-- chunk -->

### and uncovering new solutions. In 2019, the National Institutes of Health

<!-- chunk -->

### (NIH)

<!-- chunk -->

### spent $3.2 billion on mental health research. What do we have to show for

<!-- chunk -->

### the research that has been done?

<!-- chunk -->

### This is what Dr. Tom Insel, the former director of the National Institute

<!-- chunk -->

### of Mental Health (NIMH), had to say in 2017 after leaving the NIMH:

<!-- chunk -->

## I spent thirteen years at NIMH really pushing on the

<!-- chunk -->

## neuroscience and genetics of mental disorders, and when I

<!-- chunk -->

## look back on that I realize that while I think I succeeded at

<!-- chunk -->

## getting lots of really cool papers published by cool scientists

<!-- chunk -->

## at fairly large costs—I think $20 billion—I don’t think we

<!-- chunk -->

## moved the needle in reducing suicide, reducing

<!-- chunk -->

## hospitalizations, improving recovery for the tens of millions of

<!-- chunk -->

## people who have mental illness.

<!-- chunk -->

### 19

<!-- chunk -->

### This was brave of Insel to acknowledge. Those in the mental health field

<!-- chunk -->

### know it to be true. So, again, what are we missing?

<!-- chunk -->

### The fact is, in order to make real progress, we have to be able to answer

<!-- chunk -->

### the question: “What causes mental illness?” And up until now, we have

<!-- chunk -->

### failed.


# What Causes Mental Illness and Why

# Does It Matter?

<!-- chunk -->

### nsanity, madness, anxiety, irrational fear, unrelenting depression,

<!-- chunk -->

### addiction, suicide: Mental illness has been described in every human

<!-- chunk -->

### culture on Earth, stretching back from the present to antiquity. While we

<!-- chunk -->

### have seen that it is on the rise, it is far from a new affliction. And yet the

<!-- chunk -->

### question of what causes it continues to perplex us. Ancient scholars,

<!-- chunk -->

### philosophers, and poets, along with modern-day neuroscientists, physicians,

<!-- chunk -->

### and psychologists, have studied this question relentlessly, and come up with

<!-- chunk -->

### no definitive answer.

<!-- chunk -->

### Many theories have been proposed over the past few millennia. In

<!-- chunk -->

### ancient times, mental illnesses were largely thought to be the result of

<!-- chunk -->

### supernatural forces. Punishment from God was a common belief. Demonic

<!-- chunk -->

### possession also had its heyday, with exorcism being the treatment of choice.

<!-- chunk -->

### While these kinds of views have persisted and resurfaced throughout

<!-- chunk -->

### history, a more scientific attitude emerged almost as soon as illness in

<!-- chunk -->

### general began to be looked at in a natural rather than supernatural light, and

<!-- chunk -->

### the conception

<!-- chunk -->

### of mental illness as a medical disorder was born. The ancient Greek

<!-- chunk -->

### physician Hippocrates was among those who took mental illnesses

<!-- chunk -->

### seriously; he postulated that they might be due to an imbalance of the

<!-- chunk -->

### body’s four vital fluids, or “humors.” An excess of one of these, black bile,

<!-- chunk -->

### was thought to cause depression—or melancholia; in fact the word

<!-- chunk -->

### “melancholy” comes from the Greek for “black bile.” (Interestingly, bodily

<!-- chunk -->

### substances—particularly feces, as it relates to the gut microbiome—are

<!-- chunk -->

### making a comeback in the theory of mental illness. More on that later.) Just

<!-- chunk -->

### as the birth of medicine transformed the way people thought of mental

<!-- chunk -->

### disorders, so, naturally, did the development of the field of psychology.

<!-- chunk -->

### Sigmund Freud famously theorized that mental disorders were due to

<!-- chunk -->

### unconscious desires or conflicts, and he framed the working of the mind in

<!-- chunk -->

### terms of nonphysical entities or forces—the id, the ego, and the superego.

<!-- chunk -->

### Other psychological theories have since been developed, many attempting

<!-- chunk -->

### to explain mental illness more “scientifically” based upon what we know

<!-- chunk -->

### about behavior and neuroscience. Modern cognitive or behavioral theories,

<!-- chunk -->

### for instance, might view an anxiety disorder as the result of internalized

<!-- chunk -->

### patterns of thought, or advocate changing certain behaviors as a way to

<!-- chunk -->

### change mental experiences. While psychological theories are still used in

<!-- chunk -->

### treatment today, most clinicians and researchers do not believe they can

<!-- chunk -->

### explain all mental disorders. From the mid-nineteenth century to the present

<!-- chunk -->

### day, there has been increasing evidence that mental illnesses have at least

<!-- chunk -->

### some biological components or influences. Chemical imbalances, brain

<!-- chunk -->

### changes, hormones, inflammation, and immune system problems are all

<!-- chunk -->

### believed to have possible roles in causing mental illness. Nonetheless, some

<!-- chunk -->

### authorities in the field feel that a physical model of mental states is too

<!-- chunk -->

### “reductionistic.” They say it reduces the complexity of human behavior,

<!-- chunk -->

### emotion, and experience to chemistry or biology, and that human

<!-- chunk -->

### experience cannot be explained by our mere molecules.

<!-- chunk -->

### In 1977, Dr. George Engel, an internist and psychiatrist, developed a

<!-- chunk -->

### working model of what causes mental illness that is still widely used today.

<!-- chunk -->

### He called it the biopsychosocial model.


<!-- chunk -->

### It asserts that there are (1)

<!-- chunk -->

### biological factors, including genes and hormones; (2) psychological factors,

<!-- chunk -->

### such as

<!-- chunk -->

### upbringing and rigid beliefs; and (3) social factors, like poverty or a lack of

<!-- chunk -->

### friends, that all come together for any given individual to produce a mental

<!-- chunk -->

### illness. Another popular model is the diathesis–stress model. “Diathesis”

<!-- chunk -->

### means a biological predisposition to becoming ill, something like genetics

<!-- chunk -->

### or a hormonal imbalance. The stress in this model can be anything in the

<!-- chunk -->

### environment, such as getting fired from a job, drug use, or even an

<!-- chunk -->

### infection, that then pushes the already predisposed person to actually

<!-- chunk -->

### become ill. This model assumes that most people who develop mental

<!-- chunk -->

### disorders were likely to do so at some point or another in their lives—they

<!-- chunk -->

### were just waiting to be triggered. Both of these models present a picture of

<!-- chunk -->

### mental illness that attempts to account for many different factors all

<!-- chunk -->

### contributing to the development of a mental disorder.

<!-- chunk -->

### Because, in fact, we have identified many factors that make people

<!-- chunk -->

### more likely to develop various mental disorders. And today, when we think

<!-- chunk -->

### about what causes mental illness, we often think in terms of these risk

<!-- chunk -->

### factors. They include things like stress, drug and alcohol use, hormonal

<!-- chunk -->

### issues, and a family history of mental illness, among others. The problem is

<!-- chunk -->

### that although we know of many such risk factors, not one of them is present

<!-- chunk -->

### in everyone with a specific disorder, and not one of them is sufficient, in

<!-- chunk -->

### and of itself, to cause any specific disorder.

<!-- chunk -->

### An obvious example is PTSD. This is the disorder that causes people to

<!-- chunk -->

### experience fear, flashbacks, excessive anxiety, and feelings of numbness for

<!-- chunk -->

### months or years after living through a traumatic event. By definition,

<!-- chunk -->

### anyone with PTSD must have been exposed to a traumatic event, but only

<!-- chunk -->

### about 15 percent of people who experience such trauma end up developing

<!-- chunk -->

### PTSD. Even when two people experience the same traumatic event, one

<!-- chunk -->

### person can end up with severe PTSD while the other is spared entirely. In

<!-- chunk -->

### other words, trauma, on its own, does not “cause” PTSD. OK, you say,

<!-- chunk -->

### that’s because it results from a combination of risk factors. Unfortunately,

<!-- chunk -->

### there is no combination of risk factors “guaranteed” to result in PTSD,

<!-- chunk -->

### either. And so it goes with almost every other mental disorder. Sometimes,

<!-- chunk -->

### it seems easy to understand why someone develops mental illness. For

<!-- chunk -->

### example, a woman who had a horrible, abusive childhood, who has a

<!-- chunk -->

### thyroid disorder,

<!-- chunk -->

### and who was just left by her husband of ten years for another woman might

<!-- chunk -->

### develop clinical depression. Most people can understand why she might be

<!-- chunk -->

### depressed because she has many risk factors for developing clinical

<!-- chunk -->

### depression. However, there are other people for whom mental illness seems

<!-- chunk -->

### to come from out of the blue for no reason at all.

# Decoding Depression

<!-- chunk -->

### Let’s take a look at one of the most clearly defined and understood mental

<!-- chunk -->

### disorders—major depression. Everyone gets depressed, but not everyone

<!-- chunk -->

### gets major depression. People with major depression feel sad or depressed

<!-- chunk -->

### most of the time, and they may experience fatigue, problems concentrating,

<!-- chunk -->

### and disrupted sleep. This disorder can rob people of all ability to experience

<!-- chunk -->

### pleasure and enjoyment from life, and it may leave them with

<!-- chunk -->

### overwhelming feelings of hopelessness and even thoughts of suicide. There

<!-- chunk -->

### are nine symptoms in all, and to be diagnosed with major depression, a

<!-- chunk -->

### person must experience at least five of them for at least two weeks.

<!-- chunk -->

### There are many clearly established risk factors for the development of

<!-- chunk -->

### major depression. They include genetics or a family history of depression,

<!-- chunk -->

### stress, the death of a loved one, the breakup of a relationship, conflict at

<!-- chunk -->

### work or school, and physical and sexual abuse. Various hormonal problems

<!-- chunk -->

### also make the list, including low thyroid hormone, high levels of cortisol,

<!-- chunk -->

### and the fluctuations in women’s hormones that can contribute to the risk of

<!-- chunk -->

### depression in the postpartum period or around the time of menstruation. In

<!-- chunk -->

### fact, just being a woman doubles the risk of depression compared to being a

<!-- chunk -->

### man. Excessive drug or alcohol use is a risk factor, and even some less

<!-- chunk -->

### obvious prescription medications, like certain antibiotics or blood pressure

<!-- chunk -->

### medications, can increase risk as well. Then there are social issues, like

<!-- chunk -->

### being bullied or teased, having no friends, or simply feeling lonely most of

<!-- chunk -->

### the time. Poverty, malnutrition, and unsafe living environments also

<!-- chunk -->

### increase risk. Sleep disturbance is a big one: either too much or too little

<!-- chunk -->

### sleep puts people at risk for depression. Many physical illnesses make the

<!-- chunk -->

### list of risk

<!-- chunk -->

### chronic pain, diabetes, heart disease, and rheumatoid arthritis. Cancer is

<!-- chunk -->

### another risk factor—but not necessarily the way you might think. Most

<!-- chunk -->

### people would be stressed by a diagnosis of cancer and assume it’s only

<!-- chunk -->

### natural that such a devastating diagnosis might make someone depressed.

<!-- chunk -->

### That does happen for some people. However, some patients get clinically

<!-- chunk -->

### depressed before they even know they have cancer. This is a common

<!-- chunk -->

### occurrence with pancreatic cancer in particular—people find themselves

<!-- chunk -->

### depressed for seemingly no good reason, and then a few months later are

<!-- chunk -->

### diagnosed with pancreatic cancer. Virtually all neurological disorders are

<!-- chunk -->

### associated with higher rates of depression, including strokes, multiple

<!-- chunk -->

### sclerosis, Parkinson’s disease, Alzheimer’s disease, and epilepsy. And

<!-- chunk -->

### interestingly, every other psychiatric disorder puts people at much higher

<!-- chunk -->

### risk for also developing major depression on top of their already existing


<!-- chunk -->

### That’s . . . a lot of risk factors. And they vary widely. Not just in the

<!-- chunk -->

### kinds of things they are—biological, psychological, and social—but in how

<!-- chunk -->

### big of a role they are believed to play. For instance, while being a woman is

<!-- chunk -->

### a risk factor for major depression, no one would say that being a woman

<!-- chunk -->

### causes major depression. But there are certain factors that contribute more

<!-- chunk -->

### directly, and in fact many competing theories hold one factor or another to

<!-- chunk -->

### be the root cause of the disorder. Unlike those who subscribe to the

<!-- chunk -->

### biopsychosocial model, some professionals believe that major depression

<!-- chunk -->

### has a purely genetic, purely biological, or purely psychological origin—the

<!-- chunk -->

### rest of the risk factors are just window dressing.

<!-- chunk -->

### One of the most widely known of these single-cause theories is the

<!-- chunk -->

### chemical imbalance theory of depression—and in fact, of mental illness.

<!-- chunk -->

### This theory suggests an imbalance in brain chemicals called

<!-- chunk -->

### neurotransmitters is the cause of all mental illness. Neurotransmitters are

<!-- chunk -->

### chemicals that relay signals between brain cells. For depression, the most

<!-- chunk -->

### popular belief is that levels of the neurotransmitter serotonin are too low;

<!-- chunk -->

### thus, medications that increase serotonin levels will treat depression. Many

<!-- chunk -->

### of the most commonly prescribed medications for depression belong to a

<!-- chunk -->

### class of antidepressants called selective serotonin reuptake inhibitors, or

<!-- chunk -->

### SSRIs (Prozac, Zoloft, and Paxil are in this category). They often do help

<!-- chunk -->

### resolve the symptoms

<!-- chunk -->

### of depression, which supports the theory that maybe a chemical imbalance

<!-- chunk -->

### is the cause. Other classes of medications that affect different

<!-- chunk -->

### neurotransmitter systems can also alleviate depression, so maybe it’s not

<!-- chunk -->

### just serotonin—maybe it’s various neurotransmitters in different people.

<!-- chunk -->

### Nonetheless, many psychiatrists and researchers believe that at its root,

<!-- chunk -->

### depression always boils down to a chemical imbalance.

<!-- chunk -->

### However, there are many questions raised by this theory:

<!-- chunk -->

### •What causes the chemical imbalance in the first place?

<!-- chunk -->

### •If people are born with this chemical imbalance, why aren’t they

<!-- chunk -->

### depressed all the time, starting from birth?

<!-- chunk -->

### •Why do medications like SSRIs take weeks or months to work? We

<!-- chunk -->

### know they change neurotransmitter levels within hours, so why don’t

<!-- chunk -->

### they work immediately?

<!-- chunk -->

### •If it’s a fixed chemical imbalance, why do the symptoms wax and

<!-- chunk -->

### wane, even over short periods of time? Put another way, why do

<!-- chunk -->

### people have good days and bad days, even when taking medications

<!-- chunk -->

### consistently?

<!-- chunk -->

### •Why do the medications stop working in so many people? Why

<!-- chunk -->

### would the imbalance change, and if it does change, what makes it

<!-- chunk -->

### change?

<!-- chunk -->

### These questions are begging for answers, not just in relation to the

<!-- chunk -->

### diagnosis of depression, but for all psychiatric diagnoses. Unfortunately, the

<!-- chunk -->

### chemical imbalance theory doesn’t provide answers.

<!-- chunk -->

### Another widely known theory for the cause of major depression is the

<!-- chunk -->

### learned helplessness theory. In a nutshell, it holds that when people are

<!-- chunk -->

### unable to change adverse circumstances in their lives, they “learn” that they

<!-- chunk -->

### are helpless. This can apply to something like not being able to find a

<!-- chunk -->

### romantic relationship despite numerous attempts, or more direly, an abused

<!-- chunk -->

### child trying to get his father to stop hitting him. In either case,

<!-- chunk -->

### these people begin to feel powerless, and then they get depressed.

<!-- chunk -->

### Eventually they stop trying to do much at all. Why bother? Some experts

<!-- chunk -->

### assert that the cause of these people’s depression is their psychology. They

<!-- chunk -->

### have learned, and believe, that they are helpless. Obviously, getting the

<!-- chunk -->

### abused child out of that environment is of paramount importance. But even

<!-- chunk -->

### years later, that boy may still be depressed. The treatment is often based on

<!-- chunk -->

### cognitive behavioral therapy (CBT), a form of talk therapy that focuses on

<!-- chunk -->

### identifying and changing thoughts, emotions, and behaviors. This therapy is

<!-- chunk -->

### based on the belief that when people are clinically depressed, it is likely

<!-- chunk -->

### because of thoughts that are based not so much on the reality of their

<!-- chunk -->

### current situation as on the helpless mindset developed in the past. The goal

<!-- chunk -->

### is to empower patients to challenge these thoughts and replace them with

<!-- chunk -->

### ones not so dire and hopeless. This will help them feel better and make

<!-- chunk -->

### changes in their lives, which will make them feel less helpless still, and this

<!-- chunk -->

### cycle will reinforce itself. This treatment works, at least for some people,

<!-- chunk -->

### which again supports the theory that this kind of problem might be a cause

<!-- chunk -->

### of depression.

<!-- chunk -->

### There are many other theories about specific factors believed to be the

<!-- chunk -->

### cause of major depression—biological, psychological, and social. Many

<!-- chunk -->

### have led to the development of specific treatments and interventions that

<!-- chunk -->

### work with real people, at least some of the time. In fact, the theories

<!-- chunk -->

### themselves are often shaped by treatments that are effective for depression,

<!-- chunk -->

### using the logic that if a treatment works, even in some people, then it must

<!-- chunk -->

### be correcting a problem that was causing the illness.

<!-- chunk -->

### Medications used to treat major depression include those specifically

<!-- chunk -->

### known as “antidepressants,” which are commonly sorted into five different

<!-- chunk -->

### classes. These classes act on different neurotransmitters and receptors,

<!-- chunk -->

### including ones for serotonin, dopamine, and norepinephrine. However,

<!-- chunk -->

### antidepressants aren’t the only medications used to treat major depression.

<!-- chunk -->

### Others include anxiety medications, mood stabilizers, antipsychotics,

<!-- chunk -->

### stimulants, antiepileptic medications, hormones, vitamins, and a wide

<!-- chunk -->

### variety of supplements, such as St. John’s wort. These all work in very

<!-- chunk -->

### different ways,

<!-- chunk -->

### and yet all are used routinely in treating depression, and all have been

<!-- chunk -->

### shown to work for at least some people, some of the time.

<!-- chunk -->

### Psychotherapy to treat depression also comes in many varieties. Some

<!-- chunk -->

### focus on relationships, others on thoughts and feelings, and others on

<!-- chunk -->

### behaviors; some focus solely on changes in the present, and others on

<!-- chunk -->

### revisiting your past or childhood. Different types of psychotherapy can be

<!-- chunk -->

### very different from each other, yet there is at least some evidence that they

<!-- chunk -->

### can all be helpful to at least some people with depression.

<!-- chunk -->

### Finally, there are more aggressive treatments, like TMS, ECT, and even

<!-- chunk -->

### surgery, in which parts of the brain are severed or electrodes are implanted

<!-- chunk -->

### to stimulate the brain or the vagus nerve, the main nerve of the

<!-- chunk -->

### parasympathetic nervous system.

<!-- chunk -->

### That’s a lot of different treatments! It’s difficult to understand how they

<!-- chunk -->

### can all treat the same set of symptoms. However, not one of these works for

<!-- chunk -->

### all people with depression. Why not? Are there just different causes of

<!-- chunk -->

### major depression in different people that require different treatments? And

<!-- chunk -->

### sadly, as I reviewed in the last chapter, there are millions of people who try

<!-- chunk -->

### treatment after treatment without finding even one that works.

<!-- chunk -->

### On the flip side, it’s important to point out that not everyone who

<!-- chunk -->

### develops major depression even gets treatment—in fact, the majority of

<!-- chunk -->

### sufferers throughout the world do not. Yet major depression will often

<!-- chunk -->

### resolve on its own. The symptoms can come and go, sometimes lasting a

<!-- chunk -->

### few weeks or months and then spontaneously disappearing. What causes

<!-- chunk -->

### some people’s symptoms to go away without any treatment? Why, for

<!-- chunk -->

### others, does depression become a chronic and debilitating illness? If we

<!-- chunk -->

### truly understand what causes this disorder, we should be able to answer

<!-- chunk -->

### these questions.

<!-- chunk -->

### But the picture gets more complicated still. In addition to risk factors or

<!-- chunk -->

### theories about what causes major depression, we have good evidence of

<!-- chunk -->

### physical changes in the body that are associated with major depression—

<!-- chunk -->

### that is, they are found more often in those with the disease than those

<!-- chunk -->

### without it. These are changes that have been observed in people who

<!-- chunk -->

### already have the diagnosis, but they may also provide clues about the cause

<!-- chunk -->

### of the disorder.

<!-- chunk -->

### Inflammation is a big one. We know that when compared to those without

<!-- chunk -->

### depression, people with chronic depression, on average, have higher levels

<!-- chunk -->

### of inflammation, as measured by different biomarkers, such as C-reactive

<!-- chunk -->

### protein and interleukins.


<!-- chunk -->

### At this point, however, we don’t know for sure if

<!-- chunk -->

### the inflammation is causing the depression or if the depression is causing

<!-- chunk -->

### the inflammation. And if the inflammation is causing the depression, what

<!-- chunk -->

### starts the inflammation in the first place? Is it one or more of the risk factors

<!-- chunk -->

### that we’ve discussed so far? Or is it something else altogether that we just

<!-- chunk -->

### haven’t discovered yet? As usual, many people have theories—some

<!-- chunk -->

### speculate that it is a chronic infection, or an autoimmune disease, or

<!-- chunk -->

### exposure to a toxin, or a bad diet, or having a “leaky gut,” and on and on—

<!-- chunk -->

### but these theories are not answers. What’s more, not everyone with chronic

<!-- chunk -->

### depression has higher levels of inflammation, at least not that we can

<!-- chunk -->

### measure. The research showing higher levels of inflammation is based on

<!-- chunk -->

### comparisons between groups of people: When looking at a group of people

<!-- chunk -->

### with depression and a group without, the group with depression has more

<!-- chunk -->

### inflammation . . . but not every individual in the depressed group will have

<!-- chunk -->

### higher levels of inflammation than the individuals in the group without

<!-- chunk -->

### depression. In fact, researchers and clinicians have not identified any

<!-- chunk -->

### parameter for measuring inflammation in the body or brain that will

<!-- chunk -->

### consistently separate people who are suffering from depression from those

<!-- chunk -->

### who aren’t.

<!-- chunk -->

### In addition to differences in inflammation levels, we have identified

<!-- chunk -->

### differences in the brains of people with chronic depression. Some people

<!-- chunk -->

### with depression have shrinkage, or atrophy, of specific brain regions, and

<!-- chunk -->

### this can progress over time. Because these types of changes are often seen

<!-- chunk -->

### in neuro-degenerative disorders, some researchers speculate that depression

<!-- chunk -->

### might be a neurodegenerative disorder as well, or that it could represent the

<!-- chunk -->

### early stages of another neurodegenerative disorder such as Alzheimer’s or

<!-- chunk -->

### Parkinson’s.


<!-- chunk -->

### Other researchers speculate that these changes might be the

<!-- chunk -->

### result of the increased inflammation associated with depression. We know

<!-- chunk -->

### that inflammation over a prolonged period of time can cause damage to

<!-- chunk -->

### tissue. For example, when a person’s knee is inflamed by arthritis, we know

<!-- chunk -->

### that permanent damage can result; the longer the inflammation lasts, the

<!-- chunk -->

### more the damage

<!-- chunk -->

### progresses. Maybe something like that is happening in the brain—the

<!-- chunk -->

### inflammation comes first and causes damage to these brain regions.

<!-- chunk -->

### Research has also found a number of differences in the way the brains

<!-- chunk -->

### of depressed people function. When comparing functional MRI scans of

<!-- chunk -->

### those with major depression to those without, depressed people seem to

<!-- chunk -->

### have decreased activity in some brain regions and increased activity in

<!-- chunk -->

### others, as well as differences in the way brain regions communicate with

<!-- chunk -->

### each other.


<!-- chunk -->

### However, as with all of these brain changes we’ve discussed,

<!-- chunk -->

### the studies have only shown relative differences between groups. And once

<!-- chunk -->

### again, we don’t know if these changes are the cause of depression or a

<!-- chunk -->

### consequence of it. Could another process be causing both the depression

<!-- chunk -->

### and these brain changes? We just don’t know yet.

<!-- chunk -->

### Finally, let’s throw another wrench into the works—the gut microbiome.

<!-- chunk -->

### The human digestive system contains trillions of microorganisms, including

<!-- chunk -->

### bacteria, viruses, and fungi. They produce hormones, neurotransmitters, and

<!-- chunk -->
