### inflammatory molecules that get released into our gut and then absorbed

<!-- chunk -->

### into our bloodstreams. Research suggests that these microbes play a role in

<!-- chunk -->

### obesity, diabetes, cardiovascular disease, depression, anxiety, autism, and

<!-- chunk -->

### even schizophrenia.


<!-- chunk -->

### But microbiome research is relatively new, and we

<!-- chunk -->

### don’t yet know the details of which specific microorganisms might be

<!-- chunk -->

### beneficial and which ones harmful, or in fact if it’s about the mere presence

<!-- chunk -->

### or absence of certain organisms at all; it could also be that the key lies in the

<!-- chunk -->

### balance of different types of organisms. More to the point, although some

<!-- chunk -->

### research in mice has found changes in depressive symptoms mediated

<!-- chunk -->

### through changes in the gut microbiome, we don’t yet know how to use this

<!-- chunk -->

### information to effectively treat depression or most other disorders.


<!-- chunk -->

### So, that’s a whirlwind tour through major depression—many of the risk

<!-- chunk -->

### factors, an overview of some theories about what causes it and the

<!-- chunk -->

### treatments aimed at those causes, and some of the biological and brain

<!-- chunk -->

### changes that are seen in people with the disorder. So, given all that, how do

<!-- chunk -->

### we answer the question: “What causes major depression?”

<!-- chunk -->

### This is why the biopsychosocial model makes sense—there are

<!-- chunk -->

### biological things, psychological things, and social factors that may come

<!-- chunk -->

### together

<!-- chunk -->

### differently in different people to result in major depression. Put another

<!-- chunk -->

### way, there are different causes in different people. Some researchers and

<!-- chunk -->

### clinicians claim that there must be different types of depression—maybe

<!-- chunk -->

### one type that’s caused by social stressors and another type that’s caused by

<!-- chunk -->

### biological factors. Maybe there are dozens of different types of depression

<!-- chunk -->

### —all caused by these various risk factors. Maybe certain factors are

<!-- chunk -->

### responsible for certain symptoms, and if we did a better job of clustering

<!-- chunk -->

### these symptoms, we could identify these types and get a better handle on

<!-- chunk -->

### these causes. Unfortunately, this doesn’t appear to be the answer. Clinicians

<!-- chunk -->

### and researchers have struggled with this for decades, and the same set of

<!-- chunk -->

### symptoms continues to come up over and over again across categories,

<!-- chunk -->

### regardless of the risk factors or perceived causes of the depression, whether

<!-- chunk -->

### they are biological, psychological, social, or some combination. The same

<!-- chunk -->

### constellation of symptoms has been found in countless people, in countless

<!-- chunk -->

### varieties of circumstance. In fact, the symptoms of major depression have

<!-- chunk -->

### been described in the Bible, historic texts, literature, poetry, and medical

<!-- chunk -->

### records dating back to Hippocrates. So what causes it? There must be an

<!-- chunk -->

### answer—one that ties together all the facts about the different risk factors,

<!-- chunk -->

### the treatments that work, and the brain and body changes that we see time

<!-- chunk -->

### and again.

<!-- chunk -->

### Is it possible that there are different processes leading to the same set of

<!-- chunk -->

### symptoms in different people, completely independent of each other? Well,

<!-- chunk -->

### it’s possible, but it would be highly unlikely. You may have heard of

<!-- chunk -->

### Occam’s razor—it’s a general rule or guideline, also known as the law of

<!-- chunk -->

### parsimony. It is usually held to mean that the simplest, most unifying

<!-- chunk -->

### explanation is most likely to be correct. For example, all things being equal,

<!-- chunk -->

### if a patient comes in with a high fever, sore neck, and headache, it is less

<!-- chunk -->

### likely that the patient has a headache due to a brain hemorrhage and a sore

<!-- chunk -->

### neck due to a pinched nerve and a fever due to an infection than it is that

<!-- chunk -->

### the patient has meningitis—one diagnosis that explains all three signs and

<!-- chunk -->

### symptoms. In short, when faced with a situation like the one we have

<!-- chunk -->

### outlined for major depression, a unifying theory that can connect all the

<!-- chunk -->

### evidence in a logical and plausible way is most likely to be correct. Before

<!-- chunk -->

### we get too far down the road toward the answer, though, it is worth

<!-- chunk -->

### considering why it matters in the first place.

# Why the Cause Matters—Treating Symptoms Versus

# Disorders

<!-- chunk -->

### When diagnosing someone with a disorder, we rely on signs and symptoms.

<!-- chunk -->

### People often use the term “symptoms” as a catchall, but the difference

<!-- chunk -->

### between signs and symptoms is a crucial one. Signs are objective indicators

<!-- chunk -->

### of an illness that can be observed or measured by someone else. Signs can

<!-- chunk -->

### include things such as a seizure, a blood pressure measurement, a laboratory

<!-- chunk -->

### value, or an abnormality seen on a brain scan. Symptoms are subjective

<!-- chunk -->

### experiences that a patient must tell someone about. Symptoms can include

<!-- chunk -->

### things such as moods, thoughts, or experiences of pain or numbness. There

<!-- chunk -->

### are very few signs in psychiatry. Instead, most of our diagnoses are based

<!-- chunk -->

### on symptoms, such as irritability, anxiety, fear, depression, abnormal

<!-- chunk -->

### thoughts or perceptions, and impaired memory. Mental disorders can also

<!-- chunk -->

### include things that seem more “physical” than “mental,” like sleep

<!-- chunk -->

### disturbances, slowed movements, fatigue, and hyperactivity. Some of these

<!-- chunk -->

### can be observed, but clinicians often rely on patients to tell them about

<!-- chunk -->

### these, too, putting them into the category of symptoms as opposed to signs.

<!-- chunk -->

### Unfortunately, there are no laboratory tests, brain scans, or other objective

<!-- chunk -->

### tests that can accurately diagnose any mental disorder.

<!-- chunk -->

### Psychiatric diagnoses are all based on the concept of syndromes. A

<!-- chunk -->

### syndrome is a cluster of signs and symptoms that commonly occur together,

<!-- chunk -->

### with a cause that is not yet known. One medical example that began in the

<!-- chunk -->

### 1980s was the syndrome of unusual infections and rare cancers that we

<!-- chunk -->

### called AIDS—acquired immunodeficiency syndrome. Before we knew that

<!-- chunk -->

### it was due to a virus, it was a syndrome. In psychiatry, every diagnosis is a

<!-- chunk -->

### syndrome. This is inherent to the definition of a psychiatric disorder. When

<!-- chunk -->

### mental symptoms are caused by a medical or neurological condition, that

<!-- chunk -->

### alone excludes classifying them as a psychiatric disorder. Neurological

<!-- chunk -->

### illnesses, cancers, infections, and autoimmune diseases can all affect the

<!-- chunk -->

### brain. When people with these conditions have mental symptoms, they are

<!-- chunk -->

### not necessarily diagnosed with a psychiatric disorder. If a patient comes in

<!-- chunk -->

### suffering from irritability, depression, and memory loss, and further

<!-- chunk -->

### evaluation

<!-- chunk -->

### reveals that these symptoms are the result of an infection or cancer, they are

<!-- chunk -->

### diagnosed with that condition and treated by a medical specialist outside of

<!-- chunk -->

### psychiatry, even if their mental symptoms are indistinguishable from those

<!-- chunk -->

### of a patient who “just” has depression. Psychiatrists and other mental health

<!-- chunk -->

### professionals are left with everyone else—the ones for whom we don’t

<!-- chunk -->

### know the exact cause.

<!-- chunk -->

### This is at the heart of the difficulty we’ve had in making progress in

<!-- chunk -->

### mental health care. Without a clear cause, we end up treating symptoms as

<!-- chunk -->

### opposed to disorders.

<!-- chunk -->

### Some treatments are designed to attack the root cause of an illness. The

<!-- chunk -->

### best example is an infectious disease. A bacterial infection can cause many

<!-- chunk -->

### signs and symptoms—fever, changes in blood cell counts, chills, pain,

<!-- chunk -->

### cough, and fatigue, to name a few. Definitive treatment for the infection is

<!-- chunk -->

### an antibiotic that eliminates the bacteria from the body. This type of

<!-- chunk -->

### treatment is sometimes referred to as a disease-modifying treatment. In this

<!-- chunk -->

### case, the treatment will cure the illness; after the course of antibiotics, the

<!-- chunk -->

### person will no longer have the infection. But there is another type of

<!-- chunk -->

### treatment commonly used in the medical field; treatments in this category

<!-- chunk -->

### are known as symptomatic treatments. They are designed to reduce

<!-- chunk -->

### symptoms, which can help people feel better, but they don’t directly change

<!-- chunk -->

### the course of the illness. For example, people with bacterial infections are

<!-- chunk -->

### commonly given symptomatic treatments like Tylenol to reduce fever.

<!-- chunk -->

### Symptomatic treatments can reduce suffering and allow people to work and

<!-- chunk -->

### function normally, but they are not addressing the root cause. In the end,

<!-- chunk -->

### with or without Tylenol, either the body will fight off the infection on its

<!-- chunk -->

### own, the person will get antibiotic treatment, or the infection will progress

<!-- chunk -->

### and the person will die. Tylenol won’t really make much of a difference in

<!-- chunk -->

### which of these outcomes occur.

<!-- chunk -->

### In the mental health field, the reality is that most of our treatments are

<!-- chunk -->

### symptomatic. For most people, psychiatric medications, ECT, and TMS are

<!-- chunk -->

### usually symptomatic treatments. They don’t appear to address the root

<!-- chunk -->

### cause of the illness. For some, they can markedly reduce symptoms. In

<!-- chunk -->

### others, they can put the illness into remission, meaning that all the

<!-- chunk -->

### symptoms

<!-- chunk -->

### get completely better. There are people, like John, who can use

<!-- chunk -->

### antidepressants or other medications for a year or two and then live happily

<!-- chunk -->

### ever after without them. Does this mean that the medications were disease

<!-- chunk -->

### modifying? In some cases, like John’s, it’s possible they are. However,

<!-- chunk -->

### given the extremely high rates of continuing symptoms and relapses in most

<!-- chunk -->

### people with mental disorders, our treatments don’t appear to be modifying

<!-- chunk -->

### the diseases themselves.

<!-- chunk -->

### As for psychotherapy and social interventions, there are those who

<!-- chunk -->

### believe that these treatments are addressing root causes. In some cases, this

<!-- chunk -->

### makes sense. For example, if a woman is in a physically abusive

<!-- chunk -->

### relationship and is clinically depressed as a result, helping her leave that

<!-- chunk -->

### relationship and build a new and better life may resolve her depression.

<!-- chunk -->

### Many psychotherapists would argue that the root cause of the woman’s

<!-- chunk -->

### depression was being in an abusive relationship. However, we know that

<!-- chunk -->

### because she has gone through the experience of an abusive relationship and

<!-- chunk -->

### developed clinical depression, she is now at increased risk for developing

<!-- chunk -->

### depression again at some point in the future, even if she is never in another

<!-- chunk -->

### abusive relationship. Given this fact, it seems there may be more to the

<!-- chunk -->

### depression than just the abuse, and that treating this factor alone did not

<!-- chunk -->

### definitively address the root cause. What causes her to remain at higher risk

<!-- chunk -->

### of developing depression in the future? If we truly understand what causes

<!-- chunk -->

### mental illness, we should be able to answer this question.

<!-- chunk -->

### People in the mental health field often use circular logic to support their

<!-- chunk -->

### theories about what causes mental illness. For example, they may claim that

<!-- chunk -->

### if something works to relieve symptoms, it must have been the cause to

<!-- chunk -->

### begin with. The fact that the woman in the example above found relief

<!-- chunk -->

### when her situation changed is used as evidence that this situation was the

<!-- chunk -->

### root cause of her clinical depression. The fact that many psychiatric

<!-- chunk -->

### medications help relieve the symptoms of mental illness is used as evidence

<!-- chunk -->

### that the root cause of mental illness must be a chemical imbalance. As

<!-- chunk -->

### logical as this may seem, it isn’t always true.

<!-- chunk -->

### Here’s an example to illustrate some of the flaws in this line of

<!-- chunk -->

### reasoning. Let’s go back to an infection that is causing a fever. If we didn’t

<!-- chunk -->

### know

<!-- chunk -->

### anything about infections or the causes of fever, and we were trying to

<!-- chunk -->

### figure it all out, we might do brain scans on people with fevers, looking for

<!-- chunk -->

### clues. Guess what we would see? We would see that the hypothalamus is

<!-- chunk -->

### overactive—that’s the part of the brain that controls the fever response. If

<!-- chunk -->

### we already knew that Tylenol works to reduce fevers, we might then do

<!-- chunk -->

### scans to research how Tylenol affects the brain. Lo and behold, we would

<!-- chunk -->

### see that Tylenol decreases this excessive activity in the hypothalamus!

<!-- chunk -->

### Based on this, we might logically conclude that the cause of the fever is a

<!-- chunk -->

### brain disorder involving the hypothalamus. We would have proof that a

<!-- chunk -->

### fevered patient’s brain activity is abnormal and that Tylenol works to reduce

<!-- chunk -->

### that abnormal activity. But it would be deeply misguided to conclude that

<!-- chunk -->

### we’d identified the fever’s cause. What we really did was identify a part of

<!-- chunk -->

### the brain that is involved in the production of a fever, and prove that a

<!-- chunk -->

### treatment that reduces fever affects that part of the brain as well. But

<!-- chunk -->

### Tylenol doesn’t treat infections. Reducing fever with that treatment

<!-- chunk -->

### wouldn’t change the course of the illness. Our fever and Tylenol brain scans

<!-- chunk -->

### only identified one aspect of the body’s response to an infection. We would

<!-- chunk -->

### better understand one symptom of the illness, or one part of its mechanism.

<!-- chunk -->

### It’s useful information, but it doesn’t help us understand the root cause of

<!-- chunk -->

### the fever at all—an infection.

# Correlations, Causes, and Common Pathways

<!-- chunk -->

### To answer the question of what causes mental illness, it is important to

<!-- chunk -->

### think a little about how we go about asking such a question, and the tools

<!-- chunk -->

### and principles we use to explore it. When medical researchers are doing

<!-- chunk -->

### their detective work to determine what causes an illness, they often study

<!-- chunk -->

### groups of people with and without the illness to look for correlations. A

<!-- chunk -->

### correlation is a relationship or connection between two things, or variables.

<!-- chunk -->

### If two variables are correlated, it might imply a cause-and-effect

<!-- chunk -->

### relationship, which is ultimately what the researchers are looking for. There

<!-- chunk -->

### are many types of studies designed to look for correlations. Researchers

<!-- chunk -->

### might

<!-- chunk -->

### perform brain scans on groups of people with and without depression and

<!-- chunk -->

### look for differences—the association with inflammation mentioned earlier

<!-- chunk -->

### is a correlation, the result of noticing that two variables (depression and

<!-- chunk -->

### inflammation) seemed to occur more often together, implying a

<!-- chunk -->

### relationship. One common type of study is an epidemiology study, which

<!-- chunk -->

### assesses variables in large populations of people and looks for correlations

<!-- chunk -->

### that way. For example, researchers might measure people’s weight, follow

<!-- chunk -->

### them for ten years, and record how many suffer heart attacks over that ten-

<!-- chunk -->

### year period. They would then look at the rates of heart attacks for different

<!-- chunk -->

### groups of people based on their starting weights to see if weight was

<!-- chunk -->

### correlated with heart attacks. If they found that obese people had higher

<!-- chunk -->

### rates of heart attacks than thin people, they would conclude that there was a

<!-- chunk -->

### correlation between obesity and suffering a heart attack. Notice that I said

<!-- chunk -->

### “correlation.” Based on this study alone, they can’t say that being obese

<!-- chunk -->

### causes heart attacks. This is one of the tricky things about correlational

<!-- chunk -->

### research. People often misinterpret the findings and make assumptions that

<!-- chunk -->

### aren’t warranted.

<!-- chunk -->

### Correlation does not equal causation. Almost everyone has heard this.

<!-- chunk -->

### It means that a correlation does not necessarily tell us anything about cause

<!-- chunk -->

### and effect. Unfortunately, while most people are aware of the principle, they

<!-- chunk -->

### don’t apply it when interpreting research. If the study in the example I just

<!-- chunk -->

### discussed were released today, the headlines would likely read “Obesity

<!-- chunk -->

### Proven to Cause Heart Attacks,” further perpetuating the incorrect

<!-- chunk -->

### interpretation of studies like this. This may seem like semantics. You might

<!-- chunk -->

### be thinking, “Of course obesity causes heart attacks. What’s your point?”

<!-- chunk -->

### Well, as a matter of fact, obesity, in and of itself, does not cause heart

<!-- chunk -->

### attacks. It is a strong risk factor for having a heart attack, but it’s not a

<!-- chunk -->

### definitive cause. What’s the difference? Not all obese people experience

<!-- chunk -->

### heart attacks. If obesity causes heart attacks, all obese people should have

<!-- chunk -->

### them, and probably have them often. Also, there are plenty of people who

<!-- chunk -->

### are stricken by heart attacks who are not obese. If obesity is the cause of

<!-- chunk -->

### heart attacks, why would a thin person ever have one? Clearly, there must

<!-- chunk -->

### be more to heart attacks than obesity. So, what does cause a heart attack?

<!-- chunk -->

### The correct answer might

<!-- chunk -->

### be something along the lines of “the heart’s arteries develop atherosclerosis

<!-- chunk -->

### (thickening or hardening) and, at some point, become obstructed, resulting

<!-- chunk -->

### in some of the heart muscle dying or being damaged due to a lack of blood

<!-- chunk -->

### flow.” What causes those things to happen? That’s where obesity comes in

<!-- chunk -->

### as a risk factor, but other risk factors also contribute to this process, such as

<!-- chunk -->

### genetics, cholesterol and lipid levels, blood pressure, a lack of exercise,

<!-- chunk -->

### stress, poor sleep, and smoking. There is a cascade of events that leads to a

<!-- chunk -->

### heart attack; this cascade of events occurs over years. Understanding the

<!-- chunk -->

### entire cascade of events is important, as it offers numerous opportunities to

<!-- chunk -->

### intervene with different treatments. If we assume the cause is obesity and

<!-- chunk -->

### focus all our treatments on this risk factor, we will fail to prevent heart

<!-- chunk -->

### attacks in many people. How we define the cause of illness matters.

<!-- chunk -->

### Everyone likes simple answers. The way I just defined the cause of a heart

<!-- chunk -->

### attack is a complicated answer. As you will learn, this is also the case when

<!-- chunk -->

### answering the question: “What causes mental illness?”

<!-- chunk -->

### Correlations, or relationships between two variables, can exist for

<!-- chunk -->

### several reasons. The most common interpretations are cause or

<!-- chunk -->

### consequence: One variable causes the other or is a consequence of the other.

<!-- chunk -->

### In other words, if A and B are correlated, it might be because there is a

<!-- chunk -->

### cause-and-effect relationship, where A causes B or B causes A. However,

<!-- chunk -->

### there is another possibility—one that some people have trouble

<!-- chunk -->

### understanding. Correlations can also reveal a common pathway or,

<!-- chunk -->

### sometimes, a common root cause.

<!-- chunk -->

### Let’s assume we don’t yet know anything about the cold virus. All we

<!-- chunk -->

### know is that lots of people are showing up at the doctor with runny noses

<!-- chunk -->

### and sore throats. Some people also have other symptoms, such as headaches

<!-- chunk -->

### or fatigue, along with their runny noses and sore throats. Some have only

<!-- chunk -->

### one or the other—a runny nose or a sore throat—but many, many people

<!-- chunk -->

### have both. Researchers notice that there is a correlation between runny

<!-- chunk -->

### noses and sore throats. Since there is a correlation, there must be a

<!-- chunk -->

### relationship. But what relationship? Is it cause and effect? If so, which

<!-- chunk -->

### causes which? Many people seem to get their sore throats first and then

<!-- chunk -->

### develop runny noses, but not all—in fact, sometimes it is the opposite. So

<!-- chunk -->

### does the sore throat come first and cause the runny nose? Is it the other way

<!-- chunk -->

### around?

<!-- chunk -->

### Or are they both simply consequences of some unidentified illness that can

<!-- chunk -->

### cause both symptoms, and maybe even others?

<!-- chunk -->

### Even though this is a straightforward example of an infection with the

<!-- chunk -->

### cold virus, at one point in time this all needed puzzling out. One source of

<!-- chunk -->

### confusion might have been people with allergies developing runny noses

<!-- chunk -->

### and sore throats when pollen levels were high. These people would present

<!-- chunk -->

### with the same, or similar, symptoms, but from a different root cause—

<!-- chunk -->

### allergies instead of the cold virus. The researchers would have to work hard

<!-- chunk -->

### to tease these two groups apart, trying to sort patients in various ways. At

<!-- chunk -->

### the end of the day, the symptoms in question would likely be

<!-- chunk -->

### indistinguishable: a runny nose is a runny nose, whether it’s from allergies

<!-- chunk -->

### or a cold. Researchers might have more luck by noticing things like

<!-- chunk -->

### seasonal patterns, or that some people seem to spread their symptoms to

<!-- chunk -->

### others (those with the cold virus) while others don’t (allergies). Looking for

<!-- chunk -->

### and combining patterns would give the researchers important clues about

<!-- chunk -->

### how to distinguish the two groups. In the end, they would have to address

<!-- chunk -->

### this important question: Are the runny noses and sore throats in these two

<!-- chunk -->

### different groups of people related in some way? They are, after all, the same

<!-- chunk -->

### symptoms. Why?

<!-- chunk -->

### The answer is that they share a common pathway—inflammation.

<!-- chunk -->

### Inflammation is part of the body’s process of healing tissue and/or fighting

<!-- chunk -->

### off an attack, and it occurs whenever the immune system is activated.

<!-- chunk -->

### Whether the body is mounting a defense against a cold virus or an allergen,

<!-- chunk -->

### inflammation is causing the runny noses and sore throats. Inflammation is

<!-- chunk -->

### the common pathway or process that produces the symptoms for both

<!-- chunk -->

### groups of patients, but that pathway is downstream from the root cause. To

<!-- chunk -->

### get to the root causes, the researchers will need to determine what’s causing

<!-- chunk -->

### the inflammation.

<!-- chunk -->

### Another way researchers might try to understand the symptoms of

<!-- chunk -->

### runny noses and sore throats and their causes might be to look at them

<!-- chunk -->

### separately. Not everyone has both symptoms, and some people with both

<!-- chunk -->

### have mostly one or the other. Our researchers might sort people into a group

<!-- chunk -->

### of those with primarily or only runny noses and another of those with

<!-- chunk -->

### primarily or only sore throats. This could make sense. After all, noses are

<!-- chunk -->

### different than throats. The treatments for the two symptoms are also

<!-- chunk -->

### different. Tylenol might help soothe the pain of sore throats, but it wouldn’t

<!-- chunk -->

### help runny noses. The most effective treatments for runny noses would

<!-- chunk -->

### primarily be things like pseudoephedrine or phenylephrine, ingredients

<!-- chunk -->

### found in Sudafed and cold and flu medications. There might be some

<!-- chunk -->

### treatments that relieve both symptoms in some patients—for instance, an

<!-- chunk -->

### antihistamine in those with allergies—but Tylenol would help almost all

<!-- chunk -->

### sore throats, and pseudoephedrine almost all runny noses, while neither

<!-- chunk -->

### would affect the other symptom. This stark difference in treatments might

<!-- chunk -->

### support the categorization of people into a runny nose group or a sore throat

<!-- chunk -->

### group. The researchers might label these distinct disorders—runny nose

<!-- chunk -->

### disorder and sore throat disorder.

<!-- chunk -->

### Given that the treatments for these disorders are so distinct as well, they

<!-- chunk -->

### might instead think of these disorders as they relate to those treatments. The

<!-- chunk -->

### sore throat group might be thought of as having a Tylenol deficiency

<!-- chunk -->

### disorder: sore throats must be due to people not having enough Tylenol in

<!-- chunk -->

### their system, as correcting this deficiency seems to rectify the problem. The

<!-- chunk -->

### other group might be called pseudoephedrine deficiency disorder, as the

<!-- chunk -->

### effectiveness of the treatment clearly indicates a pseudoephedrine

<!-- chunk -->

### imbalance in the body.

<!-- chunk -->

### As facetious as this seems, this is the very same logic that we use to

<!-- chunk -->

### conclude that depression is due to a serotonin deficiency and psychosis is

<!-- chunk -->

### due to too much dopamine. It makes sense, until you think about it with an

<!-- chunk -->

### example like the cold virus, which we understand well. In this context, it

<!-- chunk -->

### seems ridiculous. Yet that’s what we are doing in the mental health field

<!-- chunk -->

### today. We look at the treatments that work and assume that this tells us the

<!-- chunk -->

### story of what’s causing the disorders. And the disorders themselves are

<!-- chunk -->

### simply clusters of symptoms we have labeled disorders—the diagnostic

<!-- chunk -->

### labels mean nothing in terms of cause and effect, or what is happening in

<!-- chunk -->

### the body or brain.

<!-- chunk -->

### Let’s return to our hypothetical researchers. These researchers have

<!-- chunk -->

### identified two distinct disorders—runny nose disorder and sore throat

<!-- chunk -->

### disorder. The disorders have different symptoms and different treatments,

<!-- chunk -->

### so the

<!-- chunk -->

### researchers are feeling pretty confident about this classification system. The

<!-- chunk -->

### problem is that, while there are people with just one disorder or the other,

<!-- chunk -->

### comorbidity is common—in other words, there are a lot of people who have

<!-- chunk -->

### both disorders. People diagnosed with runny nose disorder commonly also

<!-- chunk -->

### develop sore throat disorder. But it’s also true in reverse. Runny noses and

<!-- chunk -->

### sore throats are a good example of a bidirectional relationship. It means

<!-- chunk -->

### that if you have either disorder you are at much higher risk for developing

<!-- chunk -->

### the other one. It doesn’t matter which one starts first. A bidirectional

<!-- chunk -->

### relationship often means that the two things share some common pathway.

<!-- chunk -->

### For runny noses and sore throats, as I’ve already discussed, the common

<!-- chunk -->

### pathway is inflammation. Sometimes, in addition to a common pathway, a

<!-- chunk -->

### bidirectional relationship can also imply the same root cause. In this

<!-- chunk -->

### example, we already know that there is a common pathway (inflammation)

<!-- chunk -->

### and different root causes (the cold virus and allergies, among others).

<!-- chunk -->

### Comorbidity aside, given that the symptoms and treatments are

<!-- chunk -->

### different, researchers and clinicians might advocate for keeping runny nose

<!-- chunk -->

### disorder and sore throat disorder as separate diagnoses. But once someone

<!-- chunk -->

### comes along and identifies the common pathway or a root cause that

<!-- chunk -->

### produces both disorders, this should change. Why? Back to Occam’s razor

<!-- chunk -->

### —the law of parsimony. If there is a simpler explanation for something in

<!-- chunk -->

### medicine, that explanation is more likely to be true. In this case, the

<!-- chunk -->

### explanation of the cold virus (a root cause) causing both disorders is much

<!-- chunk -->

### simpler than that of people developing both sore throat disorder (due to a

<!-- chunk -->

### Tylenol deficiency) and runny nose disorder (due to a pseudoephedrine

<!-- chunk -->

### imbalance) at the same time. Identifying that allergies (a different root

<!-- chunk -->

### cause) can cause both disorders would be an equally valid reason to change

<!-- chunk -->

### the medical field’s approach to diagnosis based on these symptoms. And of

<!-- chunk -->

### course, identifying the common pathway (inflammation) would be

<!-- chunk -->

### especially helpful, as it would allow the development of more effective

<!-- chunk -->

### treatments—and also explain why the symptoms of two different root-cause

<!-- chunk -->

### disorders, a cold and allergies, can be identical.

<!-- chunk -->

### But the same root cause can also result in different symptoms in

<!-- chunk -->

### different people . . . especially when preexisting vulnerabilities come into

<!-- chunk -->

### play. The flu

<!-- chunk -->

### is a great example. People infected with this virus usually experience a

<!-- chunk -->

### predictable array of signs and symptoms—fever, muscle aches, lethargy,

<!-- chunk -->

### and so on. Yet even though they all have the same illness, different people

<!-- chunk -->

### can have different symptoms to varying degrees. And in people with

<!-- chunk -->

### preexisting conditions, this difference can be magnified. A healthy twenty-

<!-- chunk -->

### year-old might spend a miserable weekend feeling achy and feverish and

<!-- chunk -->

### then bounce back quickly. On the other hand, a child with preexisting

<!-- chunk -->

### asthma might develop severe airway inflammation and end up in the

<!-- chunk -->

### hospital on a ventilator. A frail eighty-year-old man might experience

<!-- chunk -->

### devastating effects resulting in organ damage and death. These people’s

<!-- chunk -->

### suffering stemmed from a single root cause—infection with the flu virus—

<!-- chunk -->

### but that cause produced very different consequences.

<!-- chunk -->

### At this point, you can probably see both why the question of what

<!-- chunk -->

### causes mental illness matters and why it has remained so persistently

<!-- chunk -->

### difficult to answer. We in the mental health field are working with

<!-- chunk -->

### syndromes defined by symptoms and symptomatic treatments. Right now,

<!-- chunk -->

### we are treating infections with Tylenol. The goal is to understand the

<!-- chunk -->

### physiology of mental disorders, enabling us to develop effective treatments,

<!-- chunk -->

### and ideally, prevent these disorders before they occur.

<!-- chunk -->

### Causation is proving that one thing causes another. Correlational studies

<!-- chunk -->

### on their own simply cannot do this. They can suggest causation, or at least

<!-- chunk -->

### provide clues, but proving causation requires something more. One type of

<!-- chunk -->

### study that can prove causation is called a randomized controlled trial. For

<!-- chunk -->

### example, to prove that the cold virus can cause a runny nose, researchers

<!-- chunk -->

### could take a group of people who are not sick, expose half of them to the

<!-- chunk -->

### cold virus by squirting the virus up their noses and the other half to a

<!-- chunk -->

### placebo (by squirting plain water up their noses). They could then record

<!-- chunk -->

### the number of people in each group who develop runny noses over the

<!-- chunk -->

### following five days. If the cold virus causes runny noses, the group exposed

<!-- chunk -->

### to the cold virus should have a much higher rate of runny noses than the

<!-- chunk -->

### placebo group. In fact, these studies have been done, and this is true.

<!-- chunk -->

### One of the challenges in proving causation of a serious or life-

<!-- chunk -->

### threatening disorder in humans is that randomized controlled trials are

<!-- chunk -->

### unethical. Thus,

<!-- chunk -->

### even if we do have a plausible theory of what causes cancer, or mental

<!-- chunk -->

### illness, it will be unethical to expose people to this cause to test the theory

<!-- chunk -->

### definitively. So, what can be done in situations like this? Researchers are

<!-- chunk -->

### sometimes allowed to do equivalent experiments on animals. In the mental

<!-- chunk -->

### health field, this can play a role, but it has some limitations given the nature

<!-- chunk -->

### of mental disorders. Another alternative would be to develop a scientific

<!-- chunk -->

### theory of what might be happening in the body or brain from beginning to

<!-- chunk -->

### end—the cascade of events that leads to mental illness, like the cascade of

<!-- chunk -->

### events we talked about earlier as leading to a heart attack. Once established,

<!-- chunk -->

### researchers can then study people who have already been exposed to

<!-- chunk -->

### different risk factors and look for evidence of this cascade of events

<!-- chunk -->

### occurring in them. As you will learn, all of this research has already taken

<!-- chunk -->

### place; the evidence has been gathered. It’s just that no one has put it all

<!-- chunk -->

### together. That’s what this book does.

# O


# Searching for a Common Pathway

<!-- chunk -->

### ne of the challenges in determining what causes mental illness lies

<!-- chunk -->

### in defining what constitutes a mental illness in the first place.

<!-- chunk -->

### Dictionaries and reference books differ in their exact wording, but a

<!-- chunk -->

### good all-purpose version might be this: A mental illness involves

<!-- chunk -->

### changes or abnormalities in emotions, cognition, motivation, and/or

<!-- chunk -->

### behaviors resulting in distress or problems functioning in life. Context

<!-- chunk -->

### matters, though. One of the tricky things about defining mental illness is

<!-- chunk -->

### that many—even most—of the symptoms are considered “normal” in at

<!-- chunk -->

### least some circumstances.

<!-- chunk -->

### We all have emotions, for example, both pleasant and unpleasant ones.

<!-- chunk -->

### We might feel anxious when we face challenging or threatening situations.

<!-- chunk -->

### We might feel depressed when we experience a significant loss, such as the

<!-- chunk -->

### death of a loved one. Even something like paranoia can have an appropriate

<!-- chunk -->

### time and place. Have you ever watched a scary movie—one that truly

<!-- chunk -->

### terrified you? If so, you were likely a bit paranoid afterward. Some people

<!-- chunk -->

### look in their closets before they go to bed after watching such a movie. Or

<!-- chunk -->

### they hear sounds outside and feel terrified, imagining it’s a scenario from

<!-- chunk -->

### the movie. All of this is normal. However, at some point, intense unpleasant

<!-- chunk -->

### feelings and states should diminish, allowing you to go on with your life as

<!-- chunk -->

### before. Therefore, it’s important that any definition of mental illness

<!-- chunk -->

### somehow account for context, duration, and appropriateness.

<!-- chunk -->

### For an example of what I mean, consider “shyness.” Are people allowed

<!-- chunk -->

### to be shy? Is that normal? Most would say yes. So, at what point does

<!-- chunk -->

### shyness turn into an anxiety disorder such as social phobia? Drawing these

<!-- chunk -->

### lines is a matter of some debate in the field. One of the most notable

<!-- chunk -->

### controversies concerns depression—specifically whether, in some

<!-- chunk -->

### situations, these symptoms are “normal” and not an illness.

<!-- chunk -->

### The Diagnostic and Statistical Manual of Mental Disorders, or DSM, is

<!-- chunk -->

### the “bible” of psychiatry. It defines all the different diagnoses, their

<!-- chunk -->

### diagnostic criteria, and provides some relevant information and statistics.

<!-- chunk -->

### The current version, updated in 2022, is known as “DSM-5-TR.” In DSM-

<!-- chunk -->

### IV, the diagnostic criteria for depression included a caveat called the

<!-- chunk -->

### bereavement exception.


<!-- chunk -->

### It suggested that if someone had symptoms of

<!-- chunk -->

### depression in the context of the loss of a loved one, clinicians should hold

<!-- chunk -->

### off on diagnosing depression. A professional might certainly offer support

<!-- chunk -->

### in the form of talk therapy, but prescribing medications wasn’t necessarily

<!-- chunk -->

### appropriate. The exception had limits—among them, the depression should

<!-- chunk -->

### not last more than two months and should not produce suicidal thoughts or

<!-- chunk -->

### psychotic symptoms. In DSM-5, however, the exception was removed

<!-- chunk -->

### altogether. This served to encourage clinicians to diagnose depression even

<!-- chunk -->

### in the context of stressful life events like the loss of a loved one. Many

<!-- chunk -->

### clinicians and researchers felt that the American Psychiatric Association

<!-- chunk -->

### (which produces the DSM) had gone too far in pathologizing experiences

<!-- chunk -->

### like grief. On the other hand, supporters of the exemption’s removal cited

<!-- chunk -->

### research showing that antidepressants can decrease symptoms of depression

<!-- chunk -->

### even in the context of grief. These advocates felt that not diagnosing the

<!-- chunk -->

### problem and offering medication treatment might be unnecessarily cruel.


<!-- chunk -->

### Despite such controversies, there are many situations that seem clear-

<!-- chunk -->

### cut. When someone has crippling hallucinations and delusions, or

<!-- chunk -->

### suffers from overwhelming fear and anxiety every time they leave their

<!-- chunk -->

### home, or can’t get out of bed for weeks at a time due to severe depression,

<!-- chunk -->

### most of us would agree that this constitutes a mental illness. The “unusual”

<!-- chunk -->

### or “inappropriate” nature or degree of their symptoms, the intensity of their

<!-- chunk -->

### distress, and their inability to function all suggest a serious problem that

<!-- chunk -->

### merits the diagnosis of a disorder.

<!-- chunk -->

### The premise of the DSM, both the current and prior versions, is that

<!-- chunk -->

### there are distinct mental disorders with clear criteria that can be used to

<!-- chunk -->

### separate them from one another. In some cases, these distinctions are

<!-- chunk -->

### obvious. Schizophrenia is very different from an anxiety disorder. Dementia

<!-- chunk -->

### is different from ADHD. These distinctions are supposed to help guide

<!-- chunk -->

### treatment, predict what will happen to people with specific diagnoses (their

<!-- chunk -->

### prognosis), serve as a tool for clinicians and researchers to communicate

<!-- chunk -->

### more effectively with each other, and so on.

<!-- chunk -->

### The diagnoses in the DSM are given tremendous importance. They are

<!-- chunk -->

### required for clinical care and reimbursement by insurance companies. They

<!-- chunk -->

### are almost always needed to get research funding given that most mental

<!-- chunk -->

### illness studies focus on just one disorder at a time. And they are critically

<!-- chunk -->

### important to the development and dissemination of treatments as well, since

<!-- chunk -->

### to get FDA approval for a drug, pharmaceutical companies must conduct

<!-- chunk -->

### large clinical trials of specific medications for specific disorders and show a

<!-- chunk -->

### benefit. Even interventions such as psychotherapy are normally studied in

<!-- chunk -->

### clinical trials designed around one specific diagnosis. So, in many ways, the

<!-- chunk -->

### mental health field revolves entirely around these diagnostic labels.

<!-- chunk -->

### The field, however, has been plagued by debates over how to diagnose

<!-- chunk -->

### different mental disorders, especially since (as we discussed in the previous

<!-- chunk -->

### chapter) there are no objective tests to definitively diagnose any mental

<!-- chunk -->

### disorder. Instead, we use checklists of symptoms and criteria. We ask

<!-- chunk -->

### patients and family members what they are feeling, witnessing, and

<!-- chunk -->

### experiencing; we investigate, cross-reference, and explore; and then we

<!-- chunk -->

### make a diagnosis based on the best match or matches.

<!-- chunk -->

### In some situations, these diagnostic labels are extremely useful.

<!-- chunk -->

### Remember John who developed major depression? His diagnosis helped

<!-- chunk -->

### inform his

<!-- chunk -->

### treatment, and the treatment worked. John got better—all the way better.

<!-- chunk -->

### After a year of doing well, he was able to stop treatment and remain well.

<!-- chunk -->

### The diagnostic criteria allowed John’s psychiatrist to recognize the disorder,

<!-- chunk -->

### understand different treatment options, choose ones that were likely to

<!-- chunk -->

### work, and then discontinue the treatments after a defined period of time.

<!-- chunk -->

### Unfortunately, it’s not so simple—or successful—for others.

# Sorting Out the Similarities

<!-- chunk -->

### One of the challenges in the mental health field is that no two people with

<!-- chunk -->

### mental disorders are completely alike, even when they get diagnosed with

<!-- chunk -->

### the same disorder. There are two primary reasons for this—heterogeneity

<!-- chunk -->

### and comorbidity.

<!-- chunk -->

### Heterogeneity refers to the fact that people diagnosed with the same

<!-- chunk -->

### disorder can have different symptoms, severity of symptoms, levels of

<!-- chunk -->

### impact on their ability to function, and courses of illness. Not one of the

<!-- chunk -->

### diagnoses requires that all criteria be met. Instead, it’s a minimum number

<!-- chunk -->

### —for example, a major depression diagnosis requires at least five of the

<!-- chunk -->

### nine criteria. This makes for a lot of variability. One person with major

<!-- chunk -->

### depression can have depressed mood, excessive sleep, problems

<!-- chunk -->

### concentrating, low energy, and be eating much more than usual, resulting in

<!-- chunk -->

### weight gain. Another person with this diagnosis might be unable to sleep

<!-- chunk -->

### more than three hours, have lost their appetite and shed twenty pounds, and

<!-- chunk -->

### along with depressed mood and low energy be thinking about suicide.

<!-- chunk -->

### These patients have very different symptoms that require differing

<!-- chunk -->

### approaches to treatment. One is thinking about harming himself while the

<!-- chunk -->

### other isn’t. One can’t sleep, so might benefit from a sleeping pill, while the

<!-- chunk -->

### other one is sleeping too much. Despite these striking differences, both

<!-- chunk -->

### might benefit from an antidepressant or psychotherapy.

<!-- chunk -->

### Dr. Alan Schatzberg is a prominent depression researcher and a

<!-- chunk -->

### professor of psychiatry and behavioral sciences at Stanford University who

<!-- chunk -->

### has called for rethinking the diagnostic criteria for major depression.


<!-- chunk -->

### Those

<!-- chunk -->

### in

<!-- chunk -->

### this field are frustrated with the lack of understanding of this common

<!-- chunk -->

### illness and continued poor treatment outcomes—as I mentioned earlier, the

<!-- chunk -->

### likelihood of a full and complete remission of major depression symptoms

<!-- chunk -->

### with the first antidepressant a patient tries is only about 30 to 40 percent.

<!-- chunk -->

### Schatzberg notes that some symptoms that commonly occur in people

<!-- chunk -->

### diagnosed with major depression are not included in the core diagnostic

<!-- chunk -->

### criteria. For example, anxiety is a common symptom in many people with

<!-- chunk -->

### depression, but it’s not among the nine in the DSM. The same goes for

<!-- chunk -->

### irritability, which is experienced by about 40 to 50 percent of people with

<!-- chunk -->

### depression.


<!-- chunk -->

### Pain is common, too, with physical pain present in about

<!-- chunk -->

### percent of people with major depression compared to only about 15 percent

<!-- chunk -->

### of the general population.


<!-- chunk -->

### Are our treatment outcomes so poor because

<!-- chunk -->

### we’re missing or failing to include the treatment of other diagnostic

<!-- chunk -->

### symptoms?

<!-- chunk -->

### It’s not just depression that causes so much confusion and debate.

<!-- chunk -->

### There’s tremendous heterogeneity in all the psychiatric diagnoses.

<!-- chunk -->

### Sometimes, the differences are stark and dramatic. Some people diagnosed

<!-- chunk -->

### with OCD are still able to work and function normally in life, while others

<!-- chunk -->

### are completely disabled by their symptoms. People diagnosed with autism-

<!-- chunk -->

### spectrum disorder can be wildly different from each other. There are high-

<!-- chunk -->

### functioning billionaire businesspeople with this diagnosis, while others live

<!-- chunk -->

### in group homes unable to care for themselves. So, are these singular

<!-- chunk -->

### diagnoses really the same disorders? Or are they all simply on a spectrum,

<!-- chunk -->

### with some people having severe forms of the illness while others have a

<!-- chunk -->

### mild form? Unfortunately, the complexities don’t end there.

<!-- chunk -->

### Comorbidity is the other big factor that accounts for differences in

<!-- chunk -->

### people with the same diagnosis. About half of the people diagnosed with

<!-- chunk -->

### any mental disorder have more than one.


<!-- chunk -->

### We talked about comorbidity a bit

<!-- chunk -->

### in the last chapter: Remember my discussion of runny nose disorder and

<!-- chunk -->

### sore throat disorder? While some people had one or the other, many had

<!-- chunk -->

### both. A similar example in the mental health field is that of depression and

<!-- chunk -->

### anxiety. The majority of people diagnosed with major depression also have

<!-- chunk -->

### anxiety, and most people diagnosed with anxiety disorders also have major

<!-- chunk -->

### depression. For example, in a survey of over nine thousand US households,

<!-- chunk -->

### 68 percent of people with major depression also met criteria for an anxiety

<!-- chunk -->

### disorder at some point in their lives, and several studies have found that

<!-- chunk -->

### one-half to two-thirds of adults with anxiety disorders also meet criteria for

<!-- chunk -->

### major depression.


<!-- chunk -->

### Antidepressants are commonly used to treat both

<!-- chunk -->

### depression and anxiety disorders, while anti-anxiety medications are

<!-- chunk -->

### commonly used to treat people with both anxiety disorders and depression.

<!-- chunk -->

### So, where the diagnoses often overlap and the treatments are sometimes

<!-- chunk -->

### identical, are they really different disorders? Is it possible that they are

<!-- chunk -->

### simply different symptoms of the same disorder? Could anxiety and

<!-- chunk -->

### depression—like runny noses and sore throats—share a common pathway?

<!-- chunk -->

### Finally, diagnoses can change over time. Symptoms can come and go

<!-- chunk -->

### and morph into very different mental disorders, further complicating

<!-- chunk -->

### treatment and diagnosis, and bedeviling the quest to investigate the nature

<!-- chunk -->

### and cause of these disorders.

<!-- chunk -->

### Let’s look at an example.

<!-- chunk -->

### Mike is a forty-three-year-old man with a chronic, disabling mental

<!-- chunk -->

### disorder. But which one? When he was a child, he was diagnosed with

<!-- chunk -->

### ADHD and started taking stimulant medications. They helped somewhat,

<!-- chunk -->

### but school remained difficult. He was often bullied and teased. He reported

<!-- chunk -->

### a lot of anxiety around these social stressors and received psychotherapy for

<!-- chunk -->

### social anxiety disorder. Some clinicians raised the possibility of Asperger’s

<!-- chunk -->

### syndrome, then a diagnosis of its own on the autism spectrum, but they

<!-- chunk -->

### didn’t officially make this diagnosis. By adolescence, he’d developed

<!-- chunk -->

### symptoms of major depression—not surprising given both his academic and

<!-- chunk -->

### social stressors. He was started on an antidepressant, which helped a little.

<!-- chunk -->

### Within a few months, however, Mike began to develop symptoms of mania

<!-- chunk -->

### and was quickly diagnosed with bipolar disorder. He had hallucinations and

<!-- chunk -->

### delusions, and he was given medication targeting both his psychotic and

<!-- chunk -->

### mood symptoms. He was hospitalized several times. Over the next year,

<!-- chunk -->

### when his psychotic symptoms persisted and failed to respond to treatment,

<!-- chunk -->

### his diagnosis was changed to schizoaffective disorder. Also during this

<!-- chunk -->

### time,

<!-- chunk -->

### Mike began to develop obsessions and compulsions, and he was diagnosed

<!-- chunk -->

### with OCD as well. Over the following several years, on top of his

<!-- chunk -->

### continuing psychiatric symptoms, he began to smoke cigarettes and use

<!-- chunk -->

### recreational drugs. Eventually, he became chronically addicted to opioids.

<!-- chunk -->

### So, what is Mike’s diagnosis? According to DSM-5, he currently can be

<!-- chunk -->

### diagnosed with schizoaffective disorder, opioid use disorder, nicotine use

<!-- chunk -->

### disorder, OCD, and social anxiety disorder. But in the past, he also had

<!-- chunk -->

### ADHD, major depression, bipolar disorder, and possibly even Asperger’s

<!-- chunk -->

### syndrome. You might make a case that the major depression diagnosis was

<!-- chunk -->

### a mistake—many people with bipolar disorder are diagnosed with

<!-- chunk -->

### depression before they have a first manic episode that clarifies the

<!-- chunk -->

### diagnostic picture. The same might be argued about the change of diagnosis

<!-- chunk -->

### from bipolar to schizoaffective disorder. But even if you remove one or two

<!-- chunk -->

### of these, you are left with a lengthy list of what are all different disorders—

<!-- chunk -->

### supposedly with different causes and certainly different treatments. Yet

<!-- chunk -->

### Mike has just one brain. Are we to believe that he’s an extraordinarily

<!-- chunk -->

### unlucky individual who developed some half dozen separate and distinct

<!-- chunk -->

### disorders?

<!-- chunk -->

### While Mike’s story is extreme, having more than one diagnosis is

<!-- chunk -->

### common, as are changes in symptoms and diagnoses. Having problems with

<!-- chunk -->

### addiction is also common in people with mental disorders. Stories like

<!-- chunk -->

### Mike’s raise serious questions about the validity of our diagnostic labels. If

<!-- chunk -->

### the diagnoses listed in DSM-5 are really separate and distinct disorders,

<!-- chunk -->

### why do so many people have more than one of them? Why do they change

<!-- chunk -->

### over the course of a lifetime? Do some psychiatric disorders lead to others?

<!-- chunk -->

### If so, which ones come first, and what exactly happens to make them cause

<!-- chunk -->

### other disorders? Alternatively, are some just different symptoms or phases

<!-- chunk -->

### of the same underlying problem? Is it like runny nose disorder and sore

<!-- chunk -->

### throat disorder—two seemingly different disorders that respond to different

<!-- chunk -->

### treatments but share the common pathway of inflammation? Is there a

<!-- chunk -->

### common pathway for mental disorders, even ones that appear to be

<!-- chunk -->

### profoundly different from each other?

# Taking a Deeper Look

<!-- chunk -->

### Researchers have been trying for decades to figure out what makes

<!-- chunk -->

### individual disorders different from each other at a biological level.

<!-- chunk -->

### Interestingly, they don’t yet have any clear answers. In fact, as I’m about to

<!-- chunk -->

### share with you, the research to date suggests that different disorders might

<!-- chunk -->

### not actually be all that different from each other, even though symptoms can

<!-- chunk -->

### vary widely.

<!-- chunk -->

### Let’s look at three of the psychotic disorders—schizophrenia,

<!-- chunk -->

### schizoaffective disorder, and bipolar disorder.

<!-- chunk -->

### The primary feature of a schizophrenia diagnosis is chronic psychotic

<!-- chunk -->

### symptoms, like hallucinations or paranoia. A diagnosis of bipolar disorder

<!-- chunk -->

### is given to people who primarily have mood symptoms—manic and

<!-- chunk -->

### depressive episodes. However, people with bipolar disorder also commonly

<!-- chunk -->

### have psychotic symptoms when they get manic, and even sometimes when

<!-- chunk -->

### they get depressed, but these psychotic symptoms go away after the mood

<!-- chunk -->

### symptoms improve. Schizoaffective disorder is a diagnosis that includes

<!-- chunk -->

### features of both schizophrenia and bipolar disorder, including chronic

<!-- chunk -->

### psychotic symptoms and prominent mood symptoms. Most people consider

<!-- chunk -->

### these disorders unequivocally “real.” Many in the field hold these disorders

<!-- chunk -->

### apart from things like depression and anxiety, sometimes calling these the

<!-- chunk -->

### “biological” disorders. So, what do we know about them? What makes

<!-- chunk -->

### them different from each other?

<!-- chunk -->

### A lot of money has been spent researching this question. The NIMH

<!-- chunk -->

### funded a multisite study called the Bipolar Schizophrenia Network on

<!-- chunk -->

### Intermediate Phenotypes (B-SNIP). This study included more than 2,400

<!-- chunk -->

### people with schizophrenia, schizoaffective disorder, or bipolar disorder;

<!-- chunk -->

### their first-degree relatives; and people without these disorders (normal

<!-- chunk -->

### controls). The researchers examined key biological and behavioral

<!-- chunk -->

### measures, examining brain scans, genetic testing, EEGs, blood parameters,

<!-- chunk -->

### inflammation levels, and performance on a variety of cognitive tests. They

<!-- chunk -->

### found that people with the disorders were different from the normal

<!-- chunk -->

### controls, but they couldn’t tell any of the diagnostic groups apart from each

<!-- chunk -->

### other. In other words, there were abnormalities in the brains and bodies of

<!-- chunk -->

### people with these disorders,

<!-- chunk -->

### but no significant differences at all between those with bipolar disorder,

<!-- chunk -->

### those with schizoaffective disorder, or those with schizophrenia. If they are

<!-- chunk -->

### truly different disorders, how can that be?

<!-- chunk -->

### On the one hand, when we consider more information, maybe these

<!-- chunk -->

### findings aren’t so surprising after all. First, although the diagnosis of

<!-- chunk -->

### schizophrenia isn’t supposed to include prominent mood symptoms, the

<!-- chunk -->

### reality is that one of the common features of schizophrenia is a group of

<!-- chunk -->

### symptoms called negative symptoms. These include blunting of facial

<!-- chunk -->

### expressions, severely reduced speech and thought, losing interest in life

<!-- chunk -->

### (apathy), getting no pleasure from life or activities (anhedonia), reduced

<!-- chunk -->

### drive to interact with others, loss of motivation, and inattention to hygiene.

<!-- chunk -->

### You might notice significant overlap with the symptoms of depression.

<!-- chunk -->

### Interestingly, DSM-5 specifically cautions clinicians against making the

<!-- chunk -->

### diagnosis of major depression in people with schizophrenia, even though

<!-- chunk -->

### many of these negative symptoms are the same symptoms found in

<!-- chunk -->

### depression. Instead, clinicians are encouraged to diagnose a schizophrenia

<!-- chunk -->

### spectrum disorder. The implication is that even though the symptoms might

<!-- chunk -->

### overlap, we shouldn’t call them the same thing. Why not? Is there science

<!-- chunk -->

### to support that recommendation? In reality, the DSM-5 acknowledges in its

<!-- chunk -->

### introductory remarks that we don’t know what causes any of the psychiatric

<!-- chunk -->

### diagnoses. Therefore, if people are having the same symptoms, how can we

<!-- chunk -->

### say they are not caused by the same process?

<!-- chunk -->

### The treatments for these disorders overlap as well—more than you

<!-- chunk -->

### might think. Mood stabilizers, such as lithium, Depakote, and Lamictal, are

<!-- chunk -->

### commonly used in bipolar disorder and have approval from the FDA for

<!-- chunk -->

### such use. However, about 34 percent of people diagnosed with

<!-- chunk -->

### schizophrenia are also prescribed these same mood stabilizers, even though

<!-- chunk -->

### by definition, those with this diagnosis aren’t supposed to have significant

<!-- chunk -->

### mood symptoms.


<!-- chunk -->

### Antidepressants are also commonly used in both bipolar

<!-- chunk -->

### disorder and schizophrenia. Studies show that almost all bipolar patients

<!-- chunk -->

### receive an antidepressant at some point in their illness for their depressive

<!-- chunk -->

### episodes, and about 40 percent of patients diagnosed with schizophrenia do,

<!-- chunk -->

### too.


<!-- chunk -->

### And then there are the antipsychotic medications. These are used for

<!-- chunk -->

### schizophrenia, bipolar disorder, and schizoaffective disorder, and are

<!-- chunk -->

### prescribed to treat all the symptoms of these disorders, not just the

<!-- chunk -->

### psychotic ones. The FDA has even approved many of these medications

<!-- chunk -->

### both as “antipsychotics” and as “mood stabilizers” for the treatment of

<!-- chunk -->

### bipolar disorder.

<!-- chunk -->

### On the other hand, while all of this suggests quite a bit of overlap

<!-- chunk -->

### between bipolar disorder, schizoaffective disorder, and schizophrenia, it is

<!-- chunk -->

### also true that the symptoms of bipolar disorder and schizophrenia can be

<!-- chunk -->

### dramatically different. Many people with bipolar disorder never have

<!-- chunk -->

### psychotic symptoms. Many are never hospitalized, and many function quite

<!-- chunk -->

### well in life. Meanwhile, almost all people with schizophrenia will

<!-- chunk -->

### experience severe impairment in functioning, with the majority qualifying

<!-- chunk -->

### as disabled.


<!-- chunk -->

### That’s not to say that there aren’t high-functioning

<!-- chunk -->

### schizophrenics, or that bipolar disorder can’t be disabling. In fact, one study

<!-- chunk -->

### that followed 146 people with bipolar disorder for almost thirteen years

<!-- chunk -->

### found that the people were symptomatically ill about 47 percent of the time

<!-- chunk -->

### despite treatment.


<!-- chunk -->

### It’s difficult to keep a job when you are ill almost half

<!-- chunk -->

### the time. But there are definite differences in the usual presentation of these

<!-- chunk -->

### diagnoses. Could it be that people with schizophrenia have a more severe

<!-- chunk -->

### form of the same illness, or one less responsive to our current treatments,

<!-- chunk -->

### while people with bipolar disorder may have a milder illness and/or

<!-- chunk -->

### symptoms that respond better to our treatments, resulting in episodes of

<!-- chunk -->

### recovery?

<!-- chunk -->

### Dr. Bruce Cuthbert, the acting director of the NIMH at the time of the

<!-- chunk -->

### B-SNIP study, suggested, “Just as fever or infection can have many

<!-- chunk -->

### different causes, multiple psychosis-causing disease processes—operating

<!-- chunk -->

### via different biological pathways—can lead to similar symptoms,

<!-- chunk -->

### confounding the search for better care.”


<!-- chunk -->

### However, the study failed to find

<!-- chunk -->

### any hallmark biological markers to distinguish the diagnoses. What

<!-- chunk -->

### Cuthbert didn’t mention is that we know that fever is itself a symptom, with

<!-- chunk -->

### one clearly defined biological pathway—inflammation that triggers the

<!-- chunk -->

### hypothalamus to increase body temperature. However, there are many

<!-- chunk -->

### things that can trigger the inflammation, such as infections or allergic

<!-- chunk -->

### reactions. Diverse infections

<!-- chunk -->

### can have the same symptoms, through common pathways, even when the

<!-- chunk -->

### infectious agents (bacterial or viral) are different.

<!-- chunk -->

### It seems very plausible that the symptoms of bipolar disorder,

<!-- chunk -->

### schizoaffective disorder, and schizophrenia all share a common pathway as

<!-- chunk -->

### well.

# Sorting Out the Overlaps

<!-- chunk -->

### I have now suggested that bipolar disorder, schizophrenia, and

<!-- chunk -->

### schizoaffective disorder are possibly the same illness, but on a spectrum of

<!-- chunk -->

### symptoms and with different responses to existing treatments. Earlier in the

<!-- chunk -->

### chapter I suggested that major depression and anxiety disorders might be

<!-- chunk -->

### similarly related and share a common pathway. For many people in the

<!-- chunk -->

### field, neither of these assertions is difficult to grasp or believe. Mental

<!-- chunk -->

### health professionals have struggled with these distinctions for decades and

<!-- chunk -->

### know all too well about the overlap in these disorders and their treatments.

<!-- chunk -->

### However, the overlap doesn’t stop with these conditions.

<!-- chunk -->

### Symptoms overlap between all kinds of mental diagnoses, not just those

<!-- chunk -->

### you would expect to be related. As I’ve mentioned, many different

<!-- chunk -->

### disorders, both mental and medical, can lead to psychotic symptoms. In

<!-- chunk -->

### fact, about 10 percent of patients diagnosed with major depression will have

<!-- chunk -->

### psychotic symptoms.


<!-- chunk -->

### Anxiety symptoms are also common in multiple

<!-- chunk -->

### diagnoses. The overall prevalence of anxiety disorders in the general

<!-- chunk -->

### population is quite high to begin with—in any given year, about 19 percent

<!-- chunk -->

### will experience an anxiety disorder. When looking at lifetime prevalence,

<!-- chunk -->

### that number rises to 33 percent, meaning that one out of three people will

<!-- chunk -->

### meet the criteria for an anxiety disorder at some point in their life.


<!-- chunk -->

### rates in people with depression, bipolar disorder, schizophrenia, and

<!-- chunk -->

### schizoaffective disorder are much higher—about double. Sometimes, we

<!-- chunk -->

### just rationalize these symptoms away: “Wouldn’t you be anxious if you had

<!-- chunk -->

### schizophrenia?” As appealing and intuitive as this sounds, it’s not so

<!-- chunk -->

### simple. There is a strong bidirectional relationship between schizophrenia

<!-- chunk -->

### and anxiety disorders. In other words, people who first manifest an anxiety

<!-- chunk -->

### disorder

<!-- chunk -->

### have anywhere from an eight-to thirteen-fold increased risk of developing

<!-- chunk -->

### schizophrenia or schizoaffective disorder.


<!-- chunk -->

### Those are not trivial increases.

<!-- chunk -->

### But why should this be?

<!-- chunk -->

### In 2005, Dr. Ronald Kessler and colleagues reported the results of the

<!-- chunk -->

### US National Comorbidity Survey Replication, a household survey that

<!-- chunk -->

### included a diagnostic interview of more than nine thousand representative

<!-- chunk -->

### people across the United States.


<!-- chunk -->

### Overall, 26 percent of people surveyed

<!-- chunk -->

### met criteria for a mental disorder in the last twelve months—that’s one in

<!-- chunk -->

### four Americans! Of those disorders, 22 percent were serious, 37 percent

<!-- chunk -->

### were moderate, and 40 percent were mild. Anxiety disorders were most

<!-- chunk -->

### common, followed by mood disorders, then impulse control disorders,

<!-- chunk -->

### which include diagnoses like ADHD. Of note, 55 percent of people had

<!-- chunk -->

### only one diagnosis, 22 percent had two diagnoses, and the rest had three or

<!-- chunk -->

### more psychiatric diagnoses. That means almost half the people met criteria

<!-- chunk -->

### for more than one disorder.

<!-- chunk -->

### Diagnostic overlap is easier to dismiss when we are talking about

<!-- chunk -->

### anxiety disorders, perhaps because anxiety is a mental state we all

<!-- chunk -->

### experience. So let’s look at autism spectrum disorder. Most people don’t

<!-- chunk -->

### think of autism as a purely “mental” illness, but more as a developmental or

<!-- chunk -->

### neurological disorder that starts early in life. Yet 70 percent of people with

<!-- chunk -->

### autism have at least one other mental disorder and almost 50 percent have

<!-- chunk -->

### two or more.


<!-- chunk -->

### It’s also interesting to note that embedded in the criteria of

<!-- chunk -->

### autism spectrum disorder are many of the symptoms of obsessive-

<!-- chunk -->

### compulsive disorder (OCD).

<!-- chunk -->

### And what happens to people with autism over longer terms? Are they at

<!-- chunk -->

### higher risk for developing additional mental disorders? Again, the answer is

<!-- chunk -->

### often yes. A prominent feature of autism is impairment in social skills, so it

<!-- chunk -->

### stands to reason that a diagnosis of social anxiety disorder could follow if

<!-- chunk -->

### interactions caused anxiety. In such a case, many would assume the autism

<!-- chunk -->

### spectrum disorder came first, and that the social anxiety was an

<!-- chunk -->

### understandable consequence of the autism. However, it’s now well

<!-- chunk -->

### documented that autism itself puts people at higher risk for developing

<!-- chunk -->

### every other type of mental disorder.


<!-- chunk -->

### This includes mood disorders,

<!-- chunk -->

### psychotic

<!-- chunk -->

### disorders, behavioral disorders, eating disorders, and substance use

<!-- chunk -->

### disorders. How can this be? Is it just that autism is stressful? We know that

<!-- chunk -->

### stress can put people at risk for all sorts of mental disorders, and having

<!-- chunk -->

### autism is undoubtedly stressful. But as you will learn, the explanation is

<!-- chunk -->

### much more complex than that.

<!-- chunk -->

### This phenomenon isn’t limited to anxiety disorders or autism spectrum

<!-- chunk -->

### disorder, either. Looking at eating disorders, bulimia nervosa occurs in

<!-- chunk -->

### about 1 percent of the population, anorexia nervosa in about 0.6 percent,

<!-- chunk -->

### and binge eating disorder (the newest disorder in the category) in about

<!-- chunk -->

### percent.


<!-- chunk -->

### Many people consider these societal disorders rather than

<!-- chunk -->

### biological brain disorders. Yet overall, 56 percent of people with anorexia,

<!-- chunk -->

### 79 percent of people with binge eating disorder, and 95 percent of people

<!-- chunk -->

### with bulimia have at least one other mental disorder.


<!-- chunk -->

### So here we go again

<!-- chunk -->

### —which one comes first? Do eating disorders cause other mental disorders,

<!-- chunk -->

### or do other disorders cause eating disorders? Both: There is a bidirectional

<!-- chunk -->

### relationship between eating disorders and other mental disorders. Which

<!-- chunk -->

### other disorders, you ask? All of them. The same is true of addiction. Again,

<!-- chunk -->

### it’s a bidirectional relationship. People with any substance use disorder are

<!-- chunk -->

### at higher risk of developing a mental disorder, and people with mental

<!-- chunk -->

### disorders are at much higher risk of using and abusing addictive substances.

<!-- chunk -->

### Why is that?

<!-- chunk -->

### I could go on this way, diagnosis by diagnosis, but I won’t—an

<!-- chunk -->

### important 2019 study clarifies the bigger picture. In this study, researchers

<!-- chunk -->

### used a Danish health registry to analyze psychiatric diagnoses in almost six

<!-- chunk -->

### million people over seventeen years.


<!-- chunk -->

### What they found was that having

<!-- chunk -->

### any mental disorder dramatically increased the chances of that person later

<!-- chunk -->

### developing another mental disorder. There were strong bidirectional

<!-- chunk -->

### relationships for everything! Even disorders that most people think are

<!-- chunk -->

### completely unrelated—schizophrenia and eating disorders, intellectual

<!-- chunk -->

### disability and schizophrenia. Mix and match them however you like. The

<!-- chunk -->

### odds ratios across the board in this study were generally between two and

<!-- chunk -->

### thirty. This means that if you were diagnosed with any mental disorder, you

<!-- chunk -->

### were two to thirty times more likely to later be diagnosed with another

<!-- chunk -->

### mental disorder. Which one? Any of them! While some of the very high

<!-- chunk -->

### odds ratios are due

<!-- chunk -->

### to overlap in symptoms between different disorders, the point is that the

<!-- chunk -->

### odds ratios were elevated for all disorders in all directions.

<!-- chunk -->

### What’s more, this bidirectional relationship also applied to mental

<!-- chunk -->

### disorders and so-called “organic” mental disorders. “Organic mental

<!-- chunk -->

### disorder” is the term used to refer to symptoms of a mental disorder that are

<!-- chunk -->

### thought to be caused by a medical condition or a medication. We discussed

<!-- chunk -->

### this briefly earlier: For example, if someone with cancer loses their appetite

<!-- chunk -->

### and gets depressed, they often are not diagnosed with major depression. The

<!-- chunk -->

### assumption is that these symptoms are due to the cancer, and not a true

<!-- chunk -->

### “mental” disorder. Yet the evidence of this study now shows that if people

<!-- chunk -->

### develop “mental” symptoms attributed to a medical problem, they are then

<!-- chunk -->

### much more likely to develop a mental disorder in the future—and vice

<!-- chunk -->

### versa. This finding certainly raises the question of whether separating

<!-- chunk -->

### “organic” mental disorders from the rest really makes sense.

<!-- chunk -->

### All told, this study raised several important questions. Bidirectional

<!-- chunk -->

### relationships, especially ones that are particularly strong in both directions,

<!-- chunk -->

### suggest a common pathway does exist. While the symptoms may differ,

<!-- chunk -->

### perhaps our diagnoses are far more similar than we’ve long thought.

<!-- chunk -->

### The Danish study wasn’t the first piece of scholarship to suggest all

<!-- chunk -->

### mental disorders might share one common pathway. In 2012, Dr. Benjamin

<!-- chunk -->

### Lahey and colleagues studied the symptoms and prognosis of eleven

<!-- chunk -->

### different mental disorders in thirty thousand people.


<!-- chunk -->

### They looked at

<!-- chunk -->

### “internalizing” versus “externalizing” disorders. Internalizing disorders are

<!-- chunk -->

### ones in which distress is thought to be directed inward, such as depression

<!-- chunk -->

### and anxiety disorders. Externalizing ones are when distress is outwardly

<!-- chunk -->

### directed, such as substance use disorders or antisocial behavior. They found

<!-- chunk -->

### tremendous overlap in these different disorders and raised the possibility of

<!-- chunk -->

### a “general factor” leading to all of them.

<!-- chunk -->

### In 2018, doctors Avshalom Caspi and Terrie Moffitt took this research

<!-- chunk -->

### further by including all mental disorders in a review article, “All for One

<!-- chunk -->

### and One for All: Mental Disorders in One Dimension.”


<!-- chunk -->

### They reviewed a

<!-- chunk -->

### tremendous amount of research, including epidemiological studies, brain

<!-- chunk -->

### imaging studies, and studies of known risk factors for mental disorders,

<!-- chunk -->

### such

<!-- chunk -->

### as genetics and childhood trauma. The data was exhaustive, encompassing

<!-- chunk -->

### research on people of different ages, including children, adolescents, and

<!-- chunk -->

### adults, and from many different parts of the world. After examining all this

<!-- chunk -->

### data, they found strong correlations among all mental disorders. When they

<!-- chunk -->

### looked at risk factors for mental disorders, they found that not one risk

<!-- chunk -->

### factor conferred risk only for a specific disorder—instead, each and every

<!-- chunk -->

### risk factor conferred risk for many. For example, one study they examined

<!-- chunk -->

### looked at the genetics of psychiatric disorders.


<!-- chunk -->

### The study evaluated more

<!-- chunk -->

### than three million siblings, hoping to identify which genes conferred risk

<!-- chunk -->

### for depression, anxiety, ADHD, alcoholism, drug abuse, schizophrenia, and

<!-- chunk -->

### schizoaffective disorder. Given that these are all different disorders, one

<!-- chunk -->

### would expect them to have different associated genes. However, the

<!-- chunk -->

### researchers found that most of the genetic variations conferred risk for a

<!-- chunk -->

### broad range of disorders. There were no genes that were specific to only

<!-- chunk -->

### one disorder. Even childhood abuse confers risk for most mental disorders,

<!-- chunk -->

### including PTSD, depression, anxiety, substance use disorders, eating

<!-- chunk -->

### disorders, bipolar disorder, and schizophrenia.

<!-- chunk -->

### Given the never-ending overlap in correlations among all mental

<!-- chunk -->

### disorders and all of their risk factors, Caspi and Moffitt used a complex

<!-- chunk -->

### mathematical model to analyze these correlations in the hope of making

<!-- chunk -->

### sense of them. This model offered a shocking conclusion. It suggested that

<!-- chunk -->

### there appears to be one common pathway to all mental illnesses. Caspi and

<!-- chunk -->

### Moffitt called it the p-factor, in which the p stands for general

<!-- chunk -->

### psychopathology. They argued that this factor appears to predict a person’s

<!-- chunk -->

### liability to develop a mental disorder, to have more than one disorder, to

<!-- chunk -->

### have a chronic disorder, and it can even predict the severity of symptoms.

<!-- chunk -->

### This p-factor is common to hundreds of different psychiatric symptoms and

<!-- chunk -->

### every psychiatric diagnosis. Subsequent research using different sets of

<!-- chunk -->

### people and different methods confirmed the existence of this p-factor.


<!-- chunk -->

### However, this research was not designed to tell us what the p-factor is. It

<!-- chunk -->

### only suggests that it exists—that there is an unidentified variable that plays

<!-- chunk -->

### a role in all mental disorders.

<!-- chunk -->

### Our job is to figure out what it could be.

# W


# Could It All Be Related?

<!-- chunk -->
