### hat if I told you this common pathway that we’re searching for

<!-- chunk -->

### might not be limited to mental health conditions?

<!-- chunk -->

### As we’ve seen, the medical field currently separates mental

<!-- chunk -->

### disorders from other medical disorders. They are viewed as separate

<!-- chunk -->

### categories that have little to nothing to do with each other.

<!-- chunk -->

### But there are many medical disorders that commonly co-occur with

<!-- chunk -->

### mental disorders and vice versa. Yes—here we go again with bidirectional

<!-- chunk -->

### relationships: Not only do mental disorders have strong bidirectional

<!-- chunk -->

### relationships with one another, many metabolic and neurological disorders

<!-- chunk -->

### also have strong bidirectional relationships with mental disorders. These

<!-- chunk -->

### relationships provide important clues about the nature of the common

<!-- chunk -->

### pathway that will help us solve the puzzle of mental illness.

<!-- chunk -->

### To explore these relationships, I’m going to focus on three metabolic

<!-- chunk -->

### disorders (obesity, diabetes, cardiovascular disease) and two neurological

<!-- chunk -->

### disorders (Alzheimer’s disease and epilepsy). All five of these conditions

<!-- chunk -->

### are commonly associated with mental symptoms like depression, anxiety,

<!-- chunk -->

### insomnia, and even psychosis. On the flip side, people who have mental

<!-- chunk -->

### disorders are at much higher risk of developing these five medical

<!-- chunk -->

### disorders. Clearly not all people with these medical disorders have a mental

<!-- chunk -->

### illness, and not all people with a mental illness develop any of these

<!-- chunk -->

### medical disorders.

<!-- chunk -->

### When patients with one of these medical conditions do have mental

<!-- chunk -->

### illness symptoms, they’re sometimes overlooked as normal reactions to

<!-- chunk -->

### difficult diseases. Those with heart failure are often depressed, which is

<!-- chunk -->

### presented as understandable given the severity of heart failure. And whether

<!-- chunk -->

### people with these conditions who are experiencing mental symptoms get

<!-- chunk -->

### diagnosed with a “mental” disorder is up to clinicians, who have the

<!-- chunk -->

### discretion to attribute these mental symptoms to the “organic” illnesses. In

<!-- chunk -->

### the end, though, the symptoms are the same regardless of what cause they

<!-- chunk -->

### are attributed to. Depression is the same. Anxiety is the same. Paranoia is

<!-- chunk -->

### the same. The treatments are the same as well: antidepressants, anti-anxiety

<!-- chunk -->

### medications, and antipsychotics are all commonly used in people with these

<!-- chunk -->

### “organic” disorders.

<!-- chunk -->

### Looking at these disorders more closely will illuminate the connections

<!-- chunk -->

### between metabolism, metabolic disorders, and disorders of the brain,

<!-- chunk -->

### whether mental or neurological. They will help us put the final pieces of the

<!-- chunk -->

### puzzle in place.

# Metabolic Disorders

<!-- chunk -->

### Let’s start with our three metabolic disorders: obesity, diabetes, and

<!-- chunk -->

### cardiovascular disease. The term “metabolic disorders” actually includes

<!-- chunk -->

### many more disorders, but it most commonly refers to disorders associated

<!-- chunk -->

### with metabolic syndrome. This is a syndrome that is diagnosed when people

<!-- chunk -->

### have three or more of the following conditions: increased blood pressure,

<!-- chunk -->

### high blood sugar, excess body fat around the waist, high triglycerides, and

<!-- chunk -->

### low HDL (or “good cholesterol”). People with metabolic syndrome are at

<!-- chunk -->

### increased risk for developing type 2 diabetes, heart attacks, and strokes.

<!-- chunk -->

## Diabetes

<!-- chunk -->

### The connection between diabetes and mental illness has been known for

<!-- chunk -->

### more than a century. In 1879, Sir Henry Maudsley wrote, “Diabetes is a

<!-- chunk -->

### disease that often shows itself in families in which insanity prevails.” Many

<!-- chunk -->

### mental disorders are associated with higher rates of diabetes. People with

<!-- chunk -->

### schizophrenia are three times more likely to develop diabetes.


<!-- chunk -->

### People

<!-- chunk -->

### diagnosed with depression are 60 percent more likely to develop diabetes.


<!-- chunk -->

### What about the other way around? Are people with diabetes more likely

<!-- chunk -->

### to develop mental disorders? Yes. Most of the research has focused on

<!-- chunk -->

### depression and diabetes. People with diabetes are two to three times more

<!-- chunk -->

### likely to develop major depression. Furthermore, when they get depressed,

<!-- chunk -->

### the depression lasts four times longer than it does in those without diabetes.

<!-- chunk -->

### At any given time, about one in four people with diabetes has clinically

<!-- chunk -->

### significant depression.


<!-- chunk -->

### What’s more, the depression appears to affect

<!-- chunk -->

### blood-glucose levels—diabetics with depression tend to have higher

<!-- chunk -->

### glucose readings than those without depression. However, it’s not just

<!-- chunk -->

### depression. One study of 1.3 million adolescents looked at rates of mental

<!-- chunk -->

### illness over the following ten years. Adolescents with diabetes were more

<!-- chunk -->

### likely to suffer from a mood disorder, attempt suicide, visit a psychiatrist, or

<!-- chunk -->

### develop any psychiatric disorder.


<!-- chunk -->

## Obesity

<!-- chunk -->

### We know that people with mental disorders are more likely to be

<!-- chunk -->

### overweight or obese. One study followed people diagnosed with

<!-- chunk -->

### schizophrenia and bipolar disorder for twenty years. When they were first

<!-- chunk -->

### diagnosed, the majority were not obese. Twenty years later, 62 percent of

<!-- chunk -->

### those with schizophrenia and 50 percent of those with bipolar disorder were

<!-- chunk -->

### obese.


<!-- chunk -->

### The obesity rate at the time for all adults in New York State, where

<!-- chunk -->

### the study was conducted, was 27 percent. Children with autism are

<!-- chunk -->

### percent more likely to be obese.


<!-- chunk -->

### One meta-analysis of 120 studies found

<!-- chunk -->

### that people with serious mental illness were three times more likely to be

<!-- chunk -->

### obese than people without a mental illness.


<!-- chunk -->

### Many people assume that our treatments are causing this obesity. While

<!-- chunk -->

### there’s no doubt that psychiatric medications are associated with weight

<!-- chunk -->

### gain—in fact it’s a common side effect of antidepressants and

<!-- chunk -->

### antipsychotics—treatments alone don’t provide the entire explanation. For

<!-- chunk -->

### example, one study looked at people with ADHD who were either treated or

<!-- chunk -->

### not treated with medications, and then it assessed their rates of obesity over

<!-- chunk -->

### the ensuing years compared to people without ADHD. They found that all

<!-- chunk -->

### people with ADHD, whether treated or not, were more likely to develop

<!-- chunk -->

### obesity. Even though the primary treatment for ADHD is usually a

<!-- chunk -->

### stimulant medication, which generally suppresses appetite, the people with

<!-- chunk -->

### ADHD who were treated with stimulants were still more likely to develop

<!-- chunk -->

### obesity than those without ADHD. Those who didn’t take stimulants were

<!-- chunk -->

### even more likely to become obese.


<!-- chunk -->

### What about people who are obese? Are they more likely to develop a

<!-- chunk -->

### mental disorder? Again, the answer is yes. People who are obese are

<!-- chunk -->

### percent more likely to develop depression or an anxiety disorder and

<!-- chunk -->

### percent more likely to develop bipolar disorder. One study found that

<!-- chunk -->

### weight gain around the time of puberty was associated with a fourfold

<!-- chunk -->

### increase in the risk of depression by age twenty-four.


<!-- chunk -->

### Obesity has been

<!-- chunk -->

### found to affect brain function in ways known to lead to mental disorders as

<!-- chunk -->

### well. For example, people with obesity have been found to have altered

<!-- chunk -->

### connections between brain regions as well as alterations in a region of the

<!-- chunk -->

### brain called the hypothalamus


<!-- chunk -->

### that are common in people with mental


<!-- chunk -->

## Cardiovascular Diseases

<!-- chunk -->

### Cardiovascular diseases—particularly heart attacks and strokes—also have

<!-- chunk -->

### bidirectional relationships with mental disorders. Again looking at

<!-- chunk -->

### depression, we find that 20 percent of people with heart attacks, 33 percent

<!-- chunk -->

### with congestive heart failure, and 31 percent with strokes experience major

<!-- chunk -->

### depression within a year of the event or condition.


<!-- chunk -->

### These rates of

<!-- chunk -->

### occurrence are three to five times higher than those in the US population as

<!-- chunk -->

### a whole.

<!-- chunk -->

### This seems easy to understand on the surface. Most people would be

<!-- chunk -->

### worried or depressed following a traumatic event like a heart attack or

<!-- chunk -->

### stroke. However, we’re witnessing another bidirectional relationship,

<!-- chunk -->

### suggesting this is more than just a psychological reaction.

<!-- chunk -->

### We know that depression affects the heart. In people who have never

<!-- chunk -->

### suffered a heart attack, experiencing major depression increases the risk of

<!-- chunk -->

### having a future heart attack by 50 to 100 percent.


<!-- chunk -->

### In people who have

<!-- chunk -->

### already suffered a heart attack, being depressed doubles the chances that

<!-- chunk -->

### they’ll have another heart attack in the next year.

<!-- chunk -->

### And it doesn’t stop with depression. People diagnosed with

<!-- chunk -->

### schizophrenia and bipolar disorder are 53 percent more likely to develop

<!-- chunk -->

### premature cardiovascular disease.


<!-- chunk -->

### This is even after controlling for risk

<!-- chunk -->

### factors like obesity and diabetes. A thirteen-year study of almost one

<!-- chunk -->

### million veterans found that individuals who were diagnosed with PTSD

<!-- chunk -->

### were twice as likely to have a transient ischemic attack (temporary

<!-- chunk -->

### symptoms of a stroke) and 62 percent more likely to have a stroke.


<!-- chunk -->

### We’ve long known that people with serious mental disorders such as

<!-- chunk -->

### schizophrenia, bipolar disorder, and severe chronic depression die at a much

<!-- chunk -->

### younger age than they should. On average, they lose between thirteen and

<!-- chunk -->

### thirty years from their normal lifespans.


<!-- chunk -->

### Recent research from a Danish

<!-- chunk -->

### population database of more than seven million people suggests something

<!-- chunk -->

### more alarming.


<!-- chunk -->

### It’s not just the “serious” mental disorders that result in a

<!-- chunk -->

### shortened lifespan. All mental disorders—even mild or common ones, like

<!-- chunk -->

### anxiety disorders or ADHD—are associated with shortened lifespans. On

<!-- chunk -->

### average, men with mental disorders lose ten years of life and women lose

<!-- chunk -->

### seven.

<!-- chunk -->

### What are these people dying from so early? Most think suicide is

<!-- chunk -->

### responsible, but it’s not. Although suicide rates are definitely higher in the

<!-- chunk -->

### mentally ill, the early deaths in this group are primarily due to heart attacks,

<!-- chunk -->

### strokes, and diabetes—metabolic disorders. We have just seen that people

<!-- chunk -->

### with mental disorders have much higher rates of these conditions.

<!-- chunk -->

### Even before they die, we now know that people with chronic mental

<!-- chunk -->

### disorders appear to be aging prematurely. We can see this through a variety

<!-- chunk -->

### of metrics of the aging process. One such metric is the length of telomeres,

<!-- chunk -->

### which are the end caps of chromosomes. They tend to get shorter

<!-- chunk -->

### as people age. Shortened telomeres have been found in people with diseases

<!-- chunk -->

### that you would expect to be associated with aging, such as obesity, cancer,

<!-- chunk -->

### cardiovascular disease, and diabetes. They have also been found to be

<!-- chunk -->

### shorter in people with depression, bipolar disorder, PTSD, and substance

<!-- chunk -->

### use disorders.


# Neurological Disorders

<!-- chunk -->

### Even though both neurological and mental disorders affect the brain and

<!-- chunk -->

### both commonly include “mental” symptoms, they are distinguished based

<!-- chunk -->

### on one thing: Neurological disorders have at least one objective test or

<!-- chunk -->

### pathological finding that can be used in diagnosing the disorder. This can be

<!-- chunk -->

### an abnormality on a brain scan or EEG, or it can be a specific pathological

<!-- chunk -->

### finding in brain tissue or the fluid surrounding the brain. As I’ve already

<!-- chunk -->

### shared with you, mental disorders have no objective tests that can be used

<!-- chunk -->

### in diagnosis.

<!-- chunk -->

## Alzheimer’s

<!-- chunk -->

### Alzheimer’s disease is the most common form of dementia, which is a

<!-- chunk -->

### group of neurological disorders that impair brain function over time.

<!-- chunk -->

### Common symptoms of all dementias include memory disturbances, changes

<!-- chunk -->

### in personality, and compromised judgment. The hallmark findings in

<!-- chunk -->

### Alzheimer’s disease are plaques and tangles in the brain. As people get

<!-- chunk -->

### older, their risk for Alzheimer’s goes up exponentially, doubling every five

<!-- chunk -->

### years after sixty-five. By age eighty-five, about 33 percent of all people will

<!-- chunk -->

### have Alzheimer’s disease.


<!-- chunk -->

### There are types of early-onset Alzheimer’s

<!-- chunk -->

### disease that can be caused by rare genetic mutations or Down’s syndrome.

<!-- chunk -->

### However, for everyone else, it’s not clear exactly what causes it. Besides

<!-- chunk -->

### age, some of the known risk factors include a family history of the disease,

<!-- chunk -->

### head trauma . . . and metabolic disorders.

<!-- chunk -->

### Obesity in midlife, diabetes, and heart disease all increase the risk of

<!-- chunk -->

### developing Alzheimer’s. So do the risk factors for metabolic disorders, like

<!-- chunk -->

### smoking cigarettes, high blood pressure, high cholesterol, and a lack of

<!-- chunk -->

### exercise. Interestingly, one of the genetic risk factors involves a gene

<!-- chunk -->

### variant called APOE4—which codes for an enzyme related to fat and

<!-- chunk -->

### cholesterol metabolism.

<!-- chunk -->

### Things often considered to be “mental” are also risk factors. Having

<!-- chunk -->

### depression earlier in life doubles a person’s chances of developing

<!-- chunk -->

### Alzheimer’s disease.


<!-- chunk -->

### Schizophrenia dramatically increases the chances,

<!-- chunk -->

### too—one study of more than eight million people found that if someone

<!-- chunk -->

### with schizophrenia lives to the relatively young age of sixty-six, they are

<!-- chunk -->

### twenty times more likely to be diagnosed with dementia than those without

<!-- chunk -->

### schizophrenia.


<!-- chunk -->

### And remember that large study of the Danish population

<!-- chunk -->

### that found bidirectional relationships among all the different psychiatric

<!-- chunk -->

### disorders? Alzheimer’s disease was included under the category of organic

<!-- chunk -->

### mental disorders, the label used to classify mental symptoms due to medical

<!-- chunk -->

### conditions, such as delirium and other types of dementia. In that study,

<!-- chunk -->

### every psychiatric disorder increased the chances of developing an organic

<!-- chunk -->

### mental disorder—anywhere from a 50 percent increase up to a twentyfold

<!-- chunk -->

### increase. Unfortunately, Alzheimer’s disease wasn’t separated from the

<!-- chunk -->

### other organic mental illnesses, but the two most common organic mental

<!-- chunk -->

### disorders are delirium and Alzheimer’s disease.

<!-- chunk -->

### The first signs of Alzheimer’s disease are usually forgetfulness and

<!-- chunk -->

### “mental” symptoms, such as depression, anxiety, or personality changes.

<!-- chunk -->

### Once Alzheimer’s is diagnosed, almost all patients will develop psychiatric

<!-- chunk -->

### symptoms—97 percent in one study.


<!-- chunk -->

### These can include just about any you

<!-- chunk -->

### can think of—anxiety, depression, personality changes, agitation, insomnia,

<!-- chunk -->

### social withdrawal, you name it. About 50 percent of Alzheimer’s patients

<!-- chunk -->

### will develop psychotic symptoms like hallucinations and delusions.


<!-- chunk -->

### So, essentially every psychiatric symptom can emerge with Alzheimer’s

<!-- chunk -->

### disease. If that’s the case, what’s causing these symptoms? Is it the same

<!-- chunk -->

### cause as in people who develop mental symptoms and disorders earlier in

<!-- chunk -->

### life? One thing is certain: this overlap of identical symptoms means we

<!-- chunk -->

### can’t truly address the question of what causes mental illness without

<!-- chunk -->

### looking at Alzheimer’s disease.

<!-- chunk -->

## Epilepsy

<!-- chunk -->

### Epilepsy is a relatively rare brain disorder that also has a bidirectional

<!-- chunk -->

### relationship with mental disorders. Epilepsy can begin at any age, but it

<!-- chunk -->

### most often begins in childhood, affecting about one in 150 children.

<!-- chunk -->

### Sometimes the cause is due to a clearly identified brain abnormality, such as

<!-- chunk -->

### a stroke, brain injury, tumor, or a rare genetic mutation. For most, however,

<!-- chunk -->

### the cause is unknown.

<!-- chunk -->

### People with epilepsy often have psychiatric symptoms. Sometimes these

<!-- chunk -->

### symptoms lead to a diagnosis of a mental disorder. Other times, however,

<!-- chunk -->

### the symptoms are assumed to be due to the seizures themselves. There is no

<!-- chunk -->

### question that seizures can produce emotions, sensations, or behaviors that

<!-- chunk -->

### are unusual. However, people with epilepsy are also more likely to

<!-- chunk -->

### experience mental symptoms even when they are not seizing.

<!-- chunk -->

### Twenty to forty percent of children with epilepsy also have an

<!-- chunk -->

### intellectual disability, ADHD, or autism diagnosis.


<!-- chunk -->

### Anxiety disorders are

<!-- chunk -->

### also common in those with epilepsy, occurring at a three- to sixfold higher

<!-- chunk -->

### rate compared to the general population.


<!-- chunk -->

### One study found that 55 percent

<!-- chunk -->

### of people with epilepsy suffered depression, with one-third of all epileptics

<!-- chunk -->

### reporting at least one suicide attempt.


<!-- chunk -->

### Interestingly, the suicide attempts

<!-- chunk -->

### often occurred before the epilepsy diagnosis.


<!-- chunk -->

### Other studies have found a

<!-- chunk -->

### sixfold increase in bipolar disorder and a ninefold increase in

<!-- chunk -->

### schizophrenia.


<!-- chunk -->

### The data make clear that psychiatric diagnoses—across the

<!-- chunk -->

### board—are extraordinarily common with epilepsy.

<!-- chunk -->

### What about the other way around? Indeed, it appears that people with

<!-- chunk -->

### mental disorders are more likely to have epilepsy or experience seizures in

<!-- chunk -->

### general. Anywhere between 6 to 27 percent of children with autism will

<!-- chunk -->

### develop seizures.


<!-- chunk -->

### Signs of epilepsy show up on the EEGs of 16 percent of

<!-- chunk -->

### children with ADHD.


<!-- chunk -->

### Additionally, children who have seizures are two

<!-- chunk -->

### and a half times more likely to already be diagnosed with ADHD.


<!-- chunk -->

### Later

<!-- chunk -->

### in life, a diagnosis of major depression increases the chances of having an

<!-- chunk -->

### unprovoked seizure sixfold.


<!-- chunk -->

### Seizures offer us an important clue on the way to our common pathway,

<!-- chunk -->

### further connecting the dots between metabolic, mental, and neurological

<!-- chunk -->

### disorders: not only is there a relationship between epilepsy and mental

<!-- chunk -->

### illness, there is a relationship between epilepsy and metabolic disorders as

<!-- chunk -->

### well.

<!-- chunk -->

### We’ve long known that hypoglycemia (low blood sugar) can cause

<!-- chunk -->

### seizures. This is commonly seen in diabetics—both types 1 and 2. Diabetics

<!-- chunk -->

### can get low blood sugar from either too much medication or not eating

<!-- chunk -->

### enough. However, are people with diabetes more likely to have seizures

<!-- chunk -->

### unrelated to severe hypoglycemia? Yes. Children with type 1 diabetes are

<!-- chunk -->

### three times more likely to develop epilepsy


<!-- chunk -->

### —six times more likely if the

<!-- chunk -->

### diabetes begins before age six.


<!-- chunk -->

### Adults sixty-five and over with type

<!-- chunk -->

### diabetes were found to have a 50 percent higher chance of developing

<!-- chunk -->

### epilepsy.


<!-- chunk -->

### What about obesity? You might think weight has nothing to do with

<!-- chunk -->

### epilepsy, and yet a large study showed that people who are extremely

<!-- chunk -->

### underweight or overweight were 60 to 70 percent more likely to develop

<!-- chunk -->

### epilepsy than people of a normal weight.


<!-- chunk -->

### That both being over- and

<!-- chunk -->

### underweight are risk factors may be a surprise, but as I’ll explain later, both

<!-- chunk -->

### extremes are stressful to metabolism. Additionally, women who are obese

<!-- chunk -->

### during pregnancy are more likely to give birth to children who go on to

<!-- chunk -->

### develop epilepsy, with rates increasing as the mothers get heavier. Women

<!-- chunk -->

### with a BMI greater than 40 have an 82 percent higher risk of having

<!-- chunk -->

### children with epilepsy—almost double the risk in the general population.


# Hiding in Plain Sight

<!-- chunk -->

### So here we are, faced with the curious fact that mental disorders have

<!-- chunk -->

### bidirectional relationships not only with each other, but with these

<!-- chunk -->

### seemingly very different medical disorders as well. Recall that bidirectional

<!-- chunk -->

### relationships suggest a possible common pathway—something in common

<!-- chunk -->

### that is causing or contributing to all these disorders. Is that possible?

<!-- chunk -->

### Many people think they already know the reason for some of these

<!-- chunk -->

### connections, especially those between metabolic and mental disorders.

<!-- chunk -->

### We’ve talked about the stigma around mental disorders, but when it

<!-- chunk -->

### comes to metabolic disorders, people are often quick to judge as well. They

<!-- chunk -->

### think those who are obese, diabetic, or have heart attacks are simply not

<!-- chunk -->

### taking good care of themselves. They eat too much, smoke, and/or don’t

<!-- chunk -->

### exercise enough. By and large, many believe these conditions arise from

<!-- chunk -->

### negligence—that they’re the fault of the diagnosed. Similarly, it seems

<!-- chunk -->

### obvious to many that mental disorders cause people to not take good care of

<!-- chunk -->

### themselves. For instance, depression causes people to lose energy and

<!-- chunk -->

### motivation. When that happens, they sit around all day, watch TV, and eat.

<!-- chunk -->

### They gain weight. They don’t exercise. Everyone knows that “stress”

<!-- chunk -->

### contributes to unhealthy habits. Almost by definition, people with mental

<!-- chunk -->

### disorders have more stress than most, or at least it feels that way to them.

<!-- chunk -->

### So, again, people with these stressful symptoms eat poorly and don’t

<!-- chunk -->

### exercise enough. It’s no wonder that people with mental disorders have

<!-- chunk -->

### higher rates of metabolic disorders. What it really comes down to, in the

<!-- chunk -->

### eyes of many, is simple—these are issues of willpower and discipline.

<!-- chunk -->

### Here’s the conundrum, though. The rates of all these disorders have

<!-- chunk -->

### been skyrocketing over the past fifty years. Obesity, diabetes,

<!-- chunk -->

### cardiovascular disease, and mental disorders. Why is that? Have we had an

<!-- chunk -->

### epidemic of laziness or self-destructive health behaviors in our society? Are

<!-- chunk -->

### people no longer capable of self-discipline? Do they just not care about

<!-- chunk -->

### their health? If you would answer “yes” to these questions, which many

<!-- chunk -->

### would, another question remains: Why? What has caused this “epidemic of

<!-- chunk -->

### laziness”?

<!-- chunk -->

### As we touched on in Chapter One, some might say it’s society. The

<!-- chunk -->

### faster pace of everything and the demands of that pace. The stress of

<!-- chunk -->

### modern life. Constant emails to attend to. Social media posts stacking up

<!-- chunk -->

### and vying for our attention. The pull to pick up your cell phone and always

<!-- chunk -->

### be watching, searching, scrolling, or checking. Others might say it’s the

<!-- chunk -->

### food supply—artificial ingredients and processed foods.

<!-- chunk -->

### As it turns out, these are likely contributing factors, but are they true

<!-- chunk -->

### causes? How do we get from any one of these “causes” to laziness, apathy,

<!-- chunk -->

### and burnout, which then cause people to overeat and not exercise, which

<!-- chunk -->

### then cause them to develop mental or metabolic disorders? How does that

<!-- chunk -->

### all actually work in the body and brain? And why doesn’t everyone subject

<!-- chunk -->

### to these forces end up diabetic and depressed? And where do the

<!-- chunk -->

### connections with neurological disorders, acknowledged as physical brain

<!-- chunk -->

### diseases, fit into all this talk of modern life and poor health habits? While

<!-- chunk -->

### most people think these mental and metabolic relationships are easy to

<!-- chunk -->

### explain, when you get down to the specifics of human physiology, things

<!-- chunk -->

### get significantly murkier.

<!-- chunk -->

### When providers talk to people about changing their health behaviors—

<!-- chunk -->

### eating less or exercising more—they often get similar answers: “It’s too

<!-- chunk -->

### hard,” or, “I don’t have enough energy.” These answers are almost always

<!-- chunk -->

### met with strong disapproval. They are viewed as excuses for laziness, or

<!-- chunk -->

### signs of not taking the issue seriously enough, or of a lack of discipline. But

<!-- chunk -->

### is it possible that instead of being excuses, answers like “It’s too hard” and

<!-- chunk -->

### “I don’t have enough energy” are actually clues giving us important

<!-- chunk -->

### information? Could inertia and lack of motivation be symptoms of a

<!-- chunk -->

### metabolic problem? Is it possible that these people literally don’t have

<!-- chunk -->

### enough energy?

<!-- chunk -->

### As it turns out, it’s not just possible; there’s an abundance of evidence

<!-- chunk -->

### that it’s true. You see, metabolism involves the production of energy inside

<!-- chunk -->

### cells. As you’ll see in the coming chapters, people who have metabolic or

<!-- chunk -->

### mental illnesses have been found to have deficits in energy production

<!-- chunk -->

### inside their cells. These people are telling the truth. They really don’t have

<!-- chunk -->

### enough energy.

<!-- chunk -->

### It’s not a motivational problem. It’s a metabolic one.

<!-- chunk -->

### We have been missing the elephant in the room.

<!-- chunk -->

### Let’s do a quick recap.

<!-- chunk -->

### •I’ve described the current state of affairs in the mental health field

<!-- chunk -->

### and why what we’re doing isn’t working.

<!-- chunk -->

### •I’ve explored evidence about the overlap and commonalities among

<!-- chunk -->

### mental disorders, as well as the limits of our current methods of

<!-- chunk -->

### differentiating between diagnoses. We’ve seen that every

<!-- chunk -->

### mental disorder leads to a much greater probability of developing

<!-- chunk -->

### another mental disorder—any one of them. These bidirectional

<!-- chunk -->

### relationships suggest that one common pathway might be involved in

<!-- chunk -->

### all mental disorders.

<!-- chunk -->

### •I have also explored the evidence of bidirectional relationships

<!-- chunk -->

### between mental disorders and at least three metabolic and at least two

<!-- chunk -->

### neurological disorders: obesity, diabetes, cardiovascular disease,

<!-- chunk -->

### Alzheimer’s disease, and epilepsy. This raises the possibility of one

<!-- chunk -->

### common pathway not just for mental disorders, but for all of these


<!-- chunk -->

### Already, this may seem impossible to reconcile. You might be yelling,

<!-- chunk -->

### “But these are all different diseases!" Schizophrenia is not the same thing as

<!-- chunk -->

### an eating disorder or a mild anxiety disorder. Cardiovascular disease,

<!-- chunk -->

### bipolar disorder, epilepsy, diabetes, and depression are all different. They

<!-- chunk -->

### have different symptoms. They affect different parts of the body. They

<!-- chunk -->

### appear at different ages. Some of them, like a stroke, can kill people

<!-- chunk -->

### quickly. Others, like mild depression that lasts only a few months, might

<!-- chunk -->

### come and go away without any intervention.

<!-- chunk -->

### It’s difficult to imagine that all these disorders share one common

<!-- chunk -->

### pathway. If there is such a common pathway, it will have to be involved in

<!-- chunk -->

### many different aspects of how the body works. It will need to tie together

<!-- chunk -->

### everything that we already know about these different disorders—their risk

<!-- chunk -->

### factors and symptoms, the treatments that work. That’s a huge role for any

<!-- chunk -->

### bodily process or function to fill.

<!-- chunk -->

### As you will see in Part Two, metabolism fills this role.

<!-- chunk -->

### Yes: we have arrived at our common thread, the underpinning factor

<!-- chunk -->

### that lets us answer our tangled questions about causes and treatments,

<!-- chunk -->

### symptoms and overlaps.

<!-- chunk -->

### Mental disorders—all of them—are metabolic disorders of the brain.

# Part II

# Brain Energy


# Mental Disorders Are Metabolic

# Disorders

<!-- chunk -->

### he following 1938 observation from physicists Albert Einstein and

<!-- chunk -->

### Leopold Infeld is critically important:

<!-- chunk -->

### Creating a new theory is not like destroying an old barn and

<!-- chunk -->

### erecting a skyscraper in its place. It is rather like climbing a

<!-- chunk -->

### mountain, gaining new and wider views, discovering unexpected

<!-- chunk -->

### connections between our starting point and its rich environment. But

<!-- chunk -->

### the point from which we started out still exists and can be seen,

<!-- chunk -->

### although it appears smaller and forms a tiny part of our broad view

<!-- chunk -->

### gained by the mastery of the obstacles on our adventurous way up.


<!-- chunk -->

### For any new theory to be taken seriously, it must incorporate what we

<!-- chunk -->

### already know to be true. It can’t just replace it; it must tie together our

<!-- chunk -->

### existing knowledge and experience into a broader understanding—one that

<!-- chunk -->

### will widen our perspective and offer new insights.

<!-- chunk -->

### Mental health professionals have various camps at the base of Einstein and

<!-- chunk -->

### Infeld’s mountain. Some believe mental illnesses are biologically based,

<!-- chunk -->

### that they arise from a chemical imbalance. They prescribe medications and

<!-- chunk -->

### have seen them work. Other professionals are focused on psychological and

<!-- chunk -->

### social issues. They have helped people through psychotherapy and social

<!-- chunk -->

### interventions and have seen these treatments work. They know with

<!-- chunk -->

### certainty that at least some mental disorders involve psychological and

<!-- chunk -->

### social issues; correcting these issues without any pills can solve the

<!-- chunk -->

### problem, at least for some patients. In reality, all these perspectives are

<!-- chunk -->

### correct. That this is so—and how it is so—can be seen clearly from the

<!-- chunk -->

### vantage point of our new theory: the theory of brain energy. This theory is

<!-- chunk -->

### based on one overarching concept—mental disorders are metabolic

<!-- chunk -->

### disorders of the brain.

<!-- chunk -->

### In the medical world, new theories allow us to better understand the

<!-- chunk -->

### connections between treatments and disorders that we currently can’t

<!-- chunk -->

### explain. They help us better predict future research findings. And they help

<!-- chunk -->

### us develop more effective treatments for the future. The theory of brain

<!-- chunk -->

### energy will do all of this for mental disorders. But its impact is bigger than

<!-- chunk -->

### just mental health. This theory ties together medical disciplines that most

<!-- chunk -->

### people think are unrelated—psychiatry, neurology, cardiology, and

<!-- chunk -->

### endocrinology. Others, too. All these disciplines have their own camps at

<!-- chunk -->

### the base of the mountain as well. Sometimes they work with each other and

<!-- chunk -->

### the practitioners see the connections between disciplines, but far too often,

<!-- chunk -->

### they don’t. A patient might visit a cardiologist who prescribes their heart

<!-- chunk -->

### medicine, an endocrinologist who manages their diabetes prescriptions, and

<!-- chunk -->

### a psychiatrist who prescribes medication for bipolar disorder, with these

<!-- chunk -->

### specialists never communicating with each other. I’m hopeful that the brain

<!-- chunk -->

### energy theory will change this, leading to better cross-specialty

<!-- chunk -->

### collaboration and more effective and comprehensive care. Given what

<!-- chunk -->

### we’ve already seen about the connections between these disorders, this kind

<!-- chunk -->

### of communication and collaboration seems only logical. It may soon be

<!-- chunk -->

### possible to treat or prevent all of these conditions with one integrated

<!-- chunk -->

### treatment plan.

<!-- chunk -->

### In order to prove, or at least strongly support, the theory of brain energy, the

<!-- chunk -->

### upcoming chapters will show that:

<!-- chunk -->

### •Metabolic abnormalities have consistently been found in people with

<!-- chunk -->

### mental disorders, even those who don’t yet have the already-

<!-- chunk -->

### recognized metabolic disorders of obesity, diabetes, or cardiovascular

<!-- chunk -->

### disease.

<!-- chunk -->

### •Essentially all the risk factors for mental and metabolic disorders are

<!-- chunk -->

### the same. The list includes biological, psychological, and social

<!-- chunk -->

### factors, ranging from things like diet and exercise, smoking, drug and

<!-- chunk -->

### alcohol use, and sleep . . . to hormones, inflammation, genetics,

<!-- chunk -->

### epigenetics, and the gut microbiome. The list also extends to

<!-- chunk -->

### relationships, love, having meaning and purpose in life, and stress

<!-- chunk -->

### levels. You can isolate any one of these factors and see that it’s been

<!-- chunk -->

### found to increase risk for both metabolic and mental disorders.

<!-- chunk -->

### •Every one of these risk factors can be tied directly to metabolism.

<!-- chunk -->

### •All the symptoms of mental disorders can be tied directly to

<!-- chunk -->

### metabolism, or more specifically, mitochondria, which are the master

<!-- chunk -->

### regulators of metabolism.

<!-- chunk -->

### •All current treatments in the mental health field, including

<!-- chunk -->

### biological, psychological, and social interventions, likely work by

<!-- chunk -->

### affecting metabolism.

<!-- chunk -->

### As we explore these lines of evidence, not only will it become clear that

<!-- chunk -->

### mental disorders are indeed metabolic disorders of the brain, it will become

<!-- chunk -->

### clear why this is important and what it means for treatment.

# The Metabolic Ripple Effect

<!-- chunk -->

### To suggest that so many different disorders stem from metabolic problems

<!-- chunk -->

### may sound far-fetched. Interestingly enough, while the medical field now

<!-- chunk -->

### groups obesity, diabetes, and cardiovascular disease together as metabolic

<!-- chunk -->

### disorders, that was not always the case. After all, they have very different

<!-- chunk -->

### symptoms, and they require different medications and different treatments.

<!-- chunk -->

### There are still different specialties that focus on these different disorders—

<!-- chunk -->

### obesity medicine (obesity), endocrinology (diabetes), cardiology (heart

<!-- chunk -->

### attacks), and neurology (strokes). However, they all affect the entire body,

<!-- chunk -->

### and people who have one such disorder are at higher risk of having another

<!-- chunk -->

### one. Not everyone who is obese has a heart attack or diabetes. Not all

<!-- chunk -->

### diabetics are obese. Not all people who have a stroke have diabetes. But

<!-- chunk -->

### while different people have different signs and symptoms, they are all

<!-- chunk -->

### interconnected.

<!-- chunk -->

### The effects of metabolic disorders on the body aren’t limited to an

<!-- chunk -->

### increased risk of the other metabolic disorders like obesity, diabetes, heart

<!-- chunk -->

### attacks, and strokes. As we’ve already discussed, these people have

<!-- chunk -->

### increased rates of Alzheimer’s disease, epilepsy, and mental problems, too.

<!-- chunk -->

### But people with metabolic disorders are also more likely to develop

<!-- chunk -->

### innumerable other illnesses not usually viewed as metabolic. These include

<!-- chunk -->

### liver problems, kidney problems, nerve problems, brain problems,

<!-- chunk -->

### hormonal problems, joint problems, gastrointestinal problems, autoimmune

<!-- chunk -->

### problems, and even cancer.

<!-- chunk -->

### Most people think that metabolic disorders are simple problems with

<!-- chunk -->

### simple solutions. They think they know the “root causes” of these disorders

<!-- chunk -->

### —behaviors like eating too much, not exercising enough, and/or smoking

<!-- chunk -->

### cigarettes. As long as people don’t overeat, or under-exercise, or smoke,

<!-- chunk -->

### they will be perfectly fine, metabolically speaking. See? Simple.

<!-- chunk -->

### But when it comes to metabolism, nothing is simple.

<!-- chunk -->

### Let’s look at an example. Mark was a seemingly healthy, thin, and fit

<!-- chunk -->

### forty-five-year-old man who developed multiple sclerosis (MS), an

<!-- chunk -->

### autoimmune disorder. To treat his MS, he was prescribed a medication

<!-- chunk -->

### called prednisone, a corticosteroid. Within weeks, he was bloated and

<!-- chunk -->

### gaining

<!-- chunk -->

### weight. Within a month, he developed pre-diabetes and was prescribed a

<!-- chunk -->

### diabetes medication. Unfortunately, weight gain and high blood sugar are

<!-- chunk -->

### both known side effects of prednisone.

<!-- chunk -->

### Over the next six months, Mark gained forty pounds. Not all of this

<!-- chunk -->

### weight came out of nowhere; his behavior—specifically, his diet and

<!-- chunk -->

### exercise routine—changed dramatically. Up until his diagnosis, he had

<!-- chunk -->

### always eaten well and exercised vigorously several times a week. But

<!-- chunk -->

### corticosteroids like prednisone are known to increase appetite, and Mark

<!-- chunk -->

### had started craving and eating a lot of junk food, something he’d never

<!-- chunk -->

### done before. He tried to keep up with his exercise routine, but as he gained

<!-- chunk -->

### weight, it became harder and harder. He still managed to exercise some, but

<!-- chunk -->

### it was nothing like before. Mark’s risk markers for cardiovascular disease

<!-- chunk -->

### worsened, including an increase in his blood pressure and lipids. He was

<!-- chunk -->

### now well on his way to a heart attack or stroke. Oh . . . and he also

<!-- chunk -->

### developed anxiety and mild depression. But who wouldn’t be depressed and

<!-- chunk -->

### anxious in his situation? His doctor told him to try yoga and go on a diet.

<!-- chunk -->

### Unfortunately, that advice wasn’t all that helpful.

<!-- chunk -->

### What is the root cause of Mark’s metabolic disorders? Within six

<!-- chunk -->

### months of starting the corticosteroid, he developed diabetes and obesity.

<!-- chunk -->

### The evidence is overwhelmingly clear that the culprit was the medication,

<!-- chunk -->

### not Mark’s willpower or discipline. His cravings and lack of energy are

<!-- chunk -->

### symptoms of metabolic dysfunction. His depression and anxiety are also

<!-- chunk -->

### known side effects of this medication. In some ways, he’s lucky that he

<!-- chunk -->

### didn’t get manic or psychotic, which are also possible side effects.

<!-- chunk -->

### Reactions like Mark’s happen all the time with medications like

<!-- chunk -->

### prednisone. Other medications can cause these kinds of metabolic

<!-- chunk -->

### problems, too, including many psychiatric medications. But the takeaway

<!-- chunk -->

### here isn’t that one should never take these medications: autoimmune

<!-- chunk -->

### disorders like Mark’s can cause permanent organ damage, and the side

<!-- chunk -->

### effects of treatment are often determined to be a price worth paying when

<!-- chunk -->

### weighed against the severity of a disease. The point is that metabolic

<!-- chunk -->

### problems are not simple, nor are they avoidable through sheer willpower.

<!-- chunk -->

### Medication is only one of many, many possible causes. For example, a

<!-- chunk -->

### person who has experienced

<!-- chunk -->

### horrible childhood abuse is likely to have altered levels of cortisol, the

<!-- chunk -->

### body’s equivalent hormone to prednisone. Perhaps unsurprisingly, people

<!-- chunk -->

### with a trauma history are more likely to develop metabolic disorders . . .

<!-- chunk -->

### and mental disorders, too. And once people develop a metabolic problem,

<!-- chunk -->

### symptoms and lifestyle changes like Mark’s aren’t unusual at all.

# What Is Metabolism?

<!-- chunk -->

### When most people hear the word “metabolism,” they picture our bodies

<!-- chunk -->

### burning fat and calories. The common wisdom is that people with a “high

<!-- chunk -->

### metabolism” are skinny and have trouble gaining weight, while people with

<!-- chunk -->

### a “low metabolism” are overweight and gain weight easily—even if they

<!-- chunk -->

### don’t eat that much. That’s where metabolism begins and ends for most

<!-- chunk -->

### people.

<!-- chunk -->

### Metabolism is about so much more than burning calories, though that’s

<!-- chunk -->

### part of it. It influences every aspect of the way our bodies function.

<!-- chunk -->

### To make energy, our bodies need food, water, vitamins and minerals,

<!-- chunk -->

### and oxygen—we breathe in oxygen and breathe out carbon dioxide, a waste

<!-- chunk -->

### product of metabolism. When we eat food, it gets broken down into

<!-- chunk -->

### carbohydrates, fats, and amino acids, along with the vitamins and minerals

<!-- chunk -->

### that are hopefully also there. All of this is absorbed into our bloodstream

<!-- chunk -->

### and shuttled around the body. Once nutrients arrive at cells and enter them,

<!-- chunk -->

### they are used as building blocks for things like proteins or membranes.

<!-- chunk -->

### Some might be stored as fat for a rainy day. But most of these nutrients will

<!-- chunk -->

### be converted into adenosine triphosphate (ATP), which is the primary

<!-- chunk -->

### energy molecule of the cell. ATP makes the machinery of the cell work.

<!-- chunk -->

### That’s the basic, high school biology version of metabolism. A one-

<!-- chunk -->

### sentence definition might be the following: Metabolism is the process of

<!-- chunk -->

### turning food into energy or building blocks for growing and maintaining

<!-- chunk -->

### cells, as well as the appropriate and efficient management of waste

<!-- chunk -->

### products. Metabolism is how our cells work. Our metabolism determines

<!-- chunk -->

### our cellular health, how our bodies and brains develop and function, and

<!-- chunk -->

### how we allocate resources

<!-- chunk -->

### to different cells at different times to optimize our survival. Metabolism

<!-- chunk -->

### allows some cells to grow and thrive and lets others shrivel up and die in a

<!-- chunk -->

### complex cost-benefit analysis that prioritizes healthy and advantageous

<!-- chunk -->

### cells over those that might be old, weak, or simply more expendable. As the

<!-- chunk -->

### body’s resource management system, metabolism is all about adaptation.

<!-- chunk -->

### Our environment is constantly changing, and so are our circumstances in

<!-- chunk -->

### the environment. As a result, our metabolism is constantly changing to keep

<!-- chunk -->

### up with the shifts around us. These adaptations in metabolism allow us to

<!-- chunk -->

### thrive in optimal environments or to simply survive in situations that are

<!-- chunk -->

### stressful to the body, like food scarcity. But the availability of food isn’t the

<!-- chunk -->

### only change metabolism responds to—numerous other factors play a role,

<!-- chunk -->

### like psychological stress, light exposure, temperature, how much sleep we

<!-- chunk -->

### get, hormone levels, and the amount of oxygen available to cells. At the end

<!-- chunk -->

### of the day, metabolism is the body’s battle to stay alive. Many biological

<!-- chunk -->

### authorities would say that metabolism defines life itself.

# Energy Imbalances

<!-- chunk -->

### Metabolism is how our body creates and uses energy. And we can think of

<!-- chunk -->

### problems with metabolism as energy imbalances.

<!-- chunk -->

### Problems with metabolism lead to problems in the way cells function.

<!-- chunk -->

### This goes for all cells in the human body. For instance, when heart cells are

<!-- chunk -->

### metabolically compromised, they don’t work as well to pump blood. Brain

<!-- chunk -->

### cells need precise control. They need to be turned on at appropriate times

<!-- chunk -->

### and then turned off at appropriate times. When brain cells are metabolically

<!-- chunk -->

### compromised, these on/off processes can be disrupted. Precision is

<!-- chunk -->

### everything when it comes to brain function, and as we will see, this

<!-- chunk -->

### disruption can result in what we know as symptoms of mental illness.

<!-- chunk -->

### The brain is the most complicated organ in the human body. In fact, the

<!-- chunk -->

### adult human brain is estimated to have about one hundred billion neurons.

<!-- chunk -->

### On top of that, there are an additional ten to fifty glial cells per neuron. The

<!-- chunk -->

### neurons are “nerve cells,” and the glial cells are often thought of as support

<!-- chunk -->

### cells to the neurons. Combined, there are about one trillion to five trillion

<!-- chunk -->

### cells in the human brain. One group of researchers challenged this estimate,

<!-- chunk -->

### suggesting it’s closer to eighty-six billion neurons and eighty-four billion

<!-- chunk -->

### glial cells, or 170 billion cells total.


<!-- chunk -->

### Regardless, it’s a lot of cells!

<!-- chunk -->

### What coordinates the function of all these cells? Many people would

<!-- chunk -->

### say neurotransmitters, the cell’s messenger chemicals. We can think of

<!-- chunk -->

### neurotransmitters as either “go” signals or “stop” signals, often categorized

<!-- chunk -->

### as excitatory (go) or inhibitory (stop). There are other variations, but these

<!-- chunk -->

### distinctions will suffice for now. They have been the primary focus of

<!-- chunk -->

### neuroscientists and biological psychiatrists for decades. But what controls

<!-- chunk -->

### neurotransmitters? How do cells know when to release them? Many would

<!-- chunk -->

### say their release is triggered by neurotransmitters from other cells. I’m sure

<!-- chunk -->

### you see the problems with this answer. It is partially correct. However, as I

<!-- chunk -->

### will discuss throughout the remainder of this book, there are numerous

<!-- chunk -->

### other factors that dictate the action of brain cells.

<!-- chunk -->

### We’ve established that cells need energy to work. This energy is used

<!-- chunk -->

### for all sorts of different things throughout the body, including making

<!-- chunk -->

### muscles function, creating and regulating hormones, and making and

<!-- chunk -->

### releasing neurotransmitters. The parts of the body that need the most energy

<!-- chunk -->

### tend to be the parts of the body most affected by metabolic problems. As

<!-- chunk -->

### you might imagine, topping the list are the brain and the heart.

<!-- chunk -->

### Although the brain makes up about 2 percent of the body mass, it uses

<!-- chunk -->

### about 20 percent of the body’s total energy at rest. Brain cells are

<!-- chunk -->

### exquisitely sensitive to disruptions in energy supply, and when there’s a

<!-- chunk -->

### metabolic problem somewhere in the body, the brain usually knows. Given

<!-- chunk -->

### that our brains are the control centers of our bodies, they ultimately control

<!-- chunk -->

### our perceptions of reality. When there is a metabolic problem somewhere in

<!-- chunk -->

### the body, we might perceive pain, experience shortness of breath, or feel

<!-- chunk -->

### fatigued or lightheaded. If there is a metabolic problem in the brain itself,

<!-- chunk -->

### the signs and symptoms can take just about any form. Sometimes they are

<!-- chunk -->

### obvious, such as confusion, hallucinations, or a complete loss of

<!-- chunk -->

### consciousness. Other times, they are more subtle, like fatigue, trouble

<!-- chunk -->

### concentrating, or mild depression.

<!-- chunk -->

### Sometimes metabolic problems are acute, meaning that they are abrupt and

<!-- chunk -->

### dramatic. These can take the form of a heart attack, a stroke, or even death.

<!-- chunk -->

### A heart attack, for example, is usually due to a blood clot in one of the

<!-- chunk -->

### arteries feeding the heart. Some of the heart cells stop getting enough blood

<!-- chunk -->

### and oxygen. This prevents them from producing enough energy. If blood

<!-- chunk -->

### flow isn’t restored quickly, the heart cells die. This is a metabolic crisis in

<!-- chunk -->

### the heart. A stroke is an acute metabolic crisis in the brain. The ultimate

<!-- chunk -->

### metabolic crisis is death itself, where the cells in the entire body stop

<!-- chunk -->

### producing energy. Many paths can lead to this whole-body energy failure—

<!-- chunk -->

### heart attacks, strokes, poisonings, severe accidents, cancer. They all result

<!-- chunk -->

### in the cells of the body no longer producing enough energy, and it is the

<!-- chunk -->

### lack of energy production that results in death.

<!-- chunk -->

### Heart attacks, strokes, and death are all examples of absolute and acute

<!-- chunk -->

### energy problems that result in cell death. However, there are less dramatic

<!-- chunk -->

### situations in which the energy supply to cells is compromised: Instead of a

<!-- chunk -->

### complete shutdown of energy production, the cells simply aren’t getting

<!-- chunk -->

### enough energy. Instead of cell death, the cells don’t work quite right. Some

<!-- chunk -->

### of these metabolic problems can last for just a few minutes, while others

<!-- chunk -->

### might last for hours. Hypoglycemia, or low blood sugar, is a good example.

<!-- chunk -->

### It most commonly occurs when people haven’t eaten in a while. In mild

<!-- chunk -->

### cases, it results in feeling hungry, irritable, fatigued, or having trouble

<!-- chunk -->

### concentrating. In moderate cases, it might result in a headache or feeling

<!-- chunk -->

### depressed. In severe cases, it can result in hallucinations, seizures, or coma.

<!-- chunk -->

### If it progresses further, it can result in absolute metabolic failure—death.

<!-- chunk -->

### Before things get so severe, however, most people implement the obvious

<!-- chunk -->

### solution—they eat something. This raises blood sugar, and things begin to

<!-- chunk -->

### function normally again. Even if they don’t eat anything, the body has

<!-- chunk -->

### systems in place that usually prevent severe hypoglycemia. However, for

<!-- chunk -->

### diabetics who inject insulin or take medication to more forcibly lower blood

<!-- chunk -->

### sugar, these severe consequences are a real possibility. You might notice

<!-- chunk -->

### that brain symptoms dominate the above list of effects, even though the

<!-- chunk -->

### hypoglycemia is occurring throughout the entire body.

<!-- chunk -->

### Other metabolic problems are not acute but instead chronic disorders with

<!-- chunk -->

### long-lasting symptoms—like diabetes, for example. Many people think of

<!-- chunk -->

### diabetes as high blood sugar. However, a paradoxical and interesting way to

<!-- chunk -->

### think about diabetes is as an energy shortage, or a deficit in energy

<!-- chunk -->

### production. Glucose is the primary fuel source for cells. In diabetes, cells

<!-- chunk -->

### have trouble converting glucose into energy. The levels of glucose in the

<!-- chunk -->

### blood can be high, sometimes very high, yet that glucose has trouble getting

<!-- chunk -->

### into cells where it can be used. Getting glucose from the bloodstream into

<!-- chunk -->

### cells requires insulin, a hormone produced by the pancreas. Diabetics have

<!-- chunk -->

### either a shortage of insulin or insulin resistance—a condition in which the

<!-- chunk -->

### body is not as responsive to insulin. When cells don’t have enough glucose,

<!-- chunk -->

### they aren’t able to produce enough energy. When they don’t have enough

<!-- chunk -->

### energy, they don’t work right.

<!-- chunk -->

### Since glucose is the primary fuel source for most cells in the body,

<!-- chunk -->

### diabetes can affect many different parts of the body. But not everyone has

<!-- chunk -->

### the same problems. The symptoms of diabetes can be wide-ranging and can

<!-- chunk -->

### change over time. In the beginning, symptoms are usually mild. They can

<!-- chunk -->

### include things like urinating too much or losing weight unexpectedly. They

<!-- chunk -->

### can also include mental symptoms such as fatigue or trouble concentrating.

<!-- chunk -->

### As the illness progresses, different organs can be affected. Some people

<!-- chunk -->

### develop problems with their eyes, nerves, or brains. Some people have heart

<!-- chunk -->

### attacks or strokes. Others experience kidney failure or contract serious

<!-- chunk -->

### infections that are difficult to treat.

<!-- chunk -->

### Why are the effects on people so different? Why don’t all diabetics end

<!-- chunk -->

### up with the same symptoms and the same body parts failing? The answer is

<!-- chunk -->

### complicated—and often related to metabolism.

<!-- chunk -->

### Metabolism is affected by numerous factors. It is always changing. And

<!-- chunk -->

### it is different in different cells of the body at different times. Some cells can

<!-- chunk -->

### be functioning normally while others are dying. Some cells may gradually

<!-- chunk -->

### malfunction as the result of chronic energy deprivation. Metabolism is not

<!-- chunk -->

### all-or-nothing. It is controlled at a variety of levels. Some of the factors that

<!-- chunk -->

### affect metabolism do so broadly, while others are specific to distinct parts

<!-- chunk -->

### of

<!-- chunk -->

### the body. Some are specific to specific organs. Some are specific to specific

<!-- chunk -->

### cells.

# Metabolism Is Like the Flow of Traffic

<!-- chunk -->

### Think of it like this—the body is like a large city with lots of roads and

<!-- chunk -->

### highways. There is a lot of traffic. Each car is like a human cell. During

<!-- chunk -->

### rush hour, it can be hectic. If you’re in a car, it can feel chaotic. So many

<!-- chunk -->

### things to pay attention to: stoplights, cars changing lanes, someone on their

<!-- chunk -->

### cell phone swerving into your lane. If you look at the traffic from above,

<!-- chunk -->

### however—say, from the top of a skyscraper—it looks pretty orderly. The

<!-- chunk -->

### roads are organized. The cars and trucks are moving along. Some cars stop

<!-- chunk -->

### while other cars go. They wait their turn and then start going again. Cars go

<!-- chunk -->

### slow on certain roads but then speed up on highways. Some cars change

<!-- chunk -->

### lanes, and the cars around them have to slow down to let them in. Others

<!-- chunk -->

### might be having problems and be stuck on the side of the road. There might

<!-- chunk -->

### be some traffic accidents, causing other cars to take a detour. If you were to

<!-- chunk -->

### try to take in the specifics of each and every car at the same time, it would

<!-- chunk -->

### be overwhelming—there are too many cars, too many stoplights, too many

<!-- chunk -->

### other factors to keep in mind. But when you look at the big picture, traffic is

<!-- chunk -->

### moving along. The city is working. People are getting to where they need to

<!-- chunk -->

### go. The city is alive. It has energy; you can see it flow. This is the way to

<!-- chunk -->

### think about metabolism in the human body.

<!-- chunk -->

### Back to the question I posed earlier—why do some people with diabetes

<!-- chunk -->

### have different symptoms? More important to the theory of brain energy, if

<!-- chunk -->

### all mental disorders are metabolic disorders, why doesn’t everyone with a

<!-- chunk -->

### mental disorder have the same symptoms?

<!-- chunk -->

### Illnesses and symptoms are like traffic jams. Either traffic isn’t flowing

<!-- chunk -->

### optimally, or it stops altogether. One highway might represent the pancreas.

<!-- chunk -->

### An access road might represent a specific brain region that controls

<!-- chunk -->

### attention and focus.

<!-- chunk -->

### What causes a traffic jam on a given road or highway? Myriad things. Car

<!-- chunk -->

### accidents, road construction, potholes, or traffic signals failing to work. The

<!-- chunk -->

### design and maintenance of the roads play a role, and the cars and drivers

<!-- chunk -->

### do, too. Some parts of the city have more frequent traffic problems. This

<!-- chunk -->

### can be due to poor design, poor maintenance, or more aggressive or careless

<!-- chunk -->

### drivers on those roads. The areas of the city with regular traffic problems

<!-- chunk -->

### represent “symptoms” or “illnesses”—places where the traffic isn’t

<!-- chunk -->

### “working” properly.

<!-- chunk -->

### When it comes to human illnesses and symptoms, we are talking about

<!-- chunk -->

### parts of the body or brain that aren’t working properly. This is usually the

<!-- chunk -->

### result of an issue in one of three areas: the development, function, or

<!-- chunk -->

### maintenance of human cells. Cells must develop properly in order to meet

<!-- chunk -->

### the needs of the body. Function is making sure all the parts are doing what

<!-- chunk -->

### they are supposed to be doing, in the right ways at the right times.

<!-- chunk -->

### Maintenance is keeping everything in good shape. This is analogous to

<!-- chunk -->

### traffic needing adequate design and construction of roads and bridges

<!-- chunk -->

### (development); all the cars, drivers, and traffic lights working properly

<!-- chunk -->

### (function); and the whole system being regularly serviced—cars tuned up,

<!-- chunk -->

### roads patched, stoplights tested, etc. (maintenance).

<!-- chunk -->

### In humans, these three things—development, function, and maintenance

<!-- chunk -->

### of cells—ultimately depend upon one thing: metabolism. If there are

<!-- chunk -->

### problems with metabolism, there will be problems in one or more of these

<!-- chunk -->

### areas. If the problems are significant enough, there will be “symptoms.”

<!-- chunk -->

### So, what affects metabolism? Just like traffic in the city, many things!

<!-- chunk -->

### Diet, light, sleep, exercise, drugs and alcohol, genes, hormones, stress,

<!-- chunk -->

### neurotransmitters, and inflammation, to name a few. However, each of these

<!-- chunk -->

### things affects different cells in different ways. Depending upon the mix of

<!-- chunk -->

### factors that someone is exposed to, different cells and organs will be

<!-- chunk -->

### affected, resulting in different symptoms and different illnesses. Just like

<!-- chunk -->

### some roads are more susceptible to traffic jams, some cells are more

<!-- chunk -->

### susceptible to metabolic failure. Sometimes, parts of the body will function

<!-- chunk -->

### normally at times of low demand but begin to malfunction at times of

<!-- chunk -->

### increased demand—just like a rush-hour meltdown on a city highway that’s

<!-- chunk -->

### overwhelmed by commuters.

<!-- chunk -->

### We’ve established that metabolism defines life itself; that it determines how

<!-- chunk -->

### cells function, that it affects and is affected by innumerable factors. In a

<!-- chunk -->

### way, of course mental disorders are related to metabolism. In essence,

<!-- chunk -->

### everything is! So what?

<!-- chunk -->

### What I’ll show in the coming chapters is that metabolism is, in fact, the

<!-- chunk -->

### only way to connect the dots of mental illness. It is the lowest common

<!-- chunk -->

### denominator for all mental disorders, all of the risk factors for mental

<!-- chunk -->

### disorders, and even all of the treatments that are currently used. And,

<!-- chunk -->

### perhaps most significantly, although metabolism is complex, solving

<!-- chunk -->

### metabolic problems is usually possible, oftentimes through straightforward

<!-- chunk -->

### interventions.

<!-- chunk -->

### Before I dive into the evidence for all of this, however, I first need to

<!-- chunk -->

### clarify what a mental disorder is in the first place. This question has long

<!-- chunk -->

### plagued the mental health field, and it centers on one issue in particular—

<!-- chunk -->

### the difference between normal mental states (especially stressful and

<!-- chunk -->

### adverse ones) and a mental disorder.

# A


# Mental States and Mental Disorders

<!-- chunk -->

### s I discussed in Part One, one of the dilemmas in the mental health

<!-- chunk -->

### field is distinguishing between normal human emotions and mental

<!-- chunk -->

### disorders, especially since the symptoms can be the same. We all get

<!-- chunk -->

### anxious or mildly depressed from time to time. If we experience a

<!-- chunk -->

### devastating loss, such as the unexpected death of a spouse, we may get

<!-- chunk -->

### severely depressed for a period. These are all normal reactions. They are

<!-- chunk -->

### hardwired into our brains.

<!-- chunk -->

### However, when people are exposed to numerous stressors all at once, or

<!-- chunk -->

### when the stressors are extreme or overwhelming (such as being violently

<!-- chunk -->

### assaulted), these normal and understandable initial reactions can quickly

<!-- chunk -->

### lead to what we call “mental illness.” The diagnoses are all over the map.

<!-- chunk -->

### Trauma or extreme stress can lead to anxiety disorders, depression, PTSD,

<!-- chunk -->

### eating disorders, substance use disorders, personality disorders, and even

<!-- chunk -->

### psychosis. How do stress and trauma lead to all these different disorders?

<!-- chunk -->

### And where is the line between a normal reaction to adversity and a

<!-- chunk -->

### disorder?

<!-- chunk -->

### Two issues have made these questions particularly difficult to answer: (1)

<!-- chunk -->

### the symptoms are the same, and (2) both mental states and mental disorders

<!-- chunk -->

### can lead to poor health outcomes. Nonetheless, distinguishing between

<!-- chunk -->

### normal mental states and mental disorders is critically important. Mental

<!-- chunk -->

### states are adaptive reactions to adversity. Mental disorders represent the

<!-- chunk -->

### brain malfunctioning. These distinctions have direct implications for

<!-- chunk -->

### treatment. Helping people cope with adversity is very different than treating

<!-- chunk -->

### a brain that is malfunctioning.

# Understanding “Normal”: Stress and the Stress

# Response

<!-- chunk -->

### Stressors are the psychological and social factors in the biopsychosocial

<!-- chunk -->

### model—the ones that people usually think of as the “mental” causes of

<!-- chunk -->

### mental illness.

<!-- chunk -->

### Many clinicians and researchers still see biological factors as separate

<!-- chunk -->

### from psychological and social ones. For example, they might believe that

<!-- chunk -->

### hallucinations are due to a biological chemical imbalance, but that someone

<!-- chunk -->

### with schizophrenia can also suffer from low self-esteem, which is a

<!-- chunk -->

### psychological problem. They may try to address both, but they often see

<!-- chunk -->

### these issues as unrelated and separate. One requires medication, and the

<!-- chunk -->

### other requires talk therapy. I disagree with this dichotomous view. I think

<!-- chunk -->

### biological, psychological, and social factors are all interconnected and

<!-- chunk -->

### inseparable. Biology influences our psychology and how we get along with

<!-- chunk -->

### others. But our psychology and our interactions with other people influence

<!-- chunk -->

### our biology. These connections can impact all mental and metabolic

<!-- chunk -->

### symptoms. To begin unpacking this, let me start by making some

<!-- chunk -->

### overarching observations about our species.

<!-- chunk -->

### Humans are meant to live in groups. We seek out and attach to other

<!-- chunk -->

### people—parents, lovers, children, friends, teachers, and community

<!-- chunk -->

### members. These connections form a network of safety and support in our

<!-- chunk -->

### lives. We are biologically driven to want, and even need, these people.

<!-- chunk -->

### There is a

<!-- chunk -->

### conundrum, though: While we must live with other people, other people are

<!-- chunk -->

### actually the primary sources of psychological and social stress. Most of

<!-- chunk -->

### these stressors revolve around relationships, roles, resources, and

<!-- chunk -->

### responsibilities. People can be stressed over expectations on them, financial

<!-- chunk -->

### problems, performance problems, relationship problems, or status in

<!-- chunk -->

### society. Some people experience chronic stressors due to socioeconomic

<!-- chunk -->

### status, abuse, neglect, race, ethnicity, religious beliefs, physical abilities,

<!-- chunk -->

### cognitive abilities, gender identity, sexual orientation, age, and so many

<!-- chunk -->

### other factors. People can be harmed or threatened by other people. We

<!-- chunk -->

### sometimes make each other feel unsafe. We sometimes make other humans

<!-- chunk -->

### feel like they aren’t good enough. There are countless reasons that humans

<!-- chunk -->

### stress other humans. And interestingly, the absence of other humans, or

<!-- chunk -->

### loneliness, is also a powerful stressor unto itself.

<!-- chunk -->

### All of these stressors lead to the stress response, a complex array of

<!-- chunk -->

### biological changes in the brain and body. The stress response includes

<!-- chunk -->

### changes in four domains:

<!-- chunk -->

### 1.The hypothalamic-pituitary-adrenal (HPA) axis, which results in

<!-- chunk -->

### cortisol flowing through the bloodstream;

<!-- chunk -->

### 2.The sympathetic-adrenal-medullary (SAM) axis, which results in

<!-- chunk -->

### adrenaline (epinephrine and norepinephrine) flowing through the

<!-- chunk -->

### bloodstream;

<!-- chunk -->

### 3.Inflammation;

<!-- chunk -->

### 4.Changes in gene expression, especially in the hippocampus.


<!-- chunk -->

### All of these changes, in turn, affect metabolism. They comprise a

<!-- chunk -->

### person’s response to adversity. They are not disorders. They set the stage

<!-- chunk -->

### for “fight or flight.” However, in most day-to-day stressful situations, we

<!-- chunk -->

### don’t fight or flee. Instead, we just stay put—but we get angry, or anxious,

<!-- chunk -->

### or irritable, or overwhelmed, or confused, or terrified, or hurt, or sad. Yet

<!-- chunk -->

### these core changes are still occurring in our bodies and brains.

<!-- chunk -->

### Different stressful situations result in different behaviors and emotions.

<!-- chunk -->

### Some stressors make you want to yell at someone, such as the driver who

<!-- chunk -->

### cuts you off in traffic and then flips you off for no reason. Other stressors

<!-- chunk -->

### might make you ruminate and not sleep well, such as feeling unprepared for

<!-- chunk -->

### an important exam the next day. Others might make you want to curl up

<!-- chunk -->

### into a ball and cry, such as getting dumped by the love of your life. All of

<!-- chunk -->

### these situations involve the stress response. While similar mechanisms are

<!-- chunk -->

### involved, clear differences trigger different brain regions to create different

<!-- chunk -->

### responses.

<!-- chunk -->

### Although these are normal, the stress response takes a toll—a metabolic

<!-- chunk -->

### toll. The body uses energy to produce these changes, meaning less energy is

<!-- chunk -->

### available for other functions. Many of these responses create a state of high

<!-- chunk -->

### alert. In some situations, the person feels threatened and prepares to fight or

<!-- chunk -->

### argue with someone. In other situations, the person may feel wounded,

<!-- chunk -->

### vulnerable, or powerless and try to hide from the world. In either case,

<!-- chunk -->

### metabolic resources are being mobilized. The heart is pumping faster.

<!-- chunk -->

### Blood pressure is increasing. Blood glucose is rising. Hormones are

<!-- chunk -->

### flowing. Inflammatory cytokines are being released. The body is mounting

<!-- chunk -->

### resources and energy for its own defense.

<!-- chunk -->

### When the stress is mild, people who are resilient and metabolically

<!-- chunk -->

### healthy deal with it. It can be over in a matter of seconds or minutes.

<!-- chunk -->

### However, if a body is metabolically compromised, or if the stress is

<!-- chunk -->

### extreme, people can be pushed over the edge, and a new mental or

<!-- chunk -->

### metabolic disorder can quickly emerge. For those with preexisting

<!-- chunk -->

### disorders, symptoms can become even worse. That’s right: Stress can

<!-- chunk -->

### exacerbate every known mental and metabolic disorder. People with

<!-- chunk -->

### depression may get more depressed. People with alcoholism may fall off the

<!-- chunk -->

### wagon. People with schizophrenia may hallucinate. People with

<!-- chunk -->

### Alzheimer’s disease may get agitated and combative. People with epilepsy

<!-- chunk -->

### may experience a seizure. People with diabetes may have their blood sugar

<!-- chunk -->

### skyrocket. And people with cardiovascular disease may have chest pain or a

<!-- chunk -->

### heart attack. Some people die—from stress alone. This is all well

<!-- chunk -->

### established.

<!-- chunk -->

### A separate medical field has tried to make sense of all of this—the field

<!-- chunk -->

### of psychosomatic medicine or mind-body medicine. Many healthcare

<!-- chunk -->

### professionals have observed the relationship between psychological and

<!-- chunk -->

### social factors affecting the health of the body. Practitioners in this field

<!-- chunk -->

### understand that all these risk factors play a role in human physiology. These

<!-- chunk -->

### factors are often referred to as the social determinants of health. Many

<!-- chunk -->

### social factors, such as poverty, abuse, or living in a high-crime

<!-- chunk -->

### neighborhood, can have huge consequences on health and longevity.

<!-- chunk -->

### Some of the most compelling data on this comes from the adverse

<!-- chunk -->

### childhood experiences (ACEs) studies that started between 1995 and

<!-- chunk -->

### and looked at the number of adverse experiences that children and

<!-- chunk -->

### adolescents encounter and their effects on long-term health outcomes, both

<!-- chunk -->

### physical and mental. These ongoing studies have looked at stressors early in

<!-- chunk -->

### life, such as physical and sexual abuse, neglect, household substance abuse,

<!-- chunk -->

### household mental illness, exposure to domestic violence, and parental

<!-- chunk -->

### divorce, and then determined if these early experiences were associated

<!-- chunk -->

### with later health outcomes. A 2017 meta-analysis of thirty-seven such

<!-- chunk -->

### studies looking at twenty-three health outcomes in more than 250,000

<!-- chunk -->

### people found that they are.


<!-- chunk -->

### The more ACEs a child has, the more likely he

<!-- chunk -->

### or she will have poor health outcomes. ACEs increase the probability for

<!-- chunk -->

### physical inactivity, obesity, and diabetes by 25 to 52 percent. They are

<!-- chunk -->

### associated with two to three times higher rates of smoking, poor self-rated

<!-- chunk -->

### health, cancer, heart disease, and respiratory disease. ACEs lead to a three-

<!-- chunk -->

### to sixfold increase in the rates of sexual risk-taking, poor mental health,

<!-- chunk -->

### problematic alcohol use, and illicit drug use. They also lead to more than a

<!-- chunk -->

### sevenfold increase in being the victim or perpetrator of violence, a tenfold

<!-- chunk -->

### increase in problematic drug use, and a thirtyfold increase in suicide

<!-- chunk -->

### attempts. ACEs clearly affect mortality. One study of 17,000 people that

<!-- chunk -->

### looked specifically at mortality data estimated that having six or more

<!-- chunk -->

### ACEs takes twenty years off a person’s life compared to those with no

<!-- chunk -->

### ACEs.


<!-- chunk -->

### These studies have led many people to conclude that ACEs cause both

<!-- chunk -->

### physical and mental illnesses. Some experts have gone so far as to suggest

<!-- chunk -->

### that ACEs, in particular childhood trauma and abuse, are likely the common

<!-- chunk -->

### pathway to all mental disorders. But let me remind you, these are

<!-- chunk -->

### correlations. They don’t prove causation. More importantly, not everyone

<!-- chunk -->

### who has

<!-- chunk -->

### a horrible childhood develops a mental disorder, and many people who end

<!-- chunk -->

### up with mental disorders have perfectly fine childhoods. Nonetheless, if

<!-- chunk -->

### these adverse experiences are playing some role in these different disorders,

<!-- chunk -->

### how does that work? What is happening in the body and brain to cause all

<!-- chunk -->

### of this?

# Limited Resources

<!-- chunk -->

### For decades, researchers have been studying the biological effects of stress

<!-- chunk -->

### on the brain and body to better understand these relationships, hoping to

<!-- chunk -->

### identify the cause-and-effect pathways from stressful life events to poor

<!-- chunk -->

### health outcomes.

<!-- chunk -->

### We know that when the body is stressed, metabolic resources are being

<!-- chunk -->

### diverted to the fight-or-flight system. This leaves less energy available for

<!-- chunk -->

### other functions. Any cells that were already struggling can begin to fail.

<!-- chunk -->

### This can lead to metabolic and mental symptoms.

<!-- chunk -->

### Stress also impairs the body’s ability to maintain itself. Cells engage in

<!-- chunk -->

### housekeeping functions daily. They get rid of damaged cell parts, various

<!-- chunk -->

### waste molecules, and misfolded proteins, and they make new ones to take

<!-- chunk -->

### their places in a process often called autophagy. Auto means “self” and

<!-- chunk -->

### phagy means “eat,” so this term literally means “to eat yourself.” Our cells

<!-- chunk -->
