### degrade these old parts in waste-disposal systems called lysosomes. Some

<!-- chunk -->

### of this material is recycled and used to make new parts. High levels of

<!-- chunk -->

### cortisol have been found to inhibit autophagy, slowing down or stopping

<!-- chunk -->

### this maintenance process.


<!-- chunk -->

### Problems with autophagy have been found in a

<!-- chunk -->

### wide variety of disorders including neurodegenerative,

<!-- chunk -->

### neurodevelopmental, autoimmune, inflammatory, cancer, schizophrenia,

<!-- chunk -->

### bipolar disorder, autism, alcoholism, and major depression.


<!-- chunk -->

### Disturbances

<!-- chunk -->

### in autophagy are known to affect neuroplasticity and the maintenance of

<!-- chunk -->

### brain cells.


<!-- chunk -->

### In addition to problems with autophagy, when cells are stressed, they

<!-- chunk -->

### also slow the process of making new proteins. This appears to conserve

<!-- chunk -->

### metabolic resources for the body’s defense system. One way they delay

<!-- chunk -->

### making

<!-- chunk -->

### these proteins is by sequestering messenger RNA molecules (the

<!-- chunk -->

### instructions for new proteins) into little bubbles called “stress granules.”


<!-- chunk -->

### These have been associated with neurodegenerative disorders, and high

<!-- chunk -->

### levels of cortisol stimulate their production.


<!-- chunk -->

### One additional way that stress can lead to maintenance problems is

<!-- chunk -->

### through sleep disruption. It is well established that stress can lead to

<!-- chunk -->

### insomnia. Sleep is critically important to both physical and mental health.

<!-- chunk -->

### It’s a time when the body prioritizes maintenance functions. When people

<!-- chunk -->

### aren’t sleeping well, their bodies aren’t doing this maintenance work. On

<!-- chunk -->

### top of that, sleep deprivation, in and of itself, is stressful and can lead to

<!-- chunk -->

### higher levels of cortisol, which can make the problem even worse.

<!-- chunk -->

### All this stress, regardless of when it occurs, results in premature aging.

<!-- chunk -->

### I’ve already mentioned that all mental disorders are associated with

<!-- chunk -->

### premature aging, but stress alone can also cause it. One study tried to

<!-- chunk -->

### quantify the effects of stress on aging.


<!-- chunk -->

### The study recruited fifty-eight

<!-- chunk -->

### healthy premenopausal women who were mothers to either healthy or

<!-- chunk -->

### chronically ill children. The women’s average age was thirty-eight, and they

<!-- chunk -->

### did not yet have identified health problems. The researchers assessed three

<!-- chunk -->

### metrics of aging and asked the mothers to rate their perceived levels of

<!-- chunk -->

### stress. The mothers with the highest levels of stress over the longest periods

<!-- chunk -->

### of time showed signs of accelerated aging compared to the lowest-stress

<!-- chunk -->

### women. On average, they aged ten years faster.

<!-- chunk -->

### Stress clearly plays a role in human health and takes a serious metabolic

<!-- chunk -->

### toll. It uses energy that could otherwise be used for proper cell function and

<!-- chunk -->

### maintenance. When people are stressed in extreme ways or for prolonged

<!-- chunk -->

### periods of time, their bodies can get worn down and begin to malfunction,

<!-- chunk -->

### resulting in various physical and mental disorders, or just plain old aging. If

<!-- chunk -->

### the brain or body are already compromised and vulnerable, stress can make

<!-- chunk -->

### symptoms worse, because the energy needed for the stress response is

<!-- chunk -->

### diverting energy from these vulnerable cells.

<!-- chunk -->

### Stress-reduction practices, such as mindfulness, meditation, or yoga,

<!-- chunk -->

### can play a powerful role in treatment (more on that in Part Three).

<!-- chunk -->

### However, they aren’t the solution for everyone. If a person is living in an

<!-- chunk -->

### adverse

<!-- chunk -->

### environment, turning off the stress response may not be possible or even

<!-- chunk -->

### advisable. Soldiers fighting in a war are in danger. And while their service

<!-- chunk -->

### directly results in greater risks for mental and metabolic disorders, their

<!-- chunk -->

### heightened stress response is protecting them. The same applies to people in

<!-- chunk -->

### dangerous neighborhoods. Instructing people in dangerous environments to

<!-- chunk -->

### take deep breaths and be mindful isn’t the full answer. When they get to

<!-- chunk -->

### safety, these strategies might play a role, but the damage may already be

<!-- chunk -->

### done by then.

<!-- chunk -->

### Furthermore, stress may not be the cause of one’s mental illness at all.

<!-- chunk -->

### In that case, stress-reduction techniques likely won’t be all that helpful.

# Understanding “Disorders”: A New Definition of

# Mental Illness

<!-- chunk -->

### As I discussed in earlier chapters, the current classification of mental

<!-- chunk -->

### disorders is fraught with problems—heterogeneity, comorbidity, and a lack

<!-- chunk -->

### of validity. None of the diagnoses are true and distinct disorders unto

<!-- chunk -->

### themselves.

<!-- chunk -->

### The NIH has recognized this for some time and developed a new

<!-- chunk -->

### framework for thinking about mental illnesses—the Research Domain

<!-- chunk -->

### Criteria (RDoC). The RDoC starts fresh, ignoring our current diagnostic

<!-- chunk -->

### labels and classifications. Instead, the framework focuses on domains of

<!-- chunk -->

### functioning—emotion, cognition, motivation, and social behavior. It

<!-- chunk -->

### assumes ranges in these constructs from normal to abnormal, and it

<!-- chunk -->

### encourages researchers to explore these constructs from a perspective other

<!-- chunk -->

### than a diagnostic label. At one point, proponents of the RDoC were calling

<!-- chunk -->

### for a complete overhaul of our current psychiatric diagnostic criteria.

<!-- chunk -->

### Changing psychiatry and the mental health field, however, is no easy feat,

<!-- chunk -->

### so our current diagnostic criteria remain, despite all the known flaws. RDoC

<!-- chunk -->

### remains only in the realm of research at this point. However, for our

<!-- chunk -->

### purposes, I am going to use this model to define mental illness in the

<!-- chunk -->

### context of the brain energy theory.

<!-- chunk -->

### It starts by setting aside DSM-5 diagnostic labels and focusing instead on

<!-- chunk -->

### symptoms. This doesn’t mean that some of the diagnoses aren’t useful.

<!-- chunk -->

### Many are. Our current diagnostic labels simply describe some of the more

<!-- chunk -->

### common ways that the brain malfunctions. After all, the brain works, or

<!-- chunk -->

### fails to work, in predictable ways, and we can use those common narratives

<!-- chunk -->

### to our benefit.

<!-- chunk -->

### The human brain is like a machine—a very sophisticated and

<!-- chunk -->

### complicated machine, but a machine nonetheless. It has many parts all

<!-- chunk -->

### designed to do certain things. Some are fairly straightforward, such as

<!-- chunk -->

### making our muscles move or sensing what we feel or see. Other functions

<!-- chunk -->

### of the brain are more complicated, like sophisticated computer algorithms

<!-- chunk -->

### that are triggered in certain situations. In one way or another, all these brain

<!-- chunk -->

### functions can be tied to helping us survive, adapt to our environments, or

<!-- chunk -->

### reproduce.

<!-- chunk -->

### Given that the human brain has billions, if not trillions, of cells, and that

<!-- chunk -->

### each cell is a complicated machine itself, we face a potentially

<!-- chunk -->

### overwhelming problem: with so many cells, it seems there’s an almost

<!-- chunk -->

### infinite number of ways that all of these “parts” could malfunction. For

<!-- chunk -->

### better or worse, this is where the mental health field has been focused, with

<!-- chunk -->

### researchers trying to understand how the machine works, step-by-step. It’s

<!-- chunk -->

### an overwhelming task, the notion of wholly mapping something as

<!-- chunk -->

### complicated as the human brain, and waiting for this work to be finished

<!-- chunk -->

### has arguably limited our progress in better understanding and treating

<!-- chunk -->

### mental illness.

<!-- chunk -->

### But it doesn’t have to be so complicated. It turns out that all symptoms

<!-- chunk -->

### of mental illness actually correspond to normal mental states or brain

<!-- chunk -->

### functions, but gone awry: present when they should not be, absent when

<!-- chunk -->

### they should be present, or more or less active or persistent than is

<!-- chunk -->

### appropriate. These brain functions include things that relate to emotions,

<!-- chunk -->

### cognition, behavior, and motivation. As I will discuss, even some of the

<!-- chunk -->

### more bizarre-seeming symptoms of mental illness, such as delusions and

<!-- chunk -->

### hallucinations, can be tied to normal brain functions. Although we don’t

<!-- chunk -->

### know precisely how all of these functions work, we know they exist. That’s

<!-- chunk -->

### enough for our purposes here.

<!-- chunk -->

### Let’s start with a simple definition, then: Mental illness is when the brain is

<!-- chunk -->

### not working properly. Normal brain functions are either overactive,

<!-- chunk -->

### underactive, or absent. An easy example is having a panic attack for no

<!-- chunk -->

### clear reason. The panic system is beneficial when facing danger. It gets us

<!-- chunk -->

### moving. When it gets triggered for no clear reason, it’s dysfunctional and

<!-- chunk -->

### maladaptive. Sometimes the opposite can occur—brain functions that fail to

<!-- chunk -->

### activate in the right situations. Consider memory impairment in someone

<!-- chunk -->

### with dementia or a lack of social skills in someone with autism.

<!-- chunk -->

### When it comes to symptoms of mental illness, many people would say

<!-- chunk -->

### that they can’t possibly correspond to normal brain functions. It can seem

<!-- chunk -->

### like somehow the brain is doing unique and highly unusual things for no

<!-- chunk -->

### clear reason. I look at it differently. Like the parts of any machine, the parts

<!-- chunk -->

### of the brain are either working or they aren’t. If they perform their usual

<!-- chunk -->

### function, but get turned on at the wrong time, it can result in symptoms that

<!-- chunk -->

### seem bizarre. The same is true when normal brain functions fail to activate,

<!-- chunk -->

### or if two unrelated brain functions are mistakenly occurring at the same

<!-- chunk -->

### time.

# A Simple Example: Three Cars

<!-- chunk -->

### Let me use an analogy to explain how I think about the way people with

<!-- chunk -->

### mental illness differ from those who are having “normal” stress reactions,

<!-- chunk -->

### even though their symptoms can be the same and they can both lead to poor

<!-- chunk -->

### health outcomes. I’ll describe three cars. Each is the same make and model,

<!-- chunk -->

### so in theory, they should have the same lifespan and overall “health.” Each

<!-- chunk -->

### represents a human being.

<!-- chunk -->

### Car A lives in California, where the skies are blue, and the roads are in

<!-- chunk -->

### great condition. The owner doesn’t drive much—maybe twice a week. Car

<!-- chunk -->

### A is housed in a garage and gets regular maintenance. Car A is living the

<!-- chunk -->

### good life!

<!-- chunk -->

### Car B lives in the mountains of New Hampshire, where the winters can

<!-- chunk -->

### be fierce, and the back roads are filled with potholes. The owner drives the

<!-- chunk -->

### car every day and doesn’t have a garage to store it in. When winter comes,

<!-- chunk -->

### Car B gets snow tires, and sometimes even snow chains. In a blizzard, Car

<!-- chunk -->

### B is using its headlights, windshield wipers, blinkers, snow tires and chains,

<!-- chunk -->

### and its four-wheel drive system. The brakes are applied often so that the

<!-- chunk -->

### driver doesn’t lose control. In these situations, Car B is getting very low gas

<!-- chunk -->

### mileage compared to Car A. Car B also has more maintenance problems,

<!-- chunk -->

### given the harsh winter environment and difficult driving conditions. In the

<!-- chunk -->

### end, Car B has more “health problems” and ends up living a shorter life

<!-- chunk -->

### than Car A.

<!-- chunk -->

### Cars A and B are “normal”—they are both doing what they are

<!-- chunk -->

### supposed to, given the environmental circumstances they’re in. Neither has

<!-- chunk -->

### a disorder. Car B has more health problems and ends up living a shorter life,

<!-- chunk -->

### but given the adversity it faces, this is normal. The adaptations it uses, such

<!-- chunk -->

### as snow tires and chains, four-wheel drive, and frequent braking, are like

<!-- chunk -->

### stress responses—depression, anxiety, fear, anger. They help Car B navigate

<!-- chunk -->

### its difficult environment and they serve extraordinarily useful purposes.

<!-- chunk -->

### Without them, Car B would be much worse off.

<!-- chunk -->

### Now let me tell you about the third car. Car C lives in Indiana, where

<!-- chunk -->

### the weather is less harsh than New Hampshire, and the roads are in decent

<!-- chunk -->

### shape. It gets driven five days a week, sometimes in good weather and

<!-- chunk -->

### sometimes in bad. But Car C has problems. It turns on its windshield wipers

<!-- chunk -->

### and blinkers even in sunny weather. The wiper blades are worn thin because

<!-- chunk -->

### they get used so much. They end up scratching the windshield. Car C

<!-- chunk -->

### sometimes uses its four-wheel drive and travels only twenty-five miles per

<!-- chunk -->

### hour on the highway, even though it’s sunny and all the other cars are going

<!-- chunk -->

### sixty. When Car C drives at night, it doesn’t turn on its lights, even though

<!-- chunk -->

### they are needed. Car C has a disorder comparable to a mental illness.

<!-- chunk -->

### Although it has the exact same features and adaptive strategies as Cars A

<!-- chunk -->

### and B, it is using some of them at the wrong times and under the wrong

<!-- chunk -->

### circumstances. Meanwhile, it’s failing to use others that it should be using.

<!-- chunk -->

### Car C ends up needing quite a bit of maintenance. It also gets into traffic

<!-- chunk -->

### accidents. Car C’s disorder is seriously affecting its health and safety and

<!-- chunk -->

### impacting its ability to get along with other cars on the road. Car C ends up

<!-- chunk -->

### dying an early death.

<!-- chunk -->

### So . . . Cars A and B are “normal,” and Car C has a disorder.

<!-- chunk -->

### People who are struggling with adversity, like Car B, often need help, even

<!-- chunk -->

### if their brains are not malfunctioning. Their biology is responding to their

<!-- chunk -->

### adverse life experiences in “normal,” predictable, and adaptive ways. To aid

<!-- chunk -->

### them, we need to change their environments or help them respond optimally

<!-- chunk -->

### to harsh conditions. For the most part, these are societal factors—things like

<!-- chunk -->

### war, poverty, food insecurity, abuse, systemic racism, homophobia,

<!-- chunk -->

### misogyny, sexual harassment, anti-Semitism, and many other societal

<!-- chunk -->

### “blizzards.” Changing society so that these blizzards no longer exist is the

<!-- chunk -->

### ideal way to deal with these issues. In the meantime, helping people cope as

<!-- chunk -->

### best as possible can also be helpful.

<!-- chunk -->

### The brains of people with mental disorders are malfunctioning. They are

<!-- chunk -->

### doing things at the wrong times or with the wrong intensity, or they are

<!-- chunk -->

### failing to do things that they should be doing—as in the case of Car C. You

<!-- chunk -->

### don’t need to know precisely how these work in order to determine if there

<!-- chunk -->

### is a problem or not, just like you don’t need to fully understand the inner

<!-- chunk -->

### workings of a car and its windshield wiper system to know if there is a

<!-- chunk -->

### problem or not. I imagine you’re thinking that the problem with Car C isn’t

<!-- chunk -->

### with the car itself, but with the driver of Car C. In fact, you’re correct. I’ll

<!-- chunk -->

### get to that soon enough.

<!-- chunk -->

### It’s important to point out that prolonged or extreme stress can lead to a

<!-- chunk -->

### disorder as well. At some point, Car B could easily develop maintenance

<!-- chunk -->

### problems that result in adaptive strategies no longer working—maybe the

<!-- chunk -->

### lights stop working or the windshield wipers get worn thin and are no

<!-- chunk -->

### longer effective (underactive functions). Or the blinkers won’t turn off

<!-- chunk -->

### (overactive function). At that point, Car B would also have a disorder.

# A Human Example: Pain

<!-- chunk -->

### Now I’ll try to show you that this really does happen in the human body by

<!-- chunk -->

### focusing on an easy, straightforward example—pain. Since pain is

<!-- chunk -->

### controlled by nerve cells and brain regions, it serves as a perfect example

<!-- chunk -->

### for most of the mental symptoms I’ll discuss.

<!-- chunk -->

### Pain is a normal, healthy experience for humans—even though it is quite

<!-- chunk -->

### unpleasant. It saves our lives. It protects us from injuring ourselves. Pain is

<!-- chunk -->

### controlled by pain receptors, a nerve that goes to the spinal cord, another

<!-- chunk -->

### nerve that goes up to the brain, and then the regions of the brain that sense

<!-- chunk -->

### and process pain. The function and dysfunction of these neurons and brain

<!-- chunk -->

### regions gives us a simple framework that will help us better understand

<!-- chunk -->

### mental disorders.

<!-- chunk -->

### Broadly speaking, disorders of the pain system can be lumped into three

<!-- chunk -->

### categories based on the function of the cells in the pain system—overactive,

<!-- chunk -->

### underactive, and absent.

<!-- chunk -->

## 1.Overactivity of the pain system is when people experience pain

<!-- chunk -->

### more frequently or intensely than they should. Clinicians and

<!-- chunk -->

### researchers will often describe this as hyperexcitability of the pain

<!-- chunk -->

### system. For example, people with diabetes can develop neuropathy,

<!-- chunk -->

### and the nerve cells or brain regions that process pain can fire when

<!-- chunk -->

### they shouldn’t or fail to turn off when they should. This causes pain

<!-- chunk -->

### even when nothing painful is happening. It can result in a chronic

<!-- chunk -->

### and debilitating pain condition for some people.

<!-- chunk -->

## 2.Underactivity can occur when people feel fewer pain signals than

<!-- chunk -->

### they should, which can also happen in people with diabetes. In

<!-- chunk -->

### addition to hyperexcitability, diabetic neuropathy can also result in

<!-- chunk -->

### reduced sensation, especially in the feet. The nerves aren’t working

<!-- chunk -->

### properly, and this results in underactivity of the pain system. We

<!-- chunk -->

### know the nerves are still there and alive because sometimes people

<!-- chunk -->

### feel something.

<!-- chunk -->

## 3.Absence of pain can occur with prolonged and severe diabetes, but

<!-- chunk -->

### also with other conditions like spinal cord injuries or strokes. People

<!-- chunk -->

### feel absolutely nothing because the cells are dead or severely injured

<!-- chunk -->

### and no longer working.

<!-- chunk -->

### These three scenarios—overactivity, underactivity, and absence of function

<!-- chunk -->

### —are all disorders. The pain system isn’t working correctly.

<!-- chunk -->

### In some cases, it can be difficult to draw a line between normal pain and

<!-- chunk -->

### a pain disorder. One example is a herniated disc in the lower back causing

<!-- chunk -->

### pain. When the disc first becomes herniated, it’s not a disorder. The pain

<!-- chunk -->

### system is doing what it is supposed to do. If the pain goes on for a

<!-- chunk -->

### prolonged period of time, however, even after surgery and multiple

<!-- chunk -->

### medications, at some point we label it a pain disorder. What makes it a

<!-- chunk -->

### disorder? The nerves can become injured from the pressure of the herniated

<!-- chunk -->

### disc. These injured nerves can become hyperexcitable. They can send pain

<!-- chunk -->

### signals too often or too intensely. The point at which the pain goes from a

<!-- chunk -->

### normal response to a disorder is difficult, if not impossible, to distinguish

<!-- chunk -->

### based on current diagnostic tests. In some cases, it’s not clear whether it’s

<!-- chunk -->

### normal or a disorder. However, when the pain becomes chronic, severe, and

<!-- chunk -->

### unprovoked, we call it a disorder.

<!-- chunk -->

### Regardless of whether pain is a normal response to an injury or a pain

<!-- chunk -->

### disorder, treating the pain is appropriate all the time. For example, we all

<!-- chunk -->

### know that people will feel pain when they get surgery. It’s normal and

<!-- chunk -->

### expected. However, we still treat it to alleviate suffering.

<!-- chunk -->

### This distinction between normal and abnormal is important. Doctors

<!-- chunk -->

### who treat pain need to have good clinical skills. They need to understand

<!-- chunk -->

### the many reasons that someone might be experiencing pain. They need to

<!-- chunk -->

### evaluate their patients for these causes before assuming that they have a

<!-- chunk -->

### pain disorder. If a patient comes in with foot pain, it might be due to a

<!-- chunk -->

### sprain, a muscle spasm, a broken bone, or a piece of glass wedged in the

<!-- chunk -->

### skin. Each cause requires very different treatments. Treating the pain as

<!-- chunk -->

### though it is due to pain disorder might bring some relief, but it won’t solve

<!-- chunk -->

### the problem. In fact, the problem may get worse. However, if no obvious

<!-- chunk -->

### causes for foot pain are present, the doctor might then diagnose a pain

<!-- chunk -->

### disorder. This same type of detailed cause-and-effect assessment is

<!-- chunk -->

### necessary when evaluating people for mental disorders. Again, helping

<!-- chunk -->

### people cope with adversity is very different than treating a malfunctioning

<!-- chunk -->

### brain.

# Back to Defining Mental Illness

<!-- chunk -->

### Here’s our new, simplified definition of mental illness: A mental illness is

<!-- chunk -->

### when the brain is not working properly. Now let’s expand on that

<!-- chunk -->

### definition: A mental illness is when the brain is not working properly over a

<!-- chunk -->

### period of time, and this causes mental symptoms, which lead to suffering or

<!-- chunk -->

### impairment in functioning.

<!-- chunk -->

### Although this is a fairly short and concise definition, every part matters,

<!-- chunk -->

### and no part can be taken out of context. This definition includes four

<!-- chunk -->

### necessary components:

<!-- chunk -->

### 1.The brain is not working properly.

<!-- chunk -->

### 2.This results in mental symptoms.

<!-- chunk -->

### 3.This malfunction occurs over a period of time.

<!-- chunk -->

### 4.The symptoms cause suffering or impairment in functioning.

<!-- chunk -->

### Although these might seem like simple concepts, they can quickly get

<!-- chunk -->

### complicated.

<!-- chunk -->

### The first component of this definition—the brain is not working

<!-- chunk -->

### properly—sounds straightforward. But it’s actually difficult to measure and

<!-- chunk -->

### assess based on current technologies, just like pain. We have many tests that

<!-- chunk -->

### can measure brain health and function, such as EEGs and neuroimaging

<!-- chunk -->

### studies. However, none of them is sensitive and specific enough to

<!-- chunk -->

### accurately diagnose a mental disorder. Measuring the function of

<!-- chunk -->

### microscopic brain regions is difficult. So, in the real world, how do we

<!-- chunk -->

### know if the brain isn’t working properly?

<!-- chunk -->

### That leads us to the second component of the definition—this results in

<!-- chunk -->

### mental symptoms. Symptoms are the best indicator of abnormal brain

<!-- chunk -->

### function. However, like pain, when it comes to symptoms of mental illness,

<!-- chunk -->

### most can be normal, healthy brain functions in the right circumstances.

<!-- chunk -->

### Even something like hallucinations can occur in most people in the right

<!-- chunk -->

### circumstances. We all have hallucinations when we dream—we see things

<!-- chunk -->

### and hear

<!-- chunk -->

### things that aren’t there. When these things occur at the wrong time or when

<!-- chunk -->

### they fail to activate at the right time, they might represent a disorder. We

<!-- chunk -->

### can categorize the symptoms into the same three basic categories I used for

<!-- chunk -->

### pain—overactivity, underactivity, and absence of function.

<!-- chunk -->

### Component three—this malfunction occurs over a period of time—

<!-- chunk -->

### reinforces that the duration of symptoms matters. All of our brains fail to

<!-- chunk -->

### work perfectly at least some of the time, and this results in what we might

<!-- chunk -->

### call symptoms. Most of us have occasional lapses in our memory.

<!-- chunk -->

### Sometimes, we think we hear a noise, but no one else hears it. Sometimes,

<!-- chunk -->

### we “wake up on the wrong side of the bed” and feel depressed for no clear

<!-- chunk -->

### reason. These are examples of the brain not working properly. These are not

<!-- chunk -->

### mental illnesses but common occurrences that can happen due to a variety

<!-- chunk -->

### of circumstances—a bad night’s sleep, an extraordinarily stressful situation,

<!-- chunk -->

### use of alcohol or drugs, or just having a bad day. These are usually short-

<!-- chunk -->

### lived experiences (also relating to metabolism) with an easy brain-body fix.

<!-- chunk -->

### Mental illnesses need to be persistent problems with brain function that

<!-- chunk -->

### result in symptoms. The persistence of symptoms is currently part of our

<!-- chunk -->

### diagnostic process in the mental health field, but the amount of time varies

<!-- chunk -->

### by diagnosis.

<!-- chunk -->

### That brings us to component four of our definition: the symptoms cause

<!-- chunk -->

### suffering or impairment in functioning. We all have changes in emotions,

<!-- chunk -->

### cognition, motivation, and behaviors over the course of our lives. We learn.

<!-- chunk -->

### We grow. We meet new people and make changes. We go through

<!-- chunk -->

### challenging experiences. We suffer losses and setbacks. These fluctuations

<!-- chunk -->

### alone are not mental illness. Only when a person is distressed by these

<!-- chunk -->

### changes in an unusual way, or the changes prevent them from functioning in

<!-- chunk -->

### life, do we begin to consider the possibility of a mental illness. There is no

<!-- chunk -->

### question that this part of the definition is tricky, and the debates around the

<!-- chunk -->

### issue of suffering and impairment in functioning are complicated. Two

<!-- chunk -->

### issues are particularly important:

<!-- chunk -->

## 1.People have the right to be unique, creative, make changes in

<!-- chunk -->

## their lives, and go against mainstream culture. Being different is

<!-- chunk -->

### not a mental illness. Yet other people’s rejection of uniqueness may

<!-- chunk -->

### cause suffering. For example, many teenagers go through a rebellious

<!-- chunk -->

### phase. This is often a normal part of growing up and separating from

<!-- chunk -->

### parents. This alone is usually not a mental illness. Many people go on

<!-- chunk -->

### diets and frequently check their weight. They begin to think more

<!-- chunk -->

### about what to eat and how they look. This is not automatically an

<!-- chunk -->

### eating disorder. Both situations involve changes in emotions,

<!-- chunk -->

### cognition, motivation, and behaviors, but unusual distress and the

<!-- chunk -->

### inability to function aren’t part of the picture.

<!-- chunk -->

## 2.Some people with mental disorders lack insight. They don’t

<!-- chunk -->

### realize that their symptoms are somehow abnormal. They don’t

<!-- chunk -->

### recognize how their symptoms affect their behavior and function.

<!-- chunk -->

### They have trouble understanding why other people perceive these

<!-- chunk -->

### changes as unusual. They may claim that they are perfectly normal.

<!-- chunk -->

### However, if their symptoms are seriously impairing their ability to

<!-- chunk -->

### function in society, then a mental illness needs to be considered.

<!-- chunk -->

### It is common for people with hallucinations and delusions to lack

<!-- chunk -->

### insight into their illness. For instance, people with paranoia will say

<!-- chunk -->

### that they are really being persecuted—it’s not “mental,” it’s real.

<!-- chunk -->

### People with eating disorders will sometimes talk about how happy

<!-- chunk -->

### they are to be losing so much weight and looking better. They view

<!-- chunk -->

### any changes in functioning, such as devoting less time to school or

<!-- chunk -->

### friends, as the sacrifice needed to lose weight and look good. They

<!-- chunk -->

### may ignore the serious health problems that are evident to everyone

<!-- chunk -->

### else. Both will claim that their changes in emotions, cognition,

<!-- chunk -->

### motivation, and behaviors are normal and expected for anyone going

<!-- chunk -->

### through their circumstances. They will often deny any impairment in

<!-- chunk -->

### functioning. So, are these mental disorders? Yes. They are causing

<!-- chunk -->

### significant distress and/or impairment of functioning (this would

<!-- chunk -->

### include health problems), even if the person doesn’t see it or

<!-- chunk -->

### acknowledge it.

<!-- chunk -->

### These nuanced dilemmas sometimes make it difficult, if not impossible, to

<!-- chunk -->

### distinguish between being different and living in an unforgiving and rigid

<!-- chunk -->

### society versus having a mental illness. The mental health field has changed

<!-- chunk -->

### its stance around issues like this over the years, such as labeling

<!-- chunk -->

### homosexuality a disorder at one point in time and then reversing that

<!-- chunk -->

### decision.

# Symptoms of Mental Illness

<!-- chunk -->

### Now that we’ve arrived at and unpacked a new definition of mental illness,

<!-- chunk -->

### let me put it into action by outlining three broad scenarios that can produce

<!-- chunk -->

### symptoms of mental illness. They follow the model that I outlined for

<!-- chunk -->

### disorders of the pain system—brain functions that are overactive,

<!-- chunk -->

### underactive, or absent.

<!-- chunk -->

## Overactive Brain Functions

<!-- chunk -->

### Overactivity or hyperexcitability of brain cells and networks has been

<!-- chunk -->

### documented in many mental disorders. When thinking about this

<!-- chunk -->

### phenomenon, we are looking for symptoms or brain functions that are

<!-- chunk -->

### occurring more often or more intensely than they should be, or at the wrong

<!-- chunk -->

### time.

<!-- chunk -->

## Fear and anxiety symptoms can result from hyperexcitability of the

<!-- chunk -->

### amygdala—one of the regions of the brain implicated in the fear response.

<!-- chunk -->

### These neurons may fire out of turn or not stop firing, which causes anxiety

<!-- chunk -->

### symptoms at inappropriate times or an exaggerated fear response.

<!-- chunk -->

## Obsessions and compulsions can result from hyperexcitable cells and

<!-- chunk -->

### networks in the brain areas associated with grooming and checking

<!-- chunk -->

### behaviors. We all normally groom ourselves and check things. OCD occurs

<!-- chunk -->

### when these systems are overactive.

<!-- chunk -->

## Psychotic symptoms, such as hallucinations and delusions, are found in

<!-- chunk -->

### many mental and neurological disorders. They also occur in many people

<!-- chunk -->

### who never get diagnosed with any disorder.

<!-- chunk -->

### The precise brain cells and regions that cause psychotic symptoms are

<!-- chunk -->

### currently unknown, despite decades of intensive research looking for them.

<!-- chunk -->

### Nonetheless, there are a few ways that we can think about what might be

<!-- chunk -->

### happening in the brain.

<!-- chunk -->

### The easiest way to understand psychotic symptoms is hyperexcitability

<!-- chunk -->

### of brain cells that process perceptions. For example, if the brain cells and

<!-- chunk -->

### networks that perceive sound are hyperexcitable, people will hear

<!-- chunk -->

### something that isn’t there—an auditory hallucination. Neurosurgeons can

<!-- chunk -->

### make people “hallucinate” by stimulating brain areas with an electrode.

<!-- chunk -->

### Hyperexcitable cells would be doing essentially the same thing.

<!-- chunk -->

### The problem might not be in the neurons that perceive sound but in

<!-- chunk -->

### other neurons that regulate them and slow them down. There are a group of

<!-- chunk -->

### neurons called “cortical interneurons.” These neurons are known to be

<!-- chunk -->

### inhibitory, as they secrete gamma-aminobutyric acid, or GABA, a

<!-- chunk -->

### neurotransmitter that slows activity in its target cells. Abnormalities in the

<!-- chunk -->

### function of these neurons have been found in many disorders, including

<!-- chunk -->

### schizophrenia, Alzheimer’s disease, epilepsy, and autism. This lack of

<!-- chunk -->

### inhibition would result in overactivity of the neurons they are supposed to

<!-- chunk -->

### be inhibiting.

<!-- chunk -->

### Another possibility is that psychotic symptoms are related to the sleep

<!-- chunk -->

### systems in the brain. As I mentioned, we all have hallucinations and

<!-- chunk -->

### delusions every day—in our sleep. When we dream, we hear things and see

<!-- chunk -->

### things that aren’t there. We can believe wild and crazy things. Many people

<!-- chunk -->

### have nightmares that include being chased or persecuted. If these

<!-- chunk -->

### experiences occur during sleep, they are just bad dreams, not mental

<!-- chunk -->

### disorders. It’s possible that the same brain cells and networks that create

<!-- chunk -->

### these experiences at night are hyperexcitable and firing erroneously during

<!-- chunk -->

### the day in people with mental disorders.

<!-- chunk -->

### For some delusions that can seem bizarre, such as Capgras syndrome in

<!-- chunk -->

### which people believe that their loved ones have been replaced by imposters,

<!-- chunk -->

### we actually do know some of the specific brain networks involved in this

<!-- chunk -->

### process.


<!-- chunk -->

### These areas of the brain appear to be overactive and/or

<!-- chunk -->

### underactive.

<!-- chunk -->

### One important observation is that hallucinations are not as uncommon

<!-- chunk -->

### as most people would think. Researchers have found that 12 to 17 percent

<!-- chunk -->

### of children ages nine to twelve and 5.8 percent of adults hallucinate during

<!-- chunk -->

### the day.


<!-- chunk -->

### Additionally, 37 percent of adults experience hallucinations when

<!-- chunk -->

### they are falling asleep, also known as “hypnagogic hallucinations.”


<!-- chunk -->

### Most

<!-- chunk -->

### of these people are not diagnosed with mental disorders.

<!-- chunk -->

## Underactive Brain Functions

<!-- chunk -->

### Underactive brain cells and brain networks have been documented in many

<!-- chunk -->

### mental disorders. This concept easily explains at least some of the

<!-- chunk -->

### symptoms that we see. I distinguish underactive function from the absence

<!-- chunk -->

### of function because underactive function implies the cells are still alive and

<!-- chunk -->

### able to work at least some of the time. This is important, as it means that

<!-- chunk -->

### symptoms will wax and wane. Sometimes things can seem normal, and

<!-- chunk -->

### other times the person can have symptoms. Here are some examples:

<!-- chunk -->

### •People with ADHD can have a reduction in the activity of

<!-- chunk -->

### norepinephrine neurons in the locus coeruleus. These neurons help

<!-- chunk -->

### people focus, plan, and stay on task, so a reduction in their activity

<!-- chunk -->

### results in symptoms of ADHD.

<!-- chunk -->

### •Cognitive problems, such as memory impairment, can be due to

<!-- chunk -->

### reduced function of the neurons that are involved in the storage and

<!-- chunk -->

### retrieval of memories. These are clearly affected in Alzheimer’s

<!-- chunk -->

### disease but also in most of the chronic psychiatric disorders. People

<!-- chunk -->

### with chronic mental disorders often have cognitive impairment, even

<!-- chunk -->

### if this isn’t part of their diagnostic criteria.

<!-- chunk -->

### •At least one aspect of depression can involve a reduction in the

<!-- chunk -->

### activity of a brain system called the default mode network.


<!-- chunk -->

### This

<!-- chunk -->

### results in a slowing or disorganization of normal brain function.

<!-- chunk -->

### •“Emotional regulation” is a term used to describe symptoms in many

<!-- chunk -->

### different disorders, including mood, personality, and anxiety

<!-- chunk -->

### disorders. There are brain systems that are designed to help us control

<!-- chunk -->

### our emotional responses and regulate our moods. In some people,

<!-- chunk -->

### these areas of the brain appear to be underactive, resulting in

<!-- chunk -->

### symptoms such as unstable moods and anger outbursts.

<!-- chunk -->

## Absence of Specific Brain Functions

<!-- chunk -->

### Some mental disorders involve permanent changes in brain cells and

<!-- chunk -->

### connections. There are two primary causes of this—developmental

<!-- chunk -->

### problems and cell death. These problems are often associated with

<!-- chunk -->

### neurodevelopmental and neurodegenerative disorders, respectively. Cell

<!-- chunk -->

### death can also occur from things like a stroke or brain injury, which are

<!-- chunk -->

### different from neurodegenerative disorders, but these, too, can also result in

<!-- chunk -->

### mental symptoms.

<!-- chunk -->

### There are many neurodevelopmental disorders. Autism is one example.

<!-- chunk -->

### Neurons and/or connections between neurons appear to be missing or at

<!-- chunk -->

### least different.

<!-- chunk -->

### Neurodegenerative disorders, such as Alzheimer’s disease, are

<!-- chunk -->

### associated with brain shrinkage and death of neurons. Once neurons die,

<!-- chunk -->

### there is usually no way to bring them back.

<!-- chunk -->

### In both cases, cells or connections that are supposed to be present are

<!-- chunk -->

### not, so the brain is unable to perform these functions. Symptoms due to

<!-- chunk -->

### these permanent changes are always present. They don’t wax and wane.

<!-- chunk -->

### The social deficits seen in autism are fixed. At least some of the cognitive

<!-- chunk -->

### deficits seen in Alzheimer’s disease are also fixed. These don’t change from

<!-- chunk -->

### day to day. However, both autism and Alzheimer’s disease are also

<!-- chunk -->

### associated with ongoing mental symptoms that do wax and wane—anxiety,

<!-- chunk -->

### psychosis, and mood changes, to name a few.

<!-- chunk -->

### These three scenarios—overactive, underactive, and absent brain functions

<!-- chunk -->

### —can account for all symptoms of mental disorders. However, there are

<!-- chunk -->

### two additional situations that are worth mentioning, because at first glance,

<!-- chunk -->

### they may not appear to fit neatly into these categories: multifaceted brain

<!-- chunk -->

### adaptations and behavioral disorders.

<!-- chunk -->

## Multifaceted Brain Adaptations

<!-- chunk -->

### The brain sometimes has complicated responses to situations that involve

<!-- chunk -->

### multiple symptoms, with some of them representing activation of some

<!-- chunk -->

### brain functions and inactivation of others. I will discuss depression,

<!-- chunk -->

### hypomania,

<!-- chunk -->

### and the trauma response. All of these can be normal and adaptive when they

<!-- chunk -->

### occur at the right times under the right circumstances. They are similar to

<!-- chunk -->

### the activation of the sympathetic and parasympathetic nervous systems,

<!-- chunk -->

### which involve a complicated array of brain and body functions, some that

<!-- chunk -->

### get turned on and others that get turned off.

<!-- chunk -->

### Depression is a normal reaction to many stressors, adversities, and

<!-- chunk -->

### losses. Almost everyone has been depressed at least once. It usually doesn’t

<!-- chunk -->

### last unrelentingly for two or more weeks, but it’s a normal brain response.

<!-- chunk -->

### Although it commonly includes changes in mood, energy, appetite, and

<!-- chunk -->

### sleep, these changes can be very different in different people. Some people

<!-- chunk -->

### appear to have overactivity of the appetite system and others appear to have

<!-- chunk -->

### underactivity, resulting in eating too much or too little, respectively.

<!-- chunk -->

### Likewise, some people can sleep too much and others can’t sleep enough.

<!-- chunk -->

### Distilling depression into distinct symptoms, some that represent overactive

<!-- chunk -->

### or underactive brain regions, is likely to be the most effective and accurate

<!-- chunk -->

### way to understand depression, even though depression often involves many

<!-- chunk -->

### symptoms.

<!-- chunk -->

### Hypomania in many ways is the opposite of depression—people can

<!-- chunk -->

### feel great or euphoric, have increased energy, be more productive, and even

<!-- chunk -->

### get by on less sleep. This can also be normal. In fact, if it occurs in

<!-- chunk -->

### isolation, it’s not a diagnosable disorder according to DSM-5. Most people

<!-- chunk -->

### have experienced symptoms of hypomania at some point or another in life.

<!-- chunk -->

### This commonly occurs when people fall in love, but it can also occur when

<!-- chunk -->

### people are excited about a project or an accomplishment, or when they have

<!-- chunk -->

### a spiritual awakening. Again, it may not last five or more days, but it can

<!-- chunk -->

### occur, which suggests that these brain functions are hardwired into all of

<!-- chunk -->

### our brains.

<!-- chunk -->

### The trauma response is also normal. These symptoms include

<!-- chunk -->

### flashbacks and nightmares, avoidance of situations that remind the person

<!-- chunk -->

### of the event, negative effects on mood and thinking (similar to depression),

<!-- chunk -->

### trouble sleeping, being on edge and hyperalert, and other symptoms. One

<!-- chunk -->

### research group studied women shortly after being raped and found that

<!-- chunk -->

### percent of them had these types of symptoms in the first weeks.


<!-- chunk -->

### So, all of

<!-- chunk -->

### these responses can be “normal.”

<!-- chunk -->

### These multifaceted brain adaptations become disorders when they are

<!-- chunk -->

### overactive. They can be activated at the wrong time, last too long, or result

<!-- chunk -->

### in excessive or exaggerated symptoms. In some cases, they can be activated

<!-- chunk -->

### out of the blue for no clear reason—a hyperexcitable activation of the

<!-- chunk -->

### system. In other cases, they can be activated for a clear reason, such as a

<!-- chunk -->

### major stressor in life, but then they fail to inactivate after an appropriate

<!-- chunk -->

### amount of time. They become “stuck” in an “on” position when they should

<!-- chunk -->

### be turning off. This is similar to hyperexcitable pain cells in many pain

<!-- chunk -->

### disorders. Sometimes they can fire for no clear reason, but other times, the

<!-- chunk -->

### slightest injury or just moving the wrong way can trigger pain.

<!-- chunk -->

## Behavioral Disorders

<!-- chunk -->

### Some disorders are seen primarily as behavioral—in particular, substance

<!-- chunk -->

### use and eating disorders. These deserve special attention as well. Recall that

<!-- chunk -->

### they show strong bidirectional relationships with all mental disorders. I

<!-- chunk -->

### have said that mental disorders can be understood broadly as overactive,

<!-- chunk -->

### underactive, or absent brain functions. But these are behaviors . . . ones that

<!-- chunk -->

### people “choose” to engage in. What do these behaviors have to do with the

<!-- chunk -->

### brain malfunctioning?

<!-- chunk -->

### There are three ways to think about it. The first is that eating and using

<!-- chunk -->

### addictive substances are behaviors that are controlled by our brains. There

<!-- chunk -->

### are clear pathways that control cravings, appetite, motivation, self-control,

<!-- chunk -->

### impulsivity, and novelty seeking. So, in some cases, if these parts of the

<!-- chunk -->

### brain are overactive or underactive, they might drive people to engage in

<!-- chunk -->

### these behaviors, which can lead to problems. The second possibility is that

<!-- chunk -->

### people may have symptoms of other mental disorders (due to overactive or

<!-- chunk -->

### underactive brain regions) and use alcohol, drugs, or change eating

<!-- chunk -->

### behaviors to cope with those symptoms. This is commonly referred to as the

<!-- chunk -->

### self-medication hypothesis. The third possibility is that some people can be

<!-- chunk -->

### perfectly fine and “normal” and begin to engage in these behaviors. Some

<!-- chunk -->

### can start using drugs or alcohol due to peer pressure alone. A person can

<!-- chunk -->

### start dieting due to peer pressure alone. As I will discuss later, all of these

<!-- chunk -->

### behaviors can have powerful effects on metabolism and the brain. They can

<!-- chunk -->

### lead to metabolic abnormalities, which can lead to overactivity and

<!-- chunk -->

### underactivity of specific brain functions, that can then trap people in vicious

<!-- chunk -->

### cycles—ones that we call eating disorders and substance use disorders.

# A Complex Puzzle

<!-- chunk -->

### One of the challenges in identifying what causes mental illness is that the

<!-- chunk -->

### findings I’ve mentioned here, such as reduced default-mode network

<!-- chunk -->

### activity leading to depression, are not consistent across people with the

<!-- chunk -->

### same disorder, or even in the same person at different times. Other than in

<!-- chunk -->

### cases of developmental abnormalities or cell death, the symptoms wax and

<!-- chunk -->

### wane, and so can the neuroscience findings. That’s why we don’t yet have

<!-- chunk -->

### diagnostic tests. The developmental abnormalities are not consistent for

<!-- chunk -->

### specific disorders, either. They can affect a wide range of cell types and

<!-- chunk -->

### brain regions in different people, even people diagnosed with the same

<!-- chunk -->

### disorder. When looking at brain changes and the way the brain functions in

<!-- chunk -->

### mental illness, heterogeneity and inconsistent findings are the name of the

<!-- chunk -->

### game.

<!-- chunk -->

### That’s a lot to account for. It’s no wonder that the puzzle of mental

<!-- chunk -->

### illness has been so difficult to solve. What makes different parts of the brain

<!-- chunk -->

### overactive or underactive, leading to symptoms of mental illness? What

<!-- chunk -->

### makes the symptoms wax and wane? What exactly is causing these

<!-- chunk -->

### developmental abnormalities or areas of cell shrinkage and death? Why are

<!-- chunk -->

### they different in different people? All of these questions need to be

<!-- chunk -->

### addressed by any theory that tries to account for all mental disorders. I’m

<!-- chunk -->

### excited to share with you that the brain energy theory can do it. And it’s all

<!-- chunk -->

### through one common pathway.


# Magnificent Mitochondria

<!-- chunk -->

### now return to the discussion of metabolism in order to continue to put

<!-- chunk -->

### the pieces of the puzzle together. Recall the traffic analogy from Chapter

<!-- chunk -->

### Five where each car was like a human cell. I described metabolism as

<!-- chunk -->

### very complicated. It’s constantly changing, and may be different in different

<!-- chunk -->

### cells at different times. To be fair, that isn’t really one common pathway. It’s

<!-- chunk -->

### more like hundreds of different metabolic pathways.

<!-- chunk -->

### But what controls metabolism? How do food and oxygen know where

<!-- chunk -->

### to go? What changes the metabolic rate in various cells? What makes some

<!-- chunk -->

### cells slow down, while others go faster? What is driving this intricate

<!-- chunk -->

### network of the human body?

<!-- chunk -->

### Some would say it’s the brain. Although the brain plays a critical role in

<!-- chunk -->

### metabolism, it can’t control metabolism in all the different cells of the body

<!-- chunk -->

### at the correct times. Like city traffic, there must be some degree of control

<!-- chunk -->

### at the level of each car, or in the case of the human body, at the level of

<!-- chunk -->

### each cell. Cells receive input from other cells telling them to stop or go.

<!-- chunk -->

### Cells in close proximity also have signals that make neighboring cells stop

<!-- chunk -->

### or go

<!-- chunk -->

### (think brake lights on a car). But some of the signals are sent all over the

<!-- chunk -->

### body. They might originate in a brain cell or a liver cell, but they then travel

<!-- chunk -->

### long distances to affect cells throughout the body. These processes all lead

<!-- chunk -->

### to a coordination of metabolism, just like city traffic is coordinated on many

<!-- chunk -->

### levels, too.

<!-- chunk -->

### There are many things that make city traffic flow. The different types of

<!-- chunk -->

### roads and highways. The different speed limits on different roads. The stop

<!-- chunk -->

### signs and stoplights. These are all important to the organization and flow of

<!-- chunk -->

### city traffic. But in the end, the true and primary force controlling the flow of

<!-- chunk -->

### traffic boils down to the drivers in the cars. They know the rules and they

<!-- chunk -->

### follow them. They make the cars go. They make the cars stop. They use

<!-- chunk -->

### turn signals. They look out for trouble. They steer the cars around problems.

<!-- chunk -->

### They drive the cars to their destinations. And even though the drivers don’t

<!-- chunk -->

### know what’s happening with all the other cars on all the other roads or

<!-- chunk -->

### highways, everything works.

<!-- chunk -->

### Do human cells have “drivers” making the cells stop and go? It turns

<!-- chunk -->

### out that they do. The drivers of human cells, and human metabolism, are

<!-- chunk -->

### called mitochondria. And they are the common pathway to mental and

<!-- chunk -->

### metabolic disorders.

<!-- chunk -->

### If you’ve ever taken a biology course, you likely remember that

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondria are the “powerhouses of the cell.” Mitochondria make energy

<!-- chunk -->

### for cells by turning food and oxygen into ATP. While there’s no question

<!-- chunk -->

### that their role in energy production is critical, mitochondria are so much

<!-- chunk -->

### more than powerhouses. Without them, life as we know it wouldn’t exist.

<!-- chunk -->

### In the 2005 book Power, Sex, Suicide: Mitochondria and the Meaning of

<!-- chunk -->

### Life, Dr. Nick Lane provides a thorough and compelling story of

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondria and their role in human evolution.


<!-- chunk -->

### Although the title might

<!-- chunk -->

### suggest a pop culture quick read, Lane provides a rigorous scientific history

<!-- chunk -->

### of mitochondria and their role in human health and life itself.

# Mitochondrial Origins

<!-- chunk -->

### Once upon a time, the first mitochondrion (mitochondria is the plural of a

<!-- chunk -->

### single mitochondrion) was a bacterium. Researchers estimate mitochondria

<!-- chunk -->

### evolved from an independent living organism sometime between one and

<!-- chunk -->

### four billion years ago. A 1998 paper published in Nature suggests they

<!-- chunk -->

### share many genes with the modern-day Rickettsia prowazekii, a bacterium

<!-- chunk -->

### that causes typhus.


<!-- chunk -->

### Billions of years ago, another single-cell organism, an

<!-- chunk -->

### archaea, engulfed this ancestral mitochondrion. Instead of the

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondrion dying after being engulfed, as usually happens, they both

<!-- chunk -->

### lived. This new organism is thought to have evolved into the first

<!-- chunk -->

### eukaryotic cell (a cell with a nucleus). The inside bacterium began to focus

<!-- chunk -->

### on making energy, and the outside organism could focus on getting food.

<!-- chunk -->

### Make no mistake—this is important. It is not a trivial fact.

<!-- chunk -->

### Thus, before there was a cell nucleus housing human DNA, and before

<!-- chunk -->

### there were other organelles, there was a mitochondrion—a single

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondrion and a single host cell. Together, they were determined to

<!-- chunk -->

### survive. Actually, not just survive, but thrive. Like all forms of life—they

<!-- chunk -->

### were in it to win it. And win it they did!

<!-- chunk -->

### Over time, it was this symbiotic arrangement that allowed for

<!-- chunk -->

### multicellular life—essentially all life that we can see with our eyes today. In

<!-- chunk -->

### all eukaryotes, these internal bacteria evolved into mitochondria. In plants

<!-- chunk -->

### and algae (also eukaryotes), some of them also evolved into what we now

<!-- chunk -->

### call chloroplasts. Although mitochondria and chloroplasts have different

<!-- chunk -->

### names, they look and function similarly, and they are thought to be

<!-- chunk -->

### descended from the same bacterium from billions of years ago.

<!-- chunk -->

### Furthermore, it’s believed that this merger happened only once, and that all

<!-- chunk -->

### plants, animals, algae, and fungi that exist today descended from this same

<!-- chunk -->

### organism. For those who believe in God, this concept of a single event

<!-- chunk -->

### starting life as we know it might be reassuring. For those who don’t believe

<!-- chunk -->

### in God, it was just one of those unusual and unlikely events that shaped

<!-- chunk -->

### evolution for billions of years to come. Regardless of what you believe, it

<!-- chunk -->

### was an important event in the story of life.

<!-- chunk -->

### In evolution, being first matters. For example, when genes overlap among

<!-- chunk -->

### different organisms, they are usually believed to be more important than

<!-- chunk -->

### genes that are unique to specific species. The unique genes are thought to

<!-- chunk -->

### have occurred more recently in the evolutionary timeline, while the

<!-- chunk -->

### common genes developed much earlier. Things that have persisted for a

<!-- chunk -->

### long time are thought to be more essential to life. There are at least two

<!-- chunk -->

### reasons for this. The first is that evolution tends to get rid of things that are

<!-- chunk -->

### not essential or don’t confer some advantage in terms of survival or

<!-- chunk -->

### reproduction. If organisms evolve to no longer need a trait, it will no longer

<!-- chunk -->

### be selected for and will often eventually disappear. The second is that new

<!-- chunk -->

### genes and traits must develop with and adapt to the genes and traits that are

<!-- chunk -->

### already there. Mitochondria were in eukaryotic cells first. Initially, it was

<!-- chunk -->

### just a single bacterium and a single outside cell. Over time, the nucleus and

<!-- chunk -->

### other organelles developed. As important as these other organelles are,

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondria were there first. They likely influenced the development of

<!-- chunk -->

### these other cell parts and became indispensable. In fact, these other cell

<!-- chunk -->

### parts don’t work correctly without mitochondria.

# Modern Mitochondria

<!-- chunk -->

### Mitochondria are no longer able to replicate themselves outside of a

<!-- chunk -->

### eukaryotic cell. In humans, mitochondria transferred most of their DNA to

<!-- chunk -->

### the cell’s nucleus, where human DNA resides. There are about 1,500

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondrial genes that are now embedded within human DNA. These

<!-- chunk -->

### 1,500 genes make proteins that are required to either create or maintain

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondria, and these proteins are shared with all the mitochondria in the

<!-- chunk -->

### cell. However, mitochondria didn’t give up all their DNA. Each one of them

<!-- chunk -->

### still has thirty-seven genes. Individual mitochondria can use that DNA on

<!-- chunk -->

### their own—and thus mitochondria maintain some degree of independence,

<!-- chunk -->

### both from each other and from the cell in which they reside. This is highly

<!-- chunk -->

### unusual in biology, and its purpose is the subject of debate. The point,

<!-- chunk -->

### however, is this: Mitochondria and human cells are now 100 percent

<!-- chunk -->

### committed to each other. Neither can survive without the other.

<!-- chunk -->

### Mitochondria are tiny. On average, each human cell has about three to four

<!-- chunk -->

### hundred mitochondria.


<!-- chunk -->

### This means that there are about ten million billion

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondria in the human body. They make up about 10 percent of our

<!-- chunk -->

### body weight despite their tiny size. In metabolically demanding cells—such

<!-- chunk -->

### as brain cells—a single cell can contain thousands of mitochondria, with

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondria making up 40-plus percent of the cell volume.

<!-- chunk -->

### Mitochondria are busy. Although small amounts of ATP can be

<!-- chunk -->

### produced without mitochondria through a process called glycolysis,

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondria produce the lion’s share of ATP, especially for brain cells. In

<!-- chunk -->

### the average human adult, they make about 9 ×


<!-- chunk -->

### ATP molecules every

<!-- chunk -->

### second.


<!-- chunk -->

### One group of researchers looked at brain cells using specialized

<!-- chunk -->

### imaging techniques and found that a single neuron in the human brain uses

<!-- chunk -->

### about 4.7 billion ATP molecules every second.


<!-- chunk -->

### That’s a lot of ATP!

<!-- chunk -->

### Mitochondria move. This is a fairly recent discovery based on new

<!-- chunk -->

### techniques for studying living cells.


<!-- chunk -->

### When a cell is dead under the

<!-- chunk -->

### microscope, nothing moves, so it’s easy to see why researchers didn’t think

<!-- chunk -->

### that mitochondria would be mobile. Other organelles typically are not. The

<!-- chunk -->

### finding that mitochondria actually move around living cells was highly

<!-- chunk -->

### unexpected. If you’d like to see a video of mitochondria moving, you can

<!-- chunk -->

### see them in the PLOS Biology article in the endnotes.


<!-- chunk -->

### There are many other

<!-- chunk -->

### videos available online. There is a network of microtubules and filaments

<!-- chunk -->

### throughout the cell, often referred to as the cytoskeleton, that mitochondria

<!-- chunk -->

### use for their movement. There are many mechanisms involved, which are

<!-- chunk -->

### beyond our scope, but the point is simple—some mitochondria move

<!-- chunk -->

### around.


<!-- chunk -->

### However, it appears that not all mitochondria are moving. Some

<!-- chunk -->

### stay in one place, while others move.

<!-- chunk -->

### Why are they moving? Well, one reason is that they appear to go to the

<!-- chunk -->

### places in the cell where things are happening and where energy is needed.

<!-- chunk -->

### Energy needs to be produced in the right amount, in the right place, at the

<!-- chunk -->

### right time, and it goes through an unimaginably fast recycling process that

<!-- chunk -->

### involves mitochondria. The mitochondria that aren’t moving appear to stay

<!-- chunk -->

### in places where things are always happening—either near factories where

<!-- chunk -->

### proteins are made (ribosomes) or synapses where there is a lot of activity,

<!-- chunk -->

### which is a very important fact relevant to how the brain functions.

<!-- chunk -->

### Researchers looking at brain cells under microscopes have known for

<!-- chunk -->

### decades how to identify where the synapses are—they look for the


<!-- chunk -->

### Mitochondria are rapid recyclers. ATP is the energy currency of human

<!-- chunk -->

### cells. When it is used as energy, a phosphate group is removed, which turns

<!-- chunk -->

### it into adenosine diphosphate, or ADP. This ADP can’t supply much energy

<!-- chunk -->

### anymore, but if a phosphate group is added back to it, it’s as good as new.

<!-- chunk -->

### That’s what mitochondria do. They take ADP and turn it back into ATP by

<!-- chunk -->

### attaching a phosphate group, then transfer it out to the cell cytoplasm where

<!-- chunk -->

### it is needed. They give one ATP and recycle one ADP simultaneously. If

<!-- chunk -->

### there is a lot of activity in a particular part of the cell, you will find

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondria there. They have to provide the ATP, but they also suck up all

<!-- chunk -->

### of the ADP and recycle it. You can think about mitochondria as little

<!-- chunk -->

### vacuum cleaners, going around the cell and sucking up ADP and churning

<!-- chunk -->

### out ATP.

<!-- chunk -->

### Remember that I said there were billions of ATP molecules being used

<!-- chunk -->

### every second in just one brain cell? Well, if there isn’t a mitochondrion or

<!-- chunk -->

### two (well . . . maybe more than that) in the right place at the right time to

<!-- chunk -->

### both deliver all that ATP and recycle all the ADP, things will back up

<!-- chunk -->

### quickly and either slow down or stop working.

<!-- chunk -->

### However, mitochondrial movement is more important than just making

<!-- chunk -->

### sure enough energy is supplied in the right place at the right time. It’s also

<!-- chunk -->

### related to mitochondrial interactions with other organelles and with each

<!-- chunk -->

### other. These interactions are critically important to almost all cell functions

<!-- chunk -->

### and even gene expression.

<!-- chunk -->

### To demonstrate the role of mitochondria, I’ll first need to review some

<!-- chunk -->

### basic information on how neurons work. Although the function of any cell

<!-- chunk -->

### is complicated, and brain cells even more so, there are some basics that are

<!-- chunk -->

### directly regulated by mitochondria. Better understanding them will allow

<!-- chunk -->

### me to tie metabolism and mitochondria to distinct functions of brain cells.

<!-- chunk -->

### I’ll use the next chapter to explain how all the symptoms of mental illness

<!-- chunk -->

### are directly related to mitochondria and metabolism.

<!-- chunk -->

### Neurons have a resting membrane potential. Basically, this means that

<!-- chunk -->

### the inside of the cell has a negative charge compared to the outside of the

<!-- chunk -->

### cell. This charge is critically important to the function of the cell. It is

<!-- chunk -->

### created by ion pumps, which pump sodium, potassium, calcium, and other

<!-- chunk -->

### ions either inside or outside of the cell, or between compartments within the

<!-- chunk -->

### cell. These pumps all require energy.

<!-- chunk -->

### Cells do a lot of ion pumping in order to set themselves up to be ready

<!-- chunk -->

### to fire. When the cell is triggered, it sets off a cascade of events that results

<!-- chunk -->

### in the cell doing its thing, whether that entails releasing a neurotransmitter

<!-- chunk -->

### or a hormone, or doing something else. It’s like setting up a row of

<!-- chunk -->

### dominoes. It takes time and work to set them up, but it’s easy to push them

<!-- chunk -->

### all over by simply nudging one of them. Once they all fall down, they need

<!-- chunk -->

### to be set up again. That requires more work. Mitochondria provide almost

<!-- chunk -->

### all the energy needed to do all of this.

# What Else Do Mitochondria Do?

<!-- chunk -->

### Calcium levels play an important role in the function of cells. High levels of

<!-- chunk -->

### calcium in the cytoplasm can trigger all sorts of things to happen. In many

<!-- chunk -->

### ways, calcium is an “on/off” switch. When levels are high, the cell is “on.”

<!-- chunk -->

### When levels are low, the cell gets turned “off.” Mitochondria are directly

<!-- chunk -->

### involved in calcium regulation. When mitochondria are prevented from

<!-- chunk -->

### functioning properly, calcium regulation is disrupted—and this important

<!-- chunk -->

### “off” switch can be as well.


<!-- chunk -->

### Therefore, mitochondria are essential in

<!-- chunk -->

### turning cells both on and off. They provide the energy needed for ion

<!-- chunk -->

### pumping, and they also regulate the calcium levels that function as essential

<!-- chunk -->

### on/off signals.

<!-- chunk -->

### Energy and mitochondria are required to turn cells both on and off. This

<!-- chunk -->

### may seem paradoxical, but it will make more sense if you think of the “off”

<!-- chunk -->

### switch as electronic brakes on a car that require energy to work. Without

<!-- chunk -->

### enough energy to apply the brakes fully and quickly at appropriate times,

<!-- chunk -->

### the car can become impossible to control and cause major disruptions in the

<!-- chunk -->

### flow of traffic. These dichotomous consequences of metabolic and

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondrial dysfunction are important to understand. Some cells will stay

<!-- chunk -->

### on

<!-- chunk -->

### too long when they are energy deprived, while other cells will fail to work. I

<!-- chunk -->

### will come back to this soon enough.

<!-- chunk -->

### Turning cells on and off is critically important. Understanding this

<!-- chunk -->

### function will help us explain most of the symptoms of mental illness.

<!-- chunk -->

### However, mitochondria actually do much more than that. Their role in

<!-- chunk -->

### human health is a cutting-edge, vigorous area of research that spans almost

<!-- chunk -->

### every field in medicine.

<!-- chunk -->

### Let’s outline some of the other roles that mitochondria play that are

<!-- chunk -->

### important to their relationship with mental health.

<!-- chunk -->

## Mitochondria Help Regulate Metabolism Broadly

<!-- chunk -->

### In 2001, a peptide called humanin was first reported to have broad effects

<!-- chunk -->

### on metabolism and health.


<!-- chunk -->

### The gene for this peptide appears to reside on

<!-- chunk -->

### both mitochondrial DNA and nuclear DNA. It was first discovered in

<!-- chunk -->

### research on Alzheimer’s disease. Since its discovery, two other peptides,

<!-- chunk -->

### MOTS-c and SHLP1–6, have been discovered and added to a new class of

<!-- chunk -->

### molecules called mitochondrially derived peptides. The genes for these

<!-- chunk -->

### peptides are on mitochondrial DNA, and these peptides are produced by

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondria. They are now of great interest to researchers. They have been

<!-- chunk -->

### shown to have beneficial effects on illnesses such as Alzheimer’s disease,

<!-- chunk -->

### strokes, diabetes, heart attacks, and certain types of cancer. They also have

<!-- chunk -->

### broad effects on metabolism, cell survival, and inflammation.


<!-- chunk -->

### existence of these peptides suggests that mitochondria are able to

<!-- chunk -->

### communicate with each other through these peptide signals in order to

<!-- chunk -->

### regulate metabolism throughout the body.

<!-- chunk -->

## Mitochondria Help Produce and Regulate Neurotransmitters

<!-- chunk -->

### Neurotransmitters have been a primary focus in the mental health field. It

<!-- chunk -->

### turns out that mitochondria play critical roles in their production, secretion,

<!-- chunk -->

### and overall regulation.

<!-- chunk -->

### Neurons often have one specific neurotransmitter that they specialize in

<!-- chunk -->

### making. Some make serotonin. Others make dopamine. The process of

<!-- chunk -->

### making a neurotransmitter takes energy and building blocks.

<!-- chunk -->

### Mitochondria provide both. They play a direct role in the production of

<!-- chunk -->

### acetylcholine, glutamate, norepinephrine, dopamine, GABA, and

<!-- chunk -->

### serotonin.


<!-- chunk -->

### Once made, neurotransmitters are stored in vesicles, or little

<!-- chunk -->

### bubbles, until they are ready to use. Vesicles filled with neurotransmitters

<!-- chunk -->

### travel down the axon to get to their ultimate release site. That takes energy.

<!-- chunk -->

### The signal to release neurotransmitters depends upon the resting membrane

<!-- chunk -->

### potential and calcium levels that I discussed. Once that signal comes, the

<!-- chunk -->

### actual release of neurotransmitters also takes energy. Fascinatingly, once

<!-- chunk -->

### neurotransmitters are released at one location, the mitochondria move to

<!-- chunk -->

### another location of the cell membrane to release a new batch of

<!-- chunk -->

### neurotransmitters.


<!-- chunk -->

### Once released, neurotransmitters have their effect on

<!-- chunk -->

### the target tissue, whether it’s another nerve, muscle, or gland cell. After

<!-- chunk -->

### they are released from the receptors on the target cell, they are sucked back

<!-- chunk -->

### into the axon terminals (a process called reuptake), and you guessed it, that

<!-- chunk -->

### takes energy. They are then repackaged back into vesicles for the next

<!-- chunk -->

### round—yet more energy.

<!-- chunk -->

### Mitochondria are normally found in large supply at synapses. When

<!-- chunk -->

### they are prevented from getting to the synapses, neurotransmitters don’t get

<!-- chunk -->

### released, even if there is ATP present.


<!-- chunk -->

### When mitochondria aren’t

<!-- chunk -->

### functioning properly, neurotransmitters can become imbalanced. Given that

<!-- chunk -->

### neurotransmitters are an important way for nerve cells to communicate with

<!-- chunk -->

### each other, imbalances can disrupt normal brain functions.

<!-- chunk -->

### The role of mitochondria in regulating neurotransmitters goes much

<!-- chunk -->

### further than just their involvement in synthesis, release, and reuptake.

<!-- chunk -->

### Mitochondria actually have receptors for some neurotransmitters, indicating

<!-- chunk -->

### a feedback cycle between neurotransmitters and mitochondria. They also

<!-- chunk -->

### have some of the enzymes involved in the breakdown of neurotransmitters,

<!-- chunk -->

### such as monoamine oxidase. They are involved in regulating the release of

<!-- chunk -->

### GABA, and they actually store GABA within themselves.


<!-- chunk -->

### Finally, several

<!-- chunk -->

### neurotransmitters are known to regulate mitochondrial function, production,

<!-- chunk -->

### and growth. Clearly, neurotransmitters are much more than just messengers

<!-- chunk -->

### between cells impacting mood. They are essential regulators of metabolism

<!-- chunk -->

### and mitochondria themselves. I’ll come back to this later.

<!-- chunk -->

## Mitochondria Help Regulate Immune System Function

<!-- chunk -->

### Mitochondria also play an essential role in immune system function.


<!-- chunk -->

### This

<!-- chunk -->

### includes fighting off viruses and bacteria, but it also includes low-grade

<!-- chunk -->

### inflammation, something that has been found in most metabolic and mental

<!-- chunk -->

### disorders to some degree. Mitochondria help regulate how immune cells

<!-- chunk -->

### engage with immune receptors. When cells are highly stressed, they often

<!-- chunk -->

### release components of mitochondria, which serve as a danger signal to the

<!-- chunk -->

### rest of the body, one that activates chronic, low-grade inflammation.


<!-- chunk -->

### One study looked at specific types of immune cells called macrophages

<!-- chunk -->

### to see how these cells coordinate the complicated repair processes in wound

<!-- chunk -->

### healing. The cells do different things during different phases of healing. Up

<!-- chunk -->

### until this study, it wasn’t known how the cells know when and how to

<!-- chunk -->

### change between phases. The researchers found that mitochondria

<!-- chunk -->

### specifically controlled these processes.


<!-- chunk -->

## Mitochondria Help Regulate Stress Responses

<!-- chunk -->

### We now know that mitochondria help control and coordinate the stress

<!-- chunk -->

### response in the human body. This includes both physical and mental

<!-- chunk -->

### stressors. Physical stressors include things like starvation, infection, or a

<!-- chunk -->

### lack of oxygen. Mental stressors are anything that threatens or challenges us

<!-- chunk -->

### (as talked about in the previous chapter).

<!-- chunk -->

### When cells are physically stressed, they initiate a process called the

<!-- chunk -->

### integrated stress response. This is a coordinated effort by the cell to adapt

<!-- chunk -->

### to and survive adverse circumstances through changes in metabolism, gene

<!-- chunk -->

### expression, and other adaptations. Many lines of research show that

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondrial stress itself leads to the integrated stress response.


<!-- chunk -->

### If the

<!-- chunk -->

### cell isn’t able to manage the stress, one of two things happens—it either

<!-- chunk -->

### triggers its own death, a process called apoptosis, or it enters into a

<!-- chunk -->

### “zombielike” state called senescence, which has been associated with aging

<!-- chunk -->

### and many health problems, such as cancer.

<!-- chunk -->

### Up until recently, it wasn’t known how the different aspects of the

<!-- chunk -->

### psychological stress response are all coordinated in the body and brain. It

<!-- chunk -->

### turns out that mitochondria play a critically important role! One brilliant

<!-- chunk -->

### study by Dr. Martin Picard and colleagues demonstrated this, and its title

<!-- chunk -->

### says it all: “Mitochondrial functions modulate neuroendocrine, metabolic,

<!-- chunk -->

### inflammatory, and transcriptional responses to acute psychological

<!-- chunk -->

### stress.”


<!-- chunk -->

### These researchers were studying mice and genetically

<!-- chunk -->

### manipulated their mitochondria to see what effects these manipulations had

<!-- chunk -->

### on the stress response. They manipulated only four different genes—two

<!-- chunk -->

### located in mitochondria themselves and two located in the cell nucleus that

<!-- chunk -->

### code for proteins used exclusively in mitochondria. Each genetic

<!-- chunk -->

### manipulation resulted in different problems with mitochondrial function.

<!-- chunk -->

### However, even with only four manipulations, they found that all the stress

<!-- chunk -->

### response factors were affected. This included changes in cortisol levels, the

<!-- chunk -->

### sympathetic nervous system, adrenaline levels, inflammation, markers of

<!-- chunk -->

### metabolism, and gene expression in the hippocampus. Their conclusion was

<!-- chunk -->

### that mitochondria are directly involved in controlling all these stress

<!-- chunk -->

### responses, and if mitochondria aren’t functioning properly, these stress

<!-- chunk -->

### responses are altered.

<!-- chunk -->

## Mitochondria Are Involved in Making, Releasing, and

<!-- chunk -->

## Responding to Hormones

<!-- chunk -->

### Mitochondria are key regulators of hormones. Cells that make hormones

<!-- chunk -->

### require more energy than most. They synthesize the hormones, package

<!-- chunk -->

### them up, and release them, just as I described for neurotransmitters. It takes

<!-- chunk -->

### a lot of ATP to do this, and mitochondria are there to deliver it.

<!-- chunk -->

### For some hormones, mitochondria are even more important—this

<!-- chunk -->

### includes well-known names like cortisol, estrogen, and testosterone. The

<!-- chunk -->

### enzymes required for initiating the production of these hormones are found

<!-- chunk -->

### only in mitochondria. Without mitochondria, these hormones aren’t made.
