<!-- chunk -->

### But there’s more. Mitochondria in other cells sometimes have receptors for

<!-- chunk -->

### these hormones. So, in some cases, these hormones can begin in

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondria in one type of cell and end with mitochondria in another type

<!-- chunk -->

### of cell.

<!-- chunk -->

## Mitochondria Create Reactive Oxygen Species (ROS) and Help

<!-- chunk -->

## Clean It Up

<!-- chunk -->

### Mitochondria burn fuel—either carbohydrates, fats, or protein. Burning fuel

<!-- chunk -->

### can sometimes create waste products. When mitochondria burn fuel,

<!-- chunk -->

### electrons flow along the electron transport chain. These electrons are a

<!-- chunk -->

### source of energy usually used to make either ATP or heat. However,

<!-- chunk -->

### sometimes these electrons leak outside of the usual system. When they do,

<!-- chunk -->

### they form what are called reactive oxygen species (ROS).


<!-- chunk -->

### These include

<!-- chunk -->

### molecules such as superoxide anion (O


<!-- chunk -->

### -), hydrogen peroxide (H


<!-- chunk -->

### O


<!-- chunk -->

### ),

<!-- chunk -->

### hydroxyl radical (•OH), and organic peroxides. At one point, researchers

<!-- chunk -->

### believed ROS were simply toxic waste products. We now know small

<!-- chunk -->

### amounts of ROS actually serve a useful signaling process inside the cell.

<!-- chunk -->

### For example, a 2016 paper published in Nature found that ROS were the

<!-- chunk -->

### primary regulators of heat production and energy expenditure—a broad

<!-- chunk -->

### measure of metabolic rate.


<!-- chunk -->

### However, large amounts of ROS are toxic and

<!-- chunk -->

### result in inflammation.


<!-- chunk -->

### You may have heard the term oxidative stress—

<!-- chunk -->

### that’s what this is! ROS are known to cause damage to mitochondria and

<!-- chunk -->

### cells. They are associated with aging and many diseases. Given that ROS

<!-- chunk -->

### are produced right in the mitochondria and are highly reactive, they often

<!-- chunk -->

### damage mitochondria first. The mitochondrial DNA is unprotected, so large

<!-- chunk -->

### amounts of ROS are known to result in mitochondrial DNA mutations.

<!-- chunk -->

### These ROS can also damage the mitochondrial machinery itself. If they

<!-- chunk -->

### leak outside of the mitochondria, they can damage many different parts of

<!-- chunk -->

### the cell.

<!-- chunk -->

### Additionally, mitochondria serve as ROS janitors. As well as producing

<!-- chunk -->

### ROS, mitochondria also clean up some of it up through an elaborate system

<!-- chunk -->

### of enzymes and other factors that serve to detoxify ROS.


<!-- chunk -->

### Cells have other

<!-- chunk -->

### antioxidant systems, too, but mitochondria play a role. When this

<!-- chunk -->

### detoxification system fails, these ROS waste products can pile up and cause

<!-- chunk -->

### damage. This can lead to cellular dysfunction, otherwise known as aging,

<!-- chunk -->

### cell death, and disease.

<!-- chunk -->

## Mitochondria Are Shape-Shifters

<!-- chunk -->

### Mitochondria change shape in response to different environmental factors.

<!-- chunk -->

### Sometimes they are long and thin. Other times they are short and

<!-- chunk -->

### fat. Sometimes they are round. In addition to changing shape, they interact

<!-- chunk -->

### with each other in profound ways. They can merge to make just one

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondrion—a process called fusion. They can divide and form two

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondria—a process called fission. These changes in shape are very

<!-- chunk -->

### important to cell function. In 2013, two articles published in the journal

<!-- chunk -->

### Cell showed that the process of mitochondria fusing with each other

<!-- chunk -->

### significantly impacts fat storage, eating behaviors, and obesity.


<!-- chunk -->

### Mitochondrial changes in shape and their fusion with each other appear to

<!-- chunk -->

### create signals that can affect the entire human body. When mitochondria are

<!-- chunk -->

### prevented from doing these things, metabolic problems ensue, not just in

<!-- chunk -->

### the cells affected, but sometimes throughout the body.

<!-- chunk -->

## Mitochondria Play a Primary Role in Gene Expression

<!-- chunk -->

### Nuclear DNA is where the human genome resides. It’s contained within the

<!-- chunk -->

### cell nucleus. Researchers once thought that genes controlled everything

<!-- chunk -->

### about the human body. They assumed that the nucleus was the control

<!-- chunk -->

### center of the cell. We now know that it’s not always about the genes

<!-- chunk -->

### themselves, but more about what causes certain genes to turn on or off. This

<!-- chunk -->

### is the field of epigenetics.

<!-- chunk -->

### Mitochondria are primary regulators of epigenetics. They send signals

<!-- chunk -->

### to the nuclear DNA in several different ways. This is sometimes referred to

<!-- chunk -->

### as the retrograde response.

<!-- chunk -->

### It has long been known that the ratio of ATP to ADP, levels of ROS, and

<!-- chunk -->

### calcium levels can all affect gene expression. As you now know, these are

<!-- chunk -->

### all directly related to mitochondrial function. However, given that these are

<!-- chunk -->

### also markers of general cellular health and function, no one thought too

<!-- chunk -->

### much of it. They certainly didn’t think of it as a way for mitochondria to

<!-- chunk -->

### directly control the expression of genes in the nucleus.

<!-- chunk -->

### In 2002, it was discovered that mitochondria are required for the

<!-- chunk -->

### transport of an important epigenetic factor, nuclear protein histone H1.


<!-- chunk -->

### This protein helps regulate gene expression and is transported from the

<!-- chunk -->

### cytoplasm to the nucleus, a process that requires ATP. Researchers

<!-- chunk -->

### discovered, however, that ATP alone isn’t enough. Mitochondria must be

<!-- chunk -->

### present in order for this transfer to occur. Without mitochondria, this

<!-- chunk -->

### transfer doesn’t happen.

<!-- chunk -->

### In 2013, it was discovered that mitochondrial ROS directly inactivate an

<!-- chunk -->

### enzyme called histone demethylase Rph1p, which regulates epigenetic gene

<!-- chunk -->

### expression in the cell nucleus.


<!-- chunk -->

### This process was found to play a role in

<!-- chunk -->

### extending lifespan in yeast and is thought to possibly play a role in humans

<!-- chunk -->

### as well.

<!-- chunk -->

### In 2018, two additional studies demonstrated even more of a role for

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondria in gene expression. The first was a report by molecular

<!-- chunk -->

### biologist Maria Dafne Cardamone and colleagues showing that a protein,

<!-- chunk -->

### GPS2, is released by mitochondria in response to metabolic stress.


<!-- chunk -->

### Metabolic stress can be caused by a lot of different things, but starvation is

<!-- chunk -->

### a clear example. After GPS2 is released by mitochondria, it enters the cell

<!-- chunk -->

### nucleus and regulates a number of genes related to mitochondrial

<!-- chunk -->

### biogenesis and metabolic stress.

<!-- chunk -->

### Another group of researchers, Dr. Kyung Hwa Kim and colleagues,

<!-- chunk -->

### found another mitochondrial protein, MOTS-c, that is coded for by

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondrial DNA and plays a role in gene expression.


<!-- chunk -->

### This was very

<!-- chunk -->

### unexpected. Up until about twenty years ago, everyone assumed that

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondrial DNA was just about machinery needed for ATP production.

<!-- chunk -->

### MOTS-c gets produced in response to metabolic stress as well. After

<!-- chunk -->

### MOTS-c is produced in the mitochondria, it makes its way into the nucleus

<!-- chunk -->

### and binds to the nuclear DNA. This results in the regulation of a broad

<!-- chunk -->

### range of genes—ones related to stress responses, metabolism, and

<!-- chunk -->

### antioxidant effects.

<!-- chunk -->

### Finally, and most spectacularly, Dr. Martin Picard and colleagues

<!-- chunk -->

### experimentally manipulated the number of mitochondria with mutations in

<!-- chunk -->

### cells and found that as they increased the number of dysfunctional

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondria, more epigenetic problems and changes occurred.


<!-- chunk -->

### impact was on almost all of the genes expressed in the cells. Ultimately, in

<!-- chunk -->

### situations in which almost all the mitochondria were dysfunctional, the cells

<!-- chunk -->

### died. This study provided evidence that mitochondria are not just involved

<!-- chunk -->

### in the expression of genes related to energy metabolism, but possibly in the

<!-- chunk -->

### expression of all genes.

<!-- chunk -->

## Mitochondria Can Multiply

<!-- chunk -->

### Under the right circumstances, cells will make more mitochondria—a

<!-- chunk -->

### process called mitochondrial biogenesis. Some cells end up with a lot of

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondria. These cells can produce more energy and function at a higher

<!-- chunk -->

### capacity. It is widely believed that the greater the number of healthy

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondria in a cell, the healthier the cell. We know that the number of

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondria decreases with age. We also know that the number of

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondria decreases with many diseases. People who are considered the

<!-- chunk -->

### “fittest” among us—athletic champions—have more mitochondria than

<!-- chunk -->

### most, and their mitochondria appear to be healthier.

<!-- chunk -->

## Mitochondria Are Involved in Cell Growth and Differentiation

<!-- chunk -->

### Cell growth and differentiation is a complicated process during which a

<!-- chunk -->

### generic stem cell becomes a specialized cell. Differentiation means that the

<!-- chunk -->

### cells become different from each other and take on specialized roles. Some

<!-- chunk -->

### become heart cells. Others become brain cells. Within the brain, different

<!-- chunk -->

### cells take on varying roles. Brain cells change throughout life. Some form

<!-- chunk -->

### new synapses. Some prune unnecessary parts. Some grow and expand when

<!-- chunk -->

### needed. This is neuroplasticity.

<!-- chunk -->

### This process of growth and differentiation involves activation of

<!-- chunk -->

### specific genes in the right cells at the right times. It also involves many

<!-- chunk -->

### signaling pathways. Lastly, it involves the production of building blocks for

<!-- chunk -->

### new cells and new cell parts, balanced with energy needs.

<!-- chunk -->

### It has long been known that mitochondria are essential to cell growth

<!-- chunk -->

### and differentiation. Most researchers assumed it was simply a matter of

<!-- chunk -->

### their powerhouse function since cell growth and differentiation require

<!-- chunk -->

### energy. Recent research, however, strongly suggests a much more active

<!-- chunk -->

### role. Their regulation of calcium levels and other signaling pathways are

<!-- chunk -->

### essential to this process.


<!-- chunk -->

### Their fusion with each other appears to send

<!-- chunk -->

### signals that activate genes in the nucleus. When mitochondria are prevented

<!-- chunk -->

### from fusing with each other, the cells don’t develop correctly.


<!-- chunk -->

### Other

<!-- chunk -->

### research has shown that mitochondrial growth and maturation is essential to

<!-- chunk -->

### proper cell differentiation.


<!-- chunk -->

### Still other research has shown a direct and

<!-- chunk -->

### essential role of

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondria in the development of brain cells.


<!-- chunk -->

### The bottom line is that

<!-- chunk -->

### cells don’t develop normally when mitochondria aren’t functioning

<!-- chunk -->

### properly.

<!-- chunk -->

## Mitochondria Help Maintain Existing Cells

<!-- chunk -->

### In the previous chapter, I discussed autophagy and cell maintenance. It

<!-- chunk -->

### turns out that mitochondria are directly involved in this process, too. They

<!-- chunk -->

### generate many of the signals, such as ROS and other metabolic factors, that

<!-- chunk -->

### play a key role in autophagy. They also interact with other parts of the cell,

<!-- chunk -->

### such as lysosomes, that are involved in the process. Maintenance work

<!-- chunk -->

### takes energy and building blocks as well, and mitochondria are there to

<!-- chunk -->

### provide both.

<!-- chunk -->

### Mitochondria appear to be in a complicated feedback cycle with

<!-- chunk -->

### autophagy, as dysfunctional mitochondria can be removed and replaced

<!-- chunk -->

### with healthy mitochondria in a process known as mitophagy. Mitochondria

<!-- chunk -->

### can be beneficiaries of autophagy, but they also play a role in stimulating

<!-- chunk -->

### autophagy more broadly for the entire cell.


<!-- chunk -->

## Mitochondria Eliminate Old and Damaged Cells

<!-- chunk -->

### Cells die every day. There are two well-known types of cell death—

<!-- chunk -->

### necrosis and apoptosis. Necrosis occurs when a cell is abruptly killed, such

<!-- chunk -->

### as a heart cell dying during a heart attack. Necrosis is a bad thing.

<!-- chunk -->

### Apoptosis occurs when cells get old or damaged. Apoptosis is a planned

<!-- chunk -->

### process often referred to as programmed cell death—the signal to die

<!-- chunk -->

### actually comes from the cell itself. Overall, apoptosis is seen as an

<!-- chunk -->

### extraordinarily good thing for human health and survival. It allows old cells

<!-- chunk -->

### to be replaced by new ones. It eliminates damaged cells that might turn into

<!-- chunk -->

### cancer. Every day, about ten billion cells in the human body die and are

<!-- chunk -->

### replaced by new ones.


<!-- chunk -->

### It was once thought that genes in the nucleus controlled apoptosis. We

<!-- chunk -->

### now know that’s not true. It’s mitochondria. When mitochondria experience

<!-- chunk -->

### high levels of stress and accumulate large amounts of ROS, they begin to

<!-- chunk -->

### degrade. When this happens, they release a protein called cytochrome c,

<!-- chunk -->

### which then activates what are called “killing enzymes”—the caspases.

<!-- chunk -->

### These enzymes degrade everything in the cell until it dies. Many of the cell

<!-- chunk -->

### parts get recycled.

<!-- chunk -->

### Autophagy and apoptosis are somewhat related, but they are different

<!-- chunk -->

### processes. Autophagy is usually about repairing and replacing parts within a

<!-- chunk -->

### cell, but the cell usually remains alive. Apoptosis is the death of an entire

<!-- chunk -->

### cell. Nonetheless, they are both required for health and longevity, and

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondria play a role in both.

<!-- chunk -->

### There are even more types of cell death, beyond the scope of this book.

<!-- chunk -->

### Nonetheless, one review was able to link all of them to the functions of


# Putting It All Together

<!-- chunk -->

### Change is hard. Models, practices, and conceptual frameworks are difficult

<!-- chunk -->

### to shift. But what if our ideas about the control of cells have been all

<!-- chunk -->

### wrong?

<!-- chunk -->

### If we go back to our automotive analogy, I suggested that each cell was

<!-- chunk -->

### like a car in the congested traffic of a large city. If we look inside that car,

<!-- chunk -->

### there are many drivers—all the mitochondria. It might be easier to change

<!-- chunk -->

### the metaphor at this level and think of the inside of each cell as a factory.

<!-- chunk -->

### The factory receives supplies, such as glucose, amino acids, and oxygen,

<!-- chunk -->

### and performs a function. Some make neurotransmitters. Others make

<!-- chunk -->

### hormones. Some are muscle cells and cause the body to move.

<!-- chunk -->

### Mitochondria are the workers inside those factories (mitochondria as

<!-- chunk -->

### workers is an analogy Nick Lane used in his book as well).


<!-- chunk -->

### There are

<!-- chunk -->

### many different roles and tasks for them. Some mitochondria help with the

<!-- chunk -->

### production and release of hormones or neurotransmitters. Others serve as

<!-- chunk -->

### janitors—helping to clean up ROS and other debris. Some help

<!-- chunk -->

### communicate with the nucleus—sending signals to turn genes on or off.

<!-- chunk -->

### They are the regulators of calcium, ROS, and other important signals in

<!-- chunk -->

### cells. They work together and communicate with each other—they fuse

<!-- chunk -->

### with each other, move around cells, and communicate with mitochondria in

<!-- chunk -->

### other cells through hormones, such as cortisol, and through other

<!-- chunk -->

### mechanisms, such as mitochondrially derived peptides. And of course, they

<!-- chunk -->

### provide most of the power—or ATP—to make the factory work. When

<!-- chunk -->

### workers in one cell aren’t doing well, they not only affect

<!-- chunk -->

### the rest of the workforce in that cell, but they can also affect the workers in

<!-- chunk -->

### other cells.

<!-- chunk -->

### Over the past twenty years, a lot of the new evidence on the role of

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondria in the cell has been shocking and unexpected. Almost no one

<!-- chunk -->

### thought that mitochondria could control the regulation of genes in the

<!-- chunk -->

### nucleus—both on a daily basis and during cell growth and differentiation.

<!-- chunk -->

### Their interaction with and regulation of other organelles, such as the

<!-- chunk -->

### endoplasmic reticulum and lysosomes, were also surprising. They were

<!-- chunk -->

### usually thought of as relatively insignificant, and very small, ATP factories.

<!-- chunk -->

### They were sometimes described as “little batteries.” Many researchers still

<!-- chunk -->

### see them this way.

<!-- chunk -->

### For centuries, researchers have been trying to figure out how cells work.

<!-- chunk -->

### Up until recently, they have focused primarily on all the big parts of cells

<!-- chunk -->

### and largely ignored the tiny little mitochondria. Many still think the

<!-- chunk -->

### nucleus, with its coveted human genome, is the control center. Others think

<!-- chunk -->

### that it’s all about the outside cell membrane and the different receptors that

<!-- chunk -->

### are embedded into it. Different neurotransmitters or hormones make cells

<!-- chunk -->

### do things. What if both takes have some truth in them, but the real story is

<!-- chunk -->

### about the mitochondria—the workers? Given all the roles that mitochondria

<!-- chunk -->

### play in so many different aspects of cell function, is it possible that they are

<!-- chunk -->

### the real answer to understanding how cells work? What if all the different

<!-- chunk -->

### organelles in a cell are just big machines or storage sites to be used by

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondria to do different tasks in a cell? Could the nucleus simply be a

<!-- chunk -->

### large storage center for the DNA, the blueprints of the cell, to be used when

<!-- chunk -->

### called for by the mitochondria? Could the other organelles be large

<!-- chunk -->

### machines, ones that make proteins (ribosomes) or waste disposal machines

<!-- chunk -->

### (lysosomes), to be used by the mitochondria for these different purposes?

<!-- chunk -->

### After all, mitochondria are the only organelles that move around the cell,

<!-- chunk -->

### interact with each other, and interact with all the other organelles.

<!-- chunk -->

### Mitochondria were in the cell first. They were the first organelle. They were

<!-- chunk -->

### also an independent living organism at one point. In many ways, the

<!-- chunk -->

### evidence can’t rule this out.

<!-- chunk -->

### To be clear, I’m not suggesting that mitochondria have brains and make

<!-- chunk -->

### independent decisions on all of these functions. Instead, I am suggesting

<!-- chunk -->

### that they are like little robotic workers, doing what they are programmed to

<!-- chunk -->

### do. They are longtime, loyal servants to human cells. But like so many

<!-- chunk -->

### unappreciated servants and workers, maybe they deserve a little more

<!-- chunk -->

### respect and recognition for all that they do.

<!-- chunk -->

### Whether you like this analogy or not, even if you want to continue to

<!-- chunk -->

### think of mitochondria as nothing more than little batteries, one thing is

<!-- chunk -->

### abundantly clear and uncontroversial—when mitochondria aren’t working,

<!-- chunk -->

### neither is the human body or brain.


# A Brain Energy Imbalance

<!-- chunk -->

### n the previous chapter, I reviewed mitochondria in all their glory. Their

<!-- chunk -->

### function affects every cell in the human body. Their involvement in all

<!-- chunk -->

### aspects of cell function, neurotransmitters, hormones, inflammation,

<!-- chunk -->

### immune system function, regulation of gene expression, development, and

<!-- chunk -->

### the maintenance and health of cells results in widespread effects throughout

<!-- chunk -->

### the body and the brain. They are the drivers of cells and metabolism. They

<!-- chunk -->

### are the workforce of the human body.

<!-- chunk -->

### But the question remains: Do we have evidence that metabolic problems

<!-- chunk -->

### are related to mental disorders? And how?

<!-- chunk -->

### Yes! There is an abundance of evidence that links problems with

<!-- chunk -->

### metabolism to mental disorders.

<!-- chunk -->

### As I discussed in Chapter Five, physicians and researchers have known

<!-- chunk -->

### for well over a century that mental disorders appear to be linked to

<!-- chunk -->

### metabolic disorders, such as diabetes. Direct evidence of metabolic

<!-- chunk -->

### abnormalities in people with mental disorders, even those who don’t yet

<!-- chunk -->

### have obesity, diabetes, or cardiovascular disease, dates back to at least the

<!-- chunk -->

### 1950s. Among the abnormalities found in markers of metabolism:

<!-- chunk -->

### differences in levels of

<!-- chunk -->

### ATP, redox markers (the balance between oxidants, such as ROS, and

<!-- chunk -->

### antioxidants), hormones, neurotransmitters, and lactate (a marker of

<!-- chunk -->

### metabolic stress). In the 1980s, it was discovered that infusing lactate into

<!-- chunk -->

### the vein of a person with panic disorder would often precipitate an

<!-- chunk -->

### immediate panic attack.


<!-- chunk -->

### As I already discussed, cortisol dysregulation also

<!-- chunk -->

### seems to play a role, at least in some people, and this is a metabolic

<!-- chunk -->

### hormone.

<!-- chunk -->

### Neuroimaging studies have provided an overwhelming amount of

<!-- chunk -->

### evidence for metabolic differences in the brains of people with mental

<!-- chunk -->

### disorders. Functional magnetic resonance imaging (fMRI) and near-infrared

<!-- chunk -->

### spectroscopic imaging (NIRSI) can measure localized changes in cerebral

<!-- chunk -->

### blood flow related to neural activity, which is an indirect marker of

<!-- chunk -->

### metabolism and brain activity. Positron emission tomography (PET), blood-

<!-- chunk -->

### oxygen-level-dependent imaging (BOLD), and single-photon emission

<!-- chunk -->

### computed tomography (SPECT) all measure some metric of metabolism—

<!-- chunk -->

### levels of glucose, oxygen, or a radioactive molecule that researchers inject

<!-- chunk -->

### into a person’s vein. All these imaging studies are measuring metabolism in

<!-- chunk -->

### the brain because metabolism is a marker of brain activity. When neurons

<!-- chunk -->

### are active, they use more energy. When they are resting, they use less.

<!-- chunk -->

### These studies have provided us with a plethora of data demonstrating

<!-- chunk -->

### differences in the brains of people with mental disorders compared to the

<!-- chunk -->

### brains of healthy controls. Some brain regions are overactive, while other

<!-- chunk -->

### brain regions are underactive. More recently, researchers have turned to

<!-- chunk -->

### functional brain connectivity studies, which look at the interactions of two

<!-- chunk -->

### or more brain regions in attempts to determine which of those regions

<!-- chunk -->

### communicate with each other to perform specific tasks. However, even with

<!-- chunk -->

### all this research, heterogeneity and inconsistent findings have been the

<!-- chunk -->

### name of the game. If you don’t believe me, the American Psychiatric

<!-- chunk -->

### Association published a “Resource Document on Neuroimaging” in 2018 in

<!-- chunk -->

### which they concluded: “There are currently no brain imaging biomarkers

<!-- chunk -->

### that are clinically useful for any diagnostic category in psychiatry.”


<!-- chunk -->

### The researchers doing this neuroimaging work, however, have known

<!-- chunk -->

### for decades that there are differences in metabolism in the brains of people

<!-- chunk -->

### with mental disorders. At first glance, they may think that the theory of

<!-- chunk -->

### brain energy offers nothing new. “Obviously mental disorders are related to

<!-- chunk -->

### metabolism! We’ve known that all along! Metabolism is everything in

<!-- chunk -->

### biology. What’s new here?”

<!-- chunk -->

### As I hope you’ll come to understand, there is something new here. And

<!-- chunk -->

### not just new, but revolutionary. While these researchers have been lost in

<!-- chunk -->

### the overwhelming complexity of metabolism and how the brain works,

<!-- chunk -->

### trying to figure out what is making some brain regions overactive and other

<!-- chunk -->

### brain regions underactive, they have failed to see the big picture of

<!-- chunk -->

### metabolism. Most importantly, they have failed to see the role of

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondria in all of it. By stepping back and looking at the bigger picture

<!-- chunk -->

### (even if that bigger picture is playing out on a microscopic level), we can

<!-- chunk -->

### find new ways to understand what’s happening with metabolism and mental

<!-- chunk -->

### health and see new ways to address these problems.

# Mitochondrial Dysfunction and Mental Health

<!-- chunk -->

### But do we have evidence that mitochondria aren’t functioning properly in

<!-- chunk -->

### people with mental disorders?

<!-- chunk -->

### Yes! We now have an abundance of evidence.

<!-- chunk -->

### Over the past several decades, it has become clear that mitochondria

<!-- chunk -->

### play a much larger role in human health than ever imagined. When

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondria don’t function properly, the human body doesn’t function

<!-- chunk -->

### properly. Mitochondrial dysfunction is the term most frequently used to

<!-- chunk -->

### describe impairment in mitochondrial function. The diseases and illnesses

<!-- chunk -->

### that have been associated with mitochondrial dysfunction are widespread,

<!-- chunk -->

### and the list includes almost all of the psychiatric disorders. It also includes

<!-- chunk -->

### the metabolic and neurological disorders that I have discussed—obesity,

<!-- chunk -->

### diabetes, cardiovascular disease, Alzheimer’s disease, and epilepsy. In

<!-- chunk -->

### reality, it includes even more disorders—many cancers and Parkinson’s

<!-- chunk -->

### disease are among them. I won’t be able to go into detail on all these

<!-- chunk -->

### different disorders. However, the framework that I am creating will apply to

<!-- chunk -->

### them as well.

<!-- chunk -->

### The specific psychiatric disorders in which mitochondrial dysfunction has

<!-- chunk -->

### been identified include the following: schizophrenia, schizoaffective

<!-- chunk -->

### disorder, bipolar disorder, major depression, autism, anxiety disorders,

<!-- chunk -->

### obsessive-compulsive disorder, posttraumatic stress disorder, attention

<!-- chunk -->

### deficit/hyperactivity disorder, anorexia nervosa, alcohol use disorder (aka

<!-- chunk -->

### alcoholism), marijuana use disorder, opioid use disorder, and borderline

<!-- chunk -->

### personality disorder. Dementia and delirium, often thought of as

<!-- chunk -->

### neurological illnesses, are also included.

<!-- chunk -->

### This list does not include every psychiatric diagnosis in DSM-5.

<!-- chunk -->

### However, that’s not necessarily because mitochondrial dysfunction does not

<!-- chunk -->

### exist in the other diagnoses; research just hasn’t been done on the other

<!-- chunk -->

### diagnoses yet. Nonetheless, this list is certainly broad enough to state that

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondrial dysfunction has been found in a wide range of diagnoses that

<!-- chunk -->

### include pretty much every symptom found in psychiatry.

<!-- chunk -->

### If all this evidence has existed for a while, why hasn’t anyone else

<!-- chunk -->

### suggested that mitochondrial dysfunction is the common pathway to

<!-- chunk -->

### metabolic or mental disorders?

<!-- chunk -->

### Well . . . they have! To most people reading this book, this may seem

<!-- chunk -->

### like new information. However, this book is not the first to assert the

<!-- chunk -->

### importance of mitochondria in human health and disease.

<!-- chunk -->

### Dr. Raymond Pearl published a book on the rate of living theory in

<!-- chunk -->

### 1928, in which he argued that longevity and diseases of aging, which

<!-- chunk -->

### include most of the metabolic diseases, are due to metabolic rate. In 1954,

<!-- chunk -->

### Dr. Denham Harman proposed the free radical theory of aging, which

<!-- chunk -->

### focused on ROS as the cause of age-related diseases. In 1972, he further

<!-- chunk -->

### developed this theory and proposed the mitochondrial theory of aging,

<!-- chunk -->

### which recognized the central role of mitochondria in the production of

<!-- chunk -->

### ROS. In recent years, there has been an explosion of research on

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondria and their relationship to obesity, diabetes, cardiovascular

<!-- chunk -->

### disease, and aging itself, with tens of thousands of research articles

<!-- chunk -->

### published in the medical literature.

<!-- chunk -->

### The psychiatric literature is filled with articles from respected scientists

<!-- chunk -->

### highlighting the role of mitochondria in mental disorders. A 2021 medical

<!-- chunk -->

### literature search came up with more than four hundred articles relating to

<!-- chunk -->

### schizophrenia and bipolar disorder, more than three thousand relating to

<!-- chunk -->

### depression, more than four thousand relating to Alzheimer’s disease, and

<!-- chunk -->

### more than eleven thousand relating to alcohol use. Some of this pioneering

<!-- chunk -->

### research has been close to home for me, coming from respected and

<!-- chunk -->

### internationally renowned colleagues, such as Professors Bruce Cohen and

<!-- chunk -->

### Dost Öngür at McLean Hospital and Harvard Medical School, where I have

<!-- chunk -->

### worked for more than twenty-five years.

<!-- chunk -->

### In 2017, Dr. Douglas Wallace, the founder of the field of mitochondrial

<!-- chunk -->

### genetics, published an article in JAMA Psychiatry, one of the leading

<!-- chunk -->

### psychiatric journals, audaciously claiming (as I do in this book) that all

<!-- chunk -->

### psychiatric disorders are the result of mitochondrial dysfunction.


<!-- chunk -->

### Being a

<!-- chunk -->

### geneticist, Wallace focused on mitochondrial genes. They mutate often due

<!-- chunk -->

### to ROS and the lack of protection of mitochondrial DNA. Wallace argued

<!-- chunk -->

### that the brain is the organ that will be most affected by a problem with

<!-- chunk -->

### energy production by mitochondria. He argued that different parts of the

<!-- chunk -->

### brain might fail first—likely because they are more sensitive to energy

<!-- chunk -->

### deprivation than others. This makes sense, given that most machines do

<!-- chunk -->

### have “weakest links.” The brain is likely the same. So, a small amount of

<!-- chunk -->

### energy deprivation might result in ADHD or depression and a larger

<!-- chunk -->

### amount of energy deprivation might result in other disorders, such as

<!-- chunk -->

### schizophrenia.

<!-- chunk -->

### The rebuttal was swift. Dr. Tamas Kozicz and colleagues argued that

<!-- chunk -->

### although people like “simple” explanations, psychiatric disorders do not

<!-- chunk -->

### lend themselves to such a simple explanation.


<!-- chunk -->

### They acknowledged that

<!-- chunk -->

### “suboptimal mitochondrial function” does appear to play a role in most

<!-- chunk -->

### psychiatric disorders. However, focusing solely on mitochondrial energy

<!-- chunk -->

### production cannot account for the diversity of symptoms that we see in the

<!-- chunk -->

### billions of people with mental disorders. Furthermore, it can’t even account

<!-- chunk -->

### for the diversity of symptoms that we see in people with rare genetic

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondrial diseases. Even people with the same mitochondrial genetic

<!-- chunk -->

### mutation can have different symptoms. They argued that mental disorders

<!-- chunk -->

### are far too complex and different from person to person to be explained by a

<!-- chunk -->

### single factor.

<!-- chunk -->

### What these researchers failed to consider is how many other roles

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondria play in cells apart from the production of energy. They also

<!-- chunk -->

### failed

<!-- chunk -->

### to recognize just how many different factors affect the function and health

<!-- chunk -->

### of mitochondria. When mitochondria don’t function properly, neither does

<!-- chunk -->

### the brain. When brain metabolism is not properly controlled, the brain

<!-- chunk -->

### doesn’t work properly. Symptoms can be highly variable, but mitochondrial

<!-- chunk -->

### dysfunction is both necessary and sufficient to explain all the symptoms of

<!-- chunk -->

### mental illness.

# Defining This Root Cause

<!-- chunk -->

### As I just discussed in the previous chapter, mitochondria do a lot of

<!-- chunk -->

### different things. Defining what dysfunction means is difficult and has been

<!-- chunk -->

### a challenge to scientists; it can mean very different things in different

<!-- chunk -->

### research studies.

<!-- chunk -->

### The same can be said for cars. If a car is “dysfunctional,” what does that

<!-- chunk -->

### mean? It could mean that the engine sputters when traveling down the

<!-- chunk -->

### highway. It could mean that a tire is flat, and the car can’t move along the

<!-- chunk -->

### road as easily. It could mean that the lights and the turn signals aren’t

<!-- chunk -->

### working. Those are all different problems with a car. They all result from

<!-- chunk -->

### different causes. But here’s an important point: Regardless of what is wrong

<!-- chunk -->

### with the car, if it’s on the highway with any of those problems, it will affect

<!-- chunk -->

### the other cars on the road. It will be more likely to slow down traffic or

<!-- chunk -->

### cause an accident. Traffic can slow or completely stop. The highway could

<!-- chunk -->

### “stop working” due to one car. In reality, the overwhelming majority of car

<!-- chunk -->

### accidents aren’t about the cars themselves but about the drivers of the cars.

<!-- chunk -->

### Drivers can also be “dysfunctional.” They can be on their cell phones. They

<!-- chunk -->

### can fall asleep at the wheel. They can be drunk. They can be high. They can

<!-- chunk -->

### have road rage. Regardless of the cause of the dysfunction, whether it’s the

<!-- chunk -->

### car or the driver, these wayward cars and drivers affect traffic in similar

<!-- chunk -->

### ways.

<!-- chunk -->

### Mitochondrial dysfunction is the same. It can be caused by many

<!-- chunk -->

### different things and can result in different problems for the mitochondria

<!-- chunk -->

### and for the cells in which they reside.


<!-- chunk -->

### Measuring the function of

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondria is difficult. Remember how tiny they are? There are usually

<!-- chunk -->

### hundreds and sometimes thousands of them in one cell. Cells are pretty tiny

<!-- chunk -->

### on their own.

<!-- chunk -->

### Mitochondrial dysfunction can stem from problems with the mitochondria

<!-- chunk -->

### themselves. This includes genetic mutations or a shortage of mitochondria

<!-- chunk -->

### in the cell. As I mentioned, mitochondria have their own DNA. It is not

<!-- chunk -->

### protected in the same way that the human genome is protected; therefore,

<!-- chunk -->

### it’s prone to mutations. Mitochondria are producing ROS, and if they

<!-- chunk -->

### produce too much of it, it can damage the mitochondrial DNA or other

<!-- chunk -->

### parts. This can lead to defective mitochondria. When mitochondria are

<!-- chunk -->

### defective, they are supposed to be disposed of and recycled, with new ones

<!-- chunk -->

### taking their place. If this doesn’t happen, a cell can be left with a workforce

<!-- chunk -->

### shortage. It’s well established that the number of mitochondria in our cells

<!-- chunk -->

### decreases as we get older, resulting in less metabolic capacity for cells.

<!-- chunk -->

### When the workforce is decreased, whether through aging or

<!-- chunk -->

### malfunctioning mitochondria, productivity goes down. As the mitochondria

<!-- chunk -->

### continue to decline, the cell usually dies. This leads to organs and tissues

<!-- chunk -->

### shrinking. As the cells die off, the organs get weaker and more vulnerable

<!-- chunk -->

### to stress. Brains shrink. People lose muscle mass. The heart isn’t as strong.

<!-- chunk -->

### This phenomenon is also seen in people with chronic mental disorders. As I

<!-- chunk -->

### already mentioned, accelerated aging has been found in people with all

<!-- chunk -->

### mental disorders.

<!-- chunk -->

### The more important cause of mitochondrial impairment is one that I’ll

<!-- chunk -->

### call mitochondrial dysregulation. Many of the factors that affect

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondrial function come from outside the cell. They include

<!-- chunk -->

### neurotransmitters, hormones, peptides, inflammatory signals, and even

<!-- chunk -->

### something like alcohol. Yep! Alcohol affects the function of mitochondria. I

<!-- chunk -->

### refer to this as dysregulation, as opposed to dysfunction, because in some

<!-- chunk -->

### cases, the mitochondria were functioning just fine, but their environment

<!-- chunk -->

### became hostile quickly and caused impairment—similar to people doing the

<!-- chunk -->

### best they can when they are highly stressed.

<!-- chunk -->

### Defining which mitochondrial functions to focus on is critically

<!-- chunk -->

### important and varies in different studies. Some of these studies are done

<!-- chunk -->

### while mitochondria are still in living cells (in vivo), and others are done

<!-- chunk -->

### with mitochondria laid bare in a laboratory dish (in vitro).


<!-- chunk -->

### Many researchers have focused on ATP production. They can measure the

<!-- chunk -->

### amount of ATP relative to ADP in the cell cytoplasm and make assumptions

<!-- chunk -->

### about how well the mitochondria are functioning. The implications of this

<!-- chunk -->

### research are straightforward; ATP provides energy for the cell to work. If

<!-- chunk -->

### the levels are reduced, the cell won’t work as well. The levels of ATP to

<!-- chunk -->

### ADP are also important signals within cells. This ratio affects numerous

<!-- chunk -->

### aspects of cell function, including gene expression. Reduced levels of ATP

<!-- chunk -->

### have been found in a wide variety of disorders, including schizophrenia,

<!-- chunk -->

### bipolar disorder, major depression, alcoholism, PTSD, autism, OCD,

<!-- chunk -->

### Alzheimer’s disease, epilepsy, cardiovascular disease, type 2 diabetes, and

<!-- chunk -->

### obesity. Even though most people think of obesity as a surplus of energy,

<!-- chunk -->

### many cells in the bodies and brains of people with obesity are actually

<!-- chunk -->

### deprived of ATP, due to mitochondrial dysfunction.


<!-- chunk -->

### Other researchers have focused on oxidative stress. Remember, this is a

<!-- chunk -->

### term used to describe the buildup of ROS. Recall that mitochondria create

<!-- chunk -->

### ROS, but they also help detoxify them through antioxidants. When

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondria aren’t working properly, ROS build up and can cause damage

<!-- chunk -->

### to the cell in general, but more often to the mitochondria themselves,

<!-- chunk -->

### leading to a vicious feedback cycle. Numerous studies have found higher

<!-- chunk -->

### levels of oxidative stress in essentially all of the metabolic, neurological,

<!-- chunk -->

### and mental disorders that I have been discussing. This has been linked to

<!-- chunk -->

### cell damage and accelerated aging.

<!-- chunk -->

### There have been three major shortfalls in the research on the role of

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondria in health and disease to date:

<!-- chunk -->

## 1.Focus on just one function. Most studies have focused on only one

<!-- chunk -->

### function or aspect of mitochondria. They often fail to consider all the

<!-- chunk -->

### diverse functions. Some mitochondrial functions can be normal,

<!-- chunk -->

### while others are abnormal. Additionally, some functions can affect

<!-- chunk -->

### other functions. For example, studies that have looked at

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondrial ATP production often view this as the primary, and

<!-- chunk -->

### sometimes only, role of mitochondria. Any adverse outcomes that

<!-- chunk -->

### they see in the cells they are studying are attributed to this

<!-- chunk -->

### impaired ATP production. In reality, mitochondria that aren’t

<!-- chunk -->

### producing enough ATP may also have trouble fusing with each other,

<!-- chunk -->

### or might be leaking large amounts of ROS, or might have trouble

<!-- chunk -->

### managing calcium levels in the cell. These functions may be more

<!-- chunk -->

### important to the cell deficits observed by the researchers, even if

<!-- chunk -->

### those functions weren’t measured. In some cases, ATP production

<!-- chunk -->

### might be normal, while these other functions are abnormal, and the

<!-- chunk -->

### researchers might conclude that the mitochondria were functioning

<!-- chunk -->

### just fine when, in fact, they weren’t.

<!-- chunk -->

## 2.Differences between cells. Mitochondria are affected by numerous

<!-- chunk -->

### factors, both within the cell and from outside the cell. The number

<!-- chunk -->

### and health of mitochondria are not equally distributed among all cells

<!-- chunk -->

### in the body and brain. Some cells can have perfectly healthy

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondria in abundance, while other cells can have defective or

<!-- chunk -->

### insufficient mitochondria. Researchers must study specific cells to

<!-- chunk -->

### determine whether mitochondria in those cells are playing a role in

<!-- chunk -->

### an illness. Studying a healthy immune cell may not offer any

<!-- chunk -->

### information about what’s happening in a malfunctioning brain cell.

<!-- chunk -->

## 3.The role of feedback loops. The question of which comes first, the

<!-- chunk -->

### chicken or the egg, has led many researchers astray. Does

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondrial dysfunction cause illness? Or does illness cause

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondrial dysfunction? Are mitochondria simply innocent

<!-- chunk -->

### bystanders and the victims of some other destructive process?

<!-- chunk -->

### When thinking about causes and consequences, things can get

<!-- chunk -->

### confusing. There are many causes of mitochondrial dysfunction.

<!-- chunk -->

### There are also many consequences. I’ll get to this soon enough.

<!-- chunk -->

### What’s confusing, though, is that causes can lead to consequences,

<!-- chunk -->

### but consequences can lead to causes. When we see this type of a

<!-- chunk -->

### pattern, we need to think about feedback loops. When it comes to

<!-- chunk -->

### metabolism and mitochondria, almost all things are regulated in a

<!-- chunk -->

### feedback loop.

<!-- chunk -->

### One example is in Alzheimer’s disease research. We know that

<!-- chunk -->

### the abnormal protein beta-amyloid accumulates in the brains of

<!-- chunk -->

### people with Alzheimer’s disease. This protein has been the primary

<!-- chunk -->

### target of research. We know the more beta-amyloid that is present,

<!-- chunk -->

### the more likely it is that someone will develop Alzheimer’s disease.

<!-- chunk -->

### We also know it is toxic to mitochondria and causes mitochondrial

<!-- chunk -->

### dysfunction.


<!-- chunk -->

### Many researchers have stopped there. They feel they

<!-- chunk -->

### have enough evidence that mitochondria are innocent bystanders of

<!-- chunk -->

### this destructive protein. What causes beta-amyloid to accumulate?

<!-- chunk -->

### They don’t know. They are still looking for that cause. What they

<!-- chunk -->

### have missed, however, is that mitochondrial dysfunction might very

<!-- chunk -->

### well be the cause of the accumulation of beta-amyloid itself. We

<!-- chunk -->

### have evidence that mitochondrial dysfunction begins even before

<!-- chunk -->

### beta-amyloid starts accumulating.


<!-- chunk -->

### It’s possible that it’s a positive

<!-- chunk -->

### feedback loop. Mitochondrial dysfunction causes a maintenance

<!-- chunk -->

### problem in cells. This results in the accumulation of beta-amyloid (a

<!-- chunk -->

### protein that should be getting disposed of and recycled). This beta-

<!-- chunk -->

### amyloid accumulation then makes mitochondrial dysfunction even

<!-- chunk -->

### worse. This feedback loop results in the downward spiral that we

<!-- chunk -->

### call Alzheimer’s disease.

<!-- chunk -->

### Fortunately, research in the last twenty years has broadened

<!-- chunk -->

### substantially regarding the scope of mitochondrial function—all the

<!-- chunk -->

### different roles mentioned in the prior Chapter are from different studies

<!-- chunk -->

### looking at the diverse functions that mitochondria perform.

<!-- chunk -->

### Mitochondrial dysfunction or dysregulation ties everything that we

<!-- chunk -->

### already know about mental and metabolic disorders together in a coherent

<!-- chunk -->

### way. Mitochondria are the common pathway. For the scientists or purists, a

<!-- chunk -->

### better name for this theory might be the metabolic and mitochondrial theory

<!-- chunk -->

### of mental illness, as this would include the myriad tasks of mitochondria

<!-- chunk -->

### and how mitochondria can be influenced by all things that affect

<!-- chunk -->

### metabolism. (I’ll get to these in Part Three of the book.) However, because

<!-- chunk -->

### energy dysregulation does appear to account for the majority of symptoms

<!-- chunk -->

### of mental illness, the short and catchy phrase “brain energy theory” works

<!-- chunk -->

### for me.

# How Mitochondrial Malfunction Leads to Mental

# Illness

<!-- chunk -->

### I will now show you how mitochondrial dysfunction can result in all the

<!-- chunk -->

### brain changes and symptoms that we see in mental disorders. Mitochondria

<!-- chunk -->

### affect the development of the brain, the expression of different genes,

<!-- chunk -->

### synapse formation and destruction, and brain activity. They affect structural

<!-- chunk -->

### problems and functional problems. They tie together so much of what we

<!-- chunk -->

### already know and put it into one common pathway. Let’s walk through

<!-- chunk -->

### some of the science.

<!-- chunk -->

### Recall that in order to understand much of the existing neuroscience on

<!-- chunk -->

### mental disorders, I need to explain why some brain areas might be

<!-- chunk -->

### overactive, while others might be underactive, resulting in symptoms. I’m

<!-- chunk -->

### also looking for reasons why cells might develop abnormally and why some

<!-- chunk -->

### cells end up shrinking and dying, resulting in brain functions that are

<!-- chunk -->

### permanently absent. These concrete mechanisms of action will help us

<!-- chunk -->

### understand the symptoms of mental illness. They align with the framework

<!-- chunk -->

### that I outlined in Chapter Six.

<!-- chunk -->

### You might also recall from Chapter Five that I said that human illnesses

<!-- chunk -->

### are usually due to a problem in one of three areas: the development,

<!-- chunk -->

### function, or maintenance of cells.

<!-- chunk -->

### As it turns out, mitochondria play a role in all of these.

<!-- chunk -->

### I will now walk you through five broad consequences of mitochondrial

<!-- chunk -->

### dysfunction and dysregulation that can address all of this: decreased cell

<!-- chunk -->

### maintenance, overactive brain functions, underactive brain functions,

<!-- chunk -->

### developmental problems, and cell shrinkage and cell death.

<!-- chunk -->

## Decreased Cell Maintenance

<!-- chunk -->

### One of the unique things about living cells, as opposed to non-living

<!-- chunk -->

### machines like cars, is that they require energy and metabolic resources to

<!-- chunk -->

### maintain themselves. Cell parts need to be repaired and replaced in

<!-- chunk -->

### constant, never-ending turnover. All of this requires energy and metabolic

<!-- chunk -->

### building blocks. One study estimates about one-third of the brain’s ATP

<!-- chunk -->

### production goes to cell maintenance or “housekeeping” functions.


<!-- chunk -->

### I’ve

<!-- chunk -->

### already described the roles of stress, cortisol, and mitochondria themselves

<!-- chunk -->

### in the process of autophagy, which is critically important in cell

<!-- chunk -->

### maintenance. But, as usual, there’s more to the story.

<!-- chunk -->

### Mitochondria interact with other organelles to facilitate routine

<!-- chunk -->

### maintenance functions. For example, they interact with lysosomes. When

<!-- chunk -->

### these interactions are prevented in experiments, waste products accumulate

<!-- chunk -->

### in the lysosomes.


<!-- chunk -->

### They also interact with the endoplasmic reticulum (ER),

<!-- chunk -->

### which has many roles including the folding of proteins. Many

<!-- chunk -->

### neurodegenerative disorders are associated with misfolded proteins in the

<!-- chunk -->

### ER. When these misfolded proteins accumulate, a process called the

<!-- chunk -->

### unfolded protein response (UPR) tries to mitigate the damage. One group of

<!-- chunk -->

### researchers found that there is a microprotein on the outer membrane of

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondria, PIGBOS, that plays a key role in the UPR. When this protein

<!-- chunk -->

### was eliminated, the cells were much more likely to die.


<!-- chunk -->

### This strongly

<!-- chunk -->

### suggests that mitochondria play a key role in this process as well. These are

<!-- chunk -->

### just some of the ways that mitochondrial dysfunction can cause problems

<!-- chunk -->

### with cell maintenance, which can lead to all of the maintenance problems

<!-- chunk -->

### and structural defects found in people with mental disorders.

<!-- chunk -->

### In some cases, structural defects in cells can lead to a positive feedback

<!-- chunk -->

### cycle that affects metabolism and can make it harder for cells to work. One

<!-- chunk -->

### specific example is myelin, which is an outer protective coating for neurons

<!-- chunk -->

### made by support cells called oligodendrocytes. Myelin makes it easier for

<!-- chunk -->

### neurons to send electrical signals. If a neuron has defects in its myelin

<!-- chunk -->

### coating, it will require more energy to work. An extreme example of this is

<!-- chunk -->

### multiple sclerosis, in which myelin is destroyed by an autoimmune process.

<!-- chunk -->

### Mitochondrial dysfunction has been associated with problems in myelin

<!-- chunk -->

### production and maintenance. Consistent with the brain energy theory,

<!-- chunk -->

### defects in myelin have been identified in the brains of people with

<!-- chunk -->

### schizophrenia, major depression, bipolar disorder, alcoholism, epilepsy,

<!-- chunk -->

### Alzheimer’s disease, diabetes, and even obesity.


<!-- chunk -->

### Debris in the cell, another structural defect and maintenance issue, can

<!-- chunk -->

### impair mitochondria from moving around. For example, Alzheimer’s

<!-- chunk -->

### disease is associated with the accumulation of a protein called tau, in

<!-- chunk -->

### addition to the beta-amyloid described earlier. Researchers who looked at

<!-- chunk -->

### the effects of tau protein on mitochondria found that it severely limited

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondria’s ability to move around a cell.


<!-- chunk -->

### Their paths became

<!-- chunk -->

### obstructed from all the debris, and the tau proteins interfered with the

<!-- chunk -->

### cytoskeleton that mitochondria use for movement. When mitochondria are

<!-- chunk -->

### prevented from moving around a cell, the cell has trouble working properly,

<!-- chunk -->

### and the cell may shrink and/or die.

<!-- chunk -->

## Overactive Brain Functions

<!-- chunk -->

### Remember our discussion of overactivity or hyperexcitability from Chapter

<!-- chunk -->

### Six? Mitochondrial dysfunction or dysregulation can cause this! Again, this

<!-- chunk -->

### is probably the most paradoxical thing about mitochondrial dysfunction.

<!-- chunk -->

### Sometimes when mitochondria aren’t working properly, parts of the brain

<!-- chunk -->

### can actually become overactive as opposed to underactive—even though

<!-- chunk -->

### they may not have enough ATP.

<!-- chunk -->

### In the real world, hyperexcitability of cells is actually pretty common.

<!-- chunk -->

### There are many medical conditions that are reflections of hyperexcitable

<!-- chunk -->

### cells. Seizures are a clear and extreme example in the brain. A heart

<!-- chunk -->

### arrhythmia can be due to hyperexcitable heart cells. A muscle spasm is a

<!-- chunk -->

### hyperexcitable muscle cell. Chronic pain is due to a hyperexcitable nerve

<!-- chunk -->

### cell. These are all examples of cells firing when they shouldn’t, or not

<!-- chunk -->

### stopping when they should.

<!-- chunk -->

### Mitochondrial dysfunction can lead to overactivity and

<!-- chunk -->

### hyperexcitability. There are at least three ways this can happen:

<!-- chunk -->

### 1.Recall that mitochondria are involved in ion pumping and calcium

<!-- chunk -->

### regulation, which are both required to turn cells “off.” If

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondria aren’t functioning properly, these processes will take

<!-- chunk -->

### longer to perform, and cells can become hyperexcitable.

<!-- chunk -->

### 2.Sometimes, overactivity or hyperexcitability can be due to

<!-- chunk -->

### dysfunction in cells that are meant to slow down other cells, such as

<!-- chunk -->

### GABA cells. If GABA cells aren’t working properly, then the cells

<!-- chunk -->

### they are

<!-- chunk -->

### supposed to inhibit will become unleashed and hyperexcitable.

<!-- chunk -->

### Recall from Chapter Six that cortical interneurons are one such

<!-- chunk -->

### example, and their dysfunction has been found in many mental and

<!-- chunk -->

### neurological disorders.

<!-- chunk -->

### 3.I discussed how maintenance problems can alter the structure of

<!-- chunk -->

### cells, such as problems with myelin or beta-amyloid. These

<!-- chunk -->

### maintenance problems can cause hyperexcitability; for example, a

<!-- chunk -->

### lack of myelin can allow ions to leak into a cell and cause it to fire

<!-- chunk -->

### when it shouldn’t.

<!-- chunk -->

### One research group directly demonstrated that mitochondrial dysfunction

<!-- chunk -->

### causes hyperexcitability by deleting a protein in mice, sirtuin 3, known to

<!-- chunk -->

### be essential to mitochondrial health. Sure enough, the mice developed

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondrial dysfunction, hyperexcitability, and seizures, and they died

<!-- chunk -->

### early deaths.


<!-- chunk -->

### Another research group turned stem cells from people with

<!-- chunk -->

### bipolar disorder and healthy controls into neurons and found that the

<!-- chunk -->

### neurons from the people with bipolar disorder had mitochondrial

<!-- chunk -->

### abnormalities and the cells were hyperexcitable.


<!-- chunk -->

### Interestingly, lithium

<!-- chunk -->

### reduced this hyperexcitability.

<!-- chunk -->

### Hyperexcitability of neurons has been found in many mental and

<!-- chunk -->

### metabolic disorders. It causes seizures and can be measured in the brains of

<!-- chunk -->

### people with epilepsy. Hyperexcitability has also been found in the brains of

<!-- chunk -->

### people with delirium, PTSD, schizophrenia, bipolar disorder, autism,

<!-- chunk -->

### obsessive-compulsive disorder, and Alzheimer’s disease. It has even been

<!-- chunk -->

### measured in healthy rodents subjected to nothing more than chronic

<!-- chunk -->

### stress.


<!-- chunk -->

### Hyperexcitability can be difficult to measure, but we really don’t

<!-- chunk -->

### need to measure it. People can usually tell when it’s occurring because

<!-- chunk -->

### something is happening in their bodies or brains that shouldn’t be

<!-- chunk -->

### happening. Hyperexcitability of a pain cell causes pain. Hyperexcitability of

<!-- chunk -->

### the anxiety pathways in the brain causes anxiety. Hyperexcitability of any

<!-- chunk -->

### brain region that produces a human emotion, perception, cognition, or

<!-- chunk -->

### behavior will produce that experience.

<!-- chunk -->

## Underactive Brain Functions

<!-- chunk -->

### Mitochondrial dysfunction or dysregulation can slow down or reduce the

<!-- chunk -->

### function of cells. Cells require energy to work. Mitochondria provide it.

<!-- chunk -->

### They are also the on/off switch for cells. They control calcium levels and

<!-- chunk -->

### other signals. Brain cells need energy to make and release neurotransmitters

<!-- chunk -->

### and hormones and to function properly. Reduced cell function alone

<!-- chunk -->

### explains many of the altered levels of neurotransmitters and hormones seen

<!-- chunk -->

### in people with mental disorders. Additionally, mitochondria are directly

<!-- chunk -->

### involved in making some of the hormones, such as cortisol, estrogen, and

<!-- chunk -->

### testosterone, so if they are dysfunctional or dysregulated, these hormone

<!-- chunk -->

### levels may be dysregulated, too.

<!-- chunk -->

## Developmental Problems

<!-- chunk -->

### Beginning in the womb until early adulthood, the human brain is rapidly

<!-- chunk -->

### growing and forming connections between neurons and other brain cells.

<!-- chunk -->

### These connections are vitally important, laying the groundwork, or

<!-- chunk -->

### “hardwiring,” for life. There are developmental windows—times when the

<!-- chunk -->

### hardwiring of the brain needs to take place in clear ways. If development

<!-- chunk -->

### doesn’t occur normally, this window can close, and the brain will never

<!-- chunk -->

### again have the chance to be “normal.” Mitochondria are critically important

<!-- chunk -->

### for all these tasks. As I discussed, they play critical roles in cell growth,

<!-- chunk -->

### differentiation, and synapse formation. When mitochondria are

<!-- chunk -->

### dysfunctional or dysregulated, the brain doesn’t develop normally. This is

<!-- chunk -->

### important to keep in mind for neurodevelopmental disorders that begin in

<!-- chunk -->

### infancy or childhood, such as autism. Even at the end of life, our brains

<!-- chunk -->

### change in predictable ways. Cell growth and differentiation, as well as

<!-- chunk -->

### neuroplasticity, or the changing and adaptability of neurons, are important

<!-- chunk -->

### throughout life. When mitochondria aren’t functioning properly, problems

<!-- chunk -->

### with all of this can occur. If cells or connections between cells are missing,

<!-- chunk -->

### this can result in permanently absent brain functions. These symptoms don’t

<!-- chunk -->

### wax and wane, because the cells and connections needed to perform these

<!-- chunk -->

### functions simply aren’t present.

<!-- chunk -->

## Cell Shrinkage and Cell Death

<!-- chunk -->

### Mitochondrial dysfunction can lead to cell shrinkage, or atrophy. If the

<!-- chunk -->

### quantity or health of mitochondria declines, the cell gets stressed. Recall

<!-- chunk -->

### that mitochondria spread themselves throughout the cell. Some are always

<!-- chunk -->

### moving around, looking for work to do. If the workforce is reduced, they

<!-- chunk -->

### can’t keep the entire cell working. In some cases, mitochondria stop going

<!-- chunk -->

### to peripheral parts of the cell, such as axon terminals or dendrites. When

<!-- chunk -->

### they stop going there, those cell parts die. Inflammation ensues. Microglia,

<!-- chunk -->

### the immune cells of the brain, get to work and absorb some of these dead

<!-- chunk -->

### cell parts.


<!-- chunk -->

### As more and more mitochondria become impaired, more and

<!-- chunk -->

### more of the cell shrinks. If the process continues, the cell dies.

<!-- chunk -->

### It is well documented that the brains of people with chronic mental

<!-- chunk -->

### disorders show signs of cell shrinkage over time. Recall that they are aging

<!-- chunk -->

### prematurely. Different brain regions are affected in different people. Some

<!-- chunk -->

### areas, such as the hippocampus, are more commonly impacted, but even in

<!-- chunk -->

### people with the same diagnosis, such as schizophrenia, there can be

<!-- chunk -->

### numerous differences in the brain regions impacted.


<!-- chunk -->

### This comes back to

<!-- chunk -->

### heterogeneity. Mitochondrial dysfunction and dysregulation explain this

<!-- chunk -->

### heterogeneity. Given that numerous factors affect mitochondrial function

<!-- chunk -->

### (which I’ll get to just up ahead, in Part Three), they also affect different

<!-- chunk -->

### areas of the brain. So, depending upon the mix of risk factors or causes that

<!-- chunk -->

### people have, their brains will be affected differently. As I already

<!-- chunk -->

### mentioned, timing and development also matter. People who are affected at

<!-- chunk -->

### age fourteen will have brain differences from people not affected until

<!-- chunk -->

### thirty-nine.

<!-- chunk -->

### Let’s put this all together using our car analogy. A car has many parts—a

<!-- chunk -->

### fuel tank, fuel that goes into the tank (gasoline), an engine, a battery for

<!-- chunk -->

### electrical energy, a steering system, and a braking system for stopping.

<!-- chunk -->

### Problems can occur in different parts, and different symptoms can result. In

<!-- chunk -->

### some cases, the car may sputter or move slower (underactive function) if it

<!-- chunk -->

### gets water in the gas tank or if a spark plug has gone bad. If the battery

<!-- chunk -->

### begins to fail, it might be that the lights dim, or the windshield wipers slow

<!-- chunk -->

### down, or the radio doesn’t work, or the car won’t start at all (all underactive

<!-- chunk -->

### functions). These are very different symptoms, but they’re all related to

<!-- chunk -->

### energy—and usually with just one cause. Now let’s make the car like a

<!-- chunk -->

### living cell. Suddenly it requires energy to work and energy to maintain

<!-- chunk -->

### itself. Without enough energy, the tires start to deflate, the wheels get

<!-- chunk -->

### wobbly, the doors develop rust and holes (maintenance problems). The

<!-- chunk -->

### engine and battery get old. Battery acid starts to leak all over the engine

<!-- chunk -->

### (ROS). The oil hasn’t been changed in a while, and engine parts are getting

<!-- chunk -->

### damaged. This lack of maintenance makes the engine even worse (a

<!-- chunk -->

### positive feedback cycle). At some point, this car will become a danger to

<!-- chunk -->

### the other cars and the flow of traffic on the highway. The brakes stop

<!-- chunk -->

### working (hyperexcitability due to impaired off switch). Eventually, it may

<!-- chunk -->

### cause a car crash and shut down the highway. The car can be towed to the

<!-- chunk -->

### junkyard for recycling (apoptosis). If someone attempts to drive it again, it

<!-- chunk -->

### will continue to pose a danger to the other cars on the highway and the flow

<!-- chunk -->

### of traffic (metabolic and mental disorders).

# Brain Energy Theory Realized

<!-- chunk -->

### Now let’s see the brain energy theory in action. I’ll walk you through some

<!-- chunk -->

### of the evidence focusing on three different mental disorders and how we

<!-- chunk -->

### can conceptualize what’s happening to produce symptoms from a

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondrial and metabolic point of view.

<!-- chunk -->

## Major Depression

<!-- chunk -->

### We know from many studies that mitochondria are not functioning properly

<!-- chunk -->

### in people with chronic depression.


<!-- chunk -->

### For example, several lines of evidence

<!-- chunk -->

### have found that people with depression have lower levels of ATP, not only

<!-- chunk -->

### in brain cells, but also in muscle cells and circulating immune cells.

<!-- chunk -->

### Decreased ATP production has been found in animal models of depression

<!-- chunk -->

### as well. Autopsy studies looking at brain tissue of people with chronic

<!-- chunk -->

### depression have found specific abnormalities in mitochondrial proteins,

<!-- chunk -->

### clearly implicating mitochondrial dysfunction.


<!-- chunk -->

### And as I already

<!-- chunk -->

### mentioned, levels of oxidative stress are elevated in people with depression.

<!-- chunk -->

### Another line of evidence includes blood biomarkers of depression.

<!-- chunk -->

### Many researchers have taken blood samples from thousands of people with

<!-- chunk -->

### depression looking for abnormalities or differences compared to healthy

<!-- chunk -->

### controls. Numerous biomarkers have been identified. A meta-analysis of

<!-- chunk -->

### forty-six such studies tried to make sense of the differences to see if there

<!-- chunk -->

### might be a common pathway or theme. There was! The biomarkers related

<!-- chunk -->

### primarily to amino acid and lipid metabolism, both of which relate to

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondrial function.


<!-- chunk -->

### One specific biomarker of great interest is acetyl-L-carnitine (ALC).

<!-- chunk -->

### This molecule is produced within mitochondria and is important to energy

<!-- chunk -->

### production. It is critical for the function of the hippocampus, a brain region

<!-- chunk -->

### often implicated in depression. One group of researchers looked at levels of

<!-- chunk -->

### ALC in depressed and non-depressed people and found that the depressed

<!-- chunk -->

### people, on average, had lower levels.


<!-- chunk -->

### Furthermore, lower levels of ALC

<!-- chunk -->

### predicted the severity of depression, the chronicity of the illness, treatment

<!-- chunk -->

### resistance, and even a history of emotional neglect. A subsequent study in

<!-- chunk -->

### 460 patients with depression found that ALC levels improved with effective

<!-- chunk -->

### antidepressant treatments, and that these levels could help predict who

<!-- chunk -->

### would experience a full remission.


<!-- chunk -->

### These researchers concluded, “New

<!-- chunk -->

### strategies targeting mitochondria should be explored to improve treatments

<!-- chunk -->

### of Major Depressive Disorder.”

<!-- chunk -->

### Perhaps the most direct and stunning evidence for the role of

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondria in depression comes from an elegant study done in rats.


<!-- chunk -->

### Researchers identified rats that had high levels of anxiety and depression-

<!-- chunk -->

### like behaviors and then studied a specific part of their brains, the nucleus

<!-- chunk -->

### accumbens, to see if there were differences in mitochondrial function and/or

<!-- chunk -->

### how the cells developed. They found both. The anxious/depressed rats had

<!-- chunk -->

### fewer mitochondria per cell, as well as differences in the way their

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondria used oxygen to turn energy into ATP and in how

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondria interacted with another organelle, the endoplasmic reticulum

<!-- chunk -->

### (ER). The neurons themselves also looked different. Following the trail

<!-- chunk -->

### further, the researchers

<!-- chunk -->

### found that the mitochondria from these rats had lower levels of mitofusin-2

<!-- chunk -->

### (MFN2), a protein on mitochondrial membranes important to their ability to

<!-- chunk -->

### fuse with each other and with the ER. Here’s the stunning part. They then

<!-- chunk -->

### injected the anxious/depressed rats with a viral vector that significantly

<!-- chunk -->

### increased the levels of MFN2. And that changed everything! The

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondria began functioning normally, the neurons began to look

<!-- chunk -->

### normal, and the anxiety and depression-like behaviors stopped. This

<!-- chunk -->

### strongly suggests a causal role for mitochondria in depression and anxiety .

<!-- chunk -->

### . . at least in rats.

<!-- chunk -->

### Some of the symptoms of depression fall neatly into the category of

<!-- chunk -->

### underactive functions or reduced metabolism. Changes in sleep, energy,

<!-- chunk -->

### motivation, and concentration likely all relate to reduced function of brain

<!-- chunk -->

### cells. The fatigue almost certainly extends to the muscles throughout the

<!-- chunk -->

### body, given that mitochondrial dysfunction has been found there, too. In

<!-- chunk -->

### some cases, people will describe “leaden paralysis,” a situation in which

<!-- chunk -->

### they feel like their arms and legs are made of lead and it’s difficult to even

<!-- chunk -->

### move them. Mitochondrial dysfunction in their muscles might explain this.

<!-- chunk -->

### If their muscles don’t have enough energy, people will have difficulty

<!-- chunk -->

### moving them. Catatonia is an extreme version of metabolic failure—people

<!-- chunk -->

### can appear paralyzed from their illness and have severe difficulty moving or

<!-- chunk -->

### speaking.

<!-- chunk -->

## Bipolar Disorder

<!-- chunk -->

### Direct evidence for metabolic abnormalities in bipolar disorder (and

<!-- chunk -->

### schizophrenia) dates to 1956 when researchers noted abnormalities in

<!-- chunk -->

### lactate metabolism in patients.


<!-- chunk -->

### There are many studies documenting

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondrial dysfunction in bipolar disorder, with findings similar to those

<!-- chunk -->

### found in people with depression. However, one important question is: What

<!-- chunk -->

### makes depression different than mania? Anyone who has seen people with

<!-- chunk -->

### these two conditions knows there is a big difference.

<!-- chunk -->

### In 2018, researchers published a review article, “A Model of the

<!-- chunk -->

### Mitochondrial Basis of Bipolar Disorder,” in which they proposed that

<!-- chunk -->

### depressive states appear to be energy-deficient states, while manic states

<!-- chunk -->

### appear to involve increased energy production in the brain.


<!-- chunk -->

### They cited

<!-- chunk -->

### several

<!-- chunk -->

### studies showing that manic states are associated with increased glucose and

<!-- chunk -->

### lactate utilization in the brain, both suggestive of increased mitochondrial

<!-- chunk -->

### energy production. Additionally, two neurotransmitters, glutamate and

<!-- chunk -->

### dopamine, have been found to be elevated in manic states, suggesting

<!-- chunk -->

### increased activity of these neurons. So, manic states appear to be one of the

<!-- chunk -->

### few unique situations in which mitochondria, at least in some brain cells,

<!-- chunk -->

### are producing more energy than normal. Although surprising, this is still

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondrial dysfunction or dysregulation. The mitochondria are supposed

<!-- chunk -->

### to slow down at appropriate times, such as at night to allow for sleep.

<!-- chunk -->

### Different brain cells are supposed to stop at certain times—like the traffic in

<!-- chunk -->

### a major city. In manic states, the mitochondria are hyperactive in terms of

<!-- chunk -->

### their production of energy, and this is making cells “go” when they

<!-- chunk -->

### shouldn’t. They aren’t yielding or slowing down at appropriate times. Many

<!-- chunk -->

### parts of the brain appear to be overactive.

<!-- chunk -->

### Several additional lines of evidence support this model.


<!-- chunk -->

### Bipolar

<!-- chunk -->

### patients have been found to have higher than normal calcium levels,

<!-- chunk -->

### especially when they are manic—consistent with the hyperexcitability

<!-- chunk -->

### mechanism that I outlined. In fact, researchers have confirmed changes in

<!-- chunk -->

### neuronal excitability in bipolar patients. For anyone who has seen people

<!-- chunk -->

### with depression or mania, this makes perfect sense. People with mania

<!-- chunk -->

### clearly have too much energy. People with depression clearly don’t have

<!-- chunk -->

### enough energy. It’s fascinating that this has been found at the cellular level.

<!-- chunk -->

### In people with bipolar disorder, once the manic episode resolves, they

<!-- chunk -->

### continue to have mitochondrial dysfunction, but it results in too little energy

<!-- chunk -->

### production overall. One group of researchers recently identified a

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondrial biomarker in blood cells showing a significant decrease in

<!-- chunk -->

### the number of mitochondria in both manic and depressed states that

<!-- chunk -->

### normalized when people were feeling well.


<!-- chunk -->

### This suggests that something

<!-- chunk -->

### might be disrupting either mitochondrial biogenesis or mitophagy

<!-- chunk -->

### throughout the entire body during disease states, not just in the brain.

<!-- chunk -->

### During manic episodes, one of the biggest dangers with too much

<!-- chunk -->

### energy is its effect on hyperexcitable cells. These are the cells that have

<!-- chunk -->

### damaged mitochondria, too few mitochondria, or structural damage due to

<!-- chunk -->

### maintenance problems. The short burst of energy during a manic phase isn’t

<!-- chunk -->

### enough to correct the longstanding problems associated with mitochondrial

<!-- chunk -->

### dysfunction. It’s not enough energy or time to repair the cells. Instead, it’s

<!-- chunk -->

### enough to cause major problems, like psychotic symptoms, anxiety, and

<!-- chunk -->

### agitation. An easy way to think about this is to go back to our car analogy.

<!-- chunk -->

### If a car is in poor shape with deflated and misaligned tires due to poor

<!-- chunk -->

### maintenance, giving that car more gas suddenly is actually a dangerous

<!-- chunk -->

### thing. It’s not ready to handle more energy or more speed. It will be more

<!-- chunk -->

### likely to crash and burn. That’s what happens when you give a

<!-- chunk -->

### hyperexcitable cell too much energy.

<!-- chunk -->

## Posttraumatic Stress Disorder

<!-- chunk -->

### PTSD can be understood as a hyperexcitable trauma response system. This

<!-- chunk -->

### system is a normal response to life-threatening events but is now turning on

<!-- chunk -->

### when it shouldn’t or failing to turn off when it should. For some people, this

<!-- chunk -->

### system appears to develop a lower threshold for firing. For example, many

<!-- chunk -->

### people with a trauma history will have clearly identified “triggers” that set

<!-- chunk -->

### off their symptoms. These can be places, people, smells, words, or even

<!-- chunk -->

### thoughts.

<!-- chunk -->

### Two areas of the brain are commonly affected, the amygdala and the

<!-- chunk -->

### medial prefrontal cortex (mPFC). The amygdala sets off the fear response

<!-- chunk -->

### and has been found to be hyperexcitable in PTSD. The mPFC is an area of

<!-- chunk -->

### the brain that inhibits the amygdala. By doing so, it can stop a panic

<!-- chunk -->

### reaction once someone realizes that there’s no need to panic. This area of

<!-- chunk -->

### the brain has been found to be underactive in people with PTSD, meaning

<!-- chunk -->

### that the person will have trouble stopping a panic reaction. Several lines of

<!-- chunk -->

### evidence have demonstrated mitochondrial dysfunction in people with

<!-- chunk -->

### PTSD, including autopsy studies showing abnormalities in mitochondrial

<!-- chunk -->

### gene expression, reductions in total mitochondria, increased levels of

<!-- chunk -->

### oxidative stress, and reduced levels of ATP.
