# A Unifying Example

<!-- chunk -->

### But can all mental disorders really be due to metabolism and mitochondrial

<!-- chunk -->

### dysfunction?

<!-- chunk -->

### Some people may still have a difficult time with this new way of

<!-- chunk -->

### conceptualizing mental disorders, as lumping all the disorders together

<!-- chunk -->

### under the pathway of metabolism and mitochondrial dysfunction may seem

<!-- chunk -->

### like a stretch.

<!-- chunk -->

### To help address this concern, we should be able to look at situations in

<!-- chunk -->

### which we know mitochondrial function is impaired abruptly and see

<!-- chunk -->

### essentially all psychiatric symptoms emerge. As it turns out, we have a clear

<!-- chunk -->

### example for this test proof—delirium.

<!-- chunk -->

### Delirium is a serious condition defined as an acute mental disturbance.

<!-- chunk -->

### The word “acute” means that it happens rapidly. The “mental disturbance”

<!-- chunk -->

### can be any psychiatric symptom—confusion, disorientation, distractibility,

<!-- chunk -->

### fixation on certain topics, hallucinations, delusions, mood changes, anxiety,

<!-- chunk -->

### agitation, withdrawal, dramatic changes in sleep, and personality changes.

<!-- chunk -->

### Every single symptom of any psychiatric disorder can occur during

<!-- chunk -->

### delirium. Even changes in eating behaviors and perception of body image

<!-- chunk -->

### that mimic an eating disorder have been observed during delirium.

<!-- chunk -->

### So, what causes delirium? The standard answer right now: No one

<!-- chunk -->

### knows for sure how it all works, but we know that when people are

<!-- chunk -->

### seriously ill, it can happen. Almost all medical disorders can cause

<!-- chunk -->

### delirium. This includes things like infections, cancer, autoimmune

<!-- chunk -->

### disorders, heart attacks, and strokes. The more serious they are, the more

<!-- chunk -->

### likely they are to cause delirium. People admitted to intensive care units

<!-- chunk -->

### (ICUs) are much more likely to have delirium, with anywhere from 35 to

<!-- chunk -->

### percent of critically ill patients being diagnosed with it, depending upon the

<!-- chunk -->

### study.


<!-- chunk -->

### Medications can also do it. People starting new medications can have

<!-- chunk -->

### reactions that can cause delirium. Withdrawal from medications or

<!-- chunk -->

### substances, even toxic ones like heavy use of alcohol, can cause delirium.

<!-- chunk -->

### Alcohol withdrawal delirium has a special name, delirium tremens. It can

<!-- chunk -->

### be quite serious and life-threatening. The elderly are particularly vulnerable

<!-- chunk -->

### to

<!-- chunk -->

### delirium. People with preexisting dementia, such as Alzheimer’s disease,

<!-- chunk -->

### are even more vulnerable. Essentially, there are countless causes of

<!-- chunk -->

### delirium. As I will soon explore, they all affect mitochondrial function.

<!-- chunk -->

### How is delirium diagnosed? When symptoms of delirium begin,

<!-- chunk -->

### sometimes the cause is obvious. In some cases, the initial symptoms can be

<!-- chunk -->

### seen as a normal response to a medical condition. Many people who have

<!-- chunk -->

### heart attacks will have anxiety. It’s difficult to imagine not being anxious

<!-- chunk -->

### when facing a life-threatening situation. Physicians will often give people

<!-- chunk -->

### psychiatric medications, such as benzodiazepines, to reduce their anxiety. In

<!-- chunk -->

### this early phase, they usually don’t diagnose delirium or a psychiatric

<!-- chunk -->

### disorder, even though they are prescribing a psychiatric medication. The

<!-- chunk -->

### anxiety is often seen as a normal, understandable reaction. However, if

<!-- chunk -->

### these anxiety symptoms are the start of delirium, the symptoms usually

<!-- chunk -->

### become more dramatic. People can develop panic attacks and severe

<!-- chunk -->

### anxiety. This can quickly progress to confusion, disorientation, and

<!-- chunk -->

### hallucinations. This is common in frail, elderly people with heart attacks.

<!-- chunk -->

### Although these symptoms can be identical to those of dementia or

<!-- chunk -->

### schizophrenia, physicians don’t assign these diagnoses. Instead, they

<!-- chunk -->

### diagnose delirium.

<!-- chunk -->

### But how do they think about this distinction? Most healthcare

<!-- chunk -->

### professionals know that the brain simply isn’t working right under the stress

<!-- chunk -->

### of a heart attack. They will attribute any and all new psychiatric symptoms

<!-- chunk -->

### to the heart attack. Anything goes. Obsessions. Compulsions. Confusion.

<!-- chunk -->

### Depression. Agitation. Delusions. Anything! Healthcare professionals will

<!-- chunk -->

### lump all symptoms under the diagnosis of delirium. People with delirium

<!-- chunk -->

### won’t have all the symptoms of every mental disorder. They may have just

<!-- chunk -->

### a few. Some will develop symptoms of OCD. Others will look more

<!-- chunk -->

### depressed and withdrawn. Others will look manic and agitated. This doesn’t

<!-- chunk -->

### matter, either. Any combination of symptoms is irrelevant. They are all due

<!-- chunk -->

### to delirium.

<!-- chunk -->

### Delirium can sometimes occur more gradually. One of the most

<!-- chunk -->

### common causes of delirium in the elderly is a urinary tract infection, or

<!-- chunk -->

### UTI. This can be more difficult to recognize and diagnose. Oftentimes,

<!-- chunk -->

### these people don’t know they have a UTI. The brain shows the first signs of

<!-- chunk -->

### a problem, not the bladder. Elderly people who were otherwise fine a few

<!-- chunk -->

### weeks prior

<!-- chunk -->

### can begin to develop confusion and memory problems. Family members or

<!-- chunk -->

### healthcare professionals often become concerned about Alzheimer’s

<!-- chunk -->

### disease. The symptoms look exactly the same. These people can get

<!-- chunk -->

### confused often. They might get lost driving. They can have trouble

<!-- chunk -->

### remembering the names of people who they see every day. Once taken to a

<!-- chunk -->

### healthcare professional, a medical workup might then reveal the problem—

<!-- chunk -->

### a UTI. Treating the UTI can resolve all symptoms. Although it was due to

<!-- chunk -->

### an infection in the bladder, the symptoms came from the brain. Why?

<!-- chunk -->

### Because the brain is the most sensitive organ to energy deprivation, or

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondrial dysfunction. It is the weakest link. It almost always shows at

<!-- chunk -->

### least some subtle signs first.

<!-- chunk -->

### How do these different medical conditions lead to the development of

<!-- chunk -->

### essentially every psychiatric symptom? Experts speculate about

<!-- chunk -->

### neurotransmitters, stress responses, and inflammation.


<!-- chunk -->

### All of these are

<!-- chunk -->

### true. But how, exactly, do they fit together and lead to psychiatric

<!-- chunk -->

### symptoms? At this point, the medical community doesn’t have a coherent

<!-- chunk -->

### theory, but the brain energy theory offers one.

<!-- chunk -->

### I am not the first to suggest that delirium is due to metabolic problems.

<!-- chunk -->

### In 1959, George Engel, the developer of the biopsychosocial model,

<!-- chunk -->

### proposed that delirium is due to disturbed brain energy metabolism, or

<!-- chunk -->

### “cerebral metabolic insufficiency.”


<!-- chunk -->

### Many researchers have expanded on

<!-- chunk -->

### this hypothesis since then.


<!-- chunk -->

### For example, PET imaging studies have

<!-- chunk -->

### revealed decreased brain glucose metabolism in people with delirium.


<!-- chunk -->

### Many serious medical conditions are known to directly impact metabolism

<!-- chunk -->

### and mitochondrial function. However, given that the medical community

<!-- chunk -->

### hasn’t been able to explain mental symptoms, it has not been clear if or how

<!-- chunk -->

### these metabolic and mitochondrial abnormalities might play a role in

<!-- chunk -->

### causing the mental symptoms.

<!-- chunk -->

### How is delirium treated? The treatment depends upon the underlying

<!-- chunk -->

### cause or causes, and the specific symptoms. Once the medical condition

<!-- chunk -->

### that is causing delirium is identified, the standard treatments for that

<!-- chunk -->

### condition are implemented. This can be an antibiotic for the UTI or

<!-- chunk -->

### standard cardiology protocols for the heart attack. What about the mental

<!-- chunk -->

### symptoms? Even though the symptoms fall under the label of delirium, we

<!-- chunk -->

### use

<!-- chunk -->

### just about anything and everything in psychiatry to control the symptoms.

<!-- chunk -->

### Sedating medications are commonly used—antipsychotics, mood

<!-- chunk -->

### stabilizers, antidepressants, anti-anxiety medications, and sleep

<!-- chunk -->

### medications. If the symptoms of delirium are extreme depression and a lack

<!-- chunk -->

### of energy, sometimes stimulants are used. We use medications to help with

<!-- chunk -->

### the symptoms, but really, we wait for the underlying medical condition to

<!-- chunk -->

### be fully treated. The symptoms of delirium usually go away once the

<!-- chunk -->

### medical condition resolves. It’s a temporary case of mitochondrial

<!-- chunk -->

### dysfunction.

<!-- chunk -->

### Does delirium really matter? Once the primary medical condition is

<!-- chunk -->

### identified, such as the heart attack, does it really matter if the person has

<!-- chunk -->

### mental symptoms or not? Many people think it doesn’t. They don’t take the

<!-- chunk -->

### mental symptoms very seriously. They see them as annoying problems that

<!-- chunk -->

### just make delivering good care more difficult. For example, some

<!-- chunk -->

### cardiologists will ignore mental symptoms in the context of a heart attack.

<!-- chunk -->

### To them, the problem is plain and clear—it’s a heart attack. Whether the

<!-- chunk -->

### person is anxious or not doesn’t really matter. Even if a person is

<!-- chunk -->

### hallucinating, they might think that doesn’t matter either. That is a problem

<!-- chunk -->

### for a consulting psychiatrist to deal with. It doesn’t pertain to the heart or

<!-- chunk -->

### the cardiologist’s job. Unfortunately, this all-too-common perspective is

<!-- chunk -->

### shortsighted. It ignores a tremendous amount of research showing that

<!-- chunk -->

### delirium does matter. Sometimes, it’s the difference between life and death.

<!-- chunk -->

### If the brain energy theory is correct, people with delirium should have

<!-- chunk -->

### more widespread or severe mitochondrial dysfunction than people without

<!-- chunk -->

### delirium. The “mental” symptoms are giving us a warning sign. If this is

<!-- chunk -->

### true, more widespread and severe mitochondrial dysfunction should mean a

<!-- chunk -->

### whole host of things. It should mean that people with delirium will be more

<!-- chunk -->

### likely to develop a mental disorder, dementia, or a seizure. It should also

<!-- chunk -->

### mean they will be more likely to die. Are any of these things true? It turns

<!-- chunk -->

### out they all are.

<!-- chunk -->

### Mental disorders, such as anxiety disorders, depression, and PTSD, are

<!-- chunk -->

### common after an episode of delirium. Higher rates of dementia and

<!-- chunk -->

### cognitive impairment have been consistently documented at three, twelve,

<!-- chunk -->

### and eighteen months after hospital discharge in people with delirium

<!-- chunk -->

### compared

<!-- chunk -->

### to people with the same illnesses who don’t have delirium.


<!-- chunk -->

### In fact,

<!-- chunk -->

### delirium in older people results in an eightfold increased risk of subsequent

<!-- chunk -->

### dementia. Hyperexcitable brain cells have also been well documented, with

<!-- chunk -->

### seizures being the most extreme consequence. In one study of people with

<!-- chunk -->

### delirium, 84 percent had abnormal electroencephalograms (EEGs), with

<!-- chunk -->

### percent showing clear seizure activity.


<!-- chunk -->

### People with delirium are more

<!-- chunk -->

### likely to die early. During a hospital admission, people with delirium are

<!-- chunk -->

### twice as likely to die early as those without delirium.


<!-- chunk -->

### After discharge from

<!-- chunk -->

### the hospital, the one-year mortality rates for people with delirium are 35 to

<!-- chunk -->

### 40 percent, much higher than in those without delirium.


<!-- chunk -->

### How do we make sense of this? Delirium tells us that there is

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondrial dysfunction in the brain. Sometimes it can be reversible, and

<!-- chunk -->

### the person completely recovers. But not always. What this data suggests is

<!-- chunk -->

### that the mitochondrial dysfunction can persist or progress. Mitochondria in

<!-- chunk -->

### the cells can become damaged—a reduction in the workforce of the cells.

<!-- chunk -->

### This leaves cells more vulnerable to continued dysfunction. Some of the

<!-- chunk -->

### cells themselves can actually die and not get replaced. All of this results in a

<!-- chunk -->

### reduction in the reserve capacity of different brain regions. Any of these can

<!-- chunk -->

### lead to mental disorders, Alzheimer’s disease, or seizures.

<!-- chunk -->

### What about people who show signs of a more subtle mental disorder,

<!-- chunk -->

### say depression, during an ICU stay? If depression is also due to

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondrial dysfunction, then we should expect that depression would be

<!-- chunk -->

### associated with higher death rates or seizures. Is this true? Yes, it is. Recall

<!-- chunk -->

### that I discussed the research showing that people who are depressed after a

<!-- chunk -->

### heart attack are twice as likely to have another one over the following year.

<!-- chunk -->

### And elderly people with depression are six times more likely to have

<!-- chunk -->

### seizures. Similar research has been done in patients with a wide variety of

<!-- chunk -->

### medical illnesses. After an ICU stay, patients with depression were

<!-- chunk -->

### percent more likely to die than those without depression in the two years

<!-- chunk -->

### following discharge.


<!-- chunk -->

### This and other research suggests that any mental

<!-- chunk -->

### symptoms, even if not formally diagnosed as delirium, are associated with

<!-- chunk -->

### higher rates of premature death. Arguably, mental symptoms are like the

<!-- chunk -->

### canary in the coal mine; they are sometimes the first indication of metabolic

<!-- chunk -->

### and mitochondrial failure.

<!-- chunk -->

### What about people with longstanding mental disorders? If their disorders

<!-- chunk -->

### are truly due to mitochondrial dysfunction, that should leave them more

<!-- chunk -->

### vulnerable to developing delirium if, in fact, delirium is due to

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondrial dysfunction. Is this true? Yes, it is. Recall the Danish

<!-- chunk -->

### population study of more than seven million people.


<!-- chunk -->

### That study found that

<!-- chunk -->

### people with mental disorders—all of them—were more likely to develop

<!-- chunk -->

### “organic” mental disorders, which includes the diagnoses of delirium and

<!-- chunk -->

### dementia. All told, depending upon the diagnosis, people were two to

<!-- chunk -->

### twenty times more likely to develop these disorders. Chronic mental

<!-- chunk -->

### illnesses are like a warning light in a car. They give us a window into a

<!-- chunk -->

### person’s metabolic health. They tell us that the brain isn’t working right due

<!-- chunk -->

### to metabolic or mitochondrial dysfunction. If we ignore it, sometimes it will

<!-- chunk -->

### correct itself. If it continues and we turn a blind eye to it, symptoms and

<!-- chunk -->

### other illnesses will usually follow.

<!-- chunk -->

### If the example of delirium didn’t convince you, maybe this one will—

<!-- chunk -->

### the dying process. In some medical schools, students are taught a mantra of

<!-- chunk -->

### the dying process—“seizures, coma, death.” This is the sequence of events

<!-- chunk -->

### that commonly occur as people are dying. What this leaves out is delirium,

<!-- chunk -->

### which is almost universal. People will commonly hallucinate, become

<!-- chunk -->

### disoriented, have mood symptoms, or develop any of the other mental

<!-- chunk -->

### symptoms. Their brains are failing because the mitochondria in their brain

<!-- chunk -->

### cells are failing. The dying process is unequivocally associated with

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondrial failure. This short sequence of events—delirium, seizures,

<!-- chunk -->

### coma, and death—highlights all the consequences of mitochondrial

<!-- chunk -->

### dysfunction that I have been discussing. It highlights the paradox of both

<!-- chunk -->

### reduced cell function and hyperexcitable cells in the context of rapid

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondrial failure, culminating in death.

# The Question of Language and Our Path Toward

# Treatment

<!-- chunk -->

### The theory of brain energy suggests that all mental disorders have the

<!-- chunk -->

### common pathway of mitochondria. When mitochondria aren’t working

<!-- chunk -->

### right,

<!-- chunk -->

### neither is the brain. If this is true, how much do our diagnostic labels

<!-- chunk -->

### matter? What should we call mental disorders?

<!-- chunk -->

### Our current diagnostic labels will likely persist for some time. Change

<!-- chunk -->

### is difficult and takes time. Furthermore, our current diagnoses do provide

<!-- chunk -->

### some useful information. They describe constellations of symptoms that

<!-- chunk -->

### people exhibit. Symptoms matter. They will require different treatments—at

<!-- chunk -->

### least different symptomatic treatments.

<!-- chunk -->

### However, given the overlap discussed between the diagnoses, and the

<!-- chunk -->

### fact that people with the exact same diagnoses can have different

<!-- chunk -->

### symptoms, there is clearly room for improvement. Mitochondrial

<!-- chunk -->

### dysfunction or dysregulation provides an explanation for an unlimited

<!-- chunk -->

### number of symptoms in different people. We’ve seen that depending upon

<!-- chunk -->

### which brain cells and brain networks are involved, and which factors are

<!-- chunk -->

### affecting the function of mitochondria, people will have different

<!-- chunk -->

### symptoms. This establishes that a change in the way we think about mental

<!-- chunk -->

### disorders is warranted.

<!-- chunk -->

### One simple model would be to call all mental disorders delirium.

<!-- chunk -->

### Maybe we separate transient delirium and chronic delirium. The transient

<!-- chunk -->

### type resolves within two to three months, and the chronic type lasts longer.

<!-- chunk -->

### This label would remind all clinicians that they need to keep looking for the

<!-- chunk -->

### cause or causes of the metabolic brain dysfunction instead of simply

<!-- chunk -->

### administering symptomatic treatments. This would largely follow current

<!-- chunk -->

### treatment protocols for delirium already in place, but it would expand these

<!-- chunk -->

### protocols to all who are currently labeled “mentally ill.”

<!-- chunk -->

### As some will resist the use of “delirium” for all mental disorders, we

<!-- chunk -->

### could, alternatively, call all mental disorders “metabolic brain dysfunction”

<!-- chunk -->

### and add specifiers for the different symptoms that people are experiencing.

<!-- chunk -->

### For example, someone with prominent anxiety symptoms might receive the

<!-- chunk -->

### diagnosis of “metabolic brain dysfunction with anxiety symptoms.” A

<!-- chunk -->

### person with schizophrenia might receive the diagnosis of “metabolic brain

<!-- chunk -->

### dysfunction with psychotic, depressive, and cognitive symptoms.” In all

<!-- chunk -->

### cases, the primary diagnosis would remain the same, “metabolic brain

<!-- chunk -->

### dysfunction,” but the symptoms would change as treatments work or as the

<!-- chunk -->

### illness progresses or remits. Instead of having multiple diagnoses, as is

<!-- chunk -->

### commonly the case now, people would have one disorder, metabolic brain

<!-- chunk -->

### dysfunction, with the different symptoms of this disorder.

# The Brain Energy Theory . . . in a Nutshell

<!-- chunk -->

### Here’s a quick recap of the brain energy theory:

<!-- chunk -->

### Mental disorders are metabolic disorders of the brain. Although most

<!-- chunk -->

### people think of metabolism as burning calories, it’s much more than that.

<!-- chunk -->

### Metabolism affects the structure and function of all cells in the human body.

<!-- chunk -->

### Regulators of metabolism include many things, such as epigenetics,

<!-- chunk -->

### hormones, neurotransmitters, and inflammation. Mitochondria are the

<!-- chunk -->

### master regulators of metabolism, and they play a role in controlling the

<!-- chunk -->

### factors just listed. When mitochondria aren’t working properly, at least

<!-- chunk -->

### some of the cells in your body or brain won’t function properly.

<!-- chunk -->

### Symptoms of mental illness can be understood as overactive,

<!-- chunk -->

### underactive, or absent brain functions. Mitochondrial dysfunction or

<!-- chunk -->

### dysregulation can cause all of these through five distinct mechanisms: (1)

<!-- chunk -->

### cell activity can be overactive; (2) cell activity can be underactive; (3) some

<!-- chunk -->

### cells can develop abnormally (leading to absent brain functions); (4) cells

<!-- chunk -->

### can shrink and die (also leading to absent brain functions); and (5) cells can

<!-- chunk -->

### have problems maintaining themselves (which can contribute to

<!-- chunk -->

### overactivity, underactivity, or absent brain functions). For example, if the

<!-- chunk -->

### cells that control anxiety are overactive, you will have anxiety symptoms. If

<!-- chunk -->

### the cells that control memory are underactive, you will have memory

<!-- chunk -->

### impairment. If metabolic problems occur at a young age, the brain can

<!-- chunk -->

### develop abnormally, which can occur in autism. If metabolic problems

<!-- chunk -->

### occur over long periods of time, cells can shrink and die, which is found in

<!-- chunk -->

### most chronic mental disorders and Alzheimer’s disease. And finally,

<!-- chunk -->

### maintenance problems can

<!-- chunk -->

### leave cells in a state of disrepair and can contribute to any of these other

<!-- chunk -->

### problems.

<!-- chunk -->

### So, you’re likely wondering what causes metabolic and mitochondrial

<!-- chunk -->

### dysfunction or dysregulation. The answer is . . . lots of things. The good

<!-- chunk -->

### news is that many of them are probably already familiar to you. They will

<!-- chunk -->

### be the focus of the next and final section of the book. The exciting news is

<!-- chunk -->

### that most of them can be identified and addressed.

# Part III

# Causes and Solutions


# What’s Causing the Problem and

# What Can We Do?

<!-- chunk -->

### t’s time to fully revisit the known risk factors and theories for what

<!-- chunk -->

### causes mental illness through the new lens of brain energy, or

<!-- chunk -->

### metabolism and mitochondria. If all mental disorders are metabolic

<!-- chunk -->

### disorders and if mitochondria are really the common pathway, then all

<!-- chunk -->

### known risk factors for mental illness must tie directly to metabolism and

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondria, fitting together somehow. We must see evidence of cause and

<!-- chunk -->

### effect. Most of what I’m about to run through are well-established and

<!-- chunk -->

### irrefutable risk factors. Until now, though, no one has been able to connect

<!-- chunk -->

### them. In the following chapters, I will connect each one of them to

<!-- chunk -->

### metabolism and mitochondria, proving the link that has long been missing

<!-- chunk -->

### in the mental health field.

<!-- chunk -->

### The term risk factor is appropriate when cause-and-effect relationships

<!-- chunk -->

### are unknown. The brain energy theory changes this. Therefore, I will start

<!-- chunk -->

### using the term contributing causes instead of risk factors. For most people,

<!-- chunk -->

### there are several contributing causes that come together to result in illness;

<!-- chunk -->

### it’s not usually just one single root cause.

<!-- chunk -->

### A quick note on terminology: In this section, I’ll sometimes refer to

<!-- chunk -->

### metabolism and other times talk about mitochondria. They are closely

<!-- chunk -->

### related, but they are not the same thing. Going back to our traffic analogy, it

<!-- chunk -->

### might help to think of the difference this way: Metabolism is the flow of

<!-- chunk -->

### traffic, and mitochondria are the drivers and workers inside the cars. As

<!-- chunk -->

### discussed earlier, while the drivers are primarily responsible for the flow of

<!-- chunk -->

### traffic, they aren’t the only factor. Traffic is also affected by environment,

<!-- chunk -->

### weather, and unforeseen obstacles, including things like daylight or

<!-- chunk -->

### nighttime driving; rain, snow, or hailstorms; road construction; and other

<!-- chunk -->

### factors that are beyond the control of the drivers but require drivers to

<!-- chunk -->

### respond as well. So, mitochondria are always involved in metabolism, but

<!-- chunk -->

### metabolic problems or challenges are not always due to mitochondrial

<!-- chunk -->

### “dysfunction.” Sometimes the mitochondria are doing what they are

<!-- chunk -->

### supposed to be doing under the circumstances, but the environment poses

<!-- chunk -->

### metabolic challenges. To give an easy example, a woman could be

<!-- chunk -->

### metabolically healthy, but if she takes a hallucinogen, she might begin to

<!-- chunk -->

### hallucinate right away. The drug dysregulates her metabolism and

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondria, causing symptoms, but it’s unfair to mitochondria to say they

<!-- chunk -->

### are “dysfunctional.” They are simply doing their best under the

<!-- chunk -->

### circumstances, just like drivers in cars might be doing their best in a

<!-- chunk -->

### hailstorm.

<!-- chunk -->

### Many of the contributing causes I will discuss simply slow down

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondria and their function. Some of them, however, are outright

<!-- chunk -->

### assaults. Some can destroy the mitochondria in cells. Some can impair their

<!-- chunk -->

### ability to produce energy. Some can impair their ability to perform other

<!-- chunk -->

### functions, such as fusing with each other or sending signals to the DNA.

<!-- chunk -->

### While some of these causes may be minor and go unnoticed initially,

<!-- chunk -->

### together with the occurrence of additional assaults they can result in enough

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondrial impairment to produce mental symptoms. Others can be

<!-- chunk -->

### decisive and catastrophic assaults on mitochondria that result in severe

<!-- chunk -->

### mental symptoms immediately—such as mitochondrial poisons. These

<!-- chunk -->

### serious assaults often affect more than just the brain (all the cells in the

<!-- chunk -->

### body can be affected, for instance), and life-threatening situations can

<!-- chunk -->

### sometimes result.

<!-- chunk -->

### Some of the contributing causes stimulate mitochondria and increase

<!-- chunk -->

### their production of energy, at least in the short run. This can sometimes

<!-- chunk -->

### be beneficial. It can improve symptoms of reduced cell function, such as

<!-- chunk -->

### fatigue. At other times, however, it can result in problems of too much

<!-- chunk -->

### energy. This can be as simple as the inability to sleep at night after drinking

<!-- chunk -->

### coffee. Caffeine stimulates mitochondria. But remember hyperexcitable

<!-- chunk -->

### cells? If they get too much energy, it can mean trouble—anxiety, psychosis,

<!-- chunk -->

### or seizures. As different as these symptoms are, you’ll be surprised to see

<!-- chunk -->

### that sometimes one factor can trigger all of them. Prescription stimulants,

<!-- chunk -->

### such as Ritalin or Adderall, stimulate mitochondria. They can provide

<!-- chunk -->

### appropriate symptom relief in some people. However, they can also cause

<!-- chunk -->

### anxiety, psychosis, or seizures in other people.

<!-- chunk -->

### There are three important themes to note when reviewing the different

<!-- chunk -->

### contributing causes:

<!-- chunk -->

### 1.All directly impact metabolism and mitochondria.

<!-- chunk -->

### 2.All are associated with a wide variety of symptoms of mental

<!-- chunk -->

### disorders. Not one of them is specific to any one disorder or

<!-- chunk -->

### symptom. This is consistent with the observation that all mental

<!-- chunk -->

### disorders have one common pathway: mitochondria.

<!-- chunk -->

### 3.All are also associated with metabolic and neurological disorders—

<!-- chunk -->

### obesity, diabetes, cardiovascular disease, Alzheimer’s disease, and

<!-- chunk -->

### epilepsy. They are also associated with many other medical

<!-- chunk -->

### diagnoses, but I will focus on these five. The factors that I will be

<!-- chunk -->

### discussing can trigger exacerbations of these “physical” disorders as

<!-- chunk -->

### well. This supports the observation that mental disorders share a

<!-- chunk -->

### common pathway with these medical and neurological disorders.

<!-- chunk -->

### I am not going to present an exhaustive scientific review of each factor.

<!-- chunk -->

### There is a tremendous amount of science to support each one. My goal here

<!-- chunk -->

### is to provide a broad overview for how all these contributing causes relate

<!-- chunk -->

### to metabolism, mitochondria, and mental health.

<!-- chunk -->

### I will begin with the biological factors and end with psychological and

<!-- chunk -->

### social factors. This is not to imply that biological factors are more

<!-- chunk -->

### important.

<!-- chunk -->

### In many cases, they aren’t. However, reviewing the biological factors first

<!-- chunk -->

### will set the stage for how psychological and social ones affect metabolism

<!-- chunk -->

### and mitochondria.

# Why Different People Have Different Symptoms and

# Disorders—And Why Connections Persist Across

# Them

<!-- chunk -->

### I began to address this issue in Part Two, but it’s worth revisiting two

<!-- chunk -->

### questions as we move into looking at specific contributing causes and

<!-- chunk -->

### treatments. First:

<!-- chunk -->

### If all mental disorders are due to mitochondrial dysfunction or

<!-- chunk -->

### dysregulation, why is there so much variability in symptoms? How do

<!-- chunk -->

### malfunctioning mitochondria and metabolic toll cause one person to end up

<!-- chunk -->

### with depression, and another with OCD, for instance?

<!-- chunk -->

### There are two primary answers:

<!-- chunk -->

## 1.Differences in preexisting vulnerabilities. All people are different.

<!-- chunk -->

### This includes identical twins. Even when the genetic code is the same

<!-- chunk -->

### for two people, they are still different. At the end of the day, we are

<!-- chunk -->

### all products of our biological blueprints (genetics) and our past

<!-- chunk -->

### experiences and environmental exposures. Nature and nurture.

<!-- chunk -->

### Experiences and exposures include the psychological and social

<!-- chunk -->

### experiences we’ve had, but they also include metabolic

<!-- chunk -->

### environmental exposures. These begin at conception. Our bodies are

<!-- chunk -->

### constantly responding to the environment and its access to nutrients

<!-- chunk -->

### and oxygen, hormones, temperature, light, and so many other factors.

<!-- chunk -->

### These all affect our metabolism and mitochondria, but specific

<!-- chunk -->

### factors affect some cells and not others. Over time, we develop parts

<!-- chunk -->

### of our brains and bodies that are strong and resilient, but also ones

<!-- chunk -->

### that are weaker and more vulnerable to failure. Metabolic failure of

<!-- chunk -->

### specific cells or brain networks is what causes mental symptoms,

<!-- chunk -->

### so these areas of vulnerability influence which symptoms develop

<!-- chunk -->

### first. In essence, our metabolism is only as strong as its weakest link.

<!-- chunk -->

### Think of it like the muscles of your body. Some of your muscles

<!-- chunk -->

### are stronger than others. If you have to lift something very heavy—a

<!-- chunk -->

### major stressor—the weakest muscles will likely fail first. This can

<!-- chunk -->

### be different in different people. If three people lift the same heavy

<!-- chunk -->

### object, one might sprain a wrist, another might pull a muscle in his

<!-- chunk -->

### leg, and a third might throw out her back. Same stressor—different

<!-- chunk -->

### symptoms—that will require different treatments due to different

<!-- chunk -->

### vulnerabilities.

<!-- chunk -->

## 2.Differences in inputs. Cells and the mitochondria within them are

<!-- chunk -->

### affected by many inputs, ones that impact different parts of the body

<!-- chunk -->

### and brain at different times. In the following chapters, I will discuss

<!-- chunk -->

### contributing causes that affect mitochondria in one way or another.

<!-- chunk -->

### Most of these are well-known risk factors for mental illness. Some of

<!-- chunk -->

### them will impact all the cells in the body and brain. However, most

<!-- chunk -->

### won’t. Many of these factors affect only some cells but not others.

<!-- chunk -->

### When faced with different situations or tasks, different body parts or

<!-- chunk -->

### brain regions need different amounts of energy. If energy were

<!-- chunk -->

### equally distributed throughout the body and brain, not only would it

<!-- chunk -->

### be a waste of energy for the cells that don’t need it, but it would

<!-- chunk -->

### divert precious energy from the cells that do need it. This means that

<!-- chunk -->

### some of these contributing causes will impact some brain regions but

<!-- chunk -->

### not others. This can lead to different symptoms.

<!-- chunk -->

### The answers here lead directly to a second question:

<!-- chunk -->

### If there are clear differences in individual vulnerabilities and other

<!-- chunk -->

### factors that lead to differences in dysfunction, how are all mental and

<!-- chunk -->

### metabolic disorders related? Why should having metabolic problems in one

<!-- chunk -->

### type of cell have anything to do with the function of other types of cells?

<!-- chunk -->

### To answer this, let’s go back to our analogy of metabolism: traffic in a

<!-- chunk -->

### city. There are many factors that determine whether city traffic flows

<!-- chunk -->

### smoothly or not. The same is true with metabolism. And at the same time,

<!-- chunk -->

### it’s all interconnected.

<!-- chunk -->

### A traffic problem can start in one small section of the city—a single car

<!-- chunk -->

### accident blocking a busy street. Likewise, a metabolic problem can start in

<!-- chunk -->

### one group of cells that cause symptoms related to what those cells do. The

<!-- chunk -->

### problem can start off contained to only those cells due to preexisting

<!-- chunk -->

### vulnerabilities and/or different inputs as I just outlined.

<!-- chunk -->

### If the problems persist, however, the symptoms can spread. When it

<!-- chunk -->

### comes to city traffic, if the car accident isn’t quickly removed, the traffic

<!-- chunk -->

### jam will become more widespread, affecting traffic in other parts of the city.

<!-- chunk -->

### If traffic problems are caused by poor road maintenance, then it can result

<!-- chunk -->

### in long-term problems. Metabolism is the same. A problem in one area of

<!-- chunk -->

### the body will often spread over time. Why? Because metabolism is highly

<!-- chunk -->

### interconnected. It relies on feedback loops all over the body. So, if one area

<!-- chunk -->

### is not doing well, the rest of the body can be affected. If the problem isn’t

<!-- chunk -->

### corrected, it will gradually take a toll and spread—sometimes over years or

<!-- chunk -->

### decades.

# Treatments and Success Stories

<!-- chunk -->

### For each of the contributing causes of mental illness, I will outline some

<!-- chunk -->

### strategies that can be used to address problems when possible. Some of

<!-- chunk -->

### them are standard, existing treatments. (Again, a new theory doesn’t replace

<!-- chunk -->

### what we already know to work.) Some of them will be new treatments that

<!-- chunk -->

### you likely haven’t considered before. As a rule of thumb, they fall into the

<!-- chunk -->

### following broad categories:

<!-- chunk -->

### 1.Treatments that remove or reduce things that are dysregulating

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondria or metabolism, such as poor diet, sleep disturbances,

<!-- chunk -->

### alcohol or drug use, some medications, or psychological/social

<!-- chunk -->

### stressors.

<!-- chunk -->

### 2.

<!-- chunk -->

### Treatments that correct for metabolic imbalances, such as

<!-- chunk -->

### neurotransmitter or hormonal imbalances.

<!-- chunk -->

### 3.Treatments that improve metabolism. I break these strategies into

<!-- chunk -->

### three categories:

<!-- chunk -->

### •Mitochondrial biogenesis—There are ways to increase the

<!-- chunk -->

### number of mitochondria in your cells. Increasing the workforce

<!-- chunk -->

### improves metabolic capacity.

<!-- chunk -->

### •Mitophagy—Getting rid of old, defective mitochondria and

<!-- chunk -->

### replacing them with new, healthy ones can also play a role.

<!-- chunk -->

### Rejuvenating the workforce improves metabolism.

<!-- chunk -->

### •Autophagy—Repairing structural damage that has occurred to

<!-- chunk -->

### your cells because of longstanding metabolic problems can be

<!-- chunk -->

### essential to long-term healing.

<!-- chunk -->

### In Chapter Twenty, I’ll give you an overarching approach and basic

<!-- chunk -->

### strategies to use in developing a comprehensive treatment plan. Please hold

<!-- chunk -->

### off on implementing any of the treatments that I discuss until you get to the

<!-- chunk -->

### very end of the book. You’ll need to understand all the different

<!-- chunk -->

### contributing causes and treatment approaches before deciding which ones

<!-- chunk -->

### will be appropriate for you.

<!-- chunk -->

### Along the way, I will also share stories of real people who have

<!-- chunk -->

### improved their mental health using metabolic interventions. Their names

<!-- chunk -->

### have been changed to protect their privacy, but their stories are true.

# M


# Genetics and Epigenetics

<!-- chunk -->

### ental disorders run in families. This has been known for centuries

<!-- chunk -->

### and established now as fact based on tremendous amounts of

<!-- chunk -->

### research. This observation has led many people to conclude that the

<!-- chunk -->

### root cause of mental illness, at least for some people, must lie in their genes.

<!-- chunk -->

### When an illness runs in families, it’s largely assumed to be genetic, because

<!-- chunk -->

### genes are the way that information gets transmitted from parents to

<!-- chunk -->

### children. We now know that it’s not so simple.

# Genetics

<!-- chunk -->

### From 1990 to 2003, an international community of researchers began one of

<!-- chunk -->

### the largest scientific undertakings of our time: the Human Genome Project

<!-- chunk -->

### (HGP). Researchers began to sequence and map all the human genes—three

<!-- chunk -->

### billion letters, or base pairs, of DNA. The whole world was

<!-- chunk -->

### excited and full of hope about the potential for ending all kinds of illness,

<!-- chunk -->

### particularly those thought or known to be inherited. In psychiatry, we

<!-- chunk -->

### imagined that with a complete genetic blueprint, we could identify the

<!-- chunk -->

### genes responsible for each psychiatric disorder, find out what protein

<!-- chunk -->

### they’re coding for, develop medications to address the problems, and

<!-- chunk -->

### possibly even find cures.

<!-- chunk -->

### Since the HGP was completed, researchers have discovered about 1,800

<!-- chunk -->

### genes responsible for various diseases and developed about two thousand

<!-- chunk -->

### genetic tests that can measure an individual’s genetic risk for certain

<!-- chunk -->

### diseases. Some patients can be tested to see if they will metabolize

<!-- chunk -->

### medications too slowly or fast. The effort has paid off in many ways. But

<!-- chunk -->

### sadly, not in psychiatry.

<!-- chunk -->

### The search for the genes that cause mental illnesses has largely come up

<!-- chunk -->

### empty-handed. It’s not for a lack of trying. Researchers have scoured the

<!-- chunk -->

### human genome, looking at the genes for neurotransmitters, the enzymes

<!-- chunk -->

### that make them, and their receptors. These were the obvious targets—the

<!-- chunk -->

### chemicals in the chemical imbalance theory—serotonin, dopamine, and

<!-- chunk -->

### others. Unfortunately, no meaningful relationships have been found

<!-- chunk -->

### between these genes and mental illness.

<!-- chunk -->

### Next, researchers decided to scan the entire genome for genes that

<!-- chunk -->

### might be related to mental illness via genome-wide association studies

<!-- chunk -->

### (GWAS). They kept an open mind and looked at every gene, even ones

<!-- chunk -->

### seemingly unrelated to the brain or psychiatry, in order to find the genes

<!-- chunk -->

### that cause mental illness. After these exhaustive searches, researchers

<!-- chunk -->

### identified a plethora of genes that might be related to psychiatric disorders,

<!-- chunk -->

### but they found almost no genes that confer significant risk to a significant

<!-- chunk -->

### portion of the people with the disorders. Some very rare genes were

<!-- chunk -->

### identified that can confer high levels of risk, but for most people with

<!-- chunk -->

### mental disorders, specific genes don’t confer large amounts of risk. On top

<!-- chunk -->

### of that, the vast majority of genes that have been found are not specific to

<!-- chunk -->

### individual disorders. Instead, they confer risk for many different

<!-- chunk -->

### psychiatric, metabolic, and neurological disorders. For example, some risk

<!-- chunk -->

### genes for psychosis confer risk for schizophrenia, bipolar disorder, autism,

<!-- chunk -->

### developmental delay, intellectual

<!-- chunk -->

### disability, and epilepsy.


<!-- chunk -->

### So, one gene confers risk for many disorders. For

<!-- chunk -->

### major depression, there is debate in the field, with some studies suggesting

<!-- chunk -->

### that there are genes that confer tiny amounts of risk (I’ll tell you about a

<!-- chunk -->

### couple of them soon), but other studies suggest that not even one gene has

<!-- chunk -->

### been found that confers significant risk, despite looking at more than 1.2

<!-- chunk -->

### million genetic variations in human DNA.


<!-- chunk -->

### The disappointment of not finding genetic answers to these inheritable

<!-- chunk -->

### disorders isn’t limited to psychiatry. It also applies to the metabolic

<!-- chunk -->

### disorders of obesity, diabetes, and cardiovascular disease. These, too, run in

<!-- chunk -->

### families—often the same families that have mental illness. These

<!-- chunk -->

### conditions, too, have eluded easy answers in human DNA.

<!-- chunk -->

### Even though thousands of genes confer tiny amounts of risk, how can

<!-- chunk -->

### we understand this in the context of the brain energy theory? If

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondria and metabolism account for all mental disorders, what do

<!-- chunk -->

### genes have to do with mental illness at all?

<!-- chunk -->

### To begin with, many of these risk genes are directly related to

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondria and metabolism. For example, a gene called DISC1 confers

<!-- chunk -->

### risk for schizophrenia, bipolar disorder, depression, and autism. Researchers

<!-- chunk -->

### continue to look at all the roles this protein plays in cell function, but it has

<!-- chunk -->

### been found within mitochondria and is known to influence mitochondrial

<!-- chunk -->

### movement, fusion, and contact with other parts of the cell. This, in turn,

<!-- chunk -->

### affects neuron development and plasticity.


<!-- chunk -->

### One of the strongest risk genes

<!-- chunk -->

### for mood disorders, CACNA1C, plays a major role in oxidative stress and

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondrial integrity and function.


<!-- chunk -->

### Another example is the APOE gene, which increases the risk for

<!-- chunk -->

### Alzheimer’s disease. This gene codes for a protein, Apolipoprotein E, that

<!-- chunk -->

### is related to fat and cholesterol transportation and metabolism. The gene

<!-- chunk -->

### comes in three forms: APOE2, APOE3, and APOE4. About 25 percent of

<!-- chunk -->

### people carry one copy of APOE4, and 2 to 3 percent have two copies.

<!-- chunk -->

### Those with one copy are three to four times more likely to develop

<!-- chunk -->

### Alzheimer’s, and those with two copies are about nine to fifteen times more

<!-- chunk -->

### likely.


<!-- chunk -->

### In support of the brain energy theory, this gene strongly impacts

<!-- chunk -->

### both metabolism and mitochondria. People in their twenties who have the

<!-- chunk -->

### APOE4 gene

<!-- chunk -->

### are more likely to already show signs of impaired glucose metabolism in

<!-- chunk -->

### their brains, and this impairment worsens over time.


<!-- chunk -->

### Apolipoprotein E appears to have direct effects on mitochondria.

<!-- chunk -->

### Researchers looked at people who had different APOE types and measured

<!-- chunk -->

### proteins that impact mitochondrial biogenesis, dynamics (their fusion and

<!-- chunk -->

### fission with each other), and oxidative stress.


<!-- chunk -->

### Those with the APOE4 allele

<!-- chunk -->

### had lower levels of these important mitochondrial proteins, and the levels

<!-- chunk -->

### correlated directly with symptoms of Alzheimer’s. Another study looked at

<!-- chunk -->

### astrocytes, important support cells in the brain, and found that APOE4

<!-- chunk -->

### impairs autophagy, mitochondrial function, and mitophagy.


<!-- chunk -->

### The good news

<!-- chunk -->

### is that they found that giving a drug that stimulates autophagy could reverse

<!-- chunk -->

### some of these abnormalities.

<!-- chunk -->

### So, does APOE4 increase risk for all of the metabolic and mental

<!-- chunk -->

### disorders? It does increase the risk for cardiovascular disease, some other

<!-- chunk -->

### mental disorders, and epilepsy, but paradoxically, it appears to decrease the

<!-- chunk -->

### risk for obesity and type 2 diabetes.


<!-- chunk -->

### This is where the complexity of

<!-- chunk -->

### metabolism comes in. Apolipoprotein E is not found equally in all cells in

<!-- chunk -->

### the human body. In the brain, it’s found primarily in astrocytes and

<!-- chunk -->

### microglia. These cells have specific functions. It appears that APOE4

<!-- chunk -->

### results in a very slow and gradual decline in their function, and these cells

<!-- chunk -->

### are more closely related to cognitive symptoms than other symptoms. So,

<!-- chunk -->

### these “parts” of the brain are the ones that will wear down and begin to fail

<!-- chunk -->

### over time, resulting in specific symptoms. However, once they begin to fail

<!-- chunk -->

### completely, then the other mental symptoms of Alzheimer’s begin due to

<!-- chunk -->

### the interconnections of these brain regions. So, as I discussed, this is an

<!-- chunk -->

### example of a preexisting vulnerability (a risk gene) and different inputs to

<!-- chunk -->

### different cells, both playing a role in causing some symptoms and not

<!-- chunk -->

### others. Nonetheless, this line of research still directly implicates metabolism

<!-- chunk -->

### and mitochondria as the common pathway to Alzheimer’s.

<!-- chunk -->

### Mitochondrial genetics are also complicated because mitochondria are

<!-- chunk -->

### influenced by genes in both the nucleus and within mitochondria

<!-- chunk -->

### themselves. The genes within mitochondria are much more susceptible to

<!-- chunk -->

### mutations. Genetic mutations in mitochondria have been directly linked to

<!-- chunk -->

### numerous aspects of brain function, including behavior, cognition, food

<!-- chunk -->

### intake, and the stress response.


<!-- chunk -->

### Unfortunately, mitochondrial genes have

<!-- chunk -->

### not been widely looked at in large population studies because most

<!-- chunk -->

### researchers have assumed that mitochondrial genes don’t matter all that

<!-- chunk -->

### much.

<!-- chunk -->

### Even for genes related to other proteins supporting other functions in

<!-- chunk -->

### cells, they, too, will have an impact on metabolism. Genes are the blueprints

<!-- chunk -->

### for different proteins that make up the human body. Just like different parts

<!-- chunk -->

### of a car, and all the variations of these parts in different makes and models

<!-- chunk -->

### of cars, some are more reliable and fuel efficient than others. Some are

<!-- chunk -->

### adapted for adversity and have shorter lives while others are adapted for

<!-- chunk -->

### fuel efficiency and longevity. Depending upon which ones you inherit, they

<!-- chunk -->

### can influence the function of your cells, your metabolism, and your overall

<!-- chunk -->

### health. They can also confer different degrees of susceptibility to metabolic

<!-- chunk -->

### failure. When it comes to metabolism, there is always a “weakest link.”

<!-- chunk -->

### When the parts of a cell are different, some are going to be more vulnerable

<!-- chunk -->

### to failure than others.

<!-- chunk -->

### With this said and done, it is all but certain that for the majority of

<!-- chunk -->

### people with mental illness, the answer to their problem does not lie in the

<!-- chunk -->

### genes themselves. If genes don’t fully explain why mental disorders run in

<!-- chunk -->

### families, then what could it be?

# Epigenetics

<!-- chunk -->

### Epigenetics, which we covered briefly in Part Two, is the field dedicated to

<!-- chunk -->

### understanding what causes genes to turn on or off. Most of us have similar

<!-- chunk -->

### genes. We have essentially the same blueprints for how the body should

<!-- chunk -->

### work. Yes, there are obvious variations, such as height, skin color, and hair

<!-- chunk -->

### color. These are due to differences in our genes. But most of our genes are

<!-- chunk -->

### basically the same. The human body works in the same way in most people.

<!-- chunk -->

### However, what is clearly different is the expression of all these genes.

<!-- chunk -->

### Skin cells, brain cells, and liver cells all have the same DNA. However,

<!-- chunk -->

### epigenetics is responsible for making the different cells in the human body

<!-- chunk -->

### different from each other. These different cells express different genes.

<!-- chunk -->

### Throughout the day, genes are getting turned on or off in cells. This is

<!-- chunk -->

### constantly changing based on environmental circumstances and the needs of

<!-- chunk -->

### the body. In other words, the body is constantly adapting. Sometimes it

<!-- chunk -->

### needs to make a hormone, so those genes get turned on. Sometimes it needs

<!-- chunk -->

### to repair a cell, so those genes get turned on. Once done, these genes get

<!-- chunk -->

### turned off. Cells don’t waste resources.

<!-- chunk -->

### There are changes in gene expression that appear to be longer lasting

<!-- chunk -->

### than these constantly fluctuating ones. Some changes in gene expression are

<!-- chunk -->

### associated with traits in people. Some people have big muscles, others are

<!-- chunk -->

### skinny, while others are obese. Gene expression is different in these people

<!-- chunk -->

### over longer periods of time, even though they all have similar underlying

<!-- chunk -->

### genes. There are specific patterns of gene expression that have been

<!-- chunk -->

### associated with different traits, both physical and mental. These

<!-- chunk -->

### longstanding epigenetic changes are a way for the body to come up with a

<!-- chunk -->

### metabolic strategy and then stick with it. Epigenetics provides a memory of

<!-- chunk -->

### what the body has been through.

<!-- chunk -->

### There are many ways that the body controls gene expression. One way

<!-- chunk -->

### is to modify the DNA itself by applying methyl groups to specific sites on

<!-- chunk -->

### DNA. These methyl groups then influence which genes get turned on or off.

<!-- chunk -->

### The methyl groups can be added or removed as needed, but at least at some

<!-- chunk -->

### sites, they appear to become more stable over time. Another way the body

<!-- chunk -->

### influences gene expression is through histones. These are proteins that

<!-- chunk -->

### DNA gets wrapped around. They, too, influence which genes get turned on

<!-- chunk -->

### or off. In addition to methylation and histones, there are many other factors

<!-- chunk -->

### that are involved in epigenetics. More and more are being discovered every

<!-- chunk -->

### year. They include factors like micro-RNAs, hormones, neuropeptides, and

<!-- chunk -->

### others. This field quickly becomes confusing and overwhelming, as there

<!-- chunk -->

### are so many factors that are involved in the epigenetic control of our DNA.

<!-- chunk -->

### However, if you take a step back and look at the field from a broader

<!-- chunk -->

### perspective, things become less confusing. What influences epigenetics?

<!-- chunk -->

### What triggers all these different factors to change gene expression? Almost

<!-- chunk -->

### all of them revolve around metabolism and mitochondria. Factors thought

<!-- chunk -->

### to influence epigenetics include diet, exercise, drug and alcohol use,

<!-- chunk -->

### hormones, light exposure, and sleep—all related to metabolism and

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondria (as you will soon learn). As a specific example, smokers tend

<!-- chunk -->

### to have less DNA methylation of the AHRR gene compared to

<!-- chunk -->

### nonsmokers.


<!-- chunk -->

### However, if they stop smoking, this change in methylation is

<!-- chunk -->

### reversible.

<!-- chunk -->

### In the end, it’s important to think about epigenetics as metabolic

<!-- chunk -->

### blueprints for cells. Epigenetics simply reflect the gene patterns that allow

<!-- chunk -->

### cells to do their best to survive and cope with their environments. However,

<!-- chunk -->

### if they get stuck in a maladaptive pattern or if the appropriate signals aren’t

<!-- chunk -->

### being sent, that can become problematic.

<!-- chunk -->

### Recall that mitochondria are regulators of epigenetics. They influence

<!-- chunk -->

### gene expression through levels of ROS, levels of glucose and amino acids,

<!-- chunk -->

### and levels of ATP. Also recall that mitochondria appear to control the

<!-- chunk -->

### expression of essentially all genes for a cell. I already told you about the

<!-- chunk -->

### study that found that as the number of defective mitochondria in a cell

<!-- chunk -->

### increases, the number of gene expression abnormalities also increases.

<!-- chunk -->

### It turns out that epigenetic factors are heritable. This occurs in different

<!-- chunk -->

### ways. I’ll discuss a few of them.

<!-- chunk -->

## Womb Environment

<!-- chunk -->

### As a fetus is growing inside the womb, it is bathed in metabolic signals.

<!-- chunk -->

### Food, oxygen, vitamins, and minerals play an obvious and critical role.

<!-- chunk -->

### However, the mother’s hormones, neuropeptides, use of alcohol, drugs,

<!-- chunk -->

### prescription medications, and so many other factors are also playing roles.

<!-- chunk -->

### One example of epigenetics playing a clear role in the transmission of

<!-- chunk -->

### both metabolic and mental disorders is the famous Dutch winter famine,

<!-- chunk -->

### which took place between 1944 and 1945 due to the German occupation of

<!-- chunk -->

### the Netherlands. Researchers have studied the babies that were conceived or

<!-- chunk -->

### carried during this famine and compared them to the general population and

<!-- chunk -->

### even their own siblings who were born when the mothers had normal access

<!-- chunk -->

### to food. The babies born during the famine were more likely to develop

<!-- chunk -->

### both metabolic and mental disorders later in life. This and other

<!-- chunk -->

### studies led to the thrifty phenotype hypothesis, which proposes that babies

<!-- chunk -->

### deprived of proper nutrition in utero are more likely to develop obesity,

<!-- chunk -->

### diabetes, and cardiovascular disease later in life. Unfortunately, this

<!-- chunk -->

### hypothesis overlooks or ignores the fact that these babies are also more

<!-- chunk -->

### likely to develop mental disorders. These babies were found to have double

<!-- chunk -->

### the risk for schizophrenia and antisocial personality disorder, as well as

<!-- chunk -->

### increased rates of depression, bipolar disorder, and addiction.


<!-- chunk -->

### Researchers

<!-- chunk -->

### have been studying the pancreas to understand the elevated rates of

<!-- chunk -->

### diabetes, the heart to understand the elevated rates of cardiovascular

<!-- chunk -->

### disease, and the brain to understand the psychiatric and neurological

<!-- chunk -->

### disorders but have failed to see the metabolic connection among all of them.

<!-- chunk -->

## Early Life

<!-- chunk -->

### Some of the factors that regulate epigenetics, metabolism, and mitochondria

<!-- chunk -->

### get transferred to the baby after birth through behaviors and early life

<!-- chunk -->

### experiences. Many studies have looked at caregiver behaviors toward

<!-- chunk -->

### infants early in life and their impact on long-term health outcomes. They

<!-- chunk -->

### typically align with the ACEs studies that I already described. Caregiver

<!-- chunk -->

### neglect and deprivation have profound effects on children for life. They

<!-- chunk -->

### include both metabolic and mental disorders. Epigenetic mechanisms play a

<!-- chunk -->

### role in all of this.

<!-- chunk -->

### A concrete example down to the molecular level is the passing of a

<!-- chunk -->

### metabolic factor from mothers to children in breast milk. One such

<!-- chunk -->

### molecule is nicotinamide adenine dinucleotide, or NAD. This is a critically

<!-- chunk -->

### important coenzyme that can be derived from vitamin B3 (niacin), or the

<!-- chunk -->

### body can make it using the amino acid tryptophan from protein. It is

<!-- chunk -->

### essential to mitochondria for energy production but also plays a role in the

<!-- chunk -->

### maintenance of DNA and epigenetics. Low levels of this enzyme are known

<!-- chunk -->

### to impair mitochondrial function and cause epigenetic changes, and have

<!-- chunk -->

### been associated with aging and many diseases.


<!-- chunk -->

### One group of researchers

<!-- chunk -->

### looked at mice and the long-term outcomes of their babies after

<!-- chunk -->

### supplementing new mothers with NAD or not.


<!-- chunk -->

### The mothers who got extra

<!-- chunk -->

### NAD lost more weight postpartum, a nice metabolic benefit. But their

<!-- chunk -->

### babies really benefited from this! The baby mice had improved blood

<!-- chunk -->

### glucose control, physical performance,

<!-- chunk -->

### and many brain changes, including less anxiety, improved memory, reduced

<!-- chunk -->

### signs of “learned helplessness” (a marker of depression), and they even had

<!-- chunk -->

### greater neurogenesis into adulthood. Clearly, this metabolic/mitochondrial

<!-- chunk -->

### coenzyme given in infancy impacted their brains and “mental” symptoms

<!-- chunk -->

### for life. Mothers will naturally have different levels of this coenzyme that

<!-- chunk -->

### they transfer to their babies.

<!-- chunk -->

## Intergenerational Transmission of Trauma

<!-- chunk -->

### The most widely studied phenomenon of how epigenetics relate to mental

<!-- chunk -->

### health is the intergenerational transmission of trauma. Dr. Rachel Yehuda,

<!-- chunk -->

### a leading authority in this field, outlined decades of research in a

<!-- chunk -->

### comprehensive review article: “Intergenerational Transmission of Trauma

<!-- chunk -->

### Effects: Putative Role of Epigenetic Mechanisms.”


<!-- chunk -->

### This field dates back

<!-- chunk -->

### to 1966, when an astute psychiatrist, Dr. Vivian Rakoff, noticed that

<!-- chunk -->

### children of Holocaust survivors appeared to sometimes have more severe

<!-- chunk -->

### forms of mental illness than their parents, even though it was the parents

<!-- chunk -->

### who spent time in the concentration camps. She asserted that somehow

<!-- chunk -->

### these things were connected. Many at the time didn’t believe this. Those

<!-- chunk -->

### who did assumed that the parents must somehow be teaching their children

<!-- chunk -->

### to be afraid, anxious, or depressed, and this must be the reason for the

<!-- chunk -->

### connection. It must be a psychological or social cause. Numerous studies

<!-- chunk -->

### followed and began to identify a pattern of parental trauma and poor mental

<!-- chunk -->

### health outcomes in their children—and even their grandchildren. But still,

<!-- chunk -->

### almost everyone assumed that this was due to upbringing. The parents must

<!-- chunk -->

### be teaching their children to be stressed and afraid of the world.

<!-- chunk -->

### This assumption was first challenged in the 1980s when researchers

<!-- chunk -->

### discovered differences in how people respond to cortisol. People exposed to

<!-- chunk -->

### trauma—and their children—have different levels of sensitivity to

<!-- chunk -->

### glucocorticoids. In particular, exposure to high levels of cortisol in utero

<!-- chunk -->

### appear to “program” children, resulting in higher risk for developing mental

<!-- chunk -->

### and metabolic disorders later in life. With the genetic and epigenetic

<!-- chunk -->

### revolution came the discovery that many of these people have differences in

<!-- chunk -->

### methylation patterns of the glucocorticoid receptor and other DNA regions

<!-- chunk -->

### (promoter

<!-- chunk -->

### regions) associated with the stress response system. Most recently, it has

<!-- chunk -->

### been discovered that even fathers might be passing on their traumatic

<!-- chunk -->

### experiences through epigenetic mechanisms in sperm, such as micro-RNA

<!-- chunk -->

### (miRNA) molecules, which are known to modify gene expression. Studies

<!-- chunk -->

### have now shown that sperm in both mice and men have miRNAs that get

<!-- chunk -->

### transmitted to offspring. Levels of specific miRNAs (449 and 34) have been

<!-- chunk -->

### shown to be directly affected by levels of stress dating all the way back to

<!-- chunk -->

### early childhood experiences of the fathers.


<!-- chunk -->

### In mice who are exposed to

<!-- chunk -->

### early stressful life events, these levels were dramatically reduced in sperm

<!-- chunk -->

### cells, and their male offspring also had these same low levels in their sperm

<!-- chunk -->

### cells, demonstrating transgenerational transmission of stress. In human

<!-- chunk -->

### studies, men were given the ACEs questionnaire, and it turned out that men

<!-- chunk -->

### with the highest levels of stressful life events had the lowest levels of these

<!-- chunk -->

### exact same miRNAs, up to a three-hundredfold reduction.

<!-- chunk -->

### The timing of the stress appears to matter and can influence brain

<!-- chunk -->

### function in different ways.


<!-- chunk -->

### Fetal exposure to a mother’s stress results in

<!-- chunk -->

### higher rates of learning impairment, depression, and anxiety later in life. In

<!-- chunk -->

### the first few years of life, separating a child from his/her mother can result

<!-- chunk -->

### in higher levels of cortisol throughout life, while severe abuse can result in

<!-- chunk -->

### lower levels of cortisol. Although paradoxical, both states take a metabolic

<!-- chunk -->

### toll and can be tied directly to mitochondria, given that mitochondria

<!-- chunk -->

### initiate production of cortisol.

<!-- chunk -->

### This line of research continues to this day, but what it clearly

<!-- chunk -->

### demonstrates is that epigenetics appears to be playing a significant role in

<!-- chunk -->

### the transmission of mental disorders from parents to children, and even

<!-- chunk -->

### grandchildren.

# What Genetics and Epigenetics Can Tell Us About

# Causes—and Treatment

<!-- chunk -->

### Although some have been disappointed with our inability to find specific

<!-- chunk -->

### genes related to mental disorders, at the end of the day, I believe it’s a good

<!-- chunk -->

### thing. We now know that there are usually not “abnormal” genes that cause

<!-- chunk -->

### mental illness. It’s much more likely that the transmission of mental illness

<!-- chunk -->

### from parents to children takes place through epigenetic mechanisms. The

<!-- chunk -->

### hopeful aspect of this insight is that most of these epigenetic mechanisms

<!-- chunk -->

### are known to be reversible!

<!-- chunk -->

### The effects of in utero stress, micro-RNA levels, and levels of NAD are

<!-- chunk -->

### changeable, sometimes through lifestyle interventions alone. The other

<!-- chunk -->

### hopeful aspect of this is that people are not usually born with “bad genes”

<!-- chunk -->

### that make it impossible for them to be healthy.

<!-- chunk -->

### Recall my analogy of the three cars—A, B, and C. They were all the

<!-- chunk -->

### same make and model, and therefore, they all had the same blueprints (or

<!-- chunk -->

### genes). But they were very different from each other. The two primary

<!-- chunk -->

### reasons for the differences in the health, maintenance, and longevity of the

<!-- chunk -->

### cars were (1) the environment and (2) a dysfunctional driver applying

<!-- chunk -->

### adaptive strategies at the wrong times or failing to use adaptive strategies

<!-- chunk -->

### when needed. In human terms, this means that the primary causes of mental

<!-- chunk -->

### illness are usually not in our genes, but instead in our environments or the

<!-- chunk -->

### drivers of our cells, mitochondria. So, you’re likely wondering what makes

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondria dysfunctional. I’ll get to many of these factors in the

<!-- chunk -->

### remainder of the book.

<!-- chunk -->

### Even for people with the APOE4 gene allele—one that impairs

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondrial function over time—there is hope for healing. Not everyone

<!-- chunk -->

### with this gene develops Alzheimer’s disease. I mentioned the study that

<!-- chunk -->

### found that increasing autophagy can lessen the problem. I will get to more

<!-- chunk -->

### treatments in the following chapters, including some that specifically

<!-- chunk -->

### improve autophagy, but for now, understand that mental illnesses, even ones

<!-- chunk -->

### like bipolar disorder and schizophrenia, are likely not due to genetic defects

<!-- chunk -->

### that are permanent and fixed. Metabolic problems are reversible.


# Chemical Imbalances,

# Neurotransmitters, and Medications

<!-- chunk -->

### now come back to the chemical imbalance theory. The theory of brain

<!-- chunk -->

### energy doesn’t challenge the observations that neurotransmitter

<!-- chunk -->

### imbalances play a role in mental disorders, nor does it challenge the

<!-- chunk -->

### clinical trials that have demonstrated improvement in symptoms using

<!-- chunk -->

### medications that affect neurotransmitters. And I certainly don’t mean to

<!-- chunk -->

### challenge the real-world experience of the many people who have been

<!-- chunk -->

### helped, or even saved, by psychiatric medications. All of these are true and

<!-- chunk -->

### based on an abundance of evidence. However, as I’ve already pointed out,

<!-- chunk -->

### the chemical imbalance theory leaves many questions unanswered and fails

<!-- chunk -->

### to restore lives for far too many people.

<!-- chunk -->

### The brain energy theory offers new ways to understand neurotransmitter

<!-- chunk -->

### imbalances and the effects of medications. Mitochondria and metabolism

<!-- chunk -->

### explain the underactivity and overactivity/hyperexcitability of specific brain

<!-- chunk -->

### cells that result in these imbalances and the problem of either too much or

<!-- chunk -->

### too little neurotransmitter activity. However, neurotransmitters also go on to

<!-- chunk -->

### produce their own effects in target cells, resulting in stimulation or

<!-- chunk -->

### inhibition of mitochondria in those cells. This quickly becomes like a chain

<!-- chunk -->

### of dominoes, where metabolic disruption in one set of cells results in

<!-- chunk -->

### problems in other cells.

<!-- chunk -->

### Many people talk about neurotransmitters as simple entities with simple

<!-- chunk -->

### functions. Serotonin makes us feel good. Dopamine drives psychosis and

<!-- chunk -->

### addiction. Norepinephrine helps us focus. Although there is some truth to

<!-- chunk -->

### these statements, these simplistic views of neurotransmitters and the

<!-- chunk -->

### disorders associated with them are almost farcical. The brain,

<!-- chunk -->

### neurotransmitters, and mental disorders are all much more complicated than

<!-- chunk -->

### that.

<!-- chunk -->

### Neurotransmitters are not just simple on/off signals between cells.

<!-- chunk -->

### Research in the past decade has greatly expanded our view of their role in

<!-- chunk -->

### metabolism and mitochondrial function. Neurotransmitters and

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondria are in a feedback cycle with each other. Mitochondria affect

<!-- chunk -->

### the balance of neurotransmitters. Neurotransmitters affect the balance of

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondria and their function.

<!-- chunk -->

### As mentioned in Chapter Seven, mitochondria play a key role in the

<!-- chunk -->

### production of many neurotransmitters, including acetylcholine, glutamate,

<!-- chunk -->

### norepinephrine, dopamine, GABA, and serotonin. Mitochondria also have

<!-- chunk -->

### receptors for some important neurotransmitters directly on their

<!-- chunk -->

### membranes, such as benzodiazepine and GABA receptors. These aren’t

<!-- chunk -->

### present on all mitochondria in all cells, but they have been identified in at

<!-- chunk -->

### least some cell types. Mitochondria also have one important enzyme known

<!-- chunk -->

### to most psychiatrists: monoamine oxidase. This enzyme is involved in the

<!-- chunk -->

### degradation and regulation of some very important neurotransmitters, such

<!-- chunk -->

### as dopamine, epinephrine, and norepinephrine. All these neurotransmitters

<!-- chunk -->

### directly impact the function of mitochondria, and mitochondria directly

<!-- chunk -->

### impact the balance of these neurotransmitters.

<!-- chunk -->

### Serotonin, a neurotransmitter best known for its role in depression and

<!-- chunk -->

### anxiety disorders, has a very prominent and complex role in metabolism

<!-- chunk -->

### and mitochondrial function.


<!-- chunk -->

### It is a primitive and highly conserved

<!-- chunk -->

### neurotransmitter found in all animals, worms, insects, fungi, and plants. It is

<!-- chunk -->

### known to control appetite, digestive tract functions, and metabolism of

<!-- chunk -->

### nutrients broadly. About 90 percent of the serotonin in the human body is

<!-- chunk -->

### actually located in the digestive tract, not the brain. Recent research has

<!-- chunk -->

### demonstrated a direct role of serotonin in regulating the production and

<!-- chunk -->

### function of mitochondria within cortical neurons, enhancing their

<!-- chunk -->

### production of ATP and decreasing oxidative stress.


<!-- chunk -->

### So, not only does

<!-- chunk -->

### serotonin increase mitochondrial function immediately, it results in

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondrial biogenesis—one of the ways to improve metabolism! In

<!-- chunk -->

### addition to this clear and direct link, there’s even more to the story.

<!-- chunk -->

### Serotonin is converted into melatonin, an important hormone in the

<!-- chunk -->

### regulation of sleep, which plays a powerful role in metabolism as well.

<!-- chunk -->

### Serotonin is also the product of an important metabolic pathway, the

<!-- chunk -->

### kynurenine pathway, that involves the fate of the amino acid tryptophan.

<!-- chunk -->

### When people eat protein that contains tryptophan, it has many possible

<!-- chunk -->

### fates. Two important ones are getting converted into serotonin or

<!-- chunk -->

### kynurenine. Kynurenine eventually leads to higher levels of the critically

<!-- chunk -->

### important molecule NAD that I already told you about. NAD has a

<!-- chunk -->

### profound influence on the health and function of mitochondria because it is

<!-- chunk -->

### essential to energy production and managing electrons. Problems with the

<!-- chunk -->

### kynurenine metabolic pathway have been found in many psychiatric and

<!-- chunk -->

### neurological disorders, including depression, schizophrenia, anxiety

<!-- chunk -->

### disorders, Tourette’s syndrome, dementia, and others. Obviously,

<!-- chunk -->

### medications that affect serotonin levels will have a direct impact on

<!-- chunk -->

### metabolism and mitochondria through all these mechanisms. This fact

<!-- chunk -->

### likely explains why and how these medications work for disorders like

<!-- chunk -->

### depression and anxiety.

<!-- chunk -->

### GABA is also an important neurotransmitter with a wide range of

<!-- chunk -->

### functions. It is best known for its role in anxiety disorders because

<!-- chunk -->

### medications that increase GABA activity, such as Valium, Klonopin, and

<!-- chunk -->

### Xanax, produce a calming, anti-anxiety effect. However, abnormalities of

<!-- chunk -->

### GABA

<!-- chunk -->

### neurotransmission have also been found in other disorders, including

<!-- chunk -->

### schizophrenia and autism. Mitochondria directly influence and sometimes

<!-- chunk -->

### control GABA activity. One group of researchers found that mitochondrial

<!-- chunk -->

### ROS levels regulate the strength of GABA activity.


<!-- chunk -->

### Fascinatingly, another research group demonstrated a more direct link

<!-- chunk -->

### between GABA, mitochondria, and mental symptoms. This study was done

<!-- chunk -->

### in flies and involved a known, but rare, genetic defect associated with both

<!-- chunk -->

### autism and schizophrenia. The researchers showed that mitochondria

<!-- chunk -->

### actually sequester GABA inside themselves, thereby directly controlling its

<!-- chunk -->

### release. When this process was prevented by the genetic defect, social

<!-- chunk -->

### deficits resulted. When the researchers corrected GABA levels or the

<!-- chunk -->

### function of mitochondria, the social deficits were corrected. These

<!-- chunk -->

### researchers directly tied a known, but rare, genetic defect to mitochondrial

<!-- chunk -->

### function, GABA, and social deficit symptoms.


<!-- chunk -->

### GABA doesn’t just affect mental functions, it also plays a role in

<!-- chunk -->

### metabolic disorders like obesity. One group of researchers found that

<!-- chunk -->

### GABA plays an important role in brown adipose tissue, a special type of fat

<!-- chunk -->

### that gets turned on when you get cold and also plays an important role in

<!-- chunk -->

### overall body metabolism. Problems with GABA signaling in this type of fat

<!-- chunk -->

### result in mitochondrial calcium overload and metabolic abnormalities often

<!-- chunk -->

### found in people who are obese.


<!-- chunk -->

### So, these few examples illustrate how

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondria can control GABA activity, and GABA activity can affect

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondrial function, creating a feedback cycle.

<!-- chunk -->

### One final example is dopamine. Dopamine is released from neurons,

<!-- chunk -->

### binds to receptors, and then is usually taken back up into the releasing

<!-- chunk -->

### neuron for another round. However, some of it ends up inside cells and

<!-- chunk -->
