### needs to be managed . . . by, you guessed it, mitochondria. They have the

<!-- chunk -->

### enzyme, monoamine oxidase, that degrades it. This process directly

<!-- chunk -->

### stimulates mitochondria to produce more ATP.


<!-- chunk -->

### But there’s more to the

<!-- chunk -->

### story. A recent discovery showed that dopamine is directly involved in the

<!-- chunk -->

### regulation of glucose and metabolism.


<!-- chunk -->

### The dopamine D2 receptor is well

<!-- chunk -->

### known to most psychiatrists because almost all antipsychotic medications

<!-- chunk -->

### affect this specific receptor. We now know that dopamine D2 receptors

<!-- chunk -->

### aren’t located just

<!-- chunk -->

### in the brain, they are also found in the pancreas and play a critical role in

<!-- chunk -->

### the release of insulin and glucagon. It has long been known that

<!-- chunk -->

### antipsychotic medications can affect weight, diabetes, and metabolism. The

<!-- chunk -->

### science is now catching up to explain why. More intriguing, however, is the

<!-- chunk -->

### possibility that these effects on insulin might be playing a direct role in the

<!-- chunk -->

### antipsychotic effect. It may have nothing to do with dopamine D2 receptors

<!-- chunk -->

### in the brain. I’ll share more about insulin and why this might be possible in

<!-- chunk -->

### the next chapter.

<!-- chunk -->

### These few examples illustrate some of the connections between

<!-- chunk -->

### neurotransmitters, mitochondria, and metabolism.

# Psychiatric Medications, Metabolism, and

# Mitochondria

<!-- chunk -->

### Medications that increase or decrease the levels of serotonin, GABA, or

<!-- chunk -->

### dopamine will clearly have an impact on mitochondria and metabolism

<!-- chunk -->

### through the mechanisms that I have already outlined. This includes many

<!-- chunk -->

### classes of antidepressants, anti-anxiety medications, and antipsychotics.

<!-- chunk -->

### As an example, we all know that Valium can reduce anxiety. One study

<!-- chunk -->

### looked directly at the impact of Valium on anxiety and social dominance

<!-- chunk -->

### behaviors in rats to determine precisely how it was working.


<!-- chunk -->

### researchers already knew that reduced mitochondrial function in an area of

<!-- chunk -->

### the brain called the nucleus accumbens (NAc) causes social anxiety

<!-- chunk -->

### behaviors, so they wanted to determine if Valium was somehow affecting

<!-- chunk -->

### this area. They found that Valium was working by activating another area of

<!-- chunk -->

### the brain called the ventral tegmental area (VTA), which sends dopamine to

<!-- chunk -->

### the NAc. This dopamine increases mitochondrial function in the NAc,

<!-- chunk -->

### resulting in higher ATP levels, and this leads to reduced anxiety and

<!-- chunk -->

### enhanced social dominance. When the researchers blocked the effects of

<!-- chunk -->

### dopamine, this therapeutic effect was lost. But here’s the kicker—when they

<!-- chunk -->

### blocked mitochondrial respiration in the NAc, the therapeutic effects were

<!-- chunk -->

### also lost, even though those cells were still getting the enhanced dopamine.

<!-- chunk -->

### These

<!-- chunk -->

### researchers concluded that their findings “highlight mitochondrial function

<!-- chunk -->

### as a potential therapeutic target for anxiety-related social dysfunctions.”


<!-- chunk -->

### Different medications affect mitochondria in vastly different ways. One

<!-- chunk -->

### review article, “Effect of Neuropsychiatric Medications on Mitochondrial

<!-- chunk -->

### Function: For Better or For Worse,”


<!-- chunk -->

### highlights a paradox: some

<!-- chunk -->

### medications appear to improve mitochondrial functions while others impair

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondrial functions.

<!-- chunk -->

### Monoamine oxidase inhibitors, a class of antidepressants, increase the

<!-- chunk -->

### amounts of epinephrine, norepinephrine, and dopamine right outside of

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondria. These stimulate mitochondrial activity. Lithium, a mood

<!-- chunk -->

### stabilizer, has been found to increase ATP production, enhance antioxidant

<!-- chunk -->

### capacities, and improve calcium signaling within cells, all related to


<!-- chunk -->

### Quite a few antipsychotic medications are known to cause serious

<!-- chunk -->

### neurological problems, sometimes permanent ones, like tremors, muscle

<!-- chunk -->

### rigidity, and tardive dyskinesia (TD), an involuntary movement disorder.

<!-- chunk -->

### Many studies have documented impairment in mitochondrial function at the

<!-- chunk -->

### cellular level, including decreased energy production and increased

<!-- chunk -->

### oxidative stress, caused by these medications.


<!-- chunk -->

### One study looked at spinal

<!-- chunk -->

### fluid from patients with schizophrenia, some of whom had TD, and found a

<!-- chunk -->

### direct correlation between markers of impaired mitochondrial energy

<!-- chunk -->

### metabolism and the symptoms of TD.


<!-- chunk -->

### These and many other researchers

<!-- chunk -->

### have concluded that mitochondrial dysfunction is the most likely

<!-- chunk -->

### explanation for these neurological side effects.

<!-- chunk -->

### In my own work with patients for more than twenty-five years now, I

<!-- chunk -->

### have seen firsthand how psychiatric medications can impair metabolism.

<!-- chunk -->

### Weight gain, metabolic syndrome, diabetes, cardiovascular disease, and

<!-- chunk -->

### even premature death are all well-known side effects for many of these

<!-- chunk -->

### medications.

<!-- chunk -->

### How does this make sense? If mental symptoms are due to

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondrial dysfunction/dysregulation, how can impairing them further

<!-- chunk -->

### reduce mental symptoms?

<!-- chunk -->

### The answer comes down to hyperexcitable cells. When a cell is

<!-- chunk -->

### hyperexcitable, there are two ways to reduce symptoms:

<!-- chunk -->

### 1.

<!-- chunk -->

### Improve mitochondrial function and energy production so that the

<!-- chunk -->

### cell can repair itself and function normally again. However, this

<!-- chunk -->

### strategy comes with the risk that symptoms might get worse initially,

<!-- chunk -->

### given that hyperexcitable cells can’t stop themselves sometimes.

<!-- chunk -->

### Therefore, when they get more energy initially, they may not be

<!-- chunk -->

### prepared to manage it appropriately, and hyperexcitability may occur.

<!-- chunk -->

### 2.Manage these cells by shutting them down—in other words,

<!-- chunk -->

### suppress their function by inhibiting their mitochondria. This will

<!-- chunk -->

### stop symptoms, at least in the short run. However, this strategy

<!-- chunk -->

### comes with the risk that it might make matters worse over time

<!-- chunk -->

### because it may worsen the mitochondrial dysfunction.

<!-- chunk -->

### This is obviously a very concerning situation. A treatment that can help in

<!-- chunk -->

### the short run might make things worse in the long run. Unfortunately, the

<!-- chunk -->

### dilemma of hyperexcitability isn’t even this simple. The brain is

<!-- chunk -->

### complicated, and so is this issue. There are two other considerations:

<!-- chunk -->

### 1.All cells are likely not impacted in the same ways. Recall that cells

<!-- chunk -->

### have different inputs. Medications target specific cells. Some cells

<!-- chunk -->

### may have improved mitochondrial function, while other cells may

<!-- chunk -->

### not be affected, while still others may have impairment. In all the

<!-- chunk -->

### studies done, the researchers had to choose specific cells to study.

<!-- chunk -->

### They certainly didn’t study all the cells in the brain and body.

<!-- chunk -->

### 2.Even if medications impair mitochondrial function broadly, we need

<!-- chunk -->

### to consider the consequences of not treating the person.

<!-- chunk -->

### Hyperexcitable cells spewing out lots of neurotransmitters, such as

<!-- chunk -->

### glutamate or dopamine, are known to be toxic to the brain. The

<!-- chunk -->

### overall benefits to the person may still outweigh the risks. An

<!-- chunk -->

### extreme example is when someone is seizing—they are clearly

<!-- chunk -->

### suffering from a case of hyperexcitable brain cells. Stopping them is

<!-- chunk -->

### of paramount importance. People can die if they seize too long. And

<!-- chunk -->

### in fact, many epilepsy treatments, such as Depakote, are known to

<!-- chunk -->

### impair mitochondrial function, which then slows, and, with hope,

<!-- chunk -->

### stops the hyperexcitability.


<!-- chunk -->

### I know that people crave simple answers to dilemmas like this. “So,

<!-- chunk -->

### should people take medications that are known to impair mitochondria? Yes

<!-- chunk -->

### or no?” Unfortunately, I can’t offer a universal answer to this question

<!-- chunk -->

### because different situations require different interventions. Clearly, in

<!-- chunk -->

### dangerous or life-threatening situations, these medications can be

<!-- chunk -->

### lifesaving. However, I’ve mapped out some of the issues to consider. The

<!-- chunk -->

### good news is that these questions can be addressed in research studies, so

<!-- chunk -->

### more research might better inform our treatment approaches in the future.

<!-- chunk -->

### What is clear already, however, is that suppressing mitochondrial function

<!-- chunk -->

### long-term is not a path to healing. At best, it’s a path to reducing symptoms.

<!-- chunk -->

### The theory of brain energy answers numerous questions that the mental

<!-- chunk -->

### health field has been unable to answer to date. It outlines why medications

<!-- chunk -->

### that target serotonin, norepinephrine, and dopamine can all be used to treat

<!-- chunk -->

### depression. They all enhance the function of mitochondria. A logical

<!-- chunk -->

### question, then, is “Why doesn’t everyone respond to the same

<!-- chunk -->

### medications?” This comes back to preexisting vulnerabilities and different

<!-- chunk -->

### inputs to different cells. For example, the symptoms of depression come

<!-- chunk -->

### from many brain regions, not just one. Brain circuits are connected and

<!-- chunk -->

### communicate with each other. If one area is malfunctioning, it will impact

<!-- chunk -->

### the other areas, too. Some will be more responsive to serotonin while others

<!-- chunk -->

### will be more responsive to norepinephrine, and yet they are connected. So,

<!-- chunk -->

### if one brain region is metabolically compromised, it will impact the other

<!-- chunk -->

### brain regions, just like a traffic jam in one part of the city slowly backing up

<!-- chunk -->

### traffic in other parts of the city. Metabolic problems are connected and can

<!-- chunk -->

### spread.

<!-- chunk -->

### This theory also helps us understand why medications take time to

<!-- chunk -->

### work. For example, SSRIs are likely working by increasing mitochondrial

<!-- chunk -->

### biogenesis and improving the function of mitochondria. This process takes

<!-- chunk -->

### time; it doesn’t occur overnight, even though SSRIs increase serotonin in

<!-- chunk -->

### a matter of hours. It’s not the serotonin per se that results in improvement,

<!-- chunk -->

### but the impact that serotonin has on mitochondria and metabolism.

<!-- chunk -->

### Restoring metabolic health takes time—probably about two to six weeks—

<!-- chunk -->

### which is the time it usually takes for SSRIs to start working as well.

<!-- chunk -->

### We can also understand why one medication can be used for a wide

<!-- chunk -->

### variety of disorders. For example, antipsychotic medications can be used

<!-- chunk -->

### for schizophrenia, bipolar disorder, depression, anxiety, insomnia, and

<!-- chunk -->

### agitation in dementia because they reduce hyperexcitability in many cell

<!-- chunk -->

### types. Suppressing mitochondrial function can stop the problematic

<!-- chunk -->

### symptoms. But anyone who has taken these medications knows that they

<!-- chunk -->

### come with side effects, like reduced function in cognitive areas of the brain

<!-- chunk -->

### and increased appetite. In the elderly, they even come with a warning of

<!-- chunk -->

### increased risk of death.

<!-- chunk -->

### Furthermore, we can now understand why some psychiatric medications

<!-- chunk -->

### can induce other symptoms, such as antidepressants inducing anxiety,

<!-- chunk -->

### mania, and psychosis in some people. Antidepressants generally increase

<!-- chunk -->

### energy in the brain. In people with preexisting vulnerabilities who already

<!-- chunk -->

### have metabolically compromised cells, this can quickly lead to

<!-- chunk -->

### hyperexcitability and associated symptoms.

<!-- chunk -->

### On top of the usual psychiatric medications, the brain energy theory

<!-- chunk -->

### offers reasons why “metabolic” medications might also play a role in

<!-- chunk -->

### mental health. Interestingly, psychiatrists have been using some of these for

<!-- chunk -->

### decades now.

<!-- chunk -->

### Many blood pressure medications, such as clonidine, prazosin, and

<!-- chunk -->

### propranolol, are used in psychiatry. These medications are prescribed for a

<!-- chunk -->

### wide variety of disorders, including ADHD, PTSD, anxiety disorders,

<!-- chunk -->

### substance use disorders, and Tourette’s syndrome.

<!-- chunk -->

### One study looked at three classes of “metabolic” medications in more

<!-- chunk -->

### than 140,000 patients with schizophrenia, bipolar disorder, or other

<!-- chunk -->

### psychotic disorders to see if these medications had any impact on self-harm

<!-- chunk -->

### or the need for psychiatric hospitalization.


<!-- chunk -->

### They found that they did. The

<!-- chunk -->

### drug classes included “statins” for cholesterol (hydroxylmethyl glutaryl

<!-- chunk -->

### coenzyme A reductase inhibitors), blood pressure medicines (L-type

<!-- chunk -->

### calcium channel

<!-- chunk -->

### antagonists), and diabetes medicines like metformin (biguanides). Across

<!-- chunk -->

### the board, these medications had an impact on the “mental” metrics,

<!-- chunk -->

### especially in reducing self-harm. The brain energy theory offers

<!-- chunk -->

### explanations for why these might help. Statins are known to impair

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondrial function and reduce inflammation, calcium channel blockers

<!-- chunk -->

### reduce hyperexcitability by decreasing the amount of calcium in cells, and

<!-- chunk -->

### metformin is also known to play a direct role in mitochondrial function.

<!-- chunk -->

### Metformin gets confusing quickly, however, as the effects appear to be

<!-- chunk -->

### dependent upon the dose. Most studies have found that metformin impairs

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondrial function, but some have found an increase in mitochondrial

<!-- chunk -->

### biogenesis and ATP production.


<!-- chunk -->

### Finally, I want to point out that reducing or stopping psychiatric

<!-- chunk -->

### medications can be difficult and dangerous. This always needs to be done

<!-- chunk -->

### with the supervision of a medical professional. Symptoms can get worse

<!-- chunk -->

### quickly and new symptoms can emerge. Many patients become acutely

<!-- chunk -->

### depressed, suicidal, manic, or psychotic when they stop medications

<!-- chunk -->

### abruptly or decrease them too rapidly. This doesn’t mean that people can’t

<!-- chunk -->

### discontinue medications; it just means that it isn’t something to undertake

<!-- chunk -->

### on your own.


<!-- chunk -->

### •Psychiatric medications have helped countless people with mental

<!-- chunk -->

### disorders. They will continue to play a role for many.

<!-- chunk -->

### •The brain energy theory offers new ways to understand how and

<!-- chunk -->

### why medications work.

<!-- chunk -->

### •It’s important to understand what impact your medications are

<!-- chunk -->

### having on your metabolism and mitochondria.

<!-- chunk -->

### •Medications that increase metabolism and improve mitochondrial

<!-- chunk -->

### function can improve symptoms of underactive cells, but they come

<!-- chunk -->

### with the risk of exacerbating symptoms related to overactive or

<!-- chunk -->

### hyperexcitable cells.

<!-- chunk -->

### •

<!-- chunk -->

### Medications that impair mitochondrial function should be used

<!-- chunk -->

### cautiously. Although it’s clear how and why these can reduce

<!-- chunk -->

### symptoms of hyperexcitable cells in the short run, it’s possible that

<!-- chunk -->

### they might interfere with your ability to heal and recover in the long

<!-- chunk -->

### run. In some cases, they might even be the cause of symptoms.

<!-- chunk -->

### Nonetheless, in dangerous and life-threatening situations, these

<!-- chunk -->

### medications can be lifesaving.

# Success Story: Jane—Agitated in the Nursing Home

<!-- chunk -->

### Early in my career, I worked in some nursing homes as a psychiatric

<!-- chunk -->

### consultant. One person that I met was Jane, an eighty-one-year-old woman

<!-- chunk -->

### with Alzheimer’s disease. I was asked to see her for “agitation.” The nurses

<!-- chunk -->

### reported that she would stay up at night screaming, and at other times sleep

<!-- chunk -->

### for more than twelve hours at a stretch. Her screaming was disturbing the

<!-- chunk -->

### other residents, and they wanted me to prescribe something to stop this

<!-- chunk -->

### behavior. It had been going on for more than six months, and she had

<!-- chunk -->

### already been prescribed five tranquilizing medications, including two

<!-- chunk -->

### antipsychotics and anti-anxiety medications. Nothing was working. A

<!-- chunk -->

### medical workup had already been conducted and they couldn’t find

<!-- chunk -->

### anything wrong with her.

<!-- chunk -->

### I met with Jane for all of five minutes in the dining room, where she

<!-- chunk -->

### was propped into an adult high chair. When I sat down to speak with her,

<!-- chunk -->

### she couldn’t understand me. She was saying random words and phrases

<!-- chunk -->

### (something called “word salad” in psychiatry), and she was smearing her

<!-- chunk -->

### food all over herself and her high chair. I had enough information to make

<!-- chunk -->

### my diagnosis. She was delirious. The most likely cause? The sedating

<!-- chunk -->

### medications. I wrote my note and instructed the physician to get her off as

<!-- chunk -->

### many of those medications as possible, as quickly as possible, but to be

<!-- chunk -->

### mindful that some might need to be slowly tapered. The physician ended up

<!-- chunk -->

### stopping most of them right away.

<!-- chunk -->

### I came back three weeks later. As I was walking down the hall, I was

<!-- chunk -->

### confronted by an elderly woman I had never met before. She asked me if I

<!-- chunk -->

### was Dr. Palmer, and I said I was. She reached out and hugged me, with tears

<!-- chunk -->

### in her eyes, and thanked me profusely for saving her sister. I told her that

<!-- chunk -->

### she must be mistaken; I didn’t know her or her sister. She then told me that

<!-- chunk -->

### her sister was Jane. It turns out that for the past couple of years, she had

<!-- chunk -->

### been visiting Jane three times a week. They used to have pleasant

<!-- chunk -->

### conversations and share meals, but the last six months had been a

<!-- chunk -->

### nightmare. Jane was angry, confused, and just not “human” anymore. This

<!-- chunk -->

### was heartbreaking for her sister to witness. But about ten days ago, her

<!-- chunk -->

### sister told me, things began to change. Jane had stopped screaming and her

<!-- chunk -->

### sleep was improving. She knew her sister again, and they could have

<!-- chunk -->

### conversations.

<!-- chunk -->

### Anyone who has visited a nursing home knows this is not a rare story. It

<!-- chunk -->

### represents a common dilemma: people with dementia can become agitated

<!-- chunk -->

### and disruptive for a variety of reasons—an infection, poor sleep, or even a

<!-- chunk -->

### seemingly minor stressor, such as moving to a new room. These can all

<!-- chunk -->

### cause delirium. Jane was likely delirious when her symptoms began six

<!-- chunk -->

### months before I met her, before she was prescribed any psychiatric

<!-- chunk -->

### medications. Her screaming and sleep disruption were the reasons that these

<!-- chunk -->

### medications were prescribed in the first place. And they likely helped, at

<!-- chunk -->

### least temporarily. The nurses and physicians likely saw that these

<!-- chunk -->

### medications sedated Jane and decreased her screaming, and so they

<!-- chunk -->

### continued to prescribe them. When the symptoms came back, they tried

<!-- chunk -->

### increasing the doses or adding new medications.

<!-- chunk -->

### On the surface, it’s understandable how Jane ended up on so many

<!-- chunk -->

### medications. However, several of them are known to impair mitochondrial

<!-- chunk -->

### function. This means that they can help in the short run, but they come with

<!-- chunk -->

### the risk of making matters worse in the long run. That appears to be what

<!-- chunk -->

### happened to Jane. By the time I saw her, whatever caused her initial

<!-- chunk -->

### delirium had probably passed, and she was delirious because of the

<!-- chunk -->

### treatments she was receiving.

<!-- chunk -->

### Most healthcare professionals know that tranquilizing medications can

<!-- chunk -->

### sometimes make elderly people delirious. What’s going to be more difficult

<!-- chunk -->

### for the mental health field to grapple with is the possibility that this might

<!-- chunk -->

### happen in young people, too. The brain energy theory suggests that it could,

<!-- chunk -->

### and my own clinical practice for the past twenty-five years suggests that it

<!-- chunk -->

### probably does, at least in some cases. You’ll hear about one such person

<!-- chunk -->

### later in the book.

<!-- chunk -->

### This doesn’t mean that antipsychotic and mood stabilizing medications

<!-- chunk -->

### shouldn’t be used or can’t put symptoms into remission. I believe they do

<!-- chunk -->

### work for some people, and I continue to prescribe them to this day. But for

<!-- chunk -->

### Jane, they clearly ended up making her mental symptoms even worse.

<!-- chunk -->

### Removing the offending medications brought Jane back.

# H


# Hormones and Metabolic Regulators

<!-- chunk -->

### ormones are chemical messengers that are produced in one type of

<!-- chunk -->

### cell and then travel through the body to impact other cells. The

<!-- chunk -->

### human body produces numerous hormones. All of them affect

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondrial function and cause epigenetic changes in their target cells.

<!-- chunk -->

### Hormones change the metabolism of cells. In turn, they can play a role in

<!-- chunk -->

### both mental and metabolic disorders.

<!-- chunk -->

### As I’ve discussed, mitochondria supply energy for the production and

<!-- chunk -->

### release of hormones, and they initiate the process for several key hormones.

<!-- chunk -->

### Hormone levels are affected by a wide variety of factors. These include

<!-- chunk -->

### biological, psychological, and social factors. Hormones are one mechanism

<!-- chunk -->

### for the body to respond to stress and opportunities in the environment. In

<!-- chunk -->

### some cases, just the normal release of specific hormones can affect mood,

<!-- chunk -->

### energy, thoughts, motivations, and behaviors. Testosterone is an obvious

<!-- chunk -->

### example. Think of all the effects that it can have on men. Hormonal

<!-- chunk -->

### imbalances can be caused by many factors, including autoimmune

<!-- chunk -->

### disorders, stress, aging, and mitochondrial dysfunction in the cells that

<!-- chunk -->

### make the hormones.

<!-- chunk -->

### There are many regulators of metabolism and mitochondrial function

<!-- chunk -->

### beyond hormones and neurotransmitters. They include things like

<!-- chunk -->

### neuropeptides, mitokines, adipokines, myokines, RNA molecules, and other

<!-- chunk -->

### messengers. Why so many factors? Because they all control different

<!-- chunk -->

### aspects of metabolic function in different cells under different

<!-- chunk -->

### circumstances. When thinking about the control of traffic, most of the

<!-- chunk -->

### stoplights in a city are activated independently of each other. However,

<!-- chunk -->

### there are some stoplights on long roads that might be coordinated with each

<!-- chunk -->

### other. These hormones and metabolic regulators, like the different

<!-- chunk -->

### stoplights, are controlling metabolism in different cells to produce desired

<!-- chunk -->

### effects. There are many roads and desired effects in the human body, hence

<!-- chunk -->

### the need for so many regulators.

<!-- chunk -->

### I won’t provide a review of all the hormones and their relationships to

<!-- chunk -->

### mental and metabolic health. That could fill an entire book. Instead, I’ll

<!-- chunk -->

### briefly review a few—cortisol, insulin, estrogen, and thyroid hormone—in

<!-- chunk -->

### order to illustrate some of the connections between hormones, metabolism,

<!-- chunk -->

### and mitochondria.

# Cortisol

<!-- chunk -->

### There is no question that cortisol, metabolism, mitochondria, and mental

<!-- chunk -->

### symptoms are all interconnected, as I’ve discussed in previous chapters.

<!-- chunk -->

### Cortisol plays an important role in the stress response. High levels have

<!-- chunk -->

### been associated with all the metabolic disorders and numerous mental

<!-- chunk -->

### symptoms, including anxiety, fear, depression, mania, psychosis, and

<!-- chunk -->

### cognitive impairment. High levels in utero affect fetal development and

<!-- chunk -->

### play a role in epigenetics, which can lead to the later development of both

<!-- chunk -->

### metabolic and mental disorders.

<!-- chunk -->

### Cortisol always begins in mitochondria, which have the enzyme that

<!-- chunk -->

### initiates its production. After cortisol gets released into the bloodstream,

<!-- chunk -->

### it enters cells and binds to the glucocorticoid receptor (GR), which then

<!-- chunk -->

### turns thousands of genes on or off by binding to specific sites on DNA

<!-- chunk -->

### called glucocorticoid response elements (GREs). The proteins from these

<!-- chunk -->

### genes then have widespread effects on cells, all related to metabolism. In

<!-- chunk -->

### addition to GRs in the cytoplasm and GREs located within the cell nucleus,

<!-- chunk -->

### it turns out that they are also located directly on/in mitochondria, too. In

<!-- chunk -->

### some ways, it’s fair to say that cortisol begins and ends with mitochondria.

<!-- chunk -->

### At one point in psychiatry, there was hope that cortisol would be the

<!-- chunk -->

### first definitive biomarker for mental illness. The dexamethasone

<!-- chunk -->

### suppression test, which measures fluctuations in cortisol throughout the day,

<!-- chunk -->

### was widely studied. Unfortunately, cortisol levels can actually be too high

<!-- chunk -->

### or too low in different psychiatric patients. Some people have high levels

<!-- chunk -->

### throughout the day while others, especially those with severe trauma

<!-- chunk -->

### histories, can have abnormally low levels. The story gets complicated

<!-- chunk -->

### quickly, and there’s still debate about how and why this happens. However,

<!-- chunk -->

### my purpose is simply to illustrate that cortisol is a hormone that directly

<!-- chunk -->

### connects metabolism and mitochondria with both metabolic and mental

<!-- chunk -->

### disorders. That much is clear and unequivocal.

# Insulin

<!-- chunk -->

### Most people know insulin for its role in diabetes. People with type

<!-- chunk -->

### diabetes have low levels because the pancreas is not making enough. People

<!-- chunk -->

### with type 2 diabetes are “insulin resistant,” meaning that insulin isn’t

<!-- chunk -->

### working effectively to allow glucose to be used as an energy source. I’ve

<!-- chunk -->

### already discussed the strong bidirectional relationships between diabetes

<!-- chunk -->

### and mental disorders.

<!-- chunk -->

### Emerging evidence over the past fifteen years suggests that

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondria are important regulators of insulin production and secretion.

<!-- chunk -->

### Mitochondria are involved in glucose metabolism and sensing how much

<!-- chunk -->

### glucose is available. They ramp up production and secretion of insulin as

<!-- chunk -->

### needed.


<!-- chunk -->

### Mitochondria are known to play a significant role in both types 1 and

<!-- chunk -->

### diabetes, with some experts speculating that mitochondrial dysfunction may

<!-- chunk -->

### be the primary cause. Numerous lines of evidence support these views. One

<!-- chunk -->

### review paper outlining some of the evidence suggests that mitochondria

<!-- chunk -->

### appear to be important to the cause, complications, management, and

<!-- chunk -->

### prevention of both types 1 and 2 diabetes.


<!-- chunk -->

### Insulin itself stimulates

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondria to produce more ATP and also stimulates mitochondrial

<!-- chunk -->

### biogenesis, as measured in muscle tissue.


<!-- chunk -->

### However, when researchers did

<!-- chunk -->

### this study in people with type 2 diabetes, these effects were blunted or

<!-- chunk -->

### absent. This means that diabetics, over time, may develop even more

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondrial dysfunction due to insulin resistance—sparking a vicious

<!-- chunk -->

### cycle. It suggests that insulin resistance can be both a cause and a

<!-- chunk -->

### consequence of mitochondrial dysfunction.

<!-- chunk -->

### But the story of insulin in brain health only begins with diabetes. It

<!-- chunk -->

### plays a powerful and direct role in brain function, too.


<!-- chunk -->

### Insulin receptors are

<!-- chunk -->

### located throughout the brain, and they are involved in regulating whole-

<!-- chunk -->

### body metabolism, appetite, reproductive functions, liver functions, fat

<!-- chunk -->

### stores, and body temperature. Brain insulin also modulates neurotransmitter

<!-- chunk -->

### activity and mitochondrial function within brain cells. Changes in insulin

<!-- chunk -->

### signaling have been associated with impairment of neuronal function and

<!-- chunk -->

### synapse formation.

<!-- chunk -->

### Insulin has been shown to influence GABA, serotonin, and dopamine

<!-- chunk -->

### neurons specifically.


<!-- chunk -->

### One group of researchers demonstrated that insulin

<!-- chunk -->

### alone can increase GABA activity.


<!-- chunk -->

### We know that insulin resistance can

<!-- chunk -->

### occur in the brain. When it does, it can result in mitochondrial dysfunction,

<!-- chunk -->

### which can then lead to neurotransmitter imbalances, which can then lead to

<!-- chunk -->

### overactivity and underactivity of neurons. I’ll walk you through a sample of

<!-- chunk -->

### the evidence to support this.

<!-- chunk -->

### In addition to being located on neurons, insulin receptors are also found

<!-- chunk -->

### on support cells, such as astrocytes, that play a role in providing energy for

<!-- chunk -->

### neurons. These cells can affect mood and behaviors. In animal experiments,

<!-- chunk -->

### when these insulin receptors were genetically removed, it resulted in

<!-- chunk -->

### changes in brain energy metabolism and also anxiety and depressive

<!-- chunk -->

### behaviors.


<!-- chunk -->

### Insulin resistance would have similar effects.

<!-- chunk -->

### Another animal study more directly linked brain insulin with

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondrial dysfunction and behavioral abnormalities.


<!-- chunk -->

### The researchers

<!-- chunk -->

### genetically

<!-- chunk -->

### removed brain-specific insulin receptors. This resulted in mitochondrial

<!-- chunk -->

### dysfunction, as measured by decreased ATP production and increased ROS.

<!-- chunk -->

### And sure enough, the animals displayed anxiety and depressive-like

<!-- chunk -->

### behaviors.

<!-- chunk -->

### We have evidence that insulin resistance might be playing a role in

<!-- chunk -->

### people, too. Dr. Virginie-Anne Chouinard and colleagues of mine at

<!-- chunk -->

### Harvard and McLean Hospital did brain scans in people with schizophrenia

<!-- chunk -->

### and bipolar disorder and looked at the levels of insulin resistance in their

<!-- chunk -->

### brains.


<!-- chunk -->

### They included people with recent-onset psychosis, but also their

<!-- chunk -->

### siblings who did not have psychiatric symptoms and healthy controls. We

<!-- chunk -->

### know the siblings are at higher risk of developing a mental disorder since

<!-- chunk -->

### their family members developed one. What they found was fascinating. The

<!-- chunk -->

### brains of the people with psychosis had insulin resistance compared to the

<!-- chunk -->

### healthy controls, but the normal siblings also showed signs of insulin

<!-- chunk -->

### resistance, suggesting that insulin resistance might be a risk factor that runs

<!-- chunk -->

### in families. These researchers went on to find differences in mitochondrial

<!-- chunk -->

### function between the patients with psychosis and their normal siblings. This

<!-- chunk -->

### all suggests that insulin resistance might come first, which then leads to

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondrial dysfunction, which then leads to psychosis. Interestingly,

<!-- chunk -->

### none of the groups (patients, siblings, or controls) differed in terms of body

<!-- chunk -->

### mass index, body fat, cholesterol levels, or physical activity—so you would

<!-- chunk -->

### never be able to tell they had insulin resistance in their brains by looking at

<!-- chunk -->

### their outward appearance or talking to them about exercise.

<!-- chunk -->

### An even more compelling study followed almost 15,000 children as

<!-- chunk -->

### they grew from the ages of one to twenty-four.


<!-- chunk -->

### The researchers measured

<!-- chunk -->

### fasting insulin levels at ages nine, fifteen, eighteen, and twenty-four. They

<!-- chunk -->

### also measured the children’s risk for psychosis. What they found was

<!-- chunk -->

### alarming. Children who had persistently high insulin levels (a sign of

<!-- chunk -->

### insulin resistance) beginning at age nine were five times more likely to be at

<!-- chunk -->

### risk for psychosis, meaning that they were showing at least some worrisome

<!-- chunk -->

### signs, and they were three times more likely to already be diagnosed with

<!-- chunk -->

### bipolar disorder or schizophrenia by the time they turned twenty-four. This

<!-- chunk -->

### study clearly demonstrated that insulin resistance comes first, then

<!-- chunk -->

### psychosis.

<!-- chunk -->

### Alzheimer’s disease is also known to involve insulin resistance in the brain.

<!-- chunk -->

### Some are calling it “type 3 diabetes.” Strong evidence has emerged that the

<!-- chunk -->

### brains of people with Alzheimer’s disease are not getting enough energy

<!-- chunk -->

### from glucose due to insulin resistance, and that this results in mitochondrial

<!-- chunk -->

### dysfunction. The areas of the brain most affected have the most plaques and

<!-- chunk -->

### tangles, the hallmarks of this disorder.


<!-- chunk -->

## Insulin as a Treatment

<!-- chunk -->

### So, based on all this evidence, can insulin play a role in treating mental

<!-- chunk -->

### disorders?

<!-- chunk -->

### Interestingly, the use of insulin in psychiatry isn’t new. From 1927 to

<!-- chunk -->

### the 1960s, insulin coma therapy was widely used in the treatment of serious

<!-- chunk -->

### mental disorders. Clinicians would inject patients with large doses of

<!-- chunk -->

### insulin until they went into a coma. This process was repeated a few times

<!-- chunk -->

### per week. Most reports from that era suggested that it was a highly effective

<!-- chunk -->

### treatment, at least for some people. At one point, it was the most widely

<!-- chunk -->

### used treatment for psychosis and severe depression in the Western world. It

<!-- chunk -->

### fell out of favor due to the advent of psychiatric medications. By no means

<!-- chunk -->

### do I want it to come back. However, insulin is making a comeback in the

<!-- chunk -->

### mental health field.

<!-- chunk -->

### Alzheimer’s disease researchers have been using intranasal insulin in

<!-- chunk -->

### clinical trials for a few years now. Squirting insulin into the nose is the

<!-- chunk -->

### easiest and quickest way to get high levels of insulin directly into the brain,

<!-- chunk -->

### which overrides insulin resistance. Early results were promising. A pilot

<!-- chunk -->

### trial of intranasal insulin in 105 participants with mild cognitive impairment

<!-- chunk -->

### or Alzheimer’s disease showed maintenance of cognitive abilities and

<!-- chunk -->

### improved brain glucose metabolism as measured by PET imaging over four

<!-- chunk -->

### months.


<!-- chunk -->

### Unfortunately, a larger subsequent trial in 289 people over twelve

<!-- chunk -->

### months showed no benefit, but there were concerns that the insulin delivery

<!-- chunk -->

### device may have malfunctioned.


<!-- chunk -->

### One research study used intranasal insulin in sixty-two patients with

<!-- chunk -->

### bipolar disorder to see if it could improve their cognitive function over

<!-- chunk -->

### eight

<!-- chunk -->

### weeks. The people who got insulin showed improvement in executive

<!-- chunk -->

### function compared to those who got placebo.


<!-- chunk -->

### Clearly, more research is needed before insulin makes it into clinical

<!-- chunk -->

### practice, but some researchers are working on that.

<!-- chunk -->

### Much more important for treatment, however, is measuring your insulin

<!-- chunk -->

### and blood glucose levels to identify problems like insulin resistance,

<!-- chunk -->

### hypoglycemia, and other issues. Although the correlation between what’s

<!-- chunk -->

### happening in the brain and what can be measured from the blood in your

<!-- chunk -->

### veins isn’t always direct, this information can be helpful, and sometimes

<!-- chunk -->

### invaluable. There are many tests and tools available—fasting glucose and

<!-- chunk -->

### insulin levels, oral glucose tolerance tests, continuous glucose monitoring

<!-- chunk -->

### devices, and others. You’ll need to work with your healthcare provider to

<!-- chunk -->

### get these. If you identify a problem, there is a good chance this could be

<!-- chunk -->

### playing a role in your mental symptoms. There are many ways to address

<!-- chunk -->

### this problem that I’ll discuss in the coming chapters. Lifestyle changes can

<!-- chunk -->

### be a powerful intervention—in particular, diet and exercise.

# Estrogen

<!-- chunk -->

### Most people think of estrogen as it relates to reproductive capacity in

<!-- chunk -->

### women, but that’s only one of its many roles. The title of a science review

<!-- chunk -->

### article says it all, “Estrogen: A Master Regulator of Bioenergetic Systems in

<!-- chunk -->

### the Brain and Body.”


<!-- chunk -->

### Estrogen has profound effects on metabolism. It is known to play an

<!-- chunk -->

### important role in mental health, obesity, diabetes, and cardiovascular

<!-- chunk -->

### disease. It also impacts brain metabolism directly and has widespread

<!-- chunk -->

### effects on mood, cognition, and other brain functions.

<!-- chunk -->

### Mitochondria make estrogen. As with cortisol, they control the first step

<!-- chunk -->

### in its synthesis. Mitochondria also contain estrogen receptors. Like cortisol,

<!-- chunk -->

### estrogen can sometimes begin and end with mitochondria. Most estrogen

<!-- chunk -->

### receptors, however, are not on mitochondria but instead on the outside of

<!-- chunk -->

### cells. They are found widely throughout the brain on

<!-- chunk -->

### both neurons and glial cells, in both men and women. They are also found

<!-- chunk -->

### widely throughout the body. Nonetheless, many of the signaling pathways

<!-- chunk -->

### of estrogen, even when it binds to the receptor outside the cell, end up

<!-- chunk -->

### converging on mitochondria.

<!-- chunk -->

### Estrogen levels fluctuate throughout the month in menstruating women.

<!-- chunk -->

### Many women experience “mental” and “metabolic” symptoms related to

<!-- chunk -->

### changes in estrogen levels. This can include changes in mood, appetite, and

<!-- chunk -->

### cravings. In fact, there is a diagnosis—premenstrual dysphoric disorder

<!-- chunk -->

### (PMDD)—to describe some of the mental symptoms when they are severe.

<!-- chunk -->

### But for women diagnosed with other mental disorders, symptoms can also

<!-- chunk -->

### fluctuate like clockwork around their periods. This goes for all mental

<!-- chunk -->

### symptoms—depression, anxiety, bipolar symptoms, psychotic symptoms,

<!-- chunk -->

### concentration problems, etc.—true to the theory of brain energy. As I

<!-- chunk -->

### discussed earlier, women are twice as likely to develop depression as men.

<!-- chunk -->

### These hormonal fluctuations and their effects on women’s metabolism may

<!-- chunk -->

### explain some of this. Additionally, the blood loss from menstruation results

<!-- chunk -->

### in the loss of metabolic resources, such as iron, which can also take a

<!-- chunk -->

### metabolic toll.

<!-- chunk -->

### Pregnancy and the postpartum period are times of high risk for mental

<!-- chunk -->

### symptoms, likely due to both hormonal changes and, more importantly, the

<!-- chunk -->

### metabolic toll of pregnancy. The nutrients and metabolic resources that it

<!-- chunk -->

### takes to create a child are enormous. This leaves women’s bodies

<!-- chunk -->

### metabolically vulnerable. If you think about it, pregnancy comes with

<!-- chunk -->

### increased risks for both metabolic and mental disorders—weight gain (more

<!-- chunk -->

### than needed to carry a healthy child), gestational diabetes, eclampsia (which

<!-- chunk -->

### includes hypertension and seizures), and, of course, exacerbations of most

<!-- chunk -->

### mental disorders. Postpartum depression is well known, but some women

<!-- chunk -->

### experience postpartum mania or psychosis.

<!-- chunk -->

### Menopause is associated with plummeting estrogen levels. Many

<!-- chunk -->

### women experience mental symptoms, including depression, anxiety, mania,

<!-- chunk -->

### and even psychosis. Women who had depression prior to menopause are

<!-- chunk -->

### five times more likely to become depressed around the time of menopause.

<!-- chunk -->

### Brain energy metabolism decreases broadly. One study looked at forty-three

<!-- chunk -->

### women over their transition into menopause and found that not only did

<!-- chunk -->

### brain energy metabolism decline, but this was directly correlated with a

<!-- chunk -->

### reduction in mitochondrial health.


<!-- chunk -->

### After menopause, women are known to

<!-- chunk -->

### be at elevated risk for developing Alzheimer’s disease compared to men. In

<!-- chunk -->

### some women, these brain metabolism abnormalities can correct themselves

<!-- chunk -->

### over time, but in others, they appear to become permanent, likely putting

<!-- chunk -->

### these women at increased risk for mental disorders and Alzheimer’s

<!-- chunk -->

### disease. Researchers found a direct link between memory, estrogen, and

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondria in rhesus monkeys.


<!-- chunk -->

### They found that female monkeys with

<!-- chunk -->

### poor memory had more malformed, donut-shaped mitochondria at synapses

<!-- chunk -->

### in the prefrontal cortex. When they surgically induced menopause in the

<!-- chunk -->

### monkeys, sure enough, they showed signs of memory impairment and the

<!-- chunk -->

### numbers of malformed, donut-shaped mitochondria increased. When they

<!-- chunk -->

### gave the monkeys estrogen replacement therapy, the memory problems and

<!-- chunk -->

### the mitochondrial abnormalities both improved.

<!-- chunk -->

## Estrogen as a Treatment

<!-- chunk -->

### Oral contraception is used by millions of women. These pills usually

<!-- chunk -->

### contain both estrogen and progesterone. They sometimes have adverse

<!-- chunk -->

### mood effects, but ironically, are also sometimes used to treat mood

<!-- chunk -->

### symptoms, such as PMDD. So, it can be confusing: Do they help, or do

<!-- chunk -->

### they hurt? In the end, there are likely differences between women, with

<!-- chunk -->

### some experiencing benefits and others experiencing adverse effects. One

<!-- chunk -->

### study looked at more than one million women between the ages of fifteen

<!-- chunk -->

### and thirty-four taking birth control pills and found that they were somewhat

<!-- chunk -->

### more likely to experience depression or use an antidepressant compared to

<!-- chunk -->

### women not on the pills.


<!-- chunk -->

### Another study looked at half a million fifteen-

<!-- chunk -->

### year-old women and found that those who were taking birth control were

<!-- chunk -->

### twice as likely to attempt suicide and three times more likely to commit

<!-- chunk -->

### suicide.


<!-- chunk -->

### Birth control pills don’t have the same levels of hormones that

<!-- chunk -->

### naturally occur in the body, so this may explain these findings. It’s

<!-- chunk -->

### important for women with mood symptoms to work with their doctors to

<!-- chunk -->

### manage the risk of unwanted pregnancy and also their mental health needs.

<!-- chunk -->

### Hormone replacement therapy after menopause may play a role for some

<!-- chunk -->

### women. In fact, with all this emerging evidence for the role of estrogen in

<!-- chunk -->

### the brain, doses of estrogen may need to be reevaluated to optimize brain

<!-- chunk -->

### health.

# Thyroid Hormone

<!-- chunk -->

### Thyroid hormone is known as the master regulator of metabolism. As far as

<!-- chunk -->

### researchers can tell, it acts on every cell in the human body. Thyroid

<!-- chunk -->

### hormone increases metabolism, revving up mitochondria. It plays a

<!-- chunk -->

### profound role in growth, development, temperature regulation, and the

<!-- chunk -->

### function of every organ, especially the brain. When people have too much

<!-- chunk -->

### or too little thyroid hormone, problems are almost always evident.

<!-- chunk -->

### Although some of the mechanisms of action of thyroid hormone are still

<!-- chunk -->

### being worked out, what is clear and unequivocal are its effects on

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondria. Thyroid hormone, either directly or indirectly, stimulates

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondria to produce ATP or heat. Mitochondria have thyroid hormone

<!-- chunk -->

### receptors, so they sometimes get the signal directly. However, thyroid

<!-- chunk -->

### hormone also acts through genes in the nucleus, which then impact

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondria. Thyroid hormone is also known to stimulate mitochondrial

<!-- chunk -->

### biogenesis, increasing the total number of mitochondria in cells.


<!-- chunk -->

### It also

<!-- chunk -->

### induces mitophagy—the mitochondrial repair process.


<!-- chunk -->

### As you know by

<!-- chunk -->

### now, these have powerful effects on human health.

<!-- chunk -->

### Hypothyroidism occurs when the thyroid gland is underactive,

<!-- chunk -->

### producing less thyroid hormone than the body requires. It’s most commonly

<!-- chunk -->

### due to an autoimmune disorder, but there are several other causes, too. It

<!-- chunk -->

### can lead to many metabolic and mental symptoms, including weight gain,

<!-- chunk -->

### obesity, heart disease, fatigue, brain fog, and depression. What is less well

<!-- chunk -->

### known is that it is also linked to bipolar disorder, schizophrenia, and

<!-- chunk -->

### dementia.


<!-- chunk -->

### When hypothyroidism occurs during development, it can lead

<!-- chunk -->

### to profound neurological deficits (cretinism). The brain energy theory offers

<!-- chunk -->

### new ways

<!-- chunk -->

### to understand all of this. It connects all of these seemingly different

<!-- chunk -->

### illnesses through one pathway: mitochondria.

<!-- chunk -->

## Thyroid Hormone as a Treatment

<!-- chunk -->

### Thyroid hormone has been used as a treatment for mental disorders for

<!-- chunk -->

### decades, even when people have normal levels. It is commonly used in

<!-- chunk -->

### treatment-resistant depression and bipolar disorder. However, the field has

<!-- chunk -->

### not been able to explain how or why it works. The brain energy theory

<!-- chunk -->

### provides an obvious explanation. Not only does thyroid hormone increase

<!-- chunk -->

### metabolism immediately, but it also increases the health and number of

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondria. When you increase the workforce, cells function better.

<!-- chunk -->

### However, increasing metabolism comes with the risk of overstimulating

<!-- chunk -->

### cells, especially hyperexcitable ones. So for some people, thyroid hormone

<!-- chunk -->

### can cause or exacerbate unwanted symptoms.


<!-- chunk -->

### •Hormones and other metabolic regulators play a powerful role in

<!-- chunk -->

### metabolic and mental health.

<!-- chunk -->

### •If you have signs or symptoms of hormonal imbalances, you should

<!-- chunk -->

### work with your healthcare professional to have those assessed and

<!-- chunk -->

### treated.

<!-- chunk -->

### •If you have chronic mental or metabolic symptoms with no clear

<!-- chunk -->

### explanation for them, you should consider a comprehensive

<!-- chunk -->

### assessment of your hormonal status.

<!-- chunk -->

### •It’s important to take an inventory of the hormonal treatments that

<!-- chunk -->

### you are currently using, such as birth control or diabetes treatments,

<!-- chunk -->

### as they could be playing a role in your mental health (for better or

<!-- chunk -->

### worse).

# Success Story: James—“It’s My Thyroid”

<!-- chunk -->

### When I first met him, James was a fifty-four-year-old man with a thirty-

<!-- chunk -->

### year history of bipolar disorder. He had recurrent depression every fall that

<!-- chunk -->

### would last until spring, despite trying more than twenty antidepressants and

<!-- chunk -->

### mood-stabilizing medications. His depression was crippling, often leaving

<!-- chunk -->

### him unable to get out of bed. He had also been diagnosed with

<!-- chunk -->

### hypothyroidism, high blood pressure, high cholesterol, and sleep apnea. His

<!-- chunk -->

### normal doses of thyroid medication—enough to increase his hormone levels

<!-- chunk -->

### into the “healthy” range—had done nothing to improve his depression, but

<!-- chunk -->

### we decided to try high-dose thyroid hormone as a treatment. It made a huge

<!-- chunk -->

### difference! His levels of thyroid hormone were now abnormally high, so we

<!-- chunk -->

### had to keep an eye out for side effects, such as heart arrhythmias and

<!-- chunk -->

### osteoporosis. But overall, he tolerated it quite well, and it changed his life.

<!-- chunk -->

### His recurrent depressions were all but gone. After about ten years of

<!-- chunk -->

### treatment with a high-dose of thyroid hormone, he was able to decrease the

<!-- chunk -->

### dose to a normal range and has continued to do well to this day. He still

<!-- chunk -->

### uses a low-dose antidepressant and sleep medication occasionally, but he

<!-- chunk -->

### hasn’t experienced a severe depression in many years now. At the time I

<!-- chunk -->

### used this treatment with James, I didn’t know how or why it worked. Now I

<!-- chunk -->

### do: brain energy.


# Inflammation

<!-- chunk -->

### nflammation plays an important role in metabolism, mitochondrial

<!-- chunk -->

### function, mental health, and metabolic health. Therefore, it plays an

<!-- chunk -->

### important role in the theory of brain energy.

<!-- chunk -->

### Let’s start with an overarching observation—many people think of

<!-- chunk -->

### inflammation as a bad thing. Low-grade inflammation is often found in

<!-- chunk -->

### people with metabolic and mental disorders. Many speculate that

<!-- chunk -->

### neuroinflammation might be the root cause of at least some mental and

<!-- chunk -->

### neurological disorders. Cytokine storms (an overactive inflammatory

<!-- chunk -->

### response) can kill people with COVID. Lingering inflammation is one of

<!-- chunk -->

### the primary suspects for the cause of long COVID, in which people have

<!-- chunk -->

### mental and neurological symptoms for months or years after infection.

<!-- chunk -->

### Autoimmune disorders are when inflammation and the immune system are

<!-- chunk -->

### attacking a person’s own body. A “leaky gut” can cause chronic

<!-- chunk -->

### inflammation. For all these reasons, we have heard that inflammation

<!-- chunk -->

### causes much of what ails us. We are told to decrease inflammation.

<!-- chunk -->

### However, inflammation isn’t always bad. It occurs all the time. It’s usually

<!-- chunk -->

### a normal process that plays countless beneficial roles in the human body. It

<!-- chunk -->

### is involved in fighting off infections and healing injuries. It serves

<!-- chunk -->

### important signaling functions. It is involved in the normal stress response.

<!-- chunk -->

### Inflammatory cytokines are a way to send stress signals throughout the

<!-- chunk -->

### body and brain. Microglial cells, the brain’s immune cells, play a role in

<!-- chunk -->

### brain development, learning, and memory. Without inflammation, we would

<!-- chunk -->

### die.

# Inflammation, Metabolism, and Mental States

<!-- chunk -->

### Inflammation is one way that the body allocates and uses metabolic

<!-- chunk -->

### resources, thereby directly affecting metabolism.

<!-- chunk -->

### When inflammatory cytokines get released, more blood flows to that

<!-- chunk -->

### area of the body, bringing with it oxygen, glucose, amino acids, and fats to

<!-- chunk -->

### be used in some way. The inflammation is “calling” for these resources, and

<!-- chunk -->

### the body is allocating energy and supplies. This can occur due to infections

<!-- chunk -->

### or injuries, or in response to old or dying cells.

<!-- chunk -->

### Inflammation can trigger the production of more immune cells and

<!-- chunk -->

### antibodies. These can be lifesaving when fighting off viruses, bacteria, and

<!-- chunk -->

### even newly formed cancer cells, but it takes energy and resources to make

<!-- chunk -->

### these. The body prioritizes dealing with these situations as they threaten the

<!-- chunk -->

### survival of the organism. Other times, the body is allocating resources to

<!-- chunk -->

### make adaptive changes, such as increasing the size of muscles after a

<!-- chunk -->

### workout or directing metabolic resources to specific brain regions for new

<!-- chunk -->

### learning. Even in these situations, inflammation is calling resources to these

<!-- chunk -->

### sites. In all these scenarios, there are fewer metabolic resources available to

<!-- chunk -->

### all the other cells in the body. In other words, inflammation takes a toll—a

<!-- chunk -->

### metabolic toll.

<!-- chunk -->

### High levels of inflammation can change emotions, thoughts,

<!-- chunk -->

### motivations, and behaviors. For example, when people have a viral

<!-- chunk -->

### infection or cancer, the high levels of inflammation cause mental changes as

<!-- chunk -->

### well. People become lethargic, withdrawn, unmotivated, less confident, and

<!-- chunk -->

### more likely

<!-- chunk -->

### to want to climb into bed and rest. These are all adaptive. They are normal

<!-- chunk -->

### and healthy, even though they make people feel miserable. These changes

<!-- chunk -->

### allow for the conservation of metabolic resources. The body is fighting for

<!-- chunk -->

### its survival. This is no time to go out playing, exercising, or even

<!-- chunk -->

### reproducing. All available resources need to be used for survival. Some

<!-- chunk -->

### researchers have called this conservation-withdrawal behavior and have

<!-- chunk -->

### used these observations to better understand some of the symptoms of

<!-- chunk -->

### depression.

<!-- chunk -->

### But it can go in the other direction, too. Mental states can cause

<!-- chunk -->

### inflammation. One fascinating study looked at humans and monkeys who

<!-- chunk -->

### were lonely and found that loneliness increased the stress response and

<!-- chunk -->

### induced a specific pattern of immune cell activation.


<!-- chunk -->

### This left the lonely

<!-- chunk -->

### people and monkeys with chronic, low-grade inflammation. They were also

<!-- chunk -->

### more vulnerable to getting a viral infection. The researchers went so far as

<!-- chunk -->

### to infect the monkeys with a virus, and sure enough, the lonely monkeys

<!-- chunk -->

### had an impaired immune response. This helps explain why a mental

<!-- chunk -->

### symptom like loneliness has been linked to higher rates of not just mental

<!-- chunk -->

### disorders but also cardiovascular disease, Alzheimer’s disease, and

<!-- chunk -->

### premature death.


<!-- chunk -->

### When inflammation occurs for a prolonged period, or when it is

<!-- chunk -->

### extreme, the metabolic toll can trigger or exacerbate mental and metabolic

<!-- chunk -->

### disorders. When infections, allergies, cancers, and autoimmune disorders

<!-- chunk -->

### flare up, there can be an increase in new-onset mental disorders or an

<!-- chunk -->

### exacerbation of mental symptoms in people with existing disorders. To give

<!-- chunk -->

### one surprising example, people with runny noses (rhinitis) from something

<!-- chunk -->

### like hay fever are 86 percent more likely to develop depression.


<!-- chunk -->

### These

<!-- chunk -->

### inflammatory conditions are also well-known causes of delirium in the

<!-- chunk -->

### elderly. Likewise, there can be an increase in the symptoms of metabolic

<!-- chunk -->

### disorders. Blood sugar goes up in people with diabetes. People with

<!-- chunk -->

### cardiovascular disease can see an increase in blood pressure, experience

<!-- chunk -->

### chest pain, or suffer another heart attack.

<!-- chunk -->

### A large population study of more than one million children in Denmark

<!-- chunk -->

### found that children hospitalized for serious infections were 84 percent more

<!-- chunk -->

### likely to develop a subsequent mental disorder and 42 percent more likely

<!-- chunk -->

### to be prescribed a psychiatric medication.


<!-- chunk -->

### The biggest risk was within

<!-- chunk -->

### three

<!-- chunk -->

### months of the infection. In teens, there was an eightfold increase in OCD.

<!-- chunk -->

### In case you’re thinking that all the kids were just “anxious” because they

<!-- chunk -->

### got hospitalized, the most common diagnoses included schizophrenia,

<!-- chunk -->

### OCD, personality disorders, mental retardation, autism, ADHD,

<!-- chunk -->

### oppositional defiant disorder, conduct disorder, and tic disorders. These are

<!-- chunk -->

### serious brain disorders, not just “anxiety” due to a hospitalization. And as

<!-- chunk -->

### you’ll note, the diagnoses are widespread, not specific to any one disorder,

<!-- chunk -->

### which is consistent with the brain energy theory.

<!-- chunk -->

### These are just a couple of examples of the research demonstrating that

<!-- chunk -->

### inflammatory conditions can lead to the development or exacerbation of

<!-- chunk -->

### both mental and metabolic disorders. But is there evidence that

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondria are involved?

# Inflammation and Mitochondria

<!-- chunk -->

### Inflammation and mitochondria are in a complex feedback loop.

<!-- chunk -->

### Mitochondria are involved in many aspects of the normal inflammatory

<!-- chunk -->

### response, turning it both on and off. Inflammation, in turn, can impair

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondrial function. Furthermore, mitochondrial dysfunction, even from

<!-- chunk -->

### other causes, can lead to inflammation. It’s all a vicious cycle. I’ll walk you

<!-- chunk -->

### through some of the evidence supporting this.

<!-- chunk -->

### Mitochondria play a role in normal inflammation. In the chapter on

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondria, I already shared with you the study demonstrating that they

<!-- chunk -->

### are responsible for the different phases of wound healing in macrophages.

<!-- chunk -->

### One scientific article, “Mitochondria in Innate Immune Responses,”

<!-- chunk -->

### reviewed the many complex ways in which mitochondria are directly or

<!-- chunk -->

### indirectly involved in many aspects of the immune response, including

<!-- chunk -->

### fighting viruses and bacteria, but also playing a role in cell damage and

<!-- chunk -->

### stress.


<!-- chunk -->

### Another paper published in Cell found that mitochondria appear to

<!-- chunk -->

### play a role in immune cell death when the time comes to turn off the

<!-- chunk -->

### immune response.


<!-- chunk -->

### If mitochondria are not functioning properly in these

<!-- chunk -->

### cells, there will be problems with inflammation and immune cell function.

<!-- chunk -->

### It can lead to either an overactive or

<!-- chunk -->

### underactive immune and inflammatory response. These have been observed

<!-- chunk -->

### in many mental and metabolic disorders.

<!-- chunk -->

### Inflammation directly impacts the function of mitochondria. For

<!-- chunk -->

### example, tumor necrosis factor (TNF), an inflammatory cytokine, has been

<!-- chunk -->

### found to directly inhibit mitochondrial function.


<!-- chunk -->

### A more important

<!-- chunk -->

### example is that of interferon, another inflammatory cytokine. Its production

<!-- chunk -->

### is strongly influenced by mitochondria, but it has also been shown to

<!-- chunk -->

### directly inhibit three mitochondrial genes, resulting in changes in

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondrial function.


<!-- chunk -->

### Furthermore, interferon has been shown to directly

<!-- chunk -->

### suppress mitochondrial ATP production in some brain cells.


<!-- chunk -->

### What makes

<!-- chunk -->

### this such an important example is that interferon can be given to people as a

<!-- chunk -->

### medication when treating serious infections or cancers. Shortly after

<!-- chunk -->

### starting interferon, all hell can break loose in terms of psychiatric symptoms

<!-- chunk -->

### —essentially, anything goes. The list includes depression, fatigue,

<!-- chunk -->

### irritability, insomnia, suicidal behavior, manic symptoms, anxiety, psychotic

<!-- chunk -->

### symptoms, concentration difficulties, and delirium.


<!-- chunk -->

### All existing mental

<!-- chunk -->

### disorders can get worse with interferon. So here we go again. One drug,

<!-- chunk -->

### interferon, can produce every symptom known to psychiatry. Why?


<!-- chunk -->

### There are numerous other ways in which inflammation, immune cells,

<!-- chunk -->

### and cytokines can impact mitochondrial function, but for our purposes, the

<!-- chunk -->

### bottom line is that inflammation can cause mitochondrial dysfunction.

<!-- chunk -->

### Inflammation can also affect brain development. In a fetus or young

<!-- chunk -->

### child, the brain can develop abnormally due to inflammatory conditions.

<!-- chunk -->

### For example, pregnant women with infections are 80 percent more likely to

<!-- chunk -->

### have a child with autism.


<!-- chunk -->

### There are many animal models of autism in

<!-- chunk -->

### which researchers inject inflammatory molecules into pregnant mice in

<!-- chunk -->

### order to induce autism in the offspring. How can we tie this all together?


<!-- chunk -->

### Inflammation can also be a consequence of mitochondrial dysfunction.

<!-- chunk -->

### In addition to mitochondrial dysfunction in immune cells directly impacting

<!-- chunk -->

### the inflammatory and immune response, if mitochondria in other types of

<!-- chunk -->

### cells aren’t functioning properly, that can also lead to the chronic, low-

<!-- chunk -->

### grade inflammation that we see in many people with metabolic and mental


<!-- chunk -->

### Cells that are metabolically compromised can fall into a state of disrepair.

<!-- chunk -->

### They can have maintenance problems, shrink, or die. They can have high

<!-- chunk -->

### levels of oxidative stress. All of this triggers inflammation. The cells send

<!-- chunk -->

### out signals, damage-associated molecular patterns (DAMPs), that they are

<!-- chunk -->

### in need of repair. Dead cells need to be disposed of properly. Inflammation

<!-- chunk -->

### serves these purposes. In fact, mitochondria themselves, or at least parts of

<!-- chunk -->

### them, are known to be powerful DAMPs. When they are released from a

<!-- chunk -->

### struggling cell, it triggers inflammation. Inflammation in these cases is a

<!-- chunk -->

### normal response; it’s not the primary problem, but instead a symptom of a

<!-- chunk -->

### metabolic problem. Interfering with it likely won’t change a thing. In fact,

<!-- chunk -->

### in some cases, it might make things worse, as it can interfere with the

<!-- chunk -->

### normal healing process. The low-grade inflammation associated with all the

<!-- chunk -->

### mental and metabolic disorders is likely a consequence of widespread

<!-- chunk -->

### metabolic dysfunction. To address the problem, we need to understand what

<!-- chunk -->

### is causing the metabolic problems in the first place. This can include a wide

<!-- chunk -->

### variety of factors, like a poor diet, stress, hormonal problems, a lack of

<!-- chunk -->

### sleep, heavy use of alcohol or drugs, and other toxins. You’ll hear more

<!-- chunk -->

### about some of these soon enough. In order to address the problem, we must

<!-- chunk -->

### address the metabolic dysfunction in the cells. If we can restore metabolic

<!-- chunk -->

### health, the inflammation will stop.

# The Role of Inflammation in Treatment

<!-- chunk -->

### For decades, suppressing inflammation has been an area of great interest to

<!-- chunk -->

### researchers. They have been studying antioxidants and anti-inflammatory

<!-- chunk -->

### agents for both metabolic and mental disorders. Billions of dollars have

<!-- chunk -->

### been spent on this research. The list of agents includes things like vitamin

<!-- chunk -->

### E, omega-3 fatty acids, N-acetylcysteine, and nonsteroidal anti-

<!-- chunk -->

### inflammatory medications like ibuprofen. When all is said and done, they

<!-- chunk -->

### don’t appear to be all that effective as treatments. Their use in depression,

<!-- chunk -->

### schizophrenia, Alzheimer’s disease, cardiovascular disease, obesity, and

<!-- chunk -->

### diabetes has been

<!-- chunk -->

### disappointing, at best, even though all these disorders have been associated

<!-- chunk -->

### with higher levels of chronic inflammation. One meta-analysis showed

<!-- chunk -->

### slight benefits for some of these agents for some mental disorders, but the

<!-- chunk -->

### improvements were minimal and usually not clinically meaningful.


<!-- chunk -->

### On

<!-- chunk -->

### top of that, given that inflammation plays a role in normal brain and body

<!-- chunk -->

### functions, it’s possible that suppressing it with medications may have

<!-- chunk -->

### unintended adverse consequences over the long run.

<!-- chunk -->

### So, does inflammation matter in treatment? It does.

<!-- chunk -->

### First, there are many lifestyle factors that I already mentioned that can

<!-- chunk -->

### cause widespread metabolic dysfunction, which can then lead to chronic

<!-- chunk -->

### inflammation. Addressing these can play a powerful role in decreasing

<!-- chunk -->

### inflammation and addressing metabolic and mental disorders. Taking an

<!-- chunk -->

### antioxidant to counteract the negative effects of these lifestyle factors

<!-- chunk -->

### simply doesn’t work.

<!-- chunk -->

### Autoimmune disorders are associated with high levels of inflammation

<!-- chunk -->

### and play a role in mental and metabolic disorders. Addressing these is

<!-- chunk -->

### important. Sometimes, it might require anti-inflammatory treatments. In

<!-- chunk -->

### other cases, it might require addressing hormone deficiencies. You’ll need

<!-- chunk -->

### to work with your healthcare provider on all of this.

<!-- chunk -->

### Chronic infections can also be a serious problem. When the body is

<!-- chunk -->

### unable to eliminate a viral or bacterial infection, it takes a metabolic toll

<!-- chunk -->

### and can lead to problems. HIV, chronic Lyme disease, hepatitis, and others

<!-- chunk -->

### can play a role in metabolic and mental health. You’ll need to work with

<!-- chunk -->

### your healthcare provider to address these with optimal care.

<!-- chunk -->

### Allergies can also lead to chronic inflammation. Sometimes, allergens

<!-- chunk -->

### can be avoided, but other times, you may need to work with your healthcare

<!-- chunk -->

### provider to select appropriate treatments.

<!-- chunk -->

### Dental hygiene can also affect inflammation, and in turn, can impact

<!-- chunk -->

### both metabolic and mental disorders. It’s important to brush and floss your

<!-- chunk -->

### teeth regularly and get regular dental checkups. This is one way to reduce a

<!-- chunk -->

### source of inflammation in your body.


<!-- chunk -->

### •Inflammation plays a powerful role in mental and metabolic health.

<!-- chunk -->

### •Inflammation always affects metabolism, and metabolic problems

<!-- chunk -->

### often increase levels of inflammation.

<!-- chunk -->

### •For many people, poor diet, lack of exercise, poor sleep, smoking,

<!-- chunk -->

### use of alcohol or drugs, and other lifestyle factors are the primary

<!-- chunk -->

### causes of low-grade inflammation. Addressing these directly is more

<!-- chunk -->

### important than trying to reduce inflammation through other means,

<!-- chunk -->

### such as taking an antioxidant pill.

<!-- chunk -->

### •Inflammation impacts mental states, and mental states can cause

<!-- chunk -->

### inflammation.

<!-- chunk -->

### •Mitochondria are directly and indirectly involved with inflammation

<!-- chunk -->

### and immune cell function.

<!-- chunk -->

### •Inflammation and mitochondria are in a complex feedback cycle,

<!-- chunk -->

### and this can play an important role in metabolic and mental health.

# S


# Sleep, Light, and Circadian Rhythms

<!-- chunk -->

### leep, light, and circadian rhythms are all interconnected. They play a

<!-- chunk -->

### powerful role in metabolism, mitochondrial function, metabolic

<!-- chunk -->

### disorders, and mental disorders. Although the biology of these topics

<!-- chunk -->

### is complex, I’ll offer a high-level overview and a sample of the

<!-- chunk -->

### evidence to demonstrate that these contributing causes all play a role in the

<!-- chunk -->

### theory of brain energy.

<!-- chunk -->

### When we sleep at night, our bodies and brains enter a “rest and repair”

<!-- chunk -->

### state. The overall metabolic rate and temperature of the body decline while

<!-- chunk -->

### cells perform maintenance functions and make needed repairs vital to both

<!-- chunk -->

### short-term and long-term health. The brain undergoes many changes in

<!-- chunk -->

### neurons that are thought to play a role in learning and memory

<!-- chunk -->

### consolidation. Without sleep, cells can fall into a state of disrepair and

<!-- chunk -->

### begin to malfunction.

<!-- chunk -->

### Sleep is part of the body’s overall metabolic strategy. It is guided by

<!-- chunk -->

### circadian rhythms. The body has “clocks,” both in the brain and in virtually

<!-- chunk -->

### all cells, that govern many biological processes. In the end, they all relate to

<!-- chunk -->

### metabolism. An area of the hypothalamus called the suprachiasmatic

<!-- chunk -->

### nucleus (SCN) plays a key role. The SCN detects light from our eyes and

<!-- chunk -->

### generates hormonal and nervous system responses. These signals, in turn,

<!-- chunk -->

### influence the peripheral clocks in all the cells of the body by turning

<!-- chunk -->

### thousands of genes throughout the body on or off. The circadian rhythm is

<!-- chunk -->

### largely driven by two things—light and food. It gets synchronized to cycles

<!-- chunk -->

### of light or dark and feeding or fasting.

<!-- chunk -->

### An optimal amount of sleep for adults is about seven to nine hours per

<!-- chunk -->

### night but varies among people. Age, activity level, and other factors play a

<!-- chunk -->

### role. Infants and children need more sleep as their bodies are growing. The

<!-- chunk -->

### elderly require less sleep. When people are ill, they need more sleep

<!-- chunk -->

### temporarily, as sleep helps conserve energy. Sleep allows metabolic

<!-- chunk -->

### resources to be devoted to growth, maintenance, and repair functions.

<!-- chunk -->

### When a person’s safety is threatened, sleep needs to wait. Rest and

<!-- chunk -->

### repair are never as important as survival. This includes not just physical

<!-- chunk -->

### survival but also status in society. Anything that has us worried, including

<!-- chunk -->

### most psychological and social stressors, can disrupt sleep. This is normal,

<!-- chunk -->

### not a disorder.

<!-- chunk -->

### Sleep problems can be defined as too much, too little, or poor-quality

<!-- chunk -->

### sleep. Any of them can take a metabolic toll. Problems sleeping can

<!-- chunk -->

### exacerbate all mental and metabolic disorders. Sleep deprivation can

<!-- chunk -->

### worsen depression, mania, anxiety, dementia, ADHD, schizophrenia, and

<!-- chunk -->

### substance use disorders. It can also exacerbate metabolic disorders. People

<!-- chunk -->

### with diabetes can see their blood sugars rise. People with obesity can gain

<!-- chunk -->

### more weight. People who have already had a heart attack can have another

<!-- chunk -->

### one. These are all examples of sleep problems worsening existing disorders.

<!-- chunk -->

### However, they can also be contributing causes to the onset of such

<!-- chunk -->

### disorders. There have been many studies of normal, healthy people being

<!-- chunk -->

### sleep deprived. If the sleep deprivation is extreme, it can result in

<!-- chunk -->

### depression, anxiety, cognitive impairment, mania, and psychosis. Genetic

<!-- chunk -->

### studies have found an association between the clock genes and autism,

<!-- chunk -->

### bipolar disorder, schizophrenia,

<!-- chunk -->

### depression, anxiety, and substance use disorders.


<!-- chunk -->

### Long-term studies

<!-- chunk -->

### looking at people who don’t get enough sleep have found that they are more

<!-- chunk -->

### likely to develop all the metabolic disorders as well. It can lead to and

<!-- chunk -->

### exacerbate epilepsy and Alzheimer’s disease, too.

<!-- chunk -->

### Sleep is in a feedback cycle with mental and metabolic disorders. These

<!-- chunk -->

### disorders themselves can cause sleep problems, which can then make the

<!-- chunk -->

### disorders even worse. It’s well described that sleep problems are a common

<!-- chunk -->

### symptom of most mental disorders. What’s less well known is that sleep

<!-- chunk -->

### problems are also more common in people with obesity, diabetes,

<!-- chunk -->

### cardiovascular disease, Alzheimer’s disease, and epilepsy.

<!-- chunk -->

### There are many different types of sleep disorders, including obstructive

<!-- chunk -->

### sleep apnea, when people have obstructed airways at night and stop

<!-- chunk -->

### breathing, and restless legs syndrome, when people can’t stop moving their

<!-- chunk -->

### legs at night. The most common sleep disorder, however, is plain old

<!-- chunk -->

### insomnia.

<!-- chunk -->

### So, we see strong bidirectional relationships between sleep, mental, and

<!-- chunk -->

### metabolic disorders. Clearly, something is going on here. We know that

<!-- chunk -->

### sleep problems result in a stress response and increase levels of

<!-- chunk -->

### inflammation. I’ve already discussed how those can impact mental and

<!-- chunk -->

### metabolic disorders. But there’s more to the story once again. We have

<!-- chunk -->

### several lines of evidence demonstrating a feedback cycle between

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondria, sleep, and circadian rhythms.

<!-- chunk -->

## Sleep and Circadian Rhythms Impact Mitochondrial Function

<!-- chunk -->

### Mitochondria are synchronized with our circadian rhythms. Energy

<!-- chunk -->
