### production decreases at night to allow for sleep. It increases during the day,

<!-- chunk -->

### so that we can go out into the world to work and play.

<!-- chunk -->

### Researchers have identified a specific protein, DRP1, that plays a

<!-- chunk -->

### central role in mitochondrial fission and ATP production.


<!-- chunk -->

### The circadian

<!-- chunk -->

### clock controls this protein, which then synchronizes mitochondrial function

<!-- chunk -->

### with our daily rhythm. Interestingly, DRP1 is necessary for feedback to the

<!-- chunk -->

### circadian clock, suggesting that mitochondria may be influencing the clocks

<!-- chunk -->

### themselves through this feedback mechanism.

<!-- chunk -->

### Another study looked at mice and the effects of sleep deprivation on

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondrial function in four different brain regions. They found that

<!-- chunk -->

### sleep-deprived mice had impairment of mitochondrial function in all four

<!-- chunk -->

### brain regions, but especially in the hypothalamus, an area of the brain

<!-- chunk -->

### known to regulate metabolism and many hormones, such as cortisol.


<!-- chunk -->

### Hormones play a role in sleep and mitochondrial function as well.

<!-- chunk -->

### Abnormal levels of cortisol through the night can be caused by sleep

<!-- chunk -->

### problems. Cortisol levels can then impact brain function and cognitive

<!-- chunk -->

### impairment.


<!-- chunk -->

### Melatonin, which increases at night and decreases in the

<!-- chunk -->

### morning, has been found to directly stimulate mitophagy. The lack of

<!-- chunk -->

### melatonin-induced mitophagy has been tied to cognitive deficits in mice.


<!-- chunk -->

### This research suggests that poor sleep leads to mitochondrial dysfunction,

<!-- chunk -->

### which then leads to cognitive impairment, which can then lead to

<!-- chunk -->

### Alzheimer’s disease. This hypothesis was further supported by another

<!-- chunk -->

### research group that deprived mice of sleep for nine months and then looked

<!-- chunk -->

### at mitochondrial function and the accumulation of beta-amyloid. Sure

<!-- chunk -->

### enough, the sleep-deprived mice had higher levels of mitochondrial

<!-- chunk -->

### dysfunction and beta-amyloid accumulation compared to the control

<!-- chunk -->

### group.


<!-- chunk -->

### This research helps us understand why and how chronic sleep

<!-- chunk -->

### deprivation is a known risk factor for Alzheimer’s disease.

<!-- chunk -->

### One more example—remember NAD? This metabolic coenzyme is

<!-- chunk -->

### controlled by circadian clocks and directly influences mitochondrial

<!-- chunk -->

### activity, resulting in more ATP production.


<!-- chunk -->

### So, when your circadian

<!-- chunk -->

### rhythm is off, your production of NAD will be off, which will throw off

<!-- chunk -->

### your mitochondrial function and your mental and metabolic health.

<!-- chunk -->

## Mitochondria Play a Role in Controlling Sleep

<!-- chunk -->

### The regulation of sleep involves numerous neurons and neurotransmitters,

<!-- chunk -->

### and many aspects are still being worked out. By no means is this a simple

<!-- chunk -->

### topic.

<!-- chunk -->

### However, recent research does implicate at least one direct role for

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondria. A 2019 Nature article looked at neurons that are known to

<!-- chunk -->

### induce sleep in the fruit fly to determine what makes them turn on and off.

<!-- chunk -->

### In other words, what makes these flies fall asleep? The researchers found

<!-- chunk -->

### that it’s mitochondria. The levels of ROS in mitochondria were linked

<!-- chunk -->

### directly to

<!-- chunk -->

### specific receptors that induce sleep. The researchers summarized the

<!-- chunk -->

### importance of this finding: “Energy metabolism, oxidative stress, and sleep

<!-- chunk -->

### —three processes implicated independently in lifespan, ageing, and

<!-- chunk -->

### degenerative disease—are thus mechanistically connected.”


<!-- chunk -->

### What these

<!-- chunk -->

### researchers left out are mental disorders, which are also connected to all of

<!-- chunk -->

### this.

<!-- chunk -->

### Another group of researchers looked at fruit flies with mitochondrial

<!-- chunk -->

### defects and found that they, too, had disrupted circadian rhythms and sleep

<!-- chunk -->

### patterns, further implicating mitochondria as crucial players.


<!-- chunk -->

### A study in

<!-- chunk -->

### humans with mitochondrial defects found that almost 50 percent had sleep-

<!-- chunk -->

### disordered breathing problems.


<!-- chunk -->

## Light Impacts Mitochondria and the Brain

<!-- chunk -->

### Light stimulates mitochondria, and different wavelengths of light have

<!-- chunk -->

### different effects. For example, red light tends to stimulate ATP production.

<!-- chunk -->

### However, blue light tends to inhibit ATP production and, instead, increase

<!-- chunk -->

### ROS production.


<!-- chunk -->

### The different wavelengths affect different proteins on

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondria. If too much of any spectrum of light is applied, mitochondria

<!-- chunk -->

### can produce too much ROS. This oxidative stress can damage mitochondria

<!-- chunk -->

### themselves and everything else in the cell.

<!-- chunk -->

### The clearest example of “too much” light is in skin cells. When people

<!-- chunk -->

### lay out in the sun, the photons of light stimulate mitochondria in their skin.

<!-- chunk -->

### When exposure is excessive, it can lead to premature aging of the skin

<!-- chunk -->

### (moles and wrinkles) or even skin cancer.


<!-- chunk -->

### Mitochondria are thought to

<!-- chunk -->

### play an important role in all of this.

<!-- chunk -->

### Light exposure also affects the brain. There are at least three ways this

<!-- chunk -->

### occurs:

<!-- chunk -->

### 1.I already told you about the SCN. It detects light through our eyes

<!-- chunk -->

### and sends circadian signals throughout the brain and body. These, in

<!-- chunk -->

### turn, impact mitochondrial function.

<!-- chunk -->

### 2.Light exposure on your skin increases a molecule called urocanic

<!-- chunk -->

### acid (UCA) in the bloodstream. UCA travels to the brain where it

<!-- chunk -->

### stimulates neurons that make glutamate. This has a direct impact on

<!-- chunk -->

### learning and memory.


<!-- chunk -->

### So, light exposure can help you think better.

<!-- chunk -->

### 3.Researchers can deliver red and near-infrared light to the scalp and

<!-- chunk -->

### even inside the nose. This treatment is called brain

<!-- chunk -->

### photobiomodulation. These lights increase ATP production, change

<!-- chunk -->

### calcium levels, and stimulate epigenetic signals through direct

<!-- chunk -->

### actions on mitochondria. They are thought to enhance the metabolic

<!-- chunk -->

### capacity of neurons, have anti-inflammatory effects, and stimulate

<!-- chunk -->

### neuroplasticity.


# The Effect of Sleep, Light, and Circadian Rhythms

# on Symptoms

<!-- chunk -->

### There are countless ways that modern humans mess up sleep. We take our

<!-- chunk -->

### phones to bed with us. We read in bed—with a light, of course. We wake up

<!-- chunk -->

### in the middle of the night and turn on our computers and televisions. We

<!-- chunk -->

### stay up late gaming or binge-watching Netflix. We work night shifts. We

<!-- chunk -->

### stay out all night and party. We pull all-nighters to finish important projects

<!-- chunk -->

### due the next day. We travel far distances and get jet-lagged. All these

<!-- chunk -->

### behaviors impact our circadian rhythms and sleep, taking a metabolic toll.

<!-- chunk -->

### Others can’t sleep, no matter how hard they try. Their minds race with

<!-- chunk -->

### worry and anxiety. They get restless. They wake up in a panic and can’t get

<!-- chunk -->

### back to sleep. They snore loudly and wake up constantly. They have

<!-- chunk -->

### flashbacks of their childhood abuse. They’re afraid to sleep. Their beds

<!-- chunk -->

### have become torture chambers. This also takes a metabolic toll.

<!-- chunk -->

### On a daily basis, sleep, light, and circadian rhythms have important

<!-- chunk -->

### effects on symptoms. People with mood disorders can experience

<!-- chunk -->

### fluctuations based on the time of day; this is called diurnal variation. Some

<!-- chunk -->

### wake up feeling very depressed, but their moods improve as the day goes

<!-- chunk -->

### on. People with dementia can get agitated and more confused at night—

<!-- chunk -->

### something

<!-- chunk -->

### called sundowning. Likewise, some people with schizophrenia can get more

<!-- chunk -->

### symptomatic at night, too. The brain energy theory offers a new way to

<!-- chunk -->

### understand these well-known phenomena through mitochondria and

<!-- chunk -->

### metabolism.

<!-- chunk -->

### Seasons can also affect symptoms. People with seasonal affective

<!-- chunk -->

### disorder, or depression during the winter months, are largely thought to be

<!-- chunk -->

### suffering from reduced exposure to sunlight. People with bipolar disorder

<!-- chunk -->

### can experience manic and depressive episodes around the change in

<!-- chunk -->

### seasons. The brain energy theory offers a new way to understand these

<!-- chunk -->

### changes, too.

# Sleep, Light, and Circadian Rhythms as Treatment

<!-- chunk -->

### Adequate sleep is critically important to mental and metabolic health. There

<!-- chunk -->

### are many ways it can play a role in treatment.

<!-- chunk -->

### First, you might want to assess your sleep using these basic questions

<!-- chunk -->

### (any “no” responses are worrisome):

<!-- chunk -->

### •Are you getting seven to nine hours of sleep every night?

<!-- chunk -->

### •Do you sleep well through the night?

<!-- chunk -->

### •Do you wake up feeling refreshed?

<!-- chunk -->

### •Are you able to sleep well without the use of pills or substances?

<!-- chunk -->

### •Do you feel reasonably awake and alert throughout the day?

<!-- chunk -->

### (Frequent naps or nodding off are worrisome signs.)

<!-- chunk -->

### If you have chronic sleep problems, talk with your healthcare professional

<!-- chunk -->

### to determine what might be causing them. You might have obstructive sleep

<!-- chunk -->

### apnea, restless legs syndrome, a hormonal imbalance, or other reason for

<!-- chunk -->

### your sleep problem. Interventions such as sleep hygiene and cognitive

<!-- chunk -->

### behavioral therapy for insomnia (CBT-I) can play a role in treatment. These

<!-- chunk -->

### can

<!-- chunk -->

### be done in person with a therapist, but both are now accessible over the

<!-- chunk -->

### internet, too.

<!-- chunk -->

### Sleeping pills, including over-the-counter supplements such as

<!-- chunk -->

### melatonin, can be helpful as short-term interventions for unusually stressful

<!-- chunk -->

### situations. However, sleeping pills impair the normal architecture of sleep,

<!-- chunk -->

### which may impact some of the benefits of natural sleep. They can also

<!-- chunk -->

### impair metabolism and mitochondrial function over time, so chronic use

<!-- chunk -->

### can potentially make your problems worse. Try to normalize your sleep

<!-- chunk -->

### without the use of pills. If you’ve been using sleeping pills for years, you

<!-- chunk -->

### may need professional help to get off them.

<!-- chunk -->

### Next, assess your light exposure (any “no” responses may be

<!-- chunk -->

### problematic).

<!-- chunk -->

### •Do you get exposure to natural light most days, even if just through

<!-- chunk -->

### a window?

<!-- chunk -->

### •Do you get outside?

<!-- chunk -->

### •Do you open the curtains or shades to allow light in?

<!-- chunk -->

### •When you sleep, are you in a dark room with minimal or no lights?

<!-- chunk -->

### •Do you avoid exposure to video screens while in bed (phone,

<!-- chunk -->

### television, tablet, etc.)?

<!-- chunk -->

### Correcting any problems with light exposure, either not enough during the

<!-- chunk -->

### day or too much at night, can play a role in treatment.

<!-- chunk -->

### Bright-light therapy is an intervention that involves sitting in front of a

<!-- chunk -->

### light every morning for about thirty minutes. These are special lights that

<!-- chunk -->

### are 10,000 lux (a measure of light intensity) that mimic exposure to sunlight

<!-- chunk -->

### but are generally safe on the eyes. Bright-light therapy has been used in a

<!-- chunk -->

### wide variety of disorders, including seasonal affective disorder, bipolar

<!-- chunk -->

### disorder, major depression, postpartum depression, insomnia, traumatic

<!-- chunk -->

### brain injury, and dementia.


<!-- chunk -->

### Interestingly, light exposure might even play a

<!-- chunk -->

### role in treating obesity, diabetes, and cardiovascular disease.


<!-- chunk -->

### Light

<!-- chunk -->

### therapy can

<!-- chunk -->

### help to regulate your circadian rhythms and normalize your sleep, which, as

<!-- chunk -->

### you now know, can have powerful effects on your metabolism and

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondria. I should warn you that I have seen some patients with bipolar

<!-- chunk -->

### disorder develop hypomania and even mania from bright-light therapy, so

<!-- chunk -->

### please use it cautiously if you have had mania in the past.

<!-- chunk -->

### I also mentioned brain photobiomodulation. This is still considered

<!-- chunk -->

### experimental but is being studied for a variety of conditions, such as

<!-- chunk -->

### dementia, Parkinson’s disease, stroke, traumatic brain injury, and

<!-- chunk -->

### depression.


<!-- chunk -->

### •Sleep, light, and circadian rhythms are all interconnected.

<!-- chunk -->

### •They all play a powerful role in metabolism, mitochondrial function,

<!-- chunk -->

### mental health, and metabolic health.

<!-- chunk -->

### •Diagnosing the cause of sleep problems is important, as it may

<!-- chunk -->

### require specific treatments.

<!-- chunk -->

### •There are many things that people can do to regulate sleep.

<!-- chunk -->

### •Controlling light exposure and/or using bright-light therapy can play

<!-- chunk -->

### a role in treatment for some people.

# Success Story: Kaleb—A Twelve-Year-Old Boy

# Struggling in School

<!-- chunk -->

### Kaleb lived in an upper-middle-class town and had a reasonably good life,

<!-- chunk -->

### although his parents were divorced (one on the list of adverse childhood

<!-- chunk -->

### experiences). He also had a strong family history of mental illness—his

<!-- chunk -->

### mother, father, aunts, uncles, and grandparents had suffered from

<!-- chunk -->

### depression, suicide attempts, substance abuse, bipolar disorder, and/or

<!-- chunk -->

### schizophrenia. Beginning in preschool, he had difficulties. As he grew

<!-- chunk -->

### older, he

<!-- chunk -->

### clearly met the criteria for ADHD; he ran wild at times and was often

<!-- chunk -->

### distractible. He would get frustrated with schoolwork and throw tantrums.

<!-- chunk -->

### He started psychotherapy. His parents and teachers tried many

<!-- chunk -->

### interventions, both disciplinary strategies and behavioral rewards. Nothing

<!-- chunk -->

### worked. He started a stimulant for his ADHD, which helped for about a

<!-- chunk -->

### week, but then he couldn’t sleep. That only made the problems worse.

<!-- chunk -->

### Different doses and different stimulants were tried, but the sleep problems

<!-- chunk -->

### weren’t improving. Sleeping pills were considered, but his parents decided

<!-- chunk -->

### to stop the stimulant instead.

<!-- chunk -->

### His trouble at school worsened. His IQ and learning abilities were high;

<!-- chunk -->

### they weren’t the problem. He got support at school via an individualized

<!-- chunk -->

### education program (IEP), and eventually he was enrolled in a special

<!-- chunk -->

### education track for students struggling with social/emotional problems. He

<!-- chunk -->

### began reporting chronic depression. When frustrated, he would jab himself

<!-- chunk -->

### with a sharp pencil. When really frustrated, he would threaten suicide. In

<!-- chunk -->

### seventh grade, both the school and his therapist began recommending a

<!-- chunk -->

### mood stabilizer for presumed bipolar disorder. His parents refused and

<!-- chunk -->

### instead wanted to try a “metabolic” treatment plan.

<!-- chunk -->

### We chose two interventions based on the metabolic basis of bipolar

<!-- chunk -->

### disorder. One issue we set out to address was insulin resistance. For the past

<!-- chunk -->

### couple of years, Kaleb had been gaining weight, especially around his

<!-- chunk -->

### waist, which is a marker of insulin resistance. He had taken to eating lots of

<!-- chunk -->

### sweets immediately after school “to deal with the stress of the day” and also

<!-- chunk -->

### after dinner “as a treat.” His parents allowed it given how stressful school

<!-- chunk -->

### had been for him. To address this, we recommended that he cut out all

<!-- chunk -->

### sweets during the school week. He wasn’t thrilled about this part of the

<!-- chunk -->

### treatment plan but agreed to try it. The second intervention was aimed at

<!-- chunk -->

### better regulating his circadian rhythms and sleep, both of which are known

<!-- chunk -->

### to play a role in bipolar disorder. We used bright-light therapy every

<!-- chunk -->

### morning for at least thirty minutes. This has been shown to be effective for

<!-- chunk -->

### bipolar depression, at least in some people, and it has few side effects.


<!-- chunk -->

### He

<!-- chunk -->

### already played video games every morning “to wake up,” so we introduced

<!-- chunk -->

### the bright light while he played his video games so that it didn’t require a

<!-- chunk -->

### change in his routine.

<!-- chunk -->

### Within one month, things began to improve. His tantrums at school stopped.

<!-- chunk -->

### His depression and his focus improved. School was becoming more

<!-- chunk -->

### manageable for him.

<!-- chunk -->

### The following year, in eighth grade, Kaleb got his best grades ever—

<!-- chunk -->

### straight As. In 2020, two years after beginning these interventions, he

<!-- chunk -->

### started high school during the COVID-19 pandemic. Although many of his

<!-- chunk -->

### peers were struggling with depression, anxiety, and social isolation, he

<!-- chunk -->

### thrived. He got straight As again and was taken off his IEP after the first

<!-- chunk -->

### semester. The new school couldn’t believe this well-behaved, top student

<!-- chunk -->

### was on an IEP in the first place.

<!-- chunk -->

### Kaleb has been on this treatment plan for four years now and continues

<!-- chunk -->

### to thrive. Clearly, this specific plan won’t work for all kids who are

<!-- chunk -->

### struggling, but it worked for Kaleb. The brain energy theory helps us

<!-- chunk -->

### understand how and why.

# W


# Food, Fasting, and Your Gut

<!-- chunk -->

### hat we eat, when we eat, and how much we eat have direct effects

<!-- chunk -->

### on metabolism and mitochondria. Everyone knows that diet plays a

<!-- chunk -->

### role in obesity, diabetes, and cardiovascular disease. What most

<!-- chunk -->

### people might not know is that diet also has profound effects on mental

<!-- chunk -->

### health and the brain.

<!-- chunk -->

### This field is massive. Tens of thousands of research articles and

<!-- chunk -->

### countless textbooks have explored the effects of diet on metabolism and

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondria. Most of this research has focused on obesity, diabetes,

<!-- chunk -->

### cardiovascular disease, Alzheimer’s disease, aging, and longevity. Although

<!-- chunk -->

### these researchers don’t usually see the connection with mental health, by

<!-- chunk -->

### now, I hope you do.

<!-- chunk -->

### The connections go far beyond correlations. They overlap at the level of

<!-- chunk -->

### neural circuits in the brain and, of course, the entire network of metabolism

<!-- chunk -->

### and mitochondria within the human body. For example, the neural circuits

<!-- chunk -->

### that drive appetite and eating behaviors have also been directly implicated

<!-- chunk -->

### in addiction to tobacco, alcohol, and heroin.


<!-- chunk -->

### That’s not too surprising to

<!-- chunk -->

### most people. What might be more surprising is that the neural circuits for

<!-- chunk -->

### loneliness overlap directly with the neural circuits that warn of starvation.


<!-- chunk -->

### This study, published in Nature, showed that chronic social isolation in the

<!-- chunk -->

### fruit fly led to increased eating and decreased sleep. A “social” problem led

<!-- chunk -->

### to changes in appetite and sleep. When the researchers artificially

<!-- chunk -->

### stimulated the neural circuit for social isolation, it caused the flies to eat

<!-- chunk -->

### more and sleep less. Another study identified specific GABA and serotonin

<!-- chunk -->

### neural circuits that were directly involved in obesity and anxiety and

<!-- chunk -->

### depression.


<!-- chunk -->

### One neural circuit plays a role in how much you weigh and

<!-- chunk -->

### how you feel.

<!-- chunk -->

### Some people call this field nutritional psychiatry, one that looks at the

<!-- chunk -->

### role of diet in mental health. Personally, I feel this is too narrow. It’s more

<!-- chunk -->

### than how diet affects brain function. It’s also about how our mental states

<!-- chunk -->

### affect our metabolism, which can impact appetite and feeding behavior,

<!-- chunk -->

### which can affect overall health. It’s a bidirectional relationship. Metabolic

<!-- chunk -->

### affects mental, and mental affects metabolic.

<!-- chunk -->

### As I said, this field is massive. I can’t possibly do it justice in one

<!-- chunk -->

### chapter. Nonetheless, I will give you a tiny taste (see what I did there?) of

<!-- chunk -->

### how this field relates to mental health by running through several food-

<!-- chunk -->

### related topics and tracing how they act as contributing causes under the

<!-- chunk -->

### brain energy theory.

# Vitamins and Nutrients

<!-- chunk -->

### One of the easiest places to start is with vitamins and nutrients. Several

<!-- chunk -->

### vitamin deficiencies are known to cause mental and neurological disorders.

<!-- chunk -->

### Correcting these vitamin deficiencies can sometimes completely solve the

<!-- chunk -->

### problem. Along with hormonal imbalances, vitamin deficiencies are one of

<!-- chunk -->

### the few examples in psychiatry where there is a clearly identified problem

<!-- chunk -->

### with a simple treatment.

<!-- chunk -->

### Three of the best-known vitamin deficiencies that can result in mental

<!-- chunk -->

### and neurological symptoms include thiamine, folate, and vitamin B12.

<!-- chunk -->

### These vitamins should be routinely checked in patients with psychiatric and

<!-- chunk -->

### neurological disorders because if they are low, there is a clear treatment for

<!-- chunk -->

### them. What do these vitamins do? They are all required for energy

<!-- chunk -->

### metabolism within mitochondria. If a person is deficient in these vitamins,

<!-- chunk -->

### they will have impaired energy production within mitochondria, aka

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondrial dysfunction.

<!-- chunk -->

### True to the theory of brain energy, the symptoms associated with these

<!-- chunk -->

### deficiencies are widespread and include most diagnostic categories. There

<!-- chunk -->

### are many physical symptoms as well as mental symptoms. The mental ones

<!-- chunk -->

### include depression, apathy, loss of appetite, irritability, confusion, memory

<!-- chunk -->

### impairment, sleep disturbances, fatigue, hallucinations, and delusions, to

<!-- chunk -->

### name just some of them. Deficiencies in these vitamins in pregnant women

<!-- chunk -->

### can also result in developmental abnormalities in their children,

<!-- chunk -->

### highlighting the role of mitochondria in development.

<!-- chunk -->

### There are many other vitamins and nutrients that can easily be

<!-- chunk -->

### connected to mitochondria and metabolism, but I’ll move on. As I said, this

<!-- chunk -->

### field is massive.

# Food Quality

<!-- chunk -->

### Our food supply has changed dramatically in the last fifty years. Plants have

<!-- chunk -->

### been genetically modified. Cattle, pigs, and chickens are pumped with

<!-- chunk -->

### antibiotics and growth hormones to make them fatter. Processed foods are

<!-- chunk -->

### filled with artificial ingredients and often devoid of nutrition, including

<!-- chunk -->

### fiber, vitamins, minerals, and phytonutrients. Understanding the effects of

<!-- chunk -->

### all these hormones and chemicals on human metabolism is far from clear,

<!-- chunk -->

### but research suggests they do have an impact.

<!-- chunk -->

### Junk food is often described as “junk” not only because it lacks

<!-- chunk -->

### important nutrients, but also because it usually contains highly processed

<!-- chunk -->

### and unnatural ingredients, which have been linked to poor metabolic health.

<!-- chunk -->

### We have all heard arguments about which ingredients are bad for us. Some

<!-- chunk -->

### blame fat; others blame carbohydrates; still others blame animal-sourced

<!-- chunk -->

### products. The controversies are endless. I’ll walk through three examples of

<!-- chunk -->

### dietary factors that have been directly linked to mitochondrial function and

<!-- chunk -->

### both metabolic and mental health.

<!-- chunk -->

### Trans fatty acids (TFAs) are man-made, processed fats that were

<!-- chunk -->

### originally marketed as a healthier alternative to saturated fats. We were told

<!-- chunk -->

### that “healthy vegetable shortening” was far better than lard. For years,

<!-- chunk -->

### TFAs were ubiquitous in the US food supply. Tragically, it turns out that

<!-- chunk -->

### they are in fact toxic to human health, and they have now been banned in

<!-- chunk -->

### the US. Their use has been associated with increased risk of cardiovascular

<!-- chunk -->

### disease, depression, behavioral aggression, irritability, and Alzheimer’s

<!-- chunk -->

### disease.


<!-- chunk -->

### Although the exact mechanisms are still unclear, one animal study

<!-- chunk -->

### tried to figure it out by assessing what impact TFAs might have on rats and

<!-- chunk -->

### their babies.


<!-- chunk -->

### Researchers gave pregnant and lactating rats either TFAs or

<!-- chunk -->

### soybean/fish oil with their diets. When the baby rats were born, they got a

<!-- chunk -->

### normal diet without TFAs. At sixty days, the babies from the mothers who

<!-- chunk -->

### got TFAs showed greater anxiety, higher levels of ROS, greater

<!-- chunk -->

### inflammation, and reduced glucocorticoid receptors in the hippocampus.

<!-- chunk -->

### This one study demonstrates how several things I’ve discussed so far are all

<!-- chunk -->

### interconnected. A single factor in a mother’s diet ended up affecting her

<!-- chunk -->

### children’s anxiety, mitochondrial function, inflammation, and

<!-- chunk -->

### glucocorticoid receptor levels, which plays a role in the stress response.

<!-- chunk -->

### Wow! Luckily, these have been banned in the US as of 2018, but could

<!-- chunk -->

### something like this account for the higher rates of depression and anxiety in

<!-- chunk -->

### the youth of America? I described how parents can transmit a vulnerability

<!-- chunk -->

### to mental illness from their own trauma history. What this research suggests

<!-- chunk -->

### is that if your mother ate trans fats while she was pregnant with you, it is

<!-- chunk -->

### possible that this might play a role in your metabolic health.

<!-- chunk -->

### Sometimes, junk food isn’t called junk because of the “bad” things in it,

<!-- chunk -->

### but instead because of the “good” things that it doesn’t contain. Let’s look

<!-- chunk -->

### at fiber. As you likely know, fiber is found in fruits, vegetables, and whole

<!-- chunk -->

### grains and is highly recommended these days. Most experts are certain that

<!-- chunk -->

### it plays a beneficial role in metabolic health and aging. Some studies

<!-- chunk -->

### suggest that it also plays a role in mental health. High levels of adherence to

<!-- chunk -->

### the Mediterranean diet, which includes lots of fruits, vegetables, whole

<!-- chunk -->

### grains, and olive oil, has been associated with lower rates of depression and

<!-- chunk -->

### cognitive impairment.


<!-- chunk -->

### One of the biggest benefits of fiber is its conversion

<!-- chunk -->

### by microbes in the gut into butyrate, a short-chain fatty acid. Butyrate, in

<!-- chunk -->

### turn, serves as a primary fuel source for the mitochondria in gut cells

<!-- chunk -->

### (colonocytes). It also plays a role in liver cells. One research group found

<!-- chunk -->

### that butyrate directly changes mitochondrial function, efficiency, and

<!-- chunk -->

### dynamics (fusion/fission), and that these changes directly impact insulin

<!-- chunk -->

### resistance, fat accumulation in the liver, and overall metabolism.


<!-- chunk -->

### There are

<!-- chunk -->

### strong connections between the gut and the brain that I’ll get to shortly, but

<!-- chunk -->

### interestingly, butyrate itself appears to directly play a role in sleep! And

<!-- chunk -->

### even more fascinating is that the mechanism appears to be located in the

<!-- chunk -->

### liver or the vein going to the liver (portal vein). Researchers studied mice to

<!-- chunk -->

### work this all out.


<!-- chunk -->

### They injected butyrate into their guts or their portal veins

<!-- chunk -->

### and found that the mice slept 50 to 70 percent more. When they injected

<!-- chunk -->

### butyrate into other parts of their bodies, it did nothing for sleep. Another

<!-- chunk -->

### research study found that butyrate decreases neuroinflammation in aging

<!-- chunk -->

### mice, something that might protect against Alzheimer’s disease.


<!-- chunk -->

### Sometimes, it’s not about one specific ingredient. Instead, it might have

<!-- chunk -->

### more to do with how much we eat. Junk food can be addictive, at least for

<!-- chunk -->

### some of us. We all know the saying, “You can’t eat just one.” Is overeating

<!-- chunk -->

### the problem? This can lead to high levels of insulin and blood glucose,

<!-- chunk -->

### especially in people with insulin resistance. I already told you how insulin

<!-- chunk -->

### resistance is related to mental disorders and mitochondria. Does high blood

<!-- chunk -->

### glucose play a more direct role? Some research suggests that it does.

<!-- chunk -->

### One study in diabetic rats found that high levels of glucose directly

<!-- chunk -->

### impaired mitochondria, as measured by decreased ATP production,

<!-- chunk -->

### increased oxidative stress, and decreased antioxidant capacities, and that all

<!-- chunk -->

### of this likely damages neurons.


<!-- chunk -->

### Another study looked at human endothelial cells (cells that line arteries)

<!-- chunk -->

### to see if high glucose levels affect their mitochondrial function. They found

<!-- chunk -->

### that it does. Although it didn’t change baseline energy production, when the

<!-- chunk -->

### cells were stressed, those exposed to high levels of glucose had impaired

<!-- chunk -->

### ability to produce more energy. Again, a paradox. More glucose, or fuel, led

<!-- chunk -->

### to lower levels of ATP.


<!-- chunk -->

### Another study looked at twenty people with diabetes to see what impact

<!-- chunk -->

### high blood glucose had on their mood and brain function.


<!-- chunk -->

### They used a

<!-- chunk -->

### clamp technology to artificially control blood glucose and exposed all the

<!-- chunk -->

### people to both normal and high glucose levels. High glucose led to

<!-- chunk -->

### impairment in processing speed, memory, and attention, and also led to

<!-- chunk -->

### reduced energy levels, increased sadness, and anxiety. This research

<!-- chunk -->

### suggests that if people with insulin resistance overeat comfort foods, it

<!-- chunk -->

### might actually cause them to feel sad and anxious and have cognitive

<!-- chunk -->

### impairment.

<!-- chunk -->

### Finally, a meta-analysis of forty-six studies that included over 98,000

<!-- chunk -->

### participants who did not yet have diabetes looked at levels of blood glucose

<!-- chunk -->

### to see if this increased risk for brain changes related to Alzheimer’s disease.

<!-- chunk -->

### The researchers found that higher levels of blood glucose increased risk for

<!-- chunk -->

### higher levels of amyloid and brain shrinkage.


<!-- chunk -->

### Could elevated blood glucose account for the higher rates of depression

<!-- chunk -->

### and Alzheimer’s disease in people with diabetes? All of this research

<!-- chunk -->

### suggests that it might play a role.

<!-- chunk -->

### But wait . . . it’s another feedback loop! It turns out mitochondria play a

<!-- chunk -->

### direct role in controlling glucose levels. A study published in Cell found

<!-- chunk -->

### that mitochondria in the cells of the ventromedial nucleus of the

<!-- chunk -->

### hypothalamus (VMH), an area of the brain known to regulate glucose levels

<!-- chunk -->

### throughout the body, were critical in this regulation.


<!-- chunk -->

### Their fission with

<!-- chunk -->

### each other and their levels of ROS directly control glucose levels

<!-- chunk -->

### throughout the body. So, if these mitochondria aren’t functioning properly,

<!-- chunk -->

### glucose regulation will be off. This, in turn, might cause sadness, anxiety,

<!-- chunk -->

### and increase the likelihood of developing Alzheimer’s disease.

# Obesity

<!-- chunk -->

### Obesity is a complex topic. Most people think of it as a problem of

<!-- chunk -->

### overeating—people are eating more calories than they are burning. But

<!-- chunk -->

### “eating more calories than they are burning” has two parts. Sometimes

<!-- chunk -->

### people are eating too much food—in that case the question is why they are

<!-- chunk -->

### eating

<!-- chunk -->

### too much. But the second part of the equation is burning calories. Some

<!-- chunk -->

### people who struggle with obesity can eat very little and still fail to lose

<!-- chunk -->

### weight. Therefore, a better question is this: Why are those who are obese

<!-- chunk -->

### storing so much fat and/or not burning it? The reality is that almost

<!-- chunk -->

### everyone overeats on occasion. Think of Thanksgiving. Those who are thin

<!-- chunk -->

### can feel stuffed even into the next day. This drives them to eat less. Or their

<!-- chunk -->

### metabolism increases to burn off the excess calories. Either way, they go on

<!-- chunk -->

### being thin. Obese people don’t have the same responses. In fact, sometimes,

<!-- chunk -->

### when obese people lose weight, their metabolism plummets. It fights their

<!-- chunk -->

### efforts to lose weight.

<!-- chunk -->

### These are complex topics. I’m not going to try to address them here.

<!-- chunk -->

### Instead, my goal is to highlight that obesity does play a role in metabolism

<!-- chunk -->

### and mitochondrial function, and mitochondria play a role in obesity. These

<!-- chunk -->

### things, in turn, are all related to mental health.

<!-- chunk -->

### I’ve mentioned that loneliness, anxiety, depression, and sleep share

<!-- chunk -->

### some neural circuits with appetite and feeding behaviors. What would

<!-- chunk -->

### happen to someone if these neural circuits were hyperexcitable? Well, that

<!-- chunk -->

### person would feel depressed, anxious, inappropriately lonely, have trouble

<!-- chunk -->

### sleeping, and would overeat. Do you know anyone like that? I can tell you

<!-- chunk -->

### that I’ve met many people exactly like this in my career as a psychiatrist.

<!-- chunk -->

### Both obesity and mental disorders are associated with mitochondrial

<!-- chunk -->

### dysfunction. When people have both, these conditions can make each other

<!-- chunk -->

### worse. Mental disorders can lead to even more weight gain. Obesity can

<!-- chunk -->

### lead to even more depression, anxiety, and bipolar symptoms. One study

<!-- chunk -->

### looked at bipolar patients, some who were obese and others who weren’t,

<!-- chunk -->

### and found that the obese patients had more depressive episodes than the

<!-- chunk -->

### thin ones.


<!-- chunk -->

### Obesity itself was playing a role in their mood symptoms.

<!-- chunk -->

### One way to understand some of this is through insulin. I’ve already

<!-- chunk -->

### shared some information about insulin, mitochondrial function, and brain

<!-- chunk -->

### function. People with obesity usually have insulin resistance, in both their

<!-- chunk -->

### bodies and brains. One group of researchers specifically looked to see if

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondrial dysfunction is playing a role, and sure enough, they found

<!-- chunk -->

### signs of mitochondrial impairment in both the brains and livers of insulin-

<!-- chunk -->

### resistant rats.


<!-- chunk -->

### Insulin resistance takes on a life of its own, though. The pancreas responds

<!-- chunk -->

### to insulin resistance by pumping out more insulin. If a little isn’t working,

<!-- chunk -->

### then send out a lot. That helps! But the problem is that higher levels of

<!-- chunk -->

### insulin usually make insulin resistance even worse over time. They drive

<!-- chunk -->

### hunger and weight gain. And one of the problems with higher and higher

<!-- chunk -->

### levels of insulin is that insulin resistance alone inhibits mitochondrial

<!-- chunk -->

### biogenesis, compounding the metabolic problem.


<!-- chunk -->

### In the brain, insulin plays a direct role in how mitochondria respond to

<!-- chunk -->

### stress. When insulin signaling is working fine, mitochondria respond to

<!-- chunk -->

### stress effectively. One way to stress mitochondria in mice is to feed them a

<!-- chunk -->

### high-fat diet (HFD), which usually leads to obesity. Researchers studied

<!-- chunk -->

### mice with insulin resistance being fed an HFD and found impairment in the

<!-- chunk -->

### stress response in mitochondria.


<!-- chunk -->

### When they gave the mice intranasal

<!-- chunk -->

### insulin, the mitochondria responded normally again, and interestingly, the

<!-- chunk -->

### mice gained less weight. So, helping mitochondria function properly helped

<!-- chunk -->

### the mice deal more effectively with the HFD.

<!-- chunk -->

### Another research group also looked at the role of mitochondria in brain

<!-- chunk -->

### cells of mice given an HFD.


<!-- chunk -->

### They found that microglial cells were

<!-- chunk -->

### causing brain inflammation in response to the HFD. This occurred before

<!-- chunk -->

### the mice gained any weight. When they looked further to see what was

<!-- chunk -->

### driving the changes in the microglial cells, they found that it was

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondria. There was an increase in a specific mitochondrial protein,

<!-- chunk -->

### UCP2, that was driving changes in mitochondrial dynamics (movement,

<!-- chunk -->

### fusion, and fission). When the researchers deleted this protein, the mice no

<!-- chunk -->

### longer had brain inflammation, and shockingly, did not develop obesity

<!-- chunk -->

### even though they continued to have access to the high-fat diet. Instead,

<!-- chunk -->

### these mice ended up eating less and were burning more calories. This study

<!-- chunk -->

### suggests that mitochondria are, in fact, key players in how the brain and

<!-- chunk -->

### body respond to high-calorie foods. Two other studies published in Cell

<!-- chunk -->

### confirm a direct role of mitochondria in brain cells in the regulation of

<!-- chunk -->

### feeding behaviors, obesity, and leptin resistance.


<!-- chunk -->

### When I first read this study, I was confused. The researchers clearly

<!-- chunk -->

### demonstrated a direct role of mitochondria in both brain inflammation and

<!-- chunk -->

### subsequent obesity. However, they interfered with what the mitochondria

<!-- chunk -->

### were normally doing. So, one way to look at this study is to think that the

<!-- chunk -->

### microglial mitochondria were dysfunctional and the researchers prevented

<!-- chunk -->

### them from making a mistake. Another way to look at it is that these

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondria were receiving erroneous signals from somewhere else in the

<!-- chunk -->

### body, maybe the gut microbiome, or gut cells, or the liver. Or maybe it was

<!-- chunk -->

### due to insulin resistance, as I described earlier. But another distinct

<!-- chunk -->

### possibility is that the mitochondria were doing exactly what they are

<!-- chunk -->

### programmed to do; they may have been looking out for the long-term health

<!-- chunk -->

### of the organism. At this point, we don’t know what the correct response

<!-- chunk -->

### should be to a toxic diet. Maybe obesity is a better survival strategy when

<!-- chunk -->

### consuming toxic foods. We can’t be certain that preventing the

<!-- chunk -->

### inflammation and obesity in this case would lead to better health outcomes

<!-- chunk -->

### or longevity, but I suppose that study would be easy enough to do. As you

<!-- chunk -->

### can see, it’s complicated. And then again . . . it’s not. If you want to prevent

<!-- chunk -->

### obesity in mice, don’t feed them the toxic diet.

<!-- chunk -->

### Just for the record, the “high-fat diet” can also contain other unhealthy

<!-- chunk -->

### ingredients, such as sucrose—it’s often the evil combination that is so

<!-- chunk -->

### fattening. I mention this because I will soon be discussing a different type

<!-- chunk -->

### of high-fat diet that usually results in weight loss and lower levels of

<!-- chunk -->

### inflammation, so it’s not the fat, per se.

# Fasting, Starvation, and Eating Disorders

<!-- chunk -->

### Fasting is going without food. For any amount of time, really. We all fast

<!-- chunk -->

### when we sleep. That’s why breakfast has its name—it’s breaking a fast.

<!-- chunk -->

### Fasting for longer periods of time results in many changes to metabolism

<!-- chunk -->

### and mitochondria. Interestingly, it can also have profound, beneficial effects

<!-- chunk -->

### on the human body. To most people, this is surprising. We usually think

<!-- chunk -->

### about our bodies needing food and nutrients. We’ve all heard that we need

<!-- chunk -->

### three square meals a day. Some of us have been told to eat six or eight times

<!-- chunk -->

### throughout the day. We need to keep fueling our bodies. We need the

<!-- chunk -->

### energy.

<!-- chunk -->

### For infants, this is unequivocally true—most need feedings every two

<!-- chunk -->

### hours. For children who are growing, this can be true as well. But for

<!-- chunk -->

### adults, there is now a tremendous amount of science to suggest that eating

<!-- chunk -->

### all the time actually harms health.

<!-- chunk -->

### Fasting prompts the body to be frugal and encourages autophagy, which

<!-- chunk -->

### has tremendous healing potential. The body hunkers down and makes do

<!-- chunk -->

### with what it has. It’s time to tap those fat stores. We all know that can be

<!-- chunk -->

### good. But there’s more to the story than that. Each and every cell responds,

<!-- chunk -->

### and mitochondria are right there to direct things. They immediately change

<!-- chunk -->

### shape. They elongate themselves, fuse with each other, and form long

<!-- chunk -->

### tubular networks.


<!-- chunk -->

### What ensues is a spring-cleaning, if you will. The cells

<!-- chunk -->

### identify old and defective proteins and cell parts. They are the first to go in

<!-- chunk -->

### a massive recycling campaign. These proteins and parts are shuttled to

<!-- chunk -->

### lysosomes for degradation. These nutrients are then recycled; some get used

<!-- chunk -->

### for energy, while others might be used for new, essential proteins and cell

<!-- chunk -->

### parts. The cells are looking for anything and everything that is expendable

<!-- chunk -->

### in a carefully orchestrated reboot.

<!-- chunk -->

### So, what about the cells’ mitochondria? Are they also getting

<!-- chunk -->

### destroyed? Well, the defective ones are, as mitophagy also gets activated

<!-- chunk -->

### during this process. But the healthy mitochondria are now working with

<!-- chunk -->

### each other in the long tubular networks. They keep up the pace of ATP

<!-- chunk -->

### production during this process, and these networks protect them from being

<!-- chunk -->

### recycled. When the person eats again, the cell parts that got destroyed get

<!-- chunk -->

### replaced. These replacement parts are new and healthy, and they often

<!-- chunk -->

### include some new mitochondria!

<!-- chunk -->

### However, if people go without food for too long, at some point, it turns

<!-- chunk -->

### into starvation. The body mounts a defense strategy. It lowers metabolism

<!-- chunk -->

### broadly to conserve energy. Heart rate slows. Body temperature declines.

<!-- chunk -->

### People become sluggish, irritable, unmotivated, distractible, obsessed with

<!-- chunk -->

### food, and somewhat depressed. Paradoxically, symptoms of hypomania can

<!-- chunk -->

### emerge within the first week or two of starvation. This is likely an adaptive

<!-- chunk -->

### strategy to give the starving person enough energy, motivation, and

<!-- chunk -->

### confidence to get food, no matter what.

<!-- chunk -->

### As I’m sure you know, this is not good. It’s life-threatening. Cells begin to

<!-- chunk -->

### malfunction and die off. Organs are struggling, and this includes the brain.

<!-- chunk -->

### The list of mental symptoms and diagnoses includes depression, irritability,

<!-- chunk -->

### insomnia, mania, eating disorders, confusion, memory disturbances,

<!-- chunk -->

### hallucinations, and delusions.

<!-- chunk -->

### Perhaps the best evidence for the mental effects of starvation come from

<!-- chunk -->

### the famous Minnesota Starvation Experiment, in which thirty-six healthy

<!-- chunk -->

### men were subjected to semi-starvation diets (half their normal daily

<!-- chunk -->

### calories) for twenty-four weeks, and then received twenty weeks of

<!-- chunk -->

### “rehabilitation.” The men lost significant amounts of weight and showed

<!-- chunk -->

### signs of slowed metabolism. They experienced wide-ranging and

<!-- chunk -->

### sometimes severe mental symptoms, including depression, anxiety, fatigue,

<!-- chunk -->

### poor concentration, and obsessions with food. Some became transiently

<!-- chunk -->

### hypomanic. Interestingly, it was during the refeeding period that some had

<!-- chunk -->

### the most difficulty. Depression got worse in some of them. Others began

<!-- chunk -->

### binging and purging. Some developed body image concerns. One man cut

<!-- chunk -->

### off three fingers. This study is often used today to understand some of the

<!-- chunk -->

### symptoms of anorexia and bulimia. Starvation itself can cause mental

<!-- chunk -->

### symptoms.


<!-- chunk -->

### This leads to a discussion of eating disorders.

<!-- chunk -->

### For some people, an eating disorder can start through societal pressure

<!-- chunk -->

### to lose weight and be thin. Young, female ballet dancers are an obvious

<!-- chunk -->

### example. Many young women who dance are told in no uncertain terms that

<!-- chunk -->

### they must be thin to compete. There is tremendous pressure to lose weight.

<!-- chunk -->

### For the girls and women who follow these recommendations, they can put

<!-- chunk -->

### themselves into starvation. This can start the vicious cycle of metabolic

<!-- chunk -->

### disturbances that impact brain function. The parts of the brain that control

<!-- chunk -->

### feeding behaviors are impacted, but so are the parts of the brain that

<!-- chunk -->

### interpret how they perceive their bodies. They can develop severe

<!-- chunk -->

### distortions in body image, thinking they are fat when they are truly

<!-- chunk -->

### emaciated. This sometimes verges on delusional, because the way they

<!-- chunk -->

### perceive themselves can be so far removed from how they really look.

<!-- chunk -->

### Researchers looked at a mouse model of anorexia to see if, in fact, there is

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondrial impairment in the brain, and sure enough, they found

<!-- chunk -->

### oxidative stress and

<!-- chunk -->

### impairment of specific parts of mitochondria within the hypothalamus.


<!-- chunk -->

### A

<!-- chunk -->

### study of forty women, half with anorexia and the other half without, found

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondrial dysfunction in the white blood cells of those with anorexia.


<!-- chunk -->

### However, other people can develop an eating disorder because of a

<!-- chunk -->

### preexisting vulnerability. Eating behaviors impact metabolism, whether it’s

<!-- chunk -->

### overeating or undereating. For some, this can result in short-term,

<!-- chunk -->

### rewarding feelings.

<!-- chunk -->

### Binge eating can make some people feel better because it provides more

<!-- chunk -->

### insulin and glucose to struggling brain cells, and it stimulates the reward

<!-- chunk -->

### centers in the brain. This may be the quickest and easiest way to overcome

<!-- chunk -->

### insulin resistance—eat a lot of sugar. Unfortunately, as I just discussed, this

<!-- chunk -->

### makes matter worse over time. For others, restricting eating can improve

<!-- chunk -->

### mood because it can provide stress hormones or ketones (you’ll hear more

<!-- chunk -->

### about these soon) that can be helpful to struggling brain cells. These two

<!-- chunk -->

### extremes of overeating and undereating can both produce rewarding

<!-- chunk -->

### experiences in different people. So, for people with preexisting mental

<!-- chunk -->

### disorders who are already struggling, changing eating behaviors can be

<!-- chunk -->

### appealing. For some, it can become a way of life, even if it causes health

<!-- chunk -->

### problems. This likely explains why people with all mental disorders are

<!-- chunk -->

### more likely to develop an eating disorder. They are looking for ways to feel

<!-- chunk -->

### better.

# The Gut-Brain Axis and the Microbiome

<!-- chunk -->

### Over the past few decades, a growing body of research suggests that our

<!-- chunk -->

### intestinal tract plays an important role in both metabolic and mental health.

<!-- chunk -->

### Many signals get sent from the digestive tract to the brain, and vice versa.

<!-- chunk -->

### There appear to be many mechanisms by which this communication occurs.

<!-- chunk -->

### I’ll briefly look at a few of them.

<!-- chunk -->

### First, it has become increasingly clear that the trillions of bacteria,

<!-- chunk -->

### fungi, and viruses in our guts play an important role in human health,

<!-- chunk -->

### especially obesity, diabetes, and cardiovascular disease. For example,

<!-- chunk -->

### animal studies

<!-- chunk -->

### have shown that gut microbes can affect weight. In one study, researchers

<!-- chunk -->

### found that the microbiome in obese mice extracts more nutrients and

<!-- chunk -->

### calories from food than in thin mice. When this obese microbiome is

<!-- chunk -->

### transferred to thin mice, the thin mice gain weight.


<!-- chunk -->

### There is also increasing evidence for the role of the gut microbiome in

<!-- chunk -->

### mental disorders. Animal models and small human trials have shown that

<!-- chunk -->

### the gut microbiome appears to play a role in depression, anxiety, autism,

<!-- chunk -->

### schizophrenia, bipolar disorder, and eating disorders. There is also evidence

<!-- chunk -->

### for the role of the gut microbiome in epilepsy and neurodegenerative


<!-- chunk -->

### Gut bacteria get first dibs on all the food we eat. They produce a variety

<!-- chunk -->

### of metabolites, neurotransmitters, and hormones that they secrete into our

<!-- chunk -->

### guts. These get absorbed into our bloodstream and can affect our

<!-- chunk -->

### metabolism and brain function.

<!-- chunk -->

### A second way that signals get sent from the gut to the brain is through

<!-- chunk -->

### hormones and neuropeptides that are produced in the cells that line the

<!-- chunk -->

### intestinal tract. These, too, are known to travel throughout the body,

<!-- chunk -->

### including to the brain, and have widespread effects on metabolism and brain

<!-- chunk -->

### function.

<!-- chunk -->

### Finally, the gut has an intricate nervous system unto itself that

<!-- chunk -->

### communicates directly with the brain and vice versa. The vagus nerve plays

<!-- chunk -->

### an important role in this communication. As I mentioned, about 90 percent

<!-- chunk -->

### of the body’s total serotonin is produced in the intestinal tract.

<!-- chunk -->

### This field of the gut-brain axis and the microbiome can quickly become

<!-- chunk -->

### overwhelming when one begins to think about all the different microbes,

<!-- chunk -->

### metabolites, hormones, neurotransmitters, neuropeptides, and other factors

<!-- chunk -->

### involved. However, there is a clear connection between all these factors.

<!-- chunk -->

### They all relate to metabolism and mitochondria. There is evidence that gut

<!-- chunk -->

### microbes send signals directly to mitochondria in the cells lining the gut

<!-- chunk -->

### and immune cells. These signals have been shown to change mitochondrial

<!-- chunk -->

### metabolism, alter the barrier function of the gut cells, and can lead to

<!-- chunk -->

### inflammation.


# Food, Fasting, and the Gut Microbiome as

# Treatments

<!-- chunk -->

### There are at least eight different ways that dietary interventions can be

<!-- chunk -->

### helpful in addressing mental symptoms:


<!-- chunk -->

### 1.Addressing nutritional deficiencies, such as folate, vitamin B12, and

<!-- chunk -->

### thiamine deficiency.

<!-- chunk -->

### 2.Removing dietary allergens or toxins. For example, some people

<!-- chunk -->

### have an autoimmune disorder called celiac disease that results in

<!-- chunk -->

### inflammation and other metabolic problems in response to gluten.

<!-- chunk -->

### This can also affect brain function. I’ve described the toxic effects of

<!-- chunk -->

### TFAs. There are many other dietary ingredients that can also impair

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondrial function.

<!-- chunk -->

### 3.Eating a “healthy diet,” such as the Mediterranean diet, may play a

<!-- chunk -->

### role for some people.

<!-- chunk -->

### 4.Exploring fasting, intermittent fasting (IF), and fasting-mimicking

<!-- chunk -->

### diets (more below on all three) that stimulate autophagy and

<!-- chunk -->

### mitophagy to improve metabolic health.

<!-- chunk -->

### 5.Improving the gut microbiome (more on how below).

<!-- chunk -->

### 6.Improving metabolism and mitochondrial function with a dietary

<!-- chunk -->

### intervention. This includes changes in insulin resistance, metabolic

<!-- chunk -->

### rate, the number of mitochondria in cells, the overall health of

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondria, hormones, inflammation, and many other known

<!-- chunk -->

### regulators of metabolism.

<!-- chunk -->

### 7.Dietary changes that result in losing weight can help to mitigate the

<!-- chunk -->

### problems associated with obesity.

<!-- chunk -->

### 8.Dietary changes that result in gaining weight can be a life-saving

<!-- chunk -->

### intervention for those who are severely underweight.

<!-- chunk -->

### A comprehensive discussion of all these areas is beyond the scope of this

<!-- chunk -->

### book. Instead, I’ll discuss a few highlights.

<!-- chunk -->

## Vitamins and Nutraceuticals

<!-- chunk -->

### Addressing vitamin and nutritional deficiencies is important. However,

<!-- chunk -->

### taking twenty-plus vitamins and supplements is not the answer to most

<!-- chunk -->

### metabolic problems. Sometimes, excessive use of vitamins and supplements

<!-- chunk -->

### can actually cause metabolic problems. Healthy metabolism is about

<!-- chunk -->

### balance—not too much and not too little.

<!-- chunk -->

### Many vitamins and supplements, or nutraceuticals, might play a role in

<!-- chunk -->

### improving mitochondrial function and production. The list of possibilities is

<!-- chunk -->

### long. It includes L-methylfolate, Vitamin B12, SAMe, N-acetylcysteine

<!-- chunk -->

### (NAC), L-tryptophan, zinc, magnesium, omega-3 fatty acids, nicotinamide

<!-- chunk -->

### riboside, alpha-lipoic acid, arginine, carnitine, citrulline, choline, co-

<!-- chunk -->

### enzyme Q10, creatine, folinic acid, niacin, riboflavin, thiamine, resveratrol,

<!-- chunk -->

### pterostilbene, and antioxidants.


<!-- chunk -->

### It’s unlikely that all of these will be

<!-- chunk -->

### beneficial for all people, and by no means should anyone take all of these at

<!-- chunk -->

### the same time.

<!-- chunk -->

### Here’s a great example to illustrate this caution. Researchers gave

<!-- chunk -->

### patients with bipolar depression one of three treatments: (1) a

<!-- chunk -->

### “mitochondrial cocktail,” (2) NAC alone, or (3) placebo for sixteen weeks

<!-- chunk -->

### as an add-on to their existing treatments.


<!-- chunk -->

### The mitochondrial cocktail

<!-- chunk -->

### included N-acetylcysteine, acetyl-L-carnitine, Co Q10, magnesium,

<!-- chunk -->

### calcium, vitamin D3, vitamin E, alpha-lipoic acid, vitamin A, biotin,

<!-- chunk -->

### thiamine, riboflavin, nicotinamide, calcium pantothenate, pyridoxine

<!-- chunk -->

### hydrochloride, folic acid, and vitamin B12. Wow! That’s a cocktail. And

<!-- chunk -->

### guess what they found? No difference in any of the groups.

<!-- chunk -->

### Again, low levels of these vitamins and other factors may simply be a

<!-- chunk -->

### consequence of mitochondrial dysfunction, not the cause of it. If that’s the

<!-- chunk -->

### case, adding more may not solve the problem. And pills like this don’t

<!-- chunk -->

### automatically stimulate mitochondrial biogenesis or mitophagy. But dietary

<!-- chunk -->

### interventions, good sleep, stress reduction, removing mitochondrially

<!-- chunk -->

### impairing medications, and exercise can!

<!-- chunk -->

## Diet and Fasting

<!-- chunk -->

### I shared with you that people who adhere to the Mediterranean diet (MD)

<!-- chunk -->

### are less likely to develop depression. But for people who are already

<!-- chunk -->

### depressed, can adopting this diet improve symptoms? It appears that it can,

<!-- chunk -->

### at least for some people. One trial, cleverly called the SMILES trial,

<!-- chunk -->

### randomized sixty-seven people with major depression to groups that

<!-- chunk -->

### encouraged people to follow the MD or a social support group (the control

<!-- chunk -->

### group). Participants continued their existing treatments for depression

<!-- chunk -->

### (medications or therapy). After twelve weeks, 32 percent of the people in

<!-- chunk -->

### the MD group achieved remission compared with only 8 percent in the

<!-- chunk -->

### control group.


<!-- chunk -->

### Is there any evidence that this is due to metabolism or

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondria? Well, we have at least one study to call upon.

<!-- chunk -->

### Researchers looked at monkeys (cynomolgus macaques) fed an MD

<!-- chunk -->

### versus a Western diet (standard American diet) for thirty months and then

<!-- chunk -->

### measured brain mitochondrial function, energy utilization patterns, and

<!-- chunk -->

### biomarkers such as insulin levels.


<!-- chunk -->

### They found diminished bioenergetic

<!-- chunk -->

### patterns between brain regions in the monkeys getting the Western diet,

<!-- chunk -->

### which correlated with levels of insulin and glucose. The mitochondria in the

<!-- chunk -->

### MD-fed animals maintained normal differences between brain regions,

<!-- chunk -->

### whereas the mitochondria in the Western-fed animals lost these normal

<!-- chunk -->

### distinctions. The brain areas affected are known to play a role in diabetes

<!-- chunk -->

### and Alzheimer’s disease.

<!-- chunk -->

### There is also evidence that fasting, intermittent fasting (IF), and fasting-

<!-- chunk -->

### mimicking diets may play a role in treating mental disorders. They all result

<!-- chunk -->

### in the production of ketone bodies, which are made when fat is being used

<!-- chunk -->

### as an energy source. Fat gets turned into ketones. And, interestingly, this

<!-- chunk -->

### process occurs exclusively in mitochondria, yet another role for these

<!-- chunk -->

### magnificent organelles.

<!-- chunk -->

### Ketones are an alternate source of energy to cells. They also serve as

<!-- chunk -->

### important metabolic signaling molecules, resulting in epigenetic changes.

<!-- chunk -->

### Ketones can be a rescue energy source to insulin-resistant brain cells. While

<!-- chunk -->

### glucose might have trouble getting into these cells, ketones can get inside

<!-- chunk -->

### pretty easily. Fasting also results in autophagy, as I’ve described. There are

<!-- chunk -->

### several versions of IF. Some restrict eating to eight to twelve hours per day.

<!-- chunk -->

### Others allow one meal a day. And others restrict nighttime eating.

<!-- chunk -->

### We have evidence that IF improves mood and cognition, and protects

<!-- chunk -->

### neurons from damage in animal models of epilepsy and Alzheimer’s

<!-- chunk -->

### disease. One group of researchers set out to understand how and why.


<!-- chunk -->

### You’ll never guess what they found—it’s mitochondria! When researchers

<!-- chunk -->

### put mice on an IF routine, they found that the hippocampus, a brain region

<!-- chunk -->

### often involved in depression, anxiety, and memory disorders, was largely

<!-- chunk -->

### driving the improvements from IF. It appeared to be due primarily to higher

<!-- chunk -->

### levels of GABA activity, which reduced hyperexcitability. Then the

<!-- chunk -->

### researchers went further to understand what was causing this change in

<!-- chunk -->

### GABA activity. They removed sirtuin 3 from the mice in two different ways

<!-- chunk -->

### —recall that this protein is exclusive and essential to mitochondrial health.

<!-- chunk -->

### When they did this, all the benefits were lost. This clearly implicates

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondria directly in the benefits of IF on brain health.

<!-- chunk -->

### Another review article outlines many of the ways that IF is thought to

<!-- chunk -->

### promote brain health, including reducing oxidative stress and inflammation,

<!-- chunk -->

### improving mitophagy and mitochondrial biogenesis, increasing brain-

<!-- chunk -->

### derived neurotropic factor (BDNF), improving neuroplasticity, and

<!-- chunk -->

### promoting cellular stress resistance.


<!-- chunk -->

### These are powerful healing

<!-- chunk -->

### interventions not currently available in a pill.

<!-- chunk -->

### Fasting-mimicking diets can replicate the benefits of fasting for longer

<!-- chunk -->

### periods of time without the risk of starvation. The best-known example is

<!-- chunk -->

### the ketogenic diet. You likely recall that it was this diet and its profound

<!-- chunk -->

### impact on one of my patients that led me on this journey.

<!-- chunk -->

### The story of the ketogenic diet begins with epilepsy. Since the time of

<!-- chunk -->

### Hippocrates, fasting was known to stop seizures and was used as a

<!-- chunk -->

### treatment in many cultures. With the advent of modern medicine, however,

<!-- chunk -->

### this was largely believed to be religious folklore and likely nonsense. That

<!-- chunk -->

### changed in the 1920s when a physician published a research article about

<!-- chunk -->

### fasting stopping seizures in a boy. The problem with fasting is that if it’s

<!-- chunk -->

### done too long, people die of starvation—not a very effective intervention.

<!-- chunk -->

### And when people start eating normally again, the seizures usually come

<!-- chunk -->

### right back. In 1921, Dr. Russell Wilder developed the ketogenic diet, a diet

<!-- chunk -->

### high in fat, moderate in protein, and low in carbohydrates, to address this

<!-- chunk -->

### challenge. His desire was to see if the diet could mimic the fasting state, but

<!-- chunk -->

### prevent starvation, to treat epilepsy. Lo and behold, it worked. The

<!-- chunk -->

### ketogenic diet reduced or stopped seizures in about 85 percent of the people

<!-- chunk -->

### who tried it. By the 1950s, it had fallen out of favor as a growing number of

<!-- chunk -->

### antiepileptic medications came to market. Taking a pill is much easier than

<!-- chunk -->

### doing this diet.

<!-- chunk -->

### Unfortunately, about 30 percent of patients with epilepsy don’t improve

<!-- chunk -->

### with any of the pills we have to offer, so the ketogenic diet was resurrected

<!-- chunk -->

### in the 1970s at Johns Hopkins for use in treatment-resistant epilepsy.

<!-- chunk -->

### Clinical use of this diet has grown around the world since. Many clinical

<!-- chunk -->

### trials have shown efficacy, and a 2020 Cochrane Review (a gold-standard

<!-- chunk -->

### meta-analysis) concludes that children with treatment-resistant epilepsy

<!-- chunk -->

### who eat ketogenic diets are three times more likely to achieve seizure

<!-- chunk -->

### freedom and six times more likely to experience a 50 percent or greater

<!-- chunk -->

### reduction in seizures than children getting usual care.


<!-- chunk -->

### The ketogenic diet is now the best-studied dietary intervention for its

<!-- chunk -->

### effects on the brain. Neurologists, neuroscientists, and pharmaceutical

<!-- chunk -->

### companies have been studying this diet for decades trying to better

<!-- chunk -->

### understand its anticonvulsant effects. It provides an alternate fuel source,

<!-- chunk -->

### which can be a lifeline to insulin-resistant brain cells. It also changes

<!-- chunk -->

### neurotransmitter levels, regulates calcium channels, decreases

<!-- chunk -->

### inflammation, improves the gut microbiome, increases overall metabolic

<!-- chunk -->

### rate, reduces insulin resistance itself, and most importantly, induces both

<!-- chunk -->

### mitophagy and mitochondrial biogenesis.


<!-- chunk -->

### After people are on this diet for

<!-- chunk -->

### months or years, their cells have more healthy mitochondria. This can result

<!-- chunk -->

### in long-term healing. Many people can stop the diet after two to five years

<!-- chunk -->

### and remain well.

<!-- chunk -->

### The research for the diet’s efficacy in treating mental health disorders is

<!-- chunk -->

### in the early stages. In my own work, I have seen people with severe,

<!-- chunk -->

### treatment-resistant psychotic disorders achieve full remission of their

<!-- chunk -->

### symptoms for long periods of time through a ketogenic diet.


<!-- chunk -->

### You’ll hear

<!-- chunk -->

### about one at the end of this chapter. The effects of the diet in the first year

<!-- chunk -->

### are just like a medication. People need to remain on the diet religiously.

<!-- chunk -->

### They can’t stop it for “cheat days,” just like they can’t stop their

<!-- chunk -->

### medications for cheat days. All hell can break loose if they do. I should

<!-- chunk -->

### point out that it’s common to use epilepsy treatments in psychiatry. We use

<!-- chunk -->

### many of them for almost all types of mental disorders. So, in many ways,

<!-- chunk -->

### this is nothing new. It just happens to be a dietary intervention. There are

<!-- chunk -->

### several clinical trials for bipolar disorder and schizophrenia getting

<!-- chunk -->

### underway currently.

<!-- chunk -->

### Alzheimer’s disease researchers looked at twenty-six patients who all

<!-- chunk -->

### got twelve weeks of the ketogenic diet and twelve weeks of a low-fat diet,

<!-- chunk -->

### separated by a ten-week washout period.


<!-- chunk -->

### Participants did the diets in

<!-- chunk -->

### different orders and the assessments were blinded. At the end of the study,

<!-- chunk -->

### the researchers found that when people were on the ketogenic diet, they had

<!-- chunk -->

### improvement in daily function and quality of life. I should point out that

<!-- chunk -->

### this is one of the few studies to demonstrate improvement in symptoms of

<!-- chunk -->

### Alzheimer’s disease. Most studies, like the intranasal insulin studies I told

<!-- chunk -->

### you about, only prevent progression of the disease. They don’t reverse it.

<!-- chunk -->

### Obviously, this was a small trial that needs to be replicated in more people

<!-- chunk -->

### over longer periods of time, but the basic science certainly supports why

<!-- chunk -->

### and how this might be working.

<!-- chunk -->

### There are many versions of the ketogenic diet, including ones for

<!-- chunk -->

### weight loss, diabetes management, and epilepsy, and they don’t always

<!-- chunk -->

### have the same effects. The foods can also be tailored to personal

<!-- chunk -->

### preferences, such as vegetarian, vegan, meat-only (the “carnivore diet”), or

<!-- chunk -->

### a diet with both animal and plant-sourced foods. Those with medical or

<!-- chunk -->

### mental disorders should only do this diet with medical supervision, as there

<!-- chunk -->

### are risks and side effects, and prescription medications usually need to be

<!-- chunk -->

### adjusted or stopped safely.

<!-- chunk -->

## The Gut Microbiome

<!-- chunk -->

### As mentioned, there is no question that the gut microbiome plays a role in

<!-- chunk -->

### mental and metabolic health. In terms of proven interventions, however, this

<!-- chunk -->

### field is in its infancy.

<!-- chunk -->

### Here are four types of interventions to consider:

<!-- chunk -->

### 1.Avoid

<!-- chunk -->

### antibiotic exposure if possible. Antibiotics are known to disrupt the

<!-- chunk -->

### microbiome, and they can sometimes directly cause mitochondrial

<!-- chunk -->

### dysfunction. In addition to not taking antibiotics unless necessary, it’s

<!-- chunk -->

### important to avoid eating foods that contain antibiotics, such as meat,

<!-- chunk -->

### fish, eggs, milk, and other products that commonly contain

<!-- chunk -->

### antibiotics fed to the animals. Look for “raised without antibiotics”

<!-- chunk -->

### labels.

<!-- chunk -->

### 2.Diet plays a critical role in the microbiome. Avoid highly processed

<!-- chunk -->

### foods. Eating foods high in fiber, such as fruits and vegetables, and a

<!-- chunk -->

### diet of real, whole foods is likely optimal.

<!-- chunk -->

### 3.Probiotics might play a role for some people, although we don’t

<!-- chunk -->

### have much evidence that they can improve metabolism or mental

<!-- chunk -->

### health. Recall that there are trillions of microbes in your gut. Taking

<!-- chunk -->

### a supplement with just one type of bacteria may or may not help.

<!-- chunk -->

### Before starting one, look into any research on that specific probiotic

<!-- chunk -->

### to see if there is evidence for its effectiveness, especially for your

<!-- chunk -->

### symptoms or diagnosis.

<!-- chunk -->

### 4.Fecal microbial transplants are being studied but are experimental at

<!-- chunk -->

### this point.


<!-- chunk -->

### •Diet plays a powerful role in metabolism and mitochondrial health.

<!-- chunk -->

### •If you have any dietary deficiencies, they need to be identified and

<!-- chunk -->

### corrected. This can include vitamins, minerals, protein, or essential

<!-- chunk -->

### fatty acids, to name just a few. You may want to work with a dietitian

<!-- chunk -->

### or your healthcare provider to fully assess your nutritional status and

<!-- chunk -->

### diet.

<!-- chunk -->

### •

<!-- chunk -->

### If you are exposing yourself to dietary factors that are harmful to

<!-- chunk -->

### your metabolism, you need to remove these from your diet. This can

<!-- chunk -->

### include allergens, but also foods that are known to be toxic, such as

<!-- chunk -->

### TFAs and junk food.

<!-- chunk -->

### •If you have insulin resistance, you likely need to change your diet to

<!-- chunk -->

### help address the underlying problem.

<!-- chunk -->

### •Even if you are following a perfectly healthy diet, your metabolism

<!-- chunk -->

### and mitochondria can become impaired. This can be due to non-

<!-- chunk -->

### dietary factors such as genetics, epigenetics, inflammation, stress,

<!-- chunk -->

### sleep problems, hormones, medications, toxins, etc. Even in cases

<!-- chunk -->

### like these, dietary interventions can still play a role in treatment. For

<!-- chunk -->

### example, IF and the ketogenic diet can both stimulate autophagy and

<!-- chunk -->

### mitophagy, regardless of what caused the problem in the first place.

<!-- chunk -->

### They can also provide ketones as a rescue fuel source to insulin-

<!-- chunk -->

### resistant cells.

<!-- chunk -->

### •Strategies to improve gut health may improve mental health.

<!-- chunk -->

### •Be skeptical of probiotics or “mito” supplements that claim to fix all

<!-- chunk -->

### your problems with one pill. In most studies to date, they don’t work.

<!-- chunk -->

### •Mental health and metabolic health are inseparable. This applies to

<!-- chunk -->

### everyone, including those simply trying to lose weight, or manage

<!-- chunk -->

### their diabetes, or prevent a heart attack or Alzheimer’s disease. Diet

<!-- chunk -->

### and exercise are often not enough. Everything that I am discussing in

<!-- chunk -->

### this book plays a role.

# Success Story: Mildred—It’s Never Too Late!

<!-- chunk -->

### Mildred had a horrible, abusive childhood. There is no doubt that she

<!-- chunk -->

### suffered from symptoms of PTSD, anxiety, and depression. At age

<!-- chunk -->

### seventeen,

<!-- chunk -->

### she was also diagnosed with schizophrenia. She began having daily

<!-- chunk -->

### hallucinations and delusions. She became chronically paranoid. Over the

<!-- chunk -->

### ensuing decades, she tried different antipsychotic and mood-stabilizing

<!-- chunk -->

### medications, but her symptoms continued. She could no longer care for

<!-- chunk -->

### herself and was assigned a court-appointed guardian. She was miserable.

<!-- chunk -->

### She tried to kill herself numerous times, once drinking a bottle of cleaning

<!-- chunk -->

### fluid. On top of her mental symptoms, she was obese, weighing

<!-- chunk -->

### pounds.

<!-- chunk -->

### At age seventy, after fifty-three years of being tormented and disabled

<!-- chunk -->

### by her schizophrenia, her doctor encouraged her to go to a weight-loss

<!-- chunk -->

### clinic at Duke University. They were using the ketogenic diet as a weight-

<!-- chunk -->

### loss method. She decided to give it a try. Within two weeks, not only did

<!-- chunk -->

### she begin to lose weight, she noticed significant improvement in her

<!-- chunk -->

### psychotic symptoms. She said that for the first time in years, she was able

<!-- chunk -->

### to hear the birds singing outside. The voices in her head were no longer

<!-- chunk -->

### drowning them out. Her mood was also improving, and she began to have

<!-- chunk -->

### hope. She was able to taper off all her psychiatric medications. Her

<!-- chunk -->

### symptoms went into full remission. She also lost 150 pounds and has kept it

<!-- chunk -->

### off to this day.

<!-- chunk -->

### Now, thirteen years later, she remains symptom-free, off medication,

<!-- chunk -->

### and doesn’t see any mental health professionals. Having learned to take care

<!-- chunk -->

### of herself, she got rid of the guardian, too. When I last spoke with Mildred,

<!-- chunk -->

### she said she was happy and excited to be alive. She asked me to share her

<!-- chunk -->

### story with anyone and everyone willing to listen. She hopes that her story

<!-- chunk -->

### might help others escape the living hell that she had to endure for decades.

<!-- chunk -->

### Stories like Mildred’s . . . just don’t happen in psychiatry. Even with the

<!-- chunk -->

### best traditional treatments that we have to offer, this is unheard of.

<!-- chunk -->

### Mildred’s story and the theory of brain energy say it is possible. It is a new

<!-- chunk -->

### day in the mental health field, one filled with hope for more stories like

<!-- chunk -->

### Mildred’s.


# Drugs and Alcohol

<!-- chunk -->

### t’s well known that drugs and alcohol can lead to mental disorders, and

<!-- chunk -->

### that people with mental disorders are more likely to use drugs and

<!-- chunk -->

### alcohol. Think of the young man smoking too much marijuana who ends

<!-- chunk -->

### up with schizophrenia. Or the alcoholic who develops dementia. Or the

<!-- chunk -->
