### cocaine addict with bipolar disorder. Most people think these are just the

<!-- chunk -->

### results of toxic drugs on the brain. Or that maybe these people were

<!-- chunk -->

### predisposed to mental illness and the drugs pushed them over the edge.

<!-- chunk -->

### Both assertions are true. But exactly how do they cause mental illness? Up

<!-- chunk -->

### until now, no one could say for sure. The brain energy theory offers clear

<!-- chunk -->

### answers: drugs and alcohol converge on metabolism and mitochondria.

<!-- chunk -->

### Most drugs fit into one of two categories—they either stimulate or

<!-- chunk -->

### inhibit cells. This includes alcohol, tobacco, marijuana, cocaine,

<!-- chunk -->

### amphetamines, and opiates. Some drugs work on specific cells in the brain

<!-- chunk -->

### or body, while others have broader effects on different types of cells. For

<!-- chunk -->

### example, alcohol and marijuana, which I’ll focus on soon, both have broad

<!-- chunk -->

### effects

<!-- chunk -->

### throughout the body. They act through receptors mostly found on the

<!-- chunk -->

### surfaces of cells, which then impact the mitochondria within those cells.

<!-- chunk -->

### However, mitochondria also have their own receptors for marijuana,

<!-- chunk -->

### nicotine, alcohol, and Valium right on their membranes. These drugs

<!-- chunk -->

### directly impact mitochondria.

<!-- chunk -->

### Drugs and alcohol form a feedback loop with metabolism and

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondria. People can enter this feedback loop in different ways, but

<!-- chunk -->

### once they get in, it can be difficult to get out.

<!-- chunk -->

### Some people start using drugs or alcohol due to peer pressure or other

<!-- chunk -->

### social influences. They can be perfectly happy and metabolically healthy

<!-- chunk -->

### when they begin. Using excessive amounts of drugs and alcohol over time,

<!-- chunk -->

### however, impairs metabolism and mitochondrial function. Once impaired,

<!-- chunk -->

### people can get to a point in which they “need” the substances to feel

<!-- chunk -->

### normal. Notice that I used the word “normal” and not “good.” Initially,

<!-- chunk -->

### when people begin using drugs and alcohol, they often feel good. This

<!-- chunk -->

### reinforces the behavior. People like feeling good. But over time, the brain

<!-- chunk -->

### adapts to these substances and tries to counteract them. As the brain

<!-- chunk -->

### changes, people can begin to feel “bad” when they aren’t using the

<!-- chunk -->

### substance. This leads to a vicious cycle in which they now need the

<!-- chunk -->

### substance just to feel normal. They often can’t get the same high that they

<!-- chunk -->

### once got. When they try to go without the substance, they suffer in some

<!-- chunk -->

### way. This usually drives them to use again. And now they are trapped.

<!-- chunk -->

### Other people get into drugs and alcohol because they are already

<!-- chunk -->

### metabolically compromised. They already suffer from depression, anxiety,

<!-- chunk -->

### insecurity, psychosis, or something distressing. They are looking to feel

<!-- chunk -->

### better. If their symptoms are bad enough, they will try anything. As a broad

<!-- chunk -->

### overview, if they suffer from symptoms of underactive cells, such as some

<!-- chunk -->

### of the symptoms of depression, taking something that is stimulating will

<!-- chunk -->

### make them feel better. If they suffer from symptoms of overactive or

<!-- chunk -->

### hyperexcitable brain cells, such as anxiety or psychosis, taking something

<!-- chunk -->

### that is sedating and inhibits cell activity can make them feel better. If the

<!-- chunk -->

### substance works well, they can get hooked. In some ways, who can blame

<!-- chunk -->

### them? They just want to feel better. Sometimes, people don’t necessarily

<!-- chunk -->

### feel “better” with substances;

<!-- chunk -->

### they just feel “different”—numb or out of it. For some, this can be

<!-- chunk -->

### preferable to the way they feel otherwise. In any case, this is probably why

<!-- chunk -->

### all mental disorders are associated with higher rates of substance use


<!-- chunk -->

### Drugs and alcohol can have immediate effects on mitochondrial

<!-- chunk -->

### function that can result in symptoms of many psychiatric disorders.

<!-- chunk -->

### Different drugs can quickly produce hallucinations, delusions, manic

<!-- chunk -->

### symptoms, depressive symptoms, cognitive impairment, and other

<!-- chunk -->

### symptoms. I can’t review all of this in one chapter, but I’ll share some

<!-- chunk -->

### highlights of how alcohol and marijuana can impact mitochondria.

# Alcohol

<!-- chunk -->

### Alcohol has profound effects on metabolism and mitochondria. When

<!-- chunk -->

### consumed in excess, it’s known to be toxic to the liver and brain.

<!-- chunk -->

### Mitochondria play a primary role in this toxicity. I’ll walk you through

<!-- chunk -->

### some of the science.

<!-- chunk -->

### When people drink alcohol, the liver does most of the processing. An

<!-- chunk -->

### enzyme called alcohol dehydrogenase (ADH) converts it into acetaldehyde,

<!-- chunk -->

### a toxic molecule to cells. Another enzyme, cytochrome P450 2E1

<!-- chunk -->

### (CYP2E1), can also make this conversion. CYP2E1 happens to be located

<!-- chunk -->

### directly on mitochondria or the endoplasmic reticulum. Yet another enzyme,

<!-- chunk -->

### aldehyde dehydrogenase (ALDH), then converts acetaldehyde into a less

<!-- chunk -->

### toxic molecule, acetate. ALDH comes in two forms: one that ends up in the

<!-- chunk -->

### cytoplasm and another that ends up inside mitochondria. Acetate then gets

<!-- chunk -->

### used by mitochondria as a fuel source. As you can see, mitochondria are

<!-- chunk -->

### playing a role in all of this.

<!-- chunk -->

### If people binge drink, these enzyme systems back up, and acetaldehyde

<!-- chunk -->

### levels rise. The first signs of problems are with mitochondria. They swell

<!-- chunk -->

### up, have trouble producing ATP, and create more ROS. Numerous studies

<!-- chunk -->

### have documented mitochondrial impairment, and even destruction, from

<!-- chunk -->

### large doses of alcohol.


<!-- chunk -->

### This is likely the cause of death in alcohol

<!-- chunk -->

### poisoning.

<!-- chunk -->

### Chronic alcohol consumption leads to chronic oxidative stress, a sign of

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondrial impairment. This leads to inflammation, which only makes

<!-- chunk -->

### the problems worse. All of this is occurring throughout the body, but

<!-- chunk -->

### particularly in the liver and brain.

<!-- chunk -->

### Even short periods of heavy alcohol use can have lasting effects.

<!-- chunk -->

### Researchers looked at adolescent rats exposed to two weeks of binge

<!-- chunk -->

### drinking and the effects on mitochondria in their brains over time.


<!-- chunk -->

### They

<!-- chunk -->

### found that binge drinking immediately impaired mitochondrial function, not

<!-- chunk -->

### surprising after what I just shared with you. However, the effects in the

<!-- chunk -->

### hippocampus lasted into adulthood, with reduced levels of mitochondrial

<!-- chunk -->

### proteins, reduced ATP production, and problems managing calcium.

<!-- chunk -->

### Dr. Nora Volkow, the director of the National Institute on Drug Abuse,

<!-- chunk -->

### has been studying the connections between addiction and metabolism for

<!-- chunk -->

### years now and is a pioneer in this field. She and others have discovered

<!-- chunk -->

### some surprising findings in chronic alcoholics. When people drink alcohol,

<!-- chunk -->

### their brains use less glucose as an energy source and instead use acetate

<!-- chunk -->

### from alcohol.


<!-- chunk -->

### Over time, alcoholics develop a problem with brain glucose

<!-- chunk -->

### metabolism. Their brain cells become energy deprived when they are

<!-- chunk -->

### sober.


<!-- chunk -->

### When they drink alcohol again, acetate fuels these struggling brain

<!-- chunk -->

### cells and provides relief. This brain energy deficit may be one of the

<!-- chunk -->

### reasons that alcoholics have trouble staying away from alcohol. Volkow and

<!-- chunk -->

### others set out to see if they could help these struggling brain cells with

<!-- chunk -->

### something other than alcohol. They turned to the ketogenic diet.

<!-- chunk -->

### They recruited thirty-three people with alcohol use disorder and

<!-- chunk -->

### admitted them to a detox unit.


<!-- chunk -->

### Half of them got a ketogenic diet and the

<!-- chunk -->

### other half got the standard American diet for twelve weeks. The researchers

<!-- chunk -->

### detoxed participants using standard protocols and conducted a variety of

<!-- chunk -->

### blood tests and brain scans looking at brain metabolism in targeted regions.

<!-- chunk -->

### They found that people who got the ketogenic diet needed less detox

<!-- chunk -->

### medication and had fewer withdrawal symptoms. They also reported fewer

<!-- chunk -->

### cravings for alcohol. Brain scans showed improved brain metabolism and

<!-- chunk -->

### reduced levels of brain inflammation compared to those on the standard

<!-- chunk -->

### American diet. This pilot study demonstrated that a dietary intervention,

<!-- chunk -->

### seemingly unrelated to alcoholism, could make a big difference in the

<!-- chunk -->

### brains, and symptoms, of real people. This is how science can change the

<!-- chunk -->

### mental health field.

<!-- chunk -->

### I want to point out one caution. As part of this work, the researchers tested

<!-- chunk -->

### what impact the diet might have on blood alcohol levels if a person were to

<!-- chunk -->

### drink. They tested rats on the ketogenic diet and found that their blood

<!-- chunk -->

### alcohol levels increased fivefold compared to rats on a normal diet, even

<!-- chunk -->

### though they all got the same amount of alcohol. This means that if people

<!-- chunk -->

### with alcohol use disorder were to try the ketogenic diet on their own, it

<!-- chunk -->

### could be dangerous if they drink. They could get much more intoxicated

<!-- chunk -->

### than usual. I don’t mean to imply that interventions like this can’t be used,

<!-- chunk -->

### but issues like this need to be considered, and people need to develop a safe

<!-- chunk -->

### way to manage these risks.

# Marijuana

<!-- chunk -->

### Marijuana is increasingly popular. Many people tout it as a “cure-all” for

<!-- chunk -->

### whatever ails you. It is thought to be good for seizures, pain disorders,

<!-- chunk -->

### nausea, anxiety, PTSD, and OCD. And yet, it can also cause mental

<!-- chunk -->

### symptoms, including learning and memory impairment, a lack of

<!-- chunk -->

### motivation, and possibly psychotic disorders.


<!-- chunk -->

### The brain energy theory offers a straightforward way to understand all

<!-- chunk -->

### these observations. They all relate to metabolism and mitochondria. The

<!-- chunk -->

### symptoms that improve are due to hyperexcitability. Any substance that

<!-- chunk -->

### reduces mitochondrial function in the correct cells could reduce these

<!-- chunk -->

### symptoms. However, such a substance could also cause symptoms if it

<!-- chunk -->

### impairs mitochondrial function too much. Is there any evidence that

<!-- chunk -->

### marijuana impacts mitochondria in these ways? Well, by now, you know I

<!-- chunk -->

### wouldn’t ask that question if the answer wasn’t a resounding yes.

<!-- chunk -->

### Marijuana affects the endocannabinoid system in the human body.

<!-- chunk -->

### Receptors for cannabinoids are found throughout the body, but they are

<!-- chunk -->

### highly concentrated in the brain. There are primarily two types of receptors

<!-- chunk -->

### —CB1 and CB2. These are located on cell membranes, but CB1 receptors

<!-- chunk -->

### are also located directly on mitochondria. Because there are different types

<!-- chunk -->

### of receptors located on a wide variety of cells throughout

<!-- chunk -->

### the body, it’s not fair to say there is one universal effect on all cells.

<!-- chunk -->

### However, the predominant theme in neurons is that marijuana slows the

<!-- chunk -->

### function of mitochondria through CB1 receptors.


<!-- chunk -->

### Brain imaging studies of

<!-- chunk -->

### almost eight hundred adolescents, some who used marijuana and others

<!-- chunk -->

### who didn’t, showed that the regions of the brain highest in CB1 receptors

<!-- chunk -->

### showed “accelerated age-related cortical thinning” in the marijuana users,

<!-- chunk -->

### meaning that marijuana’s effects on these mitochondrial receptors was

<!-- chunk -->

### likely the cause of these brain areas getting thinner.


<!-- chunk -->

### A mouse study published in Nature found that mitochondria in

<!-- chunk -->

### astrocytes have a direct role in mediating the effects of marijuana—they

<!-- chunk -->

### control the amount of glucose and lactate (energy sources) going to

<!-- chunk -->

### neurons.


<!-- chunk -->

### This, in turn, has direct effects on social behaviors. All of this is

<!-- chunk -->

### mediated through the CB1 receptors on mitochondria. When these receptors

<!-- chunk -->

### were activated by THC (the active ingredient in marijuana), it led to a

<!-- chunk -->

### reduction in both mitochondrial function and energy sources going to

<!-- chunk -->

### neurons. It also led to social withdrawal behaviors. When the researchers

<!-- chunk -->

### removed mitochondrial CB1 receptors, THC no longer had the same effects.

<!-- chunk -->

### The mitochondria weren’t impacted in the same ways; the energy sources

<!-- chunk -->

### going to neurons weren’t reduced; and the social withdrawal behaviors

<!-- chunk -->

### didn’t occur, even though the mice were still being exposed to marijuana,

<!-- chunk -->

### and CB2 receptors were available on cells.

<!-- chunk -->

### Another study from Nature tried to determine what causes the memory

<!-- chunk -->

### impairment from marijuana use. The researchers were ultimately hoping to

<!-- chunk -->

### better understand how memory works. The CB1 receptors on mitochondria

<!-- chunk -->

### again play a key role. The researchers found that marijuana’s effect on CB1

<!-- chunk -->

### receptors directly impacted mitochondrial movement, synapse function, and

<!-- chunk -->

### memory formation. When they deleted the CB1 receptors, marijuana no

<!-- chunk -->

### longer had any of these effects, and memory wasn’t impaired. These

<!-- chunk -->

### researchers concluded: “By directly linking mitochondrial activity to

<!-- chunk -->

### memory formation, these data reveal that bioenergetic processes are

<!-- chunk -->

### primary acute regulators of cognitive functions.”


<!-- chunk -->

### In other words, brain

<!-- chunk -->

### energy and mitochondria play a primary role in our ability to remember.

<!-- chunk -->

### There are many other addictive substances that impact metabolism and

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondria, but I hope these two examples give you an idea of how

<!-- chunk -->

### substance use fits into the theory of brain energy.

# Drug and Alcohol Treatment

<!-- chunk -->

### Drug and alcohol treatment programs play a powerful role in improving

<!-- chunk -->

### mental and metabolic health. Reducing or stopping the use of substances

<!-- chunk -->

### that impair mitochondrial function is critically important.

<!-- chunk -->

### Entire books are written on this topic. I won’t try to review their

<!-- chunk -->

### conclusions here. Numerous strategies are available, including inpatient

<!-- chunk -->

### detox, residential programs, outpatient therapy, group therapy, medication-

<!-- chunk -->

### assisted treatments, twelve-step programs, and halfway houses.

<!-- chunk -->

### Interestingly, a new area of research is on the use of psychedelics as a

<!-- chunk -->

### treatment for some psychiatric disorders. I’ll get to that in Chapter 18.


<!-- chunk -->

### •Drugs and alcohol affect your metabolism and mitochondria.

<!-- chunk -->

### •Withdrawing from them can also impact metabolism and

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondria in different ways.

<!-- chunk -->

### •It’s important to assess your use of substances, including tobacco,

<!-- chunk -->

### alcohol, caffeine, supplements, marijuana, and recreational drugs.

<!-- chunk -->

### These might be playing a role in your metabolic and mental health.

<!-- chunk -->

### •If you are using any of these heavily, it could be an important

<!-- chunk -->

### contributing cause to any metabolic or mental symptoms you are

<!-- chunk -->

### having. You will likely need to address this before trying other

<!-- chunk -->

### interventions. If you have trouble doing this on your own, consider

<!-- chunk -->

### seeking professional help.

# E


# Physical Activity

<!-- chunk -->

### xercise is good for health. Many studies show that people who

<!-- chunk -->

### exercise are less likely to develop metabolic disorders, such as obesity,

<!-- chunk -->

### diabetes, and cardiovascular disease. This is one reason that exercise is

<!-- chunk -->

### so strongly recommended.

<!-- chunk -->

### The same is true in relation to mental health. A study of 1.2 million

<!-- chunk -->

### Americans found that those who exercised had 43 percent fewer days of

<!-- chunk -->

### poor mental health, even after controlling for physical and

<!-- chunk -->

### sociodemographic characteristics.


<!-- chunk -->

### This study found that any type of

<!-- chunk -->

### exercise was better than no exercise, but that the largest benefits were seen

<!-- chunk -->

### for team sports, cycling, and aerobic and gym activities. The optimal “dose”

<!-- chunk -->

### was forty-five minutes, three to five times per week.

<!-- chunk -->

### Most people stop there. That’s enough information to make a

<!-- chunk -->

### recommendation. If someone exercises for forty-five minutes, three to five

<!-- chunk -->

### times per week, that should solve the problem.

<!-- chunk -->

### I truly wish it was that simple, but it’s not. I’ve seen many patients who

<!-- chunk -->

### exercise regularly and still have crippling schizophrenia or depression. I

<!-- chunk -->

### want to explore the nuances of being active. Giving simple explanations

<!-- chunk -->

### with simple answers isn’t going to address our mental health problems. If

<!-- chunk -->

### people follow the “forty-five minutes three to five times a week” advice and

<!-- chunk -->

### don’t see results, they give up in frustration and disappointment. Don’t

<!-- chunk -->

### worry if you’re an exercise advocate—I’m still going to recommend

<!-- chunk -->

### exercise in the end.

<!-- chunk -->

### The first thing to highlight was that the study with 1.2 million people

<!-- chunk -->

### was a correlational study. As you know by now, correlation doesn’t equal

<!-- chunk -->

### causation. It’s possible that people who exercise already have good mental

<!-- chunk -->

### and metabolic health, and that allows them to exercise. This would be

<!-- chunk -->

### reverse causation.

<!-- chunk -->

### To illustrate how complicated this can all be, I’ll tell you about another

<!-- chunk -->

### study. This one followed 1,700 women at midlife for twenty years to see if

<!-- chunk -->

### exercise prevents cognitive decline.


<!-- chunk -->

### Most people would assume it does.

<!-- chunk -->

### However, after controlling for socioeconomic characteristics, menopause

<!-- chunk -->

### symptoms, hormone therapy use, and presence of diabetes and

<!-- chunk -->

### hypertension, they found that exercise didn’t make any difference in

<!-- chunk -->

### cognitive symptoms. They concluded that “physical activity observed in

<!-- chunk -->

### later life may be an artifact of reverse causation.” So, the news headline for

<!-- chunk -->

### this study is that exercise doesn’t prevent cognitive decline. But this isn’t so

<!-- chunk -->

### clear-cut. They “controlled” for diabetes and hypertension, as though

<!-- chunk -->

### exercise is independent of these variables. We know that it’s not! Exercise

<!-- chunk -->

### decreases the likelihood of both, and may thereby decrease the risk of

<!-- chunk -->

### cognitive decline, too. We know they are all interrelated. And yet, some

<!-- chunk -->

### researchers and academic journals assume they aren’t.

<!-- chunk -->

### Exercise has been studied as a treatment for mental disorders, with

<!-- chunk -->

### depression being the most studied illness. The results are mixed, with some

<!-- chunk -->

### studies showing benefits and others not. A 2017 meta-analysis looking at

<!-- chunk -->

### exercise as a treatment for major depression included thirty-five studies

<!-- chunk -->

### with almost 2,500 participants.


<!-- chunk -->

### Their conclusion: “Trials with less risk of

<!-- chunk -->

### bias suggested no antidepressant effects of exercise and there were no

<!-- chunk -->

### significant

<!-- chunk -->

### effects of exercise on quality of life, depression severity or lack of

<!-- chunk -->

### remission during follow-up.” That’s disappointing!

<!-- chunk -->

### And yet the World Health Organization begs to differ. They issued a

<!-- chunk -->

### report in 2019 entitled Motion for Your Mind. They summarized their

<!-- chunk -->

### findings as follows: “A review of the evidence for the benefits of physical

<!-- chunk -->

### activity for people with depression, schizophrenia and dementia indicated

<!-- chunk -->

### improved mood, slowed cognitive decline, and delayed disease onset . . . ”


<!-- chunk -->

### So, what are we supposed to believe? Does exercise help or not? It’s

<!-- chunk -->

### tempting to err on the “safe” side and tell everyone to exercise, but if it

<!-- chunk -->

### doesn’t actually work, that just sets people up for failure and makes the

<!-- chunk -->

### person recommending it seem less credible.

<!-- chunk -->

### In otherwise healthy people, it’s known that exercise can improve

<!-- chunk -->

### metabolic health. It’s known to induce both mitochondrial biogenesis and

<!-- chunk -->

### mitophagy—the two things we are looking to do. This occurs not only in

<!-- chunk -->

### muscle cells but also in brain cells. Increasing mitochondria in brain cells

<!-- chunk -->

### should be helpful. So why don’t treatment studies consistently show a

<!-- chunk -->

### benefit?

<!-- chunk -->

### One reason may be insulin resistance. A study published in Cell found

<!-- chunk -->

### that it may block the benefits of exercise. Researchers had thirty-six people

<!-- chunk -->

### with varying levels of insulin resistance exercise and measured a plethora of

<!-- chunk -->

### biological measures before and after. They found that there were significant

<!-- chunk -->

### differences in energy metabolism, oxidative stress, inflammation, tissue

<!-- chunk -->

### repair, and growth factor responses, with most of these beneficial processes

<!-- chunk -->

### being dampened or even reversed in those with insulin resistance.


<!-- chunk -->

### As I’ve

<!-- chunk -->

### already discussed, many people with chronic mental disorders have insulin

<!-- chunk -->

### resistance, so this may explain at the cellular level why exercise might be

<!-- chunk -->

### harder for them and why it might not work.

<!-- chunk -->

### I suspect that the more important issue is that many people are taking

<!-- chunk -->

### substances and/or have lifestyle factors that impair mitochondrial function,

<!-- chunk -->

### and these are interfering with the beneficial effects of exercise.

<!-- chunk -->

### Athletes, trainers, and coaches have long known that it takes more than

<!-- chunk -->

### just exercise itself to improve performance. All the factors that I discuss in

<!-- chunk -->

### this book play a role. If someone wants to improve their physical

<!-- chunk -->

### performance through exercise, they also need to pay attention to eating a

<!-- chunk -->

### proper diet, getting good sleep, and refraining from alcohol and drug use,

<!-- chunk -->

### among other things. For example, as I already discussed, alcohol can

<!-- chunk -->

### damage mitochondria and prevent mitochondrial biogenesis and mitophagy.

<!-- chunk -->

### This is why we’ve all heard the advice that if you’re training for an

<!-- chunk -->

### important athletic event, or even if you just want to lose weight, you need to

<!-- chunk -->

### stop drinking. Improving metabolism involves many lifestyle factors in

<!-- chunk -->

### combination, not just one.

<!-- chunk -->

### Medications can also play an adverse role. In theory, any medication

<!-- chunk -->

### that impairs mitochondrial function might prevent exercise from working.

<!-- chunk -->

### One study looked directly at this issue for the commonly prescribed

<!-- chunk -->

### diabetes medication metformin. Researchers had fifty-three older adults

<!-- chunk -->

### participate in twelve weeks of aerobic training and assigned half of them to

<!-- chunk -->

### take metformin and the other half a placebo. Both groups experienced some

<!-- chunk -->

### benefits from the exercise, such as a reduction in fat mass, glucose, and

<!-- chunk -->

### insulin levels. However, improvements in muscle mitochondrial function

<!-- chunk -->

### were abrogated in the people taking metformin. The metformin group had

<!-- chunk -->

### no overall change in whole-body insulin sensitivity, even though the

<!-- chunk -->

### placebo group experienced improvement. These researchers summarized

<!-- chunk -->

### their findings in the title of their study: “Metformin Inhibits Mitochondrial

<!-- chunk -->

### Adaptations to Aerobic Exercise Training in Older Adults.”


<!-- chunk -->

### So, in all the

<!-- chunk -->

### studies looking at the effects of exercise for weight loss, diabetes, or mental

<!-- chunk -->

### disorders, we would need to know if any of those participants were taking

<!-- chunk -->

### metformin. If they were, they were likely being set up for failure to improve

<!-- chunk -->

### their mitochondrial function, and this might be the reason some of the

<!-- chunk -->

### studies showed no benefit.

<!-- chunk -->

### Metformin is one of the “mildest” diabetes medications with the fewest

<!-- chunk -->

### side effects. Many other diabetes medications, including insulin itself, cause

<!-- chunk -->

### weight gain and even more insulin resistance over time. But it’s not limited

<!-- chunk -->

### to diabetes medications. As you now know, some psychiatric medications,

<!-- chunk -->

### especially the antipsychotic medications, are known to cause serious

<!-- chunk -->

### metabolic disturbances and mitochondrial dysfunction. People taking any of

<!-- chunk -->

### these medications will likely fail to get the full benefits of exercise.

<!-- chunk -->

### The research on exercise for mental illness didn’t factor any of this into

<!-- chunk -->

### their studies.

<!-- chunk -->

### Mitochondria play a direct role in translating exercise into beneficial

<!-- chunk -->

### effects in the brain. When people exercise, one of the benefits is that they

<!-- chunk -->

### usually develop new neurons in the hippocampus from stem cells. This

<!-- chunk -->

### process has been found to be directly related to both mood and cognitive

<!-- chunk -->

### disorders. The growth of these stem cells into new neurons depends upon

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondria. When researchers genetically manipulated mitochondria to

<!-- chunk -->

### inhibit or enhance their function, the development of these new neurons

<!-- chunk -->

### was inhibited or enhanced, respectively.


<!-- chunk -->

### Based on this research, it appears

<!-- chunk -->

### likely that if someone has poor mitochondrial function in this brain region,

<!-- chunk -->

### they may not get the same benefits from exercise that other people get.

<!-- chunk -->

### However, if we can restore their mitochondrial health, it’s possible that we

<!-- chunk -->

### can change this.

<!-- chunk -->

### As a rule of thumb, exercise can do one of two things: It can help

<!-- chunk -->

### people maintain their current abilities, or it can improve their abilities. This

<!-- chunk -->

### translates into maintaining your current metabolic status or improving it.

<!-- chunk -->

### Taking a leisurely walk around the block helps people maintain their

<!-- chunk -->

### current metabolic status. This is useful. It’s certainly better than losing

<!-- chunk -->

### strength or ability. However, to improve metabolic capacity, people need to

<!-- chunk -->

### push themselves. They must work toward getting faster, stronger, more

<!-- chunk -->

### flexible, doing more reps, or achieving some other metric of increased

<!-- chunk -->

### capacity. We know that when this happens, the number of mitochondria in

<!-- chunk -->

### their muscle and brain cells increase, and the health of those mitochondria

<!-- chunk -->

### improve as well.

<!-- chunk -->

### One of the challenges with exercise is that asking people who are

<!-- chunk -->

### metabolically compromised to push themselves involves risk. There are

<!-- chunk -->

### risks of injury and even heart attacks. Therefore, exercise needs to be

<!-- chunk -->

### managed in a safe way. Physical therapists, personal trainers, and others

<!-- chunk -->

### will play an essential role for some people.

<!-- chunk -->

### The bigger challenge is getting people who are metabolically

<!-- chunk -->

### compromised to follow through with an exercise routine. They lack energy

<!-- chunk -->

### and motivation. Their metabolism is doing this to them. It’s not their fault.

<!-- chunk -->

### Overcoming this inertia can be difficult. Nonetheless, it can be done with

<!-- chunk -->

### support, encouragement, and education.

# Exercise as a Treatment

<!-- chunk -->

### Should everyone exercise? I would say yes. But it’s important to keep in

<!-- chunk -->

### mind that it will be much harder for people with chronic mental disorders,

<!-- chunk -->

### and they may not notice benefits right away. It’s also important to take an

<!-- chunk -->

### inventory of all the factors that might be impairing your mitochondria and

<!-- chunk -->

### metabolism; reducing or eliminating these will allow exercise to work.

<!-- chunk -->

### Nonetheless, exercise won’t be the answer for everyone. As I’ve been

<!-- chunk -->

### discussing, there are many factors that play a role in metabolic and mental

<!-- chunk -->

### health. Exercise is only one of them. For people with vitamin or hormone

<!-- chunk -->

### deficiencies, for example, exercise isn’t going to solve the problem, but it

<!-- chunk -->

### certainly won’t hurt.


<!-- chunk -->

### •Exercise can play a role in preventing mental and metabolic


<!-- chunk -->

### •Exercise may be more difficult if you have insulin resistance or any

<!-- chunk -->

### condition associated with mitochondrial dysfunction. It may take

<!-- chunk -->

### longer to show benefits. This doesn’t mean it won’t work; it just

<!-- chunk -->

### means that you should try to be patient and not expect immediate

<!-- chunk -->

### results.

<!-- chunk -->

### •Identifying, removing, and/or reducing substances and lifestyle

<!-- chunk -->

### factors that impair mitochondrial function will be required to realize

<!-- chunk -->

### the full benefits of exercise. These can sometimes negate the benefits

<!-- chunk -->

### of exercise.

<!-- chunk -->

### •

<!-- chunk -->

### Exercise can be an effective treatment for some people with mental

<!-- chunk -->

### disorders, but for others, it may not be the solution.

<!-- chunk -->

### •People with injuries or physical limitations should work with their

<!-- chunk -->

### healthcare provider to implement an exercise program safely. This

<!-- chunk -->

### may include working with a physical therapist.

<!-- chunk -->

### •Even if exercise doesn’t improve your mental symptoms, you should

<!-- chunk -->

### still exercise, as it offers numerous other health benefits. Humans are

<!-- chunk -->

### meant to move.

# M


# Love, Adversity, and Purpose in Life

<!-- chunk -->

### etabolic and mental health require a combination of both biological

<!-- chunk -->

### and environmental factors. I’ve told you about many of the

<!-- chunk -->

### biological factors. The environment includes many things—food,

<!-- chunk -->

### shelter, temperature, light, infections, allergens, and lifestyle choices—

<!-- chunk -->

### some of which we’ve covered. But it also includes people, experiences,

<!-- chunk -->

### love, and purpose in life. Although most people see these latter constructs

<!-- chunk -->

### as psychological and social issues and often assume they aren’t related to

<!-- chunk -->

### biology, they actually play profound roles in metabolism. They are all

<!-- chunk -->

### interrelated and inseparable. We adapt and respond to our environments, for

<!-- chunk -->

### better or worse.

# Use It or Lose It

<!-- chunk -->

### The phrase “use it or lose it” is usually associated with exercise and muscle

<!-- chunk -->

### strength. When people use, or stress, certain muscles, they become larger

<!-- chunk -->

### and more resilient. Not only do they grow in size, but they also develop

<!-- chunk -->

### more mitochondria. This is true even when the size of the muscles isn’t that

<!-- chunk -->

### much larger. For example, some long-distance runners can be very thin.

<!-- chunk -->

### Their muscles aren’t all that big, yet they contain more mitochondria than

<!-- chunk -->

### the muscles of people who don’t run. These mitochondria give their

<!-- chunk -->

### muscles the endurance they need to go long distances.

<!-- chunk -->

### Not using muscles results in atrophy, or shrinking. This can be striking

<!-- chunk -->

### when people break a bone and are in a cast for several weeks. Their muscles

<!-- chunk -->

### shrink rapidly. Why? When the body isn’t using something, it diverts

<!-- chunk -->

### metabolic resources from it. The body is always adapting and adjusting. It

<!-- chunk -->

### spends its energy wisely. If muscles aren’t being used, they don’t get much

<!-- chunk -->

### glucose or as many amino acids. They quickly shrink. The good news is

<!-- chunk -->

### that the body keeps a memory of what those muscles once were. Once the

<!-- chunk -->

### cast comes off, the muscles quickly return to their normal size if they get

<!-- chunk -->

### used in the same ways again. This truly depends on what size they were

<!-- chunk -->

### beforehand. Big bodybuilders will quickly regain their massive muscles,

<!-- chunk -->

### while frail, elderly people will only get a tiny amount of muscle growth.

<!-- chunk -->

### This concept of “use it or lose it” applies to more than just muscles. It

<!-- chunk -->

### also applies to the brain. The best evidence for this comes from studies of

<!-- chunk -->

### children as their brains are developing.

<!-- chunk -->

### Some human skills and traits need to be acquired at the right times. The

<!-- chunk -->

### brain undergoes “developmental windows” during which it is ready to learn

<!-- chunk -->

### and adapt. However, the “environment” must offer experiences needed to

<!-- chunk -->

### acquire these skills or else they can be altered for life. Social skills are one

<!-- chunk -->

### example.

<!-- chunk -->

### Social skills are important to human survival. They allow us to live in

<!-- chunk -->

### families, towns, and societies. They require two things to develop properly:

<!-- chunk -->

### (1) normal brain development to acquire and store the information and (2)

<!-- chunk -->

### learning experiences from other humans. If either is absent, problems will

<!-- chunk -->

### be

<!-- chunk -->

### obvious. The biology side can be understood in terms of brain development,

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondria, and metabolism, as I have already discussed. The

<!-- chunk -->

### environment side depends upon parents and caretakers primarily. There is a

<!-- chunk -->

### tremendous amount of literature looking at the effects of attachment,

<!-- chunk -->

### neglect, abuse, and social deprivation on human development. Many of us

<!-- chunk -->

### would say these things relate to love, or a lack thereof. They play a

<!-- chunk -->

### profound role in human development, including the acquisition of social

<!-- chunk -->

### skills. Children deprived of appropriate learning opportunities often lack the

<!-- chunk -->

### skills needed to navigate the world effectively. In extreme cases, the

<!-- chunk -->

### consequences can be catastrophic.

<!-- chunk -->

### The findings of researchers studying Romanian orphans exemplifies

<!-- chunk -->

### how tragic this can be. The orphanages where these children were housed

<!-- chunk -->

### were profoundly neglectful, and the children who experienced this neglect

<!-- chunk -->

### were found to suffer from a range of diagnostic categories including autism,

<!-- chunk -->

### learning disorders, mental retardation, PTSD, anxiety disorders, impulse

<!-- chunk -->

### control disorders, mood disorders, personality disorders, and even psychotic

<!-- chunk -->

### disorders. Once again, numerous diagnostic categories, not just one. Their

<!-- chunk -->

### brains were deprived of the appropriate opportunities to learn how to be

<!-- chunk -->

### “human” in society and the consequences were sometimes devastating. The

<!-- chunk -->

### malnutrition, stress, and trauma they experienced undoubtedly also played

<!-- chunk -->

### roles, but so did the lack of appropriate learning experiences.

<!-- chunk -->

### These children’s brains didn’t develop normally. If areas of the brain

<!-- chunk -->

### that perform certain functions aren’t being used, they don’t grow and thrive.

<!-- chunk -->

### One research group studied metabolic brain scans of ten such children and

<!-- chunk -->

### compared them to normal control and even epileptic children.


<!-- chunk -->

### Sure

<!-- chunk -->

### enough, they found widespread areas of reduced brain glucose metabolism,

<!-- chunk -->

### indicating a brain energy problem in these former Romanian orphans.

<!-- chunk -->

### Sometimes, these deficits can be corrected later in life; but in some cases,

<!-- chunk -->

### they appear to be permanent. Developmental windows can close, and the

<!-- chunk -->

### opportunity for normal brain development can be lost forever.

<!-- chunk -->

### It’s not always so extreme. For example, children exposed to more

<!-- chunk -->

### screen time are more likely to develop ADHD. There are two ways to

<!-- chunk -->

### interpret this observation. One explanation is that the environment is

<!-- chunk -->

### driving the subsequent diagnosis of ADHD. These children are learning that

<!-- chunk -->

### constant stimulation is the norm given the content on the screens. They are

<!-- chunk -->

### not learning patience, focus, and concentration even if their brains are ready

<!-- chunk -->

### to learn. These developing brain networks will receive fewer metabolic

<!-- chunk -->

### resources since they aren’t being used, much like the unused muscle. They

<!-- chunk -->

### may not develop normally, or they may not be as strong and robust as they

<!-- chunk -->

### could otherwise be. This can result in ADHD symptoms. However, it’s also

<!-- chunk -->

### possible that this could be due to reverse causation, and biology might be

<!-- chunk -->

### the problem. If these children have inadequate metabolism in specific brain

<!-- chunk -->

### regions, they may be unable to focus. This may drive them to use screens as

<!-- chunk -->

### a source of entertainment. If this explanation is correct, then correcting the

<!-- chunk -->

### metabolic issue will be the first step in solving the problem.

<!-- chunk -->

### The concept of strengthening brain regions is obvious to most of us

<!-- chunk -->

### through clichés like “practice makes perfect.” This applies to learning a

<!-- chunk -->

### new language, playing basketball, or learning to play the piano. When we

<!-- chunk -->

### use our brains in specific ways, neurons grow, adapt, and form new

<!-- chunk -->

### connections. If we use them, they grow. If we don’t, they wither. This all

<!-- chunk -->

### relates to metabolism and mitochondria. They adapt to our needs.

# Stress

<!-- chunk -->

### I now return to stress. I have discussed this throughout the book and have

<!-- chunk -->

### already told you how it plays a powerful role in mental and metabolic

<!-- chunk -->

### health. I’ll review some highlights, share some new information, and then

<!-- chunk -->

### get to treatments.

<!-- chunk -->

### Recall that the stress response requires energy and metabolic resources.

<!-- chunk -->

### These resources are being diverted from other cells throughout the brain

<!-- chunk -->

### and body, and these other cells can suffer. For example, if a young boy is

<!-- chunk -->

### chronically stressed, he will have more difficulty learning in school. It’s not

<!-- chunk -->

### necessarily because he isn’t trying. The stress response is taking energy that

<!-- chunk -->

### could otherwise be used for brain functions like focus, learning, and

<!-- chunk -->

### memory.

<!-- chunk -->

### Stress puts cellular maintenance functions on hold. If it occurs for a

<!-- chunk -->

### prolonged period, it can result in maintenance problems in cells, especially

<!-- chunk -->

### ones not being used much, which can lead to symptoms of mental and

<!-- chunk -->

### metabolic disorders. Any cells that are already metabolically compromised

<!-- chunk -->

### can begin to malfunction under stress, which can exacerbate symptoms of

<!-- chunk -->

### mental and metabolic disorders.

<!-- chunk -->

### In Part Two, I talked about how mitochondria play a critical role in the

<!-- chunk -->

### stress response. They influence all aspects of the stress response, including

<!-- chunk -->

### the production and regulation of key hormones and neurotransmitters,

<!-- chunk -->

### nervous system responses, inflammation, and epigenetic changes. When

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondria are not functioning properly, all of these can be affected.

<!-- chunk -->

### One research study demonstrated a direct relationship between everyday

<!-- chunk -->

### stress and changes in mitochondrial function in humans.


<!-- chunk -->

### The researchers

<!-- chunk -->

### developed a test of mitochondrial health that includes both the quantity and

<!-- chunk -->

### function of mitochondria within white blood cells and assessed whether this

<!-- chunk -->

### metric was associated with daily stress. They studied ninety-one mothers,

<!-- chunk -->

### some who had children with autism and others who had neurotypical

<!-- chunk -->

### children, and assessed their daily mood and stress levels to see if these were

<!-- chunk -->

### related to the mitochondrial health index (MHI). They found that they were.

<!-- chunk -->

### Overall, the mothers who had high levels of stress and low moods had

<!-- chunk -->

### lower MHI. But of course, stress levels and moods can change on a daily

<!-- chunk -->

### basis. The researchers looked at this specifically. When the mothers were in

<!-- chunk -->

### a positive mood, the MHI went up afterward, sometimes within one day. In

<!-- chunk -->

### other words, the health and function of mitochondria in white blood cells

<!-- chunk -->

### were changing in response to the mothers’ daily moods and stress levels.

<!-- chunk -->

### This research demonstrates how stress can lead to impairment in

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondrial function, which can then affect overall health.

<!-- chunk -->

### All humans experience stressful life events. In the 1960s, Dr. Thomas

<!-- chunk -->

### Holmes and Dr. Richard Rahe, both psychiatrists, studied five thousand

<!-- chunk -->

### medical patients to see how stressful life events can contribute to physical

<!-- chunk -->

### illness. They identified some common life events and ranked them by how

<!-- chunk -->

### much they impacted overall health. The Holmes-Rahe Stress Inventory is

<!-- chunk -->

### still available today and can give you a sense of what life events are most

<!-- chunk -->

### stressful. Some top ones include the death of a spouse or close family

<!-- chunk -->

### member, divorce, personal injury, getting fired, and even retiring. These

<!-- chunk -->

### select

<!-- chunk -->

### ones involve some type of loss—losing someone important to you, losing

<!-- chunk -->

### your health, or losing your job (even voluntarily). What makes these so

<!-- chunk -->

### stressful? There are many reasons, and they can be different for different

<!-- chunk -->

### stressors, but one common theme is that they all relate to purpose in life.

# Purpose in Life

<!-- chunk -->

### Humans are driven to have a sense of purpose. I believe this is hardwired

<!-- chunk -->

### into our brains, given that this single construct has been highly associated

<!-- chunk -->

### with both metabolic and mental health. When people lack a sense of

<!-- chunk -->

### purpose, it appears to induce a chronic stress response and can lead to many

<!-- chunk -->

### poor health outcomes. Purpose in life is multifaceted, however. It usually

<!-- chunk -->

### includes many things, not just one. The stress inventory that I just

<!-- chunk -->

### mentioned highlights three possibilities: relationships, taking care of

<!-- chunk -->

### yourself and staying healthy, and having a job.

<!-- chunk -->

### Dr. Viktor Frankl, an Austrian psychiatrist who was taken prisoner by

<!-- chunk -->

### the Nazis during World War II, deserves credit for highlighting the powerful

<!-- chunk -->

### role of meaning and purpose in life. In his book Man’s Search for Meaning,

<!-- chunk -->

### he described his observations of the other prisoners in the concentration

<!-- chunk -->

### camp. Most became severely depressed, for obvious reasons. However,

<!-- chunk -->

### some did not. Some of the prisoners appeared to hold on to hope that they

<!-- chunk -->

### might live and escape. Frankl argued that the common denominator among

<!-- chunk -->

### them was that they all had a sense of purpose in life: they had a reason to

<!-- chunk -->

### fight and try to stay alive.


<!-- chunk -->

### Frankl went on to develop a psychotherapy,

<!-- chunk -->

### logotherapy, based on the construct of meaning and purpose in life. Many

<!-- chunk -->

### of its tenets are still embedded in current mainstream psychotherapies.

<!-- chunk -->

### The concept of purpose in life continues to be studied today and has

<!-- chunk -->

### been highly correlated with a wide range of metabolic and mental health

<!-- chunk -->

### outcomes. It’s not surprising that a low sense of purpose in life is associated

<!-- chunk -->

### with depression, given that depression itself might make people feel this

<!-- chunk -->

### way. It may just be circular logic. However, a lack of purpose is also

<!-- chunk -->

### associated with metabolic disorders and even longevity, consistent with the

<!-- chunk -->

### brain energy

<!-- chunk -->

### theory. For example, one study of nearly seven thousand US adults ages

<!-- chunk -->

### fifty-one to sixty-one found that those who had the lowest sense of purpose

<!-- chunk -->

### in life were about 2.5 times more likely to die an early death than those with

<!-- chunk -->

### a strong sense of purpose in life.


<!-- chunk -->

### They were dying of heart attacks, strokes,

<!-- chunk -->

### respiratory disorders, and gastrointestinal conditions. These researchers

<!-- chunk -->

### noted other studies showing that a strong sense of purpose leads to lower

<!-- chunk -->

### levels of cortisol and inflammation, which might explain these health

<!-- chunk -->

### benefits. A 2016 meta-analysis of ten prospective studies that included over

<!-- chunk -->

### 136,000 participants also found that having a higher sense of purpose in life

<!-- chunk -->

### was associated with reduced all-cause mortality and cardiovascular events.


<!-- chunk -->

### When discussing purpose in life, it’s important to include spirituality

<!-- chunk -->

### and religious beliefs. For many, these play a powerful role in how they

<!-- chunk -->

### understand their existence. Researchers have studied the effects of religious

<!-- chunk -->

### beliefs and practices on a variety of health outcomes and, in general, have

<!-- chunk -->

### found many beneficial effects. For example, one study looking at adults at

<!-- chunk -->

### high risk for depression found that those who reported religion or

<!-- chunk -->

### spirituality to be highly important were 90 percent less likely to develop

<!-- chunk -->

### depression compared to those who reported them of low importance.


<!-- chunk -->

### researchers conducted brain scans on these people and found differences in

<!-- chunk -->

### the thickness of certain brain regions based on how important religion and

<!-- chunk -->

### spirituality were to the participants. These brain differences may explain the

<!-- chunk -->

### protection against depression. The Nurses’ Health Study followed almost

<!-- chunk -->

### ninety thousand women for more than fourteen years and found that women

<!-- chunk -->

### who attended religious services at least once per week were five times less

<!-- chunk -->

### likely to commit suicide than those who never attended religious services.


<!-- chunk -->

### A systematic review of religious beliefs and practices and their effects on

<!-- chunk -->

### health found a beneficial relationship with several other health outcomes,

<!-- chunk -->

### such as decreased rates of cardiovascular disease and all-cause mortality.


<!-- chunk -->

### One challenge with this type of data, however, is the possibility of reverse

<!-- chunk -->

### causation again—it might be that people who are already healthy are able to

<!-- chunk -->

### attend religious services and connect with people there, but people who are

<!-- chunk -->

### already depressed or metabolically compromised aren’t. Despite this

<!-- chunk -->

### possibility, I’ll soon share with you data that suggests some religious

<!-- chunk -->

### practices,

<!-- chunk -->

### such as meditation and ritual prayers, might directly help improve

<!-- chunk -->

### metabolism and mitochondrial health, so these may, in fact, play a causal

<!-- chunk -->

### role in improving health outcomes.

<!-- chunk -->

### For those who don’t believe in God, I don’t mean to imply that you

<!-- chunk -->

### need to start believing in God to improve your health. I share this

<!-- chunk -->

### information because it relates to purpose in life and has been found to

<!-- chunk -->

### influence both metabolic and mental health. There are other ways to find

<!-- chunk -->

### purpose that can play just as powerful a role.

# Addressing Love, Adversity, and Purpose with

# Treatment

<!-- chunk -->

### All of this can play a role in treatment. First, it’s important to point out a

<!-- chunk -->

### general rule of thumb for human health—people need to develop and

<!-- chunk -->

### maintain full lives, ones that include what I call the four Rs: close

<!-- chunk -->

### relationships, meaningful roles in which they contribute to society in some

<!-- chunk -->

### way, adherence to responsibilities and obligations (not just to the people in

<!-- chunk -->

### one’s life, but to society as a whole, such as not breaking laws), and having

<!-- chunk -->

### adequate resources (money, food, shelter, etc.).

<!-- chunk -->

### Many societal factors can interfere with people’s abilities to achieve all

<!-- chunk -->

### of this, including war, trauma, poverty, malnutrition, neglect, racism,

<!-- chunk -->

### homophobia, misogyny, all the ACEs, and so many others. Efforts are

<!-- chunk -->

### needed to address these social problems, for as long as these continue,

<!-- chunk -->

### mental illness will continue to exist. However, people who have been

<!-- chunk -->

### affected by these barriers and atrocities can still recover. They can use

<!-- chunk -->

### science-based approaches to understanding and addressing the impact of

<!-- chunk -->

### these experiences on their metabolism and mitochondria. I’m hopeful this

<!-- chunk -->

### book and the theory of brain energy will help at least some of them.

<!-- chunk -->

## Psychotherapy

<!-- chunk -->

### Addressing the myriad of psychological and social factors that can impact

<!-- chunk -->

### metabolism is an important part of treatment. Friends, family, coworkers,

<!-- chunk -->

### teachers, mentors, or people in the community can often help. Some people,

<!-- chunk -->

### however, will need professional help in the form of psychotherapy.

<!-- chunk -->

### There are countless books and academic articles on how psychotherapy

<!-- chunk -->

### can improve mental health. I won’t even try to review all the research.

<!-- chunk -->

### Instead, I’ll share just a few of the benefits and some possible reasons why

<!-- chunk -->

### it works:

<!-- chunk -->

### •Psychotherapy can help people resolve conflicts with other people

<!-- chunk -->

### and with their roles in life. When people are unable to do this on their

<!-- chunk -->

### own, it increases stress, which takes a toll on metabolism.

<!-- chunk -->

### •Psychotherapy can offer specific skills and strategies to reduce

<!-- chunk -->

### stress and cope with symptoms, which can improve metabolism

<!-- chunk -->

### broadly.

<!-- chunk -->

### •Psychotherapy can help people change behaviors. Cognitive

<!-- chunk -->

### behavioral therapists have long known that changing behaviors can

<!-- chunk -->

### sometimes result in changes in thoughts and feelings. Clinicians

<!-- chunk -->

### treating eating disorders or substance use disorders are often focused

<!-- chunk -->

### on changing behaviors. Addressing behaviors to enhance sleep can

<!-- chunk -->

### produce benefits. As I’ve discussed, all these behaviors play a direct

<!-- chunk -->

### role in metabolism and mitochondrial function.

<!-- chunk -->

### •Psychotherapy can help people understand who they are and what

<!-- chunk -->

### they want from life. This can help some people develop a sense of

<!-- chunk -->

### meaning and purpose, which impacts both mental and metabolic


<!-- chunk -->

### •Psychotherapy can provide new learning to overcome maladaptive

<!-- chunk -->

### beliefs, behaviors, and responses. For example, when people are

<!-- chunk -->

### traumatized, they can sometimes overgeneralize the danger of things

<!-- chunk -->

### that they remember from that experience. Someone who associates

<!-- chunk -->

### certain music, clothes, or cologne with their abuser might be

<!-- chunk -->

### triggered by these everyday experiences. If the abuser is no longer a

<!-- chunk -->

### threat, this is not adaptive or helpful, even though it is

<!-- chunk -->

### understandable. One type of therapy, prolonged exposure, can

<!-- chunk -->

### reduce the stress response to these triggers, which can improve

<!-- chunk -->

### metabolic health.

<!-- chunk -->

### •Psychotherapy can “exercise” underused brain circuits. Remember

<!-- chunk -->

### “use it or lose it”? If a brain region is underdeveloped, some types of

<!-- chunk -->

### psychotherapy can help. Treatments that focus on empathy,

<!-- chunk -->

### relationships, social skills, or improving cognitive abilities can all

<!-- chunk -->

### strengthen brain circuits that have been underdeveloped. This

<!-- chunk -->

### assumes that these brain regions are metabolically healthy enough to

<!-- chunk -->

### learn and store new information. In some cases, they may not be. In

<!-- chunk -->

### these situations, a different metabolic intervention might be needed

<!-- chunk -->

### first. Once brain health is restored, however, “exercising” and

<!-- chunk -->

### restoring health to these brain regions will still be needed.

<!-- chunk -->

### •Psychotherapy can simply offer a relationship with a compassionate

<!-- chunk -->

### and helpful human being. It has long been known that the

<!-- chunk -->

### “therapeutic alliance,” or a good relationship between the therapist

<!-- chunk -->

### and client, plays a role in psychotherapy outcomes. We come back to

<!-- chunk -->

### one of the realities of human existence: We all need other people. We

<!-- chunk -->

### need relationships in which we can express ourselves and be

<!-- chunk -->

### ourselves. Without them, metabolic health can become compromised

<!-- chunk -->

### due to a chronic stress response. For those who don’t have

<!-- chunk -->

### meaningful relationships, psychotherapy can provide one. Obviously,

<!-- chunk -->

### a goal should be to help these people develop sustaining relationships

<!-- chunk -->

### outside of therapy. However, this can take time. For some people,

<!-- chunk -->

### symptoms of their brain dysfunction can make it difficult to achieve.

<!-- chunk -->

## Psychedelic Therapy

<!-- chunk -->

### One emerging treatment related to all of this is the use of psychedelic drugs

<!-- chunk -->

### in psychiatry. Hallucinogens, such as psilocybin or “magic mushrooms,”

<!-- chunk -->

### are receiving increasing attention as a possible treatment for depression,

<!-- chunk -->

### PTSD, and other conditions, with small pilot trials showing benefits. One

<!-- chunk -->

### research group explored how and why these work. They noted,

<!-- chunk -->

### “psychedelics can reliably and robustly induce intense, profound, and

<!-- chunk -->

### personally meaningful

<!-- chunk -->

### experiences that have been referred to as ‘mystical-type’, ‘spiritual’,

<!-- chunk -->

### ‘religious’, ‘existential’, ‘transformative’, ‘pivotal’ or ‘peak’.”


<!-- chunk -->

### They

<!-- chunk -->

### surveyed 866 users of psychedelics over time and found alterations in

<!-- chunk -->

### metaphysical beliefs that often persisted for longer than six months. These

<!-- chunk -->

### persistent metaphysical beliefs were correlated with improved mental health

<!-- chunk -->

### outcomes. This line of research suggests that psychedelics might work by

<!-- chunk -->

### connecting people with spirituality or God, or offering them a sense of

<!-- chunk -->

### meaning and purpose. I should point out that using these on your own is not

<!-- chunk -->

### recommended. The research trials are pairing the use of psychedelics with

<!-- chunk -->

### guided therapy sessions in order to maximize benefits. Using them on your

<!-- chunk -->

### own may result in a “bad trip” or could possibly even trigger a manic or

<!-- chunk -->

### psychotic episode.

<!-- chunk -->

## Stress Reduction

<!-- chunk -->

### Reducing stress is an important part of treatment. In addition to all the ways

<!-- chunk -->

### that psychotherapy and talking with other people can help reduce stress,

<!-- chunk -->

### there are two ways that people can do this on their own: (1) reduce or

<!-- chunk -->

### eliminate stressful environmental factors or (2) attempt to reduce your

<!-- chunk -->

### stress response when it is safe to do so.

<!-- chunk -->

### The easiest way to manage stress is to reduce or eliminate stressors

<!-- chunk -->

### when possible. For some, this is a realistic goal. A highly demanding work

<!-- chunk -->

### or school environment that is overwhelmingly stressful can be managed.

<!-- chunk -->

### The employee can find a new job, or the student and parents can reduce

<!-- chunk -->

### course load, seek academic accommodations for a disability, or change

<!-- chunk -->

### schools to find a better fit. Creating a life that is manageable, pleasurable,

<!-- chunk -->

### and rewarding is something we should all strive to do.

<!-- chunk -->

### When stressful life events occur, people will experience a stress

<!-- chunk -->

### response. This is normal and expected. But when the threatening situation is

<!-- chunk -->

### no longer a danger, reducing the stress response can have powerful,

<!-- chunk -->

### beneficial effects.

<!-- chunk -->

### Stress-reduction practices have been used for millennia. Some of these

<!-- chunk -->

### are not usually thought of as “stress-reduction techniques,” but instead as

<!-- chunk -->

### longstanding religious practices, such as meditation, praying, and chanting.

<!-- chunk -->

### Additional practices include yoga, Pilates, tai chi, qigong, mindfulness, and

<!-- chunk -->

### breathing techniques. Many of these interventions have been shown to

<!-- chunk -->

### improve both mental and metabolic health. I won’t review all the

<!-- chunk -->

### interventions and health conditions, as there are many. However, I will

<!-- chunk -->

### share a couple of studies directly linking these benefits to metabolism and


<!-- chunk -->

### Researchers from Harvard Medical School had long known that the

<!-- chunk -->

### relaxation response can play a powerful role in both mental and metabolic

<!-- chunk -->

### health. The relaxation response, or RR, is a term they use to describe any of

<!-- chunk -->

### the stress-reduction techniques I already mentioned, such as meditation.

<!-- chunk -->

### Studies have demonstrated improvement in hypertension, anxiety,

<!-- chunk -->

### insomnia, diabetes, rheumatoid arthritis, and aging itself. They set out to

<!-- chunk -->

### better understand how this intervention works. They recruited nineteen

<!-- chunk -->

### healthy, long-term practitioners of daily RR practice, nineteen healthy

<!-- chunk -->

### controls, and twenty people who recently completed eight weeks of RR

<!-- chunk -->

### training. They took blood samples from all of them and looked at

<!-- chunk -->

### differences in gene expression. For those doing RR, they found significant

<!-- chunk -->

### differences in genes related to “cellular metabolism, oxidative

<!-- chunk -->

### phosphorylation, generation of reactive oxygen species and response to

<!-- chunk -->

### oxidative stress.” As you now know, these are directly related to


<!-- chunk -->

### In a follow-up study, the researchers recruited twenty-six people who

<!-- chunk -->

### had been practicing regular RR techniques for four to twenty years and

<!-- chunk -->

### another group of twenty-six people who had never regularly practiced RR

<!-- chunk -->

### but were willing to complete eight weeks of training.


<!-- chunk -->

### All the participants

<!-- chunk -->

### were then asked to listen to a twenty-minute RR recording and, in a

<!-- chunk -->

### different session, a twenty-minute health education recording. Blood

<!-- chunk -->

### samples were taken before, immediately after, and fifteen minutes after

<!-- chunk -->

### listening to each recording, and these samples were analyzed for gene

<!-- chunk -->

### expression. The researchers found that “RR practice enhanced expression of

<!-- chunk -->

### genes associated with energy metabolism, mitochondrial function, insulin

<!-- chunk -->

### secretion and telomere maintenance, and reduced expression of genes

<!-- chunk -->

### linked to inflammatory response and stress-related pathways.” A specific

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondrial protein (mitochondrial ATP synthase) and insulin were the

<!-- chunk -->

### top two upregulated molecules. These researchers concluded, “Our results

<!-- chunk -->

### for the first time indicate that RR elicitation, particularly after long-term

<!-- chunk -->

### practice,

<!-- chunk -->

### may evoke its downstream health benefits by improving mitochondrial

<!-- chunk -->

### energy production and utilization and thus promoting mitochondrial

<!-- chunk -->

### resiliency . . . ” As you now know, this is precisely what we are trying to do

<!-- chunk -->

### to improve mental and metabolic health!

<!-- chunk -->

## Rehabilitation Programs

<!-- chunk -->

### Many people with chronic mental disorders lack the skills needed to

<!-- chunk -->

### independently survive and thrive in society. Some don’t know how to make

<!-- chunk -->

### friends. Others don’t know how to manage a daily schedule. Still others

<!-- chunk -->

### don’t know how to hold a job. Many feel they lack purpose in life.

<!-- chunk -->

### Their symptoms prevent most of these people from doing these things.

<!-- chunk -->

### Even if they learned how to do them prior to their illness, they might now

<!-- chunk -->

### be out of practice. For those who developed their illnesses at a young age,

<!-- chunk -->

### they may never have learned these skills in the first place.

<!-- chunk -->

### Restoring metabolic health to their brains won’t automatically teach

<!-- chunk -->

### them everything they need to know. They need training and practice. It’s

<!-- chunk -->

### like rehabilitation after a sports injury. You have to first restore the function

<!-- chunk -->

### of the muscles, bones, ligaments, or tendons, but then the person must also

<!-- chunk -->

### practice and build up strength again. Without this practice, abilities won’t

<!-- chunk -->

### be restored.

<!-- chunk -->

### There are currently rehabilitation programs that offer education, job

<!-- chunk -->

### training, and basic life skills to people with chronic mental disorders.

<!-- chunk -->

### Unfortunately, current research suggests they aren’t very effective. This is

<!-- chunk -->

### likely because brain function was not restored first. If people are trying to

<!-- chunk -->

### do tasks when their brains aren’t working properly, they are being set up for

<!-- chunk -->

### failure. It’s like an athlete trying to run a marathon with a torn ligament.

<!-- chunk -->

### However, if we can restore proper brain function, then rehabilitation has a

<!-- chunk -->

### decent chance of working. The goal is to help people live as productive

<!-- chunk -->

### members of society. Many of them have been beaten down for years and

<!-- chunk -->

### may feel hopeless about this goal. This hopelessness also needs to be

<!-- chunk -->

### addressed.

<!-- chunk -->

### In all of this, compassion from other humans is required. Job programs

<!-- chunk -->

### and reentry into society are needed. These people have to find reasons to

<!-- chunk -->

### live. They need to feel useful. They need to feel respected. All of this

<!-- chunk -->

### requires the involvement of other people.


<!-- chunk -->

### •Our environment and experiences play a critical role in our

<!-- chunk -->

### metabolic and mental health.

<!-- chunk -->

### •Close relationships are important to human health.

<!-- chunk -->

### •Everyone should strive to have at least one role in society that

<!-- chunk -->

### allows them to contribute and feel valued. This can take the form of

<!-- chunk -->

### being a student, employee, caretaker, volunteer, mentor, or other role.

<!-- chunk -->

### It can be as simple as having household chores.

<!-- chunk -->

### •Psychotherapy can play an important role in your metabolic

<!-- chunk -->

### treatment.

<!-- chunk -->

### •Psychotherapists can add many new tools to their arsenals using the

<!-- chunk -->

### brain energy theory. They can assist people in implementing

<!-- chunk -->

### metabolic treatment plans, which may include diet, exercise,

<!-- chunk -->

### exposure to light, and all the other possibilities mentioned in this

<!-- chunk -->

### book.

<!-- chunk -->

### •People with chronic mental disorders may have a lot of catching up

<!-- chunk -->

### to do if they restore their brain health. Their full recovery will require

<!-- chunk -->

### rehabilitation, job training, and other programs.

<!-- chunk -->

### •Society needs to work together to ensure that everyone has adequate

<!-- chunk -->

### relationships, roles, resources, and responsibilities. All humans are

<!-- chunk -->

### not created with equal abilities, but that doesn’t mean that all humans

<!-- chunk -->

### can’t contribute, be safe and secure, and live meaningful lives.

<!-- chunk -->

### Compassion and kindness are essential to this process.

# Success Story: Sarah—Exercise and Finding Her

# Purpose

<!-- chunk -->

### Sarah was seventeen years old when I first met her. She had been diagnosed

<!-- chunk -->

### with ADHD and a learning disability in the eighth grade, had suffered from

<!-- chunk -->

### anxiety and insomnia for as long as she could remember, and began having

<!-- chunk -->

### panic attacks at age fourteen. She was also depressed and had low self-

<!-- chunk -->

### esteem. She struggled in school, despite taking medications for her ADHD,

<!-- chunk -->

### and she had few friends. She had a strong family history of mental illness,

<!-- chunk -->

### with her mother, brother, sister, grandmother, two uncles, and an aunt all

<!-- chunk -->

### having been diagnosed with depression, anxiety disorders, and/or bipolar

<!-- chunk -->

### disorder. That didn’t bode well for her long-term outcome. She had already

<!-- chunk -->

### tried eight different medications, which helped with her ability to

<!-- chunk -->

### concentrate, but also came with side effects and continuing symptoms. She

<!-- chunk -->

### would sometimes get so depressed that she would stay in bed all day. On

<!-- chunk -->

### top of her mental symptoms, she also had migraine headaches and frequent

<!-- chunk -->

### stomach pains.

<!-- chunk -->

### She got into college and did her best, but she struggled. Her family

<!-- chunk -->

### expected her to finish college, and this stressed her even more. She often

<!-- chunk -->

### felt that she could never live up to people’s expectations. She tried more

<!-- chunk -->

### antidepressants, but they didn’t help.

<!-- chunk -->

### Things changed when she decided to take Pilates classes. She loved

<!-- chunk -->

### them! She began exercising regularly and noticed that many of her mood

<!-- chunk -->

### and anxiety symptoms were getting better. At age twenty-three, she got a

<!-- chunk -->

### job as an instructor at the studio—she was now exercising almost every day

<!-- chunk -->

### for hours per day. That was the game-changer for her. She came to an

<!-- chunk -->

### appointment after about two months on the job saying, “I feel great! I’ve

<!-- chunk -->

### never felt this good in my entire life.” On top of the exercise, she had

<!-- chunk -->

### become passionate about helping others improve their health, had made

<!-- chunk -->

### new friends, and now had a boyfriend. Despite her parents’ expectations

<!-- chunk -->

### that she finish college, she decided to drop out and focus on exercise as her

<!-- chunk -->

### career. That was almost ten years ago and Sarah continues to do well to this

<!-- chunk -->

### day. She still takes stimulant medication for her ADHD symptoms, but was

<!-- chunk -->

### able to stop

<!-- chunk -->

### all the other medications. After tapering off them, she said she actually felt

<!-- chunk -->

### even better.

<!-- chunk -->

### Sarah’s story highlights the power of exercise as a metabolic treatment,

<!-- chunk -->

### but also the psychological and social aspects of finding meaning and

<!-- chunk -->

### purpose in life, stress-reduction practices, having a social support network,

<!-- chunk -->

### and not letting the expectations of others rule us. We are all different, and

<!-- chunk -->

### want—and need—different things. Sarah found her own way to metabolic

<!-- chunk -->

### and mental health.

# W


# Why Do Current Treatments Work?

<!-- chunk -->

### hat do talking, chemicals, electricity, magnetic fields, and brain

<!-- chunk -->

### surgery have in common? They are all evidence-based treatments

<!-- chunk -->

### for mental disorders! So, why do they work? They all impact

<!-- chunk -->

### metabolism and mitochondria.

<!-- chunk -->

### I’ve already addressed medications and psychotherapy in earlier

<!-- chunk -->

### chapters. Now, I’d like to briefly explain how I think about these other

<!-- chunk -->

### treatments in the context of the brain energy theory. If this theory is true,

<!-- chunk -->

### there should be plausible explanations for why these treatments work, at

<!-- chunk -->

### least for some people.

# ECT and TMS

<!-- chunk -->

### Electroconvulsive therapy (ECT) and transcranial magnetic stimulation

<!-- chunk -->

### (TMS) are effective interventions for a wide variety of mental disorders.

<!-- chunk -->

### For some conditions, such as severe depression or catatonia, ECT is

<!-- chunk -->

### considered

<!-- chunk -->

### the gold standard and most effective treatment that we have available. Why

<!-- chunk -->

### do they work? The field doesn’t currently offer a comprehensive

<!-- chunk -->

### explanation. Changes in neurotransmitter levels and hormones are thought

<!-- chunk -->

### to play a role, as well as increased neuroplasticity. The brain energy theory

<!-- chunk -->

### offers one comprehensive explanation.

<!-- chunk -->

### Electricity from ECT and electromagnetic energy from TMS deliver

<!-- chunk -->

### energy directly to the brain. There’s probably no better example of a

<!-- chunk -->

### treatment being related to “brain energy.” This energy stimulates

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondria, and in turn, mitochondrial biogenesis. When we push

<!-- chunk -->

### ourselves in exercise, the body senses that it needs more capacity, so it

<!-- chunk -->

### produces more mitochondria to deliver this capacity. ECT and TMS appear

<!-- chunk -->

### to do the same thing. This can improve neurotransmitter and hormonal

<!-- chunk -->

### imbalances, as well as increasing neuroplasticity. These findings can be

<!-- chunk -->

### understood through mitochondria.

<!-- chunk -->

### The direct effects of ECT on mitochondria have not been extensively

<!-- chunk -->

### studied. However, one group of researchers did demonstrate increases in

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondrial activity of the hippocampus, striatum, and cortex of rats after

<!-- chunk -->

### delivering ECT.


<!-- chunk -->

### Another group found increased mitochondrial biogenesis

<!-- chunk -->

### and enhanced synapse formation in the hippocampus after just one ECT

<!-- chunk -->

### treatment.


<!-- chunk -->

### They also found that a series of ten treatments resulted in lasting

<!-- chunk -->

### improvements in the number of mitochondria and synapse formations three

<!-- chunk -->

### months later.

<!-- chunk -->

### TMS has been shown to improve oxidative stress, reduce inflammation,

<!-- chunk -->

### increase neuroplasticity, and impact neurotransmitter levels.


<!-- chunk -->

### As you know,

<!-- chunk -->

### these all relate to mitochondrial function. The evidence for the direct effects

<!-- chunk -->

### of TMS on mitochondria, however, is also sparse. One study found an

<!-- chunk -->

### increase in ATP levels in a rat model of stroke.


<!-- chunk -->

### Another study found

<!-- chunk -->

### increased mitochondrial integrity after TMS treatment, also in a rat model

<!-- chunk -->

### of stroke.


<!-- chunk -->

### Interestingly, psychiatry isn’t the only field that uses electricity to fix

<!-- chunk -->

### metabolic problems. Cardiology commonly uses cardioversion (or shocking

<!-- chunk -->

### of the heart) when the heart is failing metabolically. Sometimes it just needs

<!-- chunk -->

### a jump-start.

# Brain Surgery and Electrical Stimulators

<!-- chunk -->

### Brain surgery is sometimes used as a last resort in people with chronic,

<!-- chunk -->

### debilitating mental disorders. Sometimes it can help. Why?

<!-- chunk -->

### This is fairly straightforward. If an area of the brain is overactive due to

<!-- chunk -->

### hyperexcitable brain cells, cutting it off from the rest of the brain can

<!-- chunk -->

### decrease symptoms. This is commonly done in epilepsy treatment. The

<!-- chunk -->

### same applies to hyperexcitable brain regions causing mental symptoms.

<!-- chunk -->

### In other cases, some brain surgeries implant electrodes to stimulate

<!-- chunk -->

### cells. This, too, is straightforward. It’s a way to stimulate underactive brain

<!-- chunk -->

### regions. It’s used in cardiology when pacemakers are implanted to deal with

<!-- chunk -->

### reduced function of the pacemaker cells of the heart. It works much the

<!-- chunk -->

### same way for underactive brain regions. Paradoxically, fast-paced

<!-- chunk -->

### stimulators can sometimes suppress overactive brain regions.

<!-- chunk -->

### Electrical stimulators have also been applied to the vagus nerve. This is

<!-- chunk -->

### called vagal nerve stimulation, or VNS. This has been helpful for epilepsy

<!-- chunk -->

### and depression, and is also being studied for PTSD, Alzheimer’s disease,

<!-- chunk -->

### schizophrenia, OCD, panic disorder, bipolar disorder, and fibromyalgia.


<!-- chunk -->

### Again, one treatment for so many seemingly unrelated conditions. The

<!-- chunk -->

### brain energy theory connects them all.


<!-- chunk -->

### For severe, treatment-resistant conditions or in life-threatening

<!-- chunk -->

### emergencies, ECT, TMS, VNS, and/or brain surgery can all play a role in

<!-- chunk -->

### treatment. However, the brain energy theory offers numerous other

<!-- chunk -->

### treatment options before these might be needed.


# Putting It All Together

# DEVELOPING YOUR METABOLIC

# TREATMENT PLAN

<!-- chunk -->

### When a flower doesn’t bloom, you fix the environment in which it grows, not

<!-- chunk -->

### the flower.

<!-- chunk -->

## -Alexander den Heijer

<!-- chunk -->

### he above quote is a powerful metaphor for addressing problems with
