<!-- chunk -->

### metabolism and mitochondria. In most cases, there is not a “defect” in

<!-- chunk -->

### the person but in the environment. “Fixing” a mental illness requires

<!-- chunk -->

### identifying the problems and addressing them. The “environment” in this

<!-- chunk -->

### case is writ large. It includes all factors that affect metabolism and

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondria, such as diet, exercise, stress, light, sleep, hormones,

<!-- chunk -->

### inflammation, relationships, love, and meaning and purpose in life, to name

<!-- chunk -->

### just a few. Yes, some people may have inherited epigenetic factors, such as

<!-- chunk -->

### micro-RNAs, and these might be a contributing cause to their mental

<!-- chunk -->

### illness, but these can be changed, too. Metabolism is malleable, and there

<!-- chunk -->

### are many ways to improve it.

<!-- chunk -->

### You will recall that I compared all mental illnesses to delirium. Each and

<!-- chunk -->

### every case of delirium is different, even though the symptoms can be

<!-- chunk -->

### similar and the diagnosis the same. Solving the problem of delirium

<!-- chunk -->

### requires medical detective work to understand what is causing it.

<!-- chunk -->

### Oftentimes, it’s more than one thing—a perfect storm in which many

<!-- chunk -->

### assaults on mitochondria are occurring at the same time. They all need to be

<!-- chunk -->

### identified and dealt with. This applies to every case of mental illness as

<!-- chunk -->

### well.

<!-- chunk -->

### The brain energy theory supports existing treatments for mental

<!-- chunk -->

### disorders. These will continue to play an important role. However, it also

<!-- chunk -->

### calls for radical changes. To address problems with metabolism,

<!-- chunk -->

### comprehensive approaches are usually required. Sometimes, identifying a

<!-- chunk -->

### simple problem and delivering a single treatment can work. Vitamin and

<!-- chunk -->

### hormone deficiencies are examples, and sometimes, simply taking an

<!-- chunk -->

### antidepressant can do the trick. But unfortunately, more often than not,

<!-- chunk -->

### simple solutions are usually not the answer.

<!-- chunk -->

### This goes against a pervasive message that we hear on a daily basis.

<!-- chunk -->

### Everyone wants simple solutions. We see on television every day that we

<!-- chunk -->

### can fix our problems with a pill. We just need to talk to our doctors and get

<!-- chunk -->

### those new prescriptions. If one pill doesn’t do it, by all means, take more.

<!-- chunk -->

### We hear the same messages from diet gurus and health experts. Don’t eat

<!-- chunk -->

### fat, and you’ll lose weight. Take this vitamin or supplement, and that will

<!-- chunk -->

### fix things.

<!-- chunk -->

### These messages, of course, are alluring. All we need to do is take a pill

<!-- chunk -->

### or do a simple intervention to fix our problems. They are much more

<!-- chunk -->

### appealing than doing the complicated work of figuring out what’s actually

<!-- chunk -->

### wrong, and then correcting the problem or problems, which might include

<!-- chunk -->

### making lifestyle changes. In reality, the simplistic fixes usually don’t work,

<!-- chunk -->

### at least not fully or permanently. The skyrocketing rates of mental and

<!-- chunk -->

### metabolic disorders are a clear testament to the failure of this approach. The

<!-- chunk -->

### field of medicine is increasingly recognizing this with its push toward

<!-- chunk -->

### personalized medicine, which acknowledges that there are many pathways

<!-- chunk -->

### to illness and that one-size-fits-all solutions often don’t work. People need

<!-- chunk -->

### unique treatment plans tailored to their individual situations and

<!-- chunk -->

### requirements.

# Working with a Clinician

<!-- chunk -->

### When treating serious mental disorders, it’s critically important that you

<!-- chunk -->

### work with a competent clinician. Serious mental disorders can be

<!-- chunk -->

### dangerous, and people should not expect to treat these without help.

<!-- chunk -->

### “Serious” symptoms include things like hallucinations, delusions, suicidal

<!-- chunk -->

### thinking or behaviors, self-injury, aggression, out-of-control substance use,

<!-- chunk -->

### severe eating disorders, and other dangerous behaviors. These are not do-it-

<!-- chunk -->

### yourself projects to handle on your own at home. You deserve competent

<!-- chunk -->

### and compassionate medical care, so please get help developing and

<!-- chunk -->

### implementing your metabolic treatment plan. It’s important that you have

<!-- chunk -->

### support and expertise to get to a healthy and safe place as quickly as

<!-- chunk -->

### possible.

<!-- chunk -->

### For those with chronic disorders, even if just mild or moderate, you may

<!-- chunk -->

### also need to work with a clinician. A thorough medical evaluation might

<!-- chunk -->

### reveal factors that are playing a role in your illness.

# Where to Start

<!-- chunk -->

### All the contributing causes and interventions I have discussed are

<!-- chunk -->

### interrelated and affect each other. If one factor is off, others will be, too. For

<!-- chunk -->

### example, if your sleep is off, your eating behavior and use of substances

<!-- chunk -->

### might be as well. Even something like the gut microbiome can be affected

<!-- chunk -->

### by sleep, light exposure, and stress. So if your microbiome is off, changing

<!-- chunk -->

### these other contributing causes might correct that issue. Alternatively,

<!-- chunk -->

### changing your microbiome might improve your sleep or stress levels. Think

<!-- chunk -->

### of everything as being part of one or more feedback loops. Therefore, all

<!-- chunk -->

### the treatments outlined in this book are possible treatments for you, even if

<!-- chunk -->

### you don’t recognize that you have a problem in these areas. Addressing

<!-- chunk -->

### mental symptoms might require changes in your sleep, diet, or light

<!-- chunk -->

### exposure.

<!-- chunk -->

### In some cases, it won’t be clear what’s causing your metabolic

<!-- chunk -->

### dysfunction. Worry not. Treatment approaches can still work. The goal of

<!-- chunk -->

### treatment will be to use interventions that are known to improve

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondrial function and/or increase the supply of mitochondria. In most

<!-- chunk -->

### cases, if our cells have enough normal and healthy mitochondria, they

<!-- chunk -->

### function properly. Mitochondria know what to do. They can often fix the

<!-- chunk -->

### problem on their own.

<!-- chunk -->

### Some of you might be overwhelmed by all the treatment options. When

<!-- chunk -->

### trying to improve your metabolic health, recognize that success requires a

<!-- chunk -->

### multifaceted approach that will take time. But this means you don’t have to

<!-- chunk -->

### do everything at once—nor should you. Start with one treatment, try it for a

<!-- chunk -->

### few weeks or months, and then add additional treatments as needed.

<!-- chunk -->

### Oftentimes, as your metabolism begins to improve, it gives you more

<!-- chunk -->

### energy and motivation. This makes it easier to make other changes. As

<!-- chunk -->

### people begin to feel better, they often surprise themselves with how much

<!-- chunk -->

### more they can accomplish. When people complete their “metabolic

<!-- chunk -->

### treatment plans,” they often don’t recognize themselves. Not only can they

<!-- chunk -->

### reduce their symptoms of mental illness, lose weight, or have more exercise

<!-- chunk -->

### endurance, but they usually also notice stress reduction, more self-

<!-- chunk -->

### confidence, more connections with people, newfound abilities, and other

<!-- chunk -->

### life-enhancing positives.

<!-- chunk -->

### In most cases, you can decide what intervention you want to start with.

<!-- chunk -->

### Once you choose one, make it a SMART goal: Specific, Measurable,

<!-- chunk -->

### Achievable, Realistic, and Timely. Once you master one intervention, add

<!-- chunk -->

### another. Keep going until you achieve your desired results.

<!-- chunk -->

### In some cases, however, one intervention may need to take priority over

<!-- chunk -->

### all others, as it could be having catastrophic effects on your metabolism.

<!-- chunk -->

### Two obvious examples are heavy substance use or living in an abusive

<!-- chunk -->

### environment. For those who are heavily using alcohol or drugs, the other

<!-- chunk -->

### interventions will likely be futile until the substance use stops, so address

<!-- chunk -->

### that first. For people in physically abusive relationships, the first step

<!-- chunk -->

### should be to focus on a plan to get out of that environment, as difficult and

<!-- chunk -->

### dangerous as that might be. People in this situation will likely need

<!-- chunk -->

### tremendous support from family, friends, or a domestic violence program.

<!-- chunk -->

### Trying other metabolic interventions without this first step will likely be

<!-- chunk -->

### futile to address their mental and metabolic problems.

<!-- chunk -->

### All the interventions I’ve discussed have the potential to change your

<!-- chunk -->

### metabolism. As a rule of thumb, metabolic interventions can have four

<!-- chunk -->

### types of effects on the body and the brain:

<!-- chunk -->

## 1.Initiation—When you first make a change, this can abruptly shift

<!-- chunk -->

### your metabolism. Sometimes, this can be helpful. Other times, it can

<!-- chunk -->

### make things worse, at least initially.

<!-- chunk -->

## 2.Adaptation—Whenever you make a metabolic change, your body

<!-- chunk -->

### will work to adapt. These adaptations are usually designed to

<!-- chunk -->

### counteract the effects of the metabolic intervention. They usually

<!-- chunk -->

### don’t negate the intervention, but they will often lessen its effects

<!-- chunk -->

### compared to the initiation phase.

<!-- chunk -->

## 3.Maintenance—At some point, your metabolism will become fully

<!-- chunk -->

### adapted to the intervention and your body and brain will feel more

<!-- chunk -->

### stable. You can always increase the dose or intensity of the

<!-- chunk -->

### intervention, which will then take you back to the initiation and

<!-- chunk -->

### adaptation phases again.

<!-- chunk -->

## 4.Withdrawal—If metabolic interventions are reduced or stopped

<!-- chunk -->

### quickly, there is usually a withdrawal reaction. Metabolism will often

<!-- chunk -->

### rebound too high or too low in these situations, which can produce

<!-- chunk -->

### withdrawal symptoms.

<!-- chunk -->

### All these situations can happen with all the treatments I’ve mentioned,

<!-- chunk -->

### including medications, light therapy, changes in diet, changes in the gut

<!-- chunk -->

### microbiome, supplement use, and even psychological and social

<!-- chunk -->

### interventions.

<!-- chunk -->

### Remember that you’re looking for interventions that will improve your

<!-- chunk -->

### metabolic health over the long run. So, even if an intervention makes things

<!-- chunk -->

### worse initially, such as experiencing irritability when starting a new diet, if

<!-- chunk -->

### the maintenance phase will lead to improved metabolic health, it’s worth

<!-- chunk -->

### pursuing. Obviously, it needs to be done in a way that is safe and tolerable,

<!-- chunk -->

### but getting to the maintenance phase is the goal. Likewise, other things,

<!-- chunk -->

### such as heavy alcohol use, can make things feel better in the short run

<!-- chunk -->

### (initiation phase), but can impair metabolism over the long run

<!-- chunk -->

### (maintenance phase). Stopping its use (withdrawal phase) can be

<!-- chunk -->

### particularly difficult and dangerous for heavy drinkers. It’s important to

<!-- chunk -->

### keep these in mind, as starting and stopping treatments needs to be done

<!-- chunk -->

### safely.

# Inpatient and Residential Treatment Programs

<!-- chunk -->

### For some people with serious mental disorders, designing a comprehensive

<!-- chunk -->

### treatment plan might be impossible to do on their own. By definition, they

<!-- chunk -->

### have impaired brain function. They may not be able to stay on task, learn

<!-- chunk -->

### new information easily, stick to a diet, or fit all the changes into a daily

<!-- chunk -->

### schedule. It doesn’t mean they can’t do it, or that they won’t benefit from it,

<!-- chunk -->

### but they may need help. For others, mental symptoms can sometimes

<!-- chunk -->

### threaten their safety or the safety of others and trying treatments on an

<!-- chunk -->

### outpatient basis may be dangerous. For all these people, we need to develop

<!-- chunk -->

### inpatient and residential metabolic treatment programs. These programs can

<!-- chunk -->

### customize treatment plans to people’s specific needs. They can provide

<!-- chunk -->

### support not only through professionals working at the program, but through

<!-- chunk -->

### the peer support from other participants. These will be environments where

<!-- chunk -->

### everyone is working to improve their mental and metabolic health together.

# Designing Your Treatment Plan

<!-- chunk -->

### •If you have serious, dangerous, or chronic symptoms, you should

<!-- chunk -->

### work with a clinician.

<!-- chunk -->

### •Identify contributing causes that are leading to severe metabolic

<!-- chunk -->

### impairment or threatening your safety (e.g., suicide attempts, severe

<!-- chunk -->

### substance use, living in a physically abusive environment, an

<!-- chunk -->

### out-of-control eating disorder, severe lack of sleep, etc.). If you have

<!-- chunk -->

### any of these, you need to address them first.

<!-- chunk -->

### •Choose one or more of the treatments outlined in this book that you

<!-- chunk -->

### think might help.

<!-- chunk -->

### •Implement the treatment and give it at least three months to start

<!-- chunk -->

### working.

<!-- chunk -->

### •If the treatment improves any of your symptoms, even if just a little

<!-- chunk -->

### bit, keep doing it.

<!-- chunk -->

### •If a treatment doesn’t help at all after three months, feel free to stop

<!-- chunk -->

### it.

<!-- chunk -->

### •If a treatment helps but doesn’t resolve all your symptoms, add

<!-- chunk -->

### another treatment. You are now developing your multifaceted

<!-- chunk -->

### treatment plan.

<!-- chunk -->

### •Continue to add or try treatments until you achieve your desired

<!-- chunk -->

### results.

<!-- chunk -->

### Your goals might change over time. Initially, you might only want to get rid

<!-- chunk -->

### of one symptom. That’s fine. As life goes on, you might decide that you’d

<!-- chunk -->

### like to improve some other areas as well. Life is a journey. We all have

<!-- chunk -->

### areas of strength, but we also all have areas of weakness and vulnerability.

<!-- chunk -->

### No one is perfect. I hope that you will make the most of your life and strive

<!-- chunk -->

### to improve your health as much as possible. But I also hope that you can

<!-- chunk -->

### reach a point of gratitude for the health and strength that you do have and

<!-- chunk -->

### simply enjoy it.

# Success Story: Beth—Overmedicated and Still Sick

<!-- chunk -->

### Beth began psychiatric treatment at age nine after being diagnosed with

<!-- chunk -->

### ADHD. She was prescribed stimulants and did well in school, getting

<!-- chunk -->

### mostly

<!-- chunk -->

### As and Bs. However, she was still impulsive and often interrupted people.

<!-- chunk -->

### As a result, she didn’t have many friends, often felt criticized or rejected by

<!-- chunk -->

### others, and struggled with low self-esteem. By high school, things were

<!-- chunk -->

### much worse. She had chronic depression, was frequently suicidal, and

<!-- chunk -->

### began cutting herself with knives or razor blades. She tried antidepressants,

<!-- chunk -->

### more stimulants, mood stabilizers, anxiety medications, and even

<!-- chunk -->

### antipsychotics, but her symptoms only worsened. By the time she was in

<!-- chunk -->

### college, she had been hospitalized many times.

<!-- chunk -->

### Beth was twenty-one when I first met her. She was diagnosed with

<!-- chunk -->

### chronic depression, panic disorder, borderline personality disorder,

<!-- chunk -->

### premenstrual dysphoric disorder, and ADHD. She was on five medications,

<!-- chunk -->

### and they clearly weren’t helping. In fact, she was often lethargic and out of

<!-- chunk -->

### it. She had been in a few car accidents as a result. She had returned home

<!-- chunk -->

### during her summer break from college for more intensive psychiatric

<!-- chunk -->

### treatment. At first, I prescribed even more antipsychotic and mood-

<!-- chunk -->

### stabilizing medications, which often gave her new side effects or simply

<!-- chunk -->

### didn’t work. At the same time, we began dialectical behavior therapy

<!-- chunk -->

### (DBT), which is a type of psychotherapy focused on skills that can help

<!-- chunk -->

### people better manage their emotions, suicidal impulses, and self-injury.

<!-- chunk -->

### Beth and I both believed that medications had not helped her and, in

<!-- chunk -->

### fact, may have contributed to her problems, so we agreed to slowly taper

<!-- chunk -->

### her off them. This was extremely difficult and dangerous. Almost every

<!-- chunk -->

### time we reduced a medication, even if just by a tiny amount, she would

<!-- chunk -->

### experience an increase in symptoms—depression, suicidal impulses, or

<!-- chunk -->

### urges to cut herself. We used DBT skills to manage these symptoms and

<!-- chunk -->

### keep her safe, but we continued on with the medication taper. It took several

<!-- chunk -->

### years to get her off everything. By the time we did, things were much better.

<!-- chunk -->

### She was able to keep herself safe, had been able to hold a job, and had

<!-- chunk -->

### finished college.

<!-- chunk -->

### The next phase of her recovery started with exercise. She began riding

<!-- chunk -->

### her bicycle outside and really got into it. She decided to work up to a

<!-- chunk -->

### charity ride. She then joined Weight Watchers to lose some weight, which

<!-- chunk -->

### helped even more. Her psychiatric symptoms were all but gone now. After

<!-- chunk -->

### many

<!-- chunk -->

### long discussions between us and her family and friends, we agreed that she

<!-- chunk -->

### no longer needed therapy or any psychiatric treatment. But that didn’t stop

<!-- chunk -->

### her metabolic treatment! Beth went on to become an accomplished athlete,

<!-- chunk -->

### competing in triathlons and ultramarathons. She got married. She got a full-

<!-- chunk -->

### time job.

<!-- chunk -->

### Today, about ten years later, Beth continues to feel great and has no

<!-- chunk -->

### psychiatric symptoms. When I ran into her father (who happens to be a

<!-- chunk -->

### physician) recently, he gave me an update on how well she was doing and

<!-- chunk -->

### said, “You know that you saved her life. You really did. I can’t imagine that

<!-- chunk -->

### she would still be here today if it wasn’t for you.”

<!-- chunk -->

### Beth’s case illustrates the common problem of having multiple

<!-- chunk -->

### diagnoses, getting lots of treatment, and still not doing well. In fact, it

<!-- chunk -->

### appears all but certain that the medications were contributing to her

<!-- chunk -->

### problems, if not causing them. This doesn’t mean that medications can’t be

<!-- chunk -->

### immensely helpful for some people. I think they can be. However, for Beth,

<!-- chunk -->

### they appeared to be making matters worse. Some of the medications that

<!-- chunk -->

### she was taking are known to impair metabolism and mitochondrial function,

<!-- chunk -->

### so the brain energy theory offers an explanation for her improvement off the

<!-- chunk -->

### medications. But that wasn’t enough for her full recovery. She also

<!-- chunk -->

### exercised, and lost weight, and found love, and a job, and self-respect.

<!-- chunk -->

### These all played a role in her extraordinary recovery.


# A New Day for Mental and

# Metabolic Health

<!-- chunk -->

### he brain energy theory offers a new model of mental health. It’s about

<!-- chunk -->

### much more than just brain function; it’s about metabolism and

<!-- chunk -->

### mitochondria, which impact almost all aspects of human health, aging,

<!-- chunk -->

### and longevity. This new model cuts across diagnostic categories and

<!-- chunk -->

### addresses many disorders all at once. It applies not only to those diagnosed

<!-- chunk -->

### with “mental” illness, but also to those who suffer from related metabolic

<!-- chunk -->

### disorders, such as obesity, diabetes, cardiovascular disease, Alzheimer’s

<!-- chunk -->

### disease, epilepsy, and chronic pain disorders. Almost all people with these

<!-- chunk -->

### disorders have at least some “mental” symptoms, and people with mental

<!-- chunk -->

### disorders are more likely to develop these related disorders. This new

<!-- chunk -->

### model offers the hope of preventing illnesses and keeping people happy,

<!-- chunk -->

### healthy, and productive for longer than is currently possible.

<!-- chunk -->

### The theory of brain energy is a significant breakthrough that finally

<!-- chunk -->

### connects the dots to give us a clearer picture of mental illness. The science

<!-- chunk -->

### and evidence are integrated into one framework, uniting the biological,

<!-- chunk -->

### psychological, and social theories of mental illness. As we embrace the idea

<!-- chunk -->

### that mental disorders are no longer syndromes but metabolic disorders of

<!-- chunk -->

### the brain, new solutions become obvious. We need to restore brain energy

<!-- chunk -->

### by normalizing metabolism and the function of mitochondria. Once we do

<!-- chunk -->

### this, the symptoms of mental illness will begin to resolve.

<!-- chunk -->

### The good news is that this new understanding lets us apply existing

<!-- chunk -->

### treatments more effectively and suggests novel treatment options we

<!-- chunk -->

### already have access to, including all the treatments I’ve shared in this book.

<!-- chunk -->

### We don’t have to wait years before trying them. This doesn’t mean that

<!-- chunk -->

### what we have now is enough—not all interventions will work for everyone,

<!-- chunk -->

### and we need more research and additional treatments. Finding new

<!-- chunk -->

### treatments, however, will be immensely easier now that we’ve connected

<!-- chunk -->

### the dots and identified the core problem. It’s a problem that can now be

<!-- chunk -->

### solved with science and research, no longer some abstract mystery waiting

<!-- chunk -->

### on a miracle.

<!-- chunk -->

### Once we begin thinking about all these disorders through the lens of

<!-- chunk -->

### metabolism and mitochondria, the possibilities for improvement are

<!-- chunk -->

### endless. We can develop diagnostic tools to assess people’s metabolic

<!-- chunk -->

### health. We can develop evidence-based strategies and therapeutics to

<!-- chunk -->

### address metabolic and mitochondrial dysfunction. We can focus on better

<!-- chunk -->

### understanding the impact on mitochondria and metabolism of medications,

<!-- chunk -->

### alcohol, tobacco, recreational drugs, diets and foods, and toxins.

<!-- chunk -->

### But we need resources to do this work. We need big changes! We need

<!-- chunk -->

### multidisciplinary healthcare teams working together to restore metabolic

<!-- chunk -->

### health in people. These teams will include physicians, nurses,

<!-- chunk -->

### psychotherapists, social workers, physical and occupational therapists,

<!-- chunk -->

### pharmacists, dietitians, personal trainers, health and wellness coaches, and

<!-- chunk -->

### many others. Health insurance will need to cover some of these costs. The

<!-- chunk -->

### biotech and pharmaceutical industries will need to rise to the challenge of

<!-- chunk -->

### developing more effective therapeutics. Government will need to be

<!-- chunk -->

### involved. We need research funding for all this work and parity for mental

<!-- chunk -->

### health services. We may also have metabolic toxins in our everyday lives

<!-- chunk -->

### that need to be regulated and/or eliminated. And of course, each and every

<!-- chunk -->

### one of us will need

<!-- chunk -->

### to do our part as well. We need self-help groups, support groups, and

<!-- chunk -->

### advocacy initiatives. We need a society that is just, fair, compassionate,

<!-- chunk -->

### peaceful, and cooperative. We need to ensure that all people have

<!-- chunk -->

### opportunities to live meaningful lives. People need to feel safe. They also

<!-- chunk -->

### benefit from feeling respected. Obviously, this is all easier said than done.

<!-- chunk -->

### In many ways, it represents a type of utopia. We all know that will take time

<!-- chunk -->

### to achieve. But we don’t need to wait for utopia in order to do something.

<!-- chunk -->

### And so, I call on you and ask for your help. To turn this hope into

<!-- chunk -->

### reality, we need a grassroots movement. Just as there were movements for

<!-- chunk -->

### HIV/AIDS and breast cancer, we desperately need a movement calling for

<!-- chunk -->

### radical changes in the way we understand and treat mental illness.

<!-- chunk -->

### Educating and informing people will take time and effort. You can help by

<!-- chunk -->

### spreading the word about the theory of brain energy. This movement needs

<!-- chunk -->

### you, your friends, and your family. I’m not asking for myself, but on behalf

<!-- chunk -->

### of the countless people who are suffering from mental illness alone and in

<!-- chunk -->

### silence; on behalf of those begging for more effective treatments and better

<!-- chunk -->

### lives; on behalf of those who are tormented on a daily basis by their mental

<!-- chunk -->

### symptoms; on behalf of those who have lost all hope; on behalf of all the

<!-- chunk -->

### people who continue to hide in their homes in shame and humiliation from

<!-- chunk -->

### the stigma of mental illness; and in memory of the countless people who

<!-- chunk -->

### couldn’t hold on and are no longer with us. Let’s put an end to this

<!-- chunk -->

### suffering, once and for all. Let’s not waste another day.

<!-- chunk -->

### Please visit www.brainenergy.com in order to learn more and get

<!-- chunk -->

### involved.

# Acknowledgments

<!-- chunk -->

### When I began writing Brain Energy, some people recommended that I make

<!-- chunk -->

### it a simple self-help book as opposed to a more rigorous science book

<!-- chunk -->

### because simple messages are easier to sell and many publishers aren’t

<!-- chunk -->

### interested in science books. I want to thank my publisher, BenBella, and in

<!-- chunk -->

### particular Leah Wilson and Alexa Stevenson, for believing in me and this

<!-- chunk -->

### book and for recognizing that people are, in fact, interested in science and

<!-- chunk -->

### complexity, especially when it has the potential to transform a field and

<!-- chunk -->

### improve people’s lives.

<!-- chunk -->

<!-- chunk -->

### feedback, and for encouraging me to include more science and evidence,

<!-- chunk -->

### not less. Your initial “healthy skepticism” of the brain energy theory pushed

<!-- chunk -->

### me to write an immensely better book.

<!-- chunk -->

### encouragement

<!-- chunk -->

### writing.

<!-- chunk -->

### including medical school in these years), every single one of you helped me

<!-- chunk -->

### write this book, because I learned something about mental illness and the

<!-- chunk -->

### human condition from each of you. Thank you for the honor and privilege

<!-- chunk -->

### of being your psychiatrist. Thanks to the ones who were willing to try my

<!-- chunk -->

### “metabolic treatments,” and especially those willing to share their stories in

<!-- chunk -->

### this book or appear publicly with me in presentations, on television, and on

<!-- chunk -->

### national radio. To the ones who never got better under my care despite

<!-- chunk -->

### trying treatment after treatment, I want to apologize on behalf of myself and

<!-- chunk -->

### my profession. It was you who taught me to question and challenge my own

<!-- chunk -->

### work and the mental health field, and to not accept the unacceptable

<!-- chunk -->

### paradigm. You inspired me to ponder endlessly about neuroscience,

<!-- chunk -->

### physiology, and human existence. You forced me to look for better answers.

<!-- chunk -->

### My greatest hope is that this book might represent the beginning of those

<!-- chunk -->

### answers.

# About the Author

<!-- chunk -->

### Christopher M. Palmer, MD, is a Harvard psychiatrist and researcher

<!-- chunk -->

### working at the interface of metabolism and mental health. He is the director

<!-- chunk -->

### of the Department of Postgraduate and Continuing Education at McLean

<!-- chunk -->

### Hospital and an assistant professor of psychiatry at Harvard Medical

<!-- chunk -->

### School. For more than two decades, he has held leadership roles in

<!-- chunk -->

### psychiatric education at Harvard, McLean Hospital, and nationally. He

<!-- chunk -->

### spent more than fifteen years conducting neuroscience research in the areas

<!-- chunk -->

### of substance use and sleep disorders. On top of these academic pursuits, he

<!-- chunk -->

### has continued to practice psychiatry, working with people who have

<!-- chunk -->

### treatment-resistant mental disorders using a variety of standard treatments.

<!-- chunk -->

### He has been pioneering the use of the medical ketogenic diet in the

<!-- chunk -->

### treatment of psychiatric disorders—conducting research in this area,

<!-- chunk -->

### treating patients, publishing academic articles, and speaking globally on this

<!-- chunk -->

### topic. Most recently, he has developed the first comprehensive theory of

<!-- chunk -->

### what causes mental illness, integrating biological, psychological, and social

<!-- chunk -->

### research into one unifying theory—the brain energy theory of mental

<!-- chunk -->

### illness.
